Acid-base abnormality

background  
- Acid-base abnormalities refer to imbalances in the body’s pH level, affecting normal physiological function  

risk factors  
- Chronic diseases such as diabetes and renal failure  
- Severe infections or sepsis  
- Respiratory conditions like COPD and asthma  
- Drug overdose or intoxication  
- Electrolyte imbalances  

causes  
- Respiratory acidosis due to hypoventilation or lung disease  
- Respiratory alkalosis related to hyperventilation  
- Metabolic acidosis from lactic acidosis or renal failure  
- Metabolic alkalosis due to vomiting or diuretic use  
- Primary disturbances in bicarbonate or carbon dioxide levels  

pathophysiology  
- Disturbance in acid-base homeostasis affects enzymatic reactions  
- Compensation occurs via respiratory or renal mechanisms  
- Transcellular shifts of hydrogen or bicarbonate ions  
- Electrolyte imbalances can exacerbate the dysfunction  

signs and symptoms  
- Confusion or altered mental status  
- Dyspnoea or difficulty breathing  
- Tachycardia and hypotension  
- Nausea or vomiting  
- Muscle twitching or weakness  

investigations  
- Arterial blood gas (ABG) analysis to determine pH and gas levels  
- Serum electrolytes to assess bicarbonate and potassium levels  
- Blood urea nitrogen and creatinine to evaluate renal function  
- Lactate levels to identify lactic acidosis  
- Urine analysis when metabolic causes are suspected  

diagnosis  
- Identify primary disturbance through ABG analysis  
- Assess compensatory mechanisms in place  
- Evaluate electrolytes to identify contributing factors  
- Exclude other conditions presenting with similar symptoms  
- Establish a clinical context and history  

management  
- Correct underlying cause (e.g., treat infection or adjust medications)  
  - Administer fluids for dehydration  
  - Provide insulin for diabetic ketoacidosis  
- Administer bicarbonate for severe metabolic acidosis when indicated  
  - Monitor pH and electrolytes during treatment  
  - Use cautiously to avoid overshoot in pH  
- Provide oxygen therapy for respiratory abnormalities  
  - Assess respiratory drive and ventilation  
  - Consider mechanical ventilation if necessary  
- Treat respiratory acidosis with ventilatory support  
  - Non-invasive ventilation for moderate cases  
  - Intubation for severe cases  
- Monitor for complications and adjust treatment accordingly  
  - Regularly reassess ABG and clinical status  
  - Adjust management based on evolving patient condition  







Acne vulgaris

background  
- Acne vulgaris is a common skin condition affecting adolescents and young adults, characterised by the presence of comedones, papules, and pustules

risk factors  
- Increased androgen levels  
- Excessive sebum production  
- Follicular hyperkeratinisation  
- Bacterial colonisation (primarily *Propionibacterium acnes*)  
- Family history of acne  

causes  
- Hormonal changes during puberty  
- Menstrual cycle in females  
- Diet (high glycaemic index foods)  
- Stress  
- Use of certain medications (e.g. corticosteroids)  

pathophysiology  
- Overproduction of sebum  
- Blockage of hair follicles by keratin and debris  
- Inflammation due to bacterial colonisation  
- Immune response leading to papule and pustule formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Open and closed comedones  
- Inflammatory papules and pustules  
- Possible formation of nodules or cysts  
- Post-inflammatory hyperpigmentation  
- Scarring in severe cases  

investigations  
- Clinical examination and history  
- Hormonal assays if endocrine causes suspected  
- Skin swabs if secondary infection suspected  
- Exclusion of other dermatoses via differential diagnosis  
- Assessment of psychological impact if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical presentation  
- Severity grading based on the number and type of lesions  
- Consideration of associated symptoms such as scarring  
- Assessment of quality of life impact  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other conditions  

management  
- Topical treatments (e.g. benzoyl peroxide, retinoids)  
  - Apply as per guidance on application frequency  
  - Monitor for skin irritation  
- Oral medications (e.g. antibiotics, isotretinoin)  
  - Consider prolonged course for moderate to severe cases  
  - Regular follow-up for adverse effects  
- Hormonal therapies in females (e.g. combined oral contraceptives)  
  - Assess for contraindications  
  - Educate on potential side effects  
- Skin care advice (e.g. gentle cleansing, non-comedogenic products)  
  - Promote a consistent skincare routine  
  - Discuss the avoidance of picking lesions  
- Referral to dermatology for severe or resistant cases  
  - Monitor progress and adverse effects  
  - Consider advanced treatment options (e.g. phototherapy)  







Acoustic neuroma

Background  
- Acoustic neuroma is a benign tumor arising from the Schwann cells of the vestibulocochlear nerve (cranial nerve VIII)

Risk factors  
- Family history of neurofibromatosis type II  
- Radiation exposure to the head and neck  
- Age between 30 and 60 years  
- Gender (more common in females)  
- Presence of other neurogenic tumours  

Causes  
- Sporadic mutations in the genetic material of Schwann cells  
- Hereditary predisposition in cases of neurofibromatosis type II  
- Environmental factors (though less clearly defined)  
- No clear infectious or inflammatory causes  
- Generally unknown triggers for sporadic cases  

Pathophysiology  
- Abnormal proliferation of Schwann cells leading to tumour formation  
- Compression of surrounding structures including the cerebellum and brainstem  
- Interference with vestibular and auditory function  
- Potential disruption of cerebrospinal fluid flow  
- Progression can lead to increased intracranial pressure  

Signs and symptoms  
- Hearing loss, typically unilateral  
- Tinnitus (ringing in the ear)  
- Balance disturbances or vertigo  
- Facial numbness or weakness in advanced cases  
- Headaches due to increased intracranial pressure  

Investigations  
- Audiometry to assess hearing loss  
- MRI scan of the brain with contrast for tumour identification  
- CT scan in certain cases where MRI is contraindicated  
- Neurological examination for cranial nerve involvement  
- Balance testing may also be performed  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmatory imaging (MRI) showing a vestibular schwannoma  
- Clinical presentation correlating with neurological assessment  
- History of unilateral hearing loss and tinnitus  
- Exclusion of other causes of similar symptoms  
- Consider genetic testing if neurofibromatosis is suspected  

Management  
- Observation for small, asymptomatic tumours  
  - Regular follow-up with MRI  
  - Audiological assessment  
- Surgical removal for symptomatic or large tumours  
  - Microsurgical excision via retrosigmoid or translabyrinthine approach  
  - Management of cranial nerve function during surgery  
- Stereotactic radiotherapy for non-surgical candidates  
  - Gamma Knife or CyberKnife techniques  
  - Close post-treatment monitoring  
- Symptomatic management for hearing loss  
  - Hearing aids for mild to moderate cases  
  - Cochlear implants for significant loss  
- Multidisciplinary team approach for rehabilitation  
  - Involvement of audiologists and physiotherapists  
  - Comprehensive support for quality of life improvements  







Acute bronchitis

Background  
- Acute bronchitis is a common respiratory infection characterised by inflammation of the bronchial tubes

Risk factors  
- Smoking or exposure to smoke  
- Young age or elderly population  
- Chronic respiratory conditions  
- Exposure to irritants or pollutants  
- Recent upper respiratory tract infection  

Causes  
- Viral infections, such as influenza and rhinovirus  
- Bacterial infections, typically secondary  
- Environmental irritants, including smoke and pollution  
- Allergens or respiratory allergens  
- Pertussis infection  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the bronchial mucosa  
- Increased mucus production leading to cough  
- Airway obstruction due to swelling and secretions  
- Possible secondary bacterial colonisation  
- Temporary impairment of airway function  

Signs and symptoms  
- Persistent cough, often productive  
- Sore throat and nasal congestion  
- Chest discomfort or tightness  
- Wheezing or shortness of breath  
- Low-grade fever may occur

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Chest auscultation for wheezing  
- Pulse oximetry to assess oxygen saturation  
- Sputum culture if bacterial infection suspected  
- Chest X-ray if pneumonia is a concern  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical presentation  
- Exclusion of other respiratory conditions  
- Usually no need for further investigations  
- Consider persistent symptoms over 3 weeks  
- Review NICE guidelines for persistent cough  

Management  
- Reassurance and advice on the condition  
  - Educate on self-limiting nature of acute bronchitis  
  - Encourage adequate hydration and rest  
- Symptomatic relief with analgesics  
  - Use paracetamol or ibuprofen for discomfort  
  - Consider cough suppressants if necessary  
- Avoidance of irritants and smoking cessation  
  - Advise on reducing exposure to pollutants  
  - Offer support for smoking cessation programmes  
- Follow-up for persistent symptoms  
  - Re-evaluate if symptoms do not improve  
  - Consider referral if complications arise  
- Use of antibiotics not routinely recommended  
  - Reserve for bacterial infections if identified  
  - Avoid over-prescription to prevent resistance







Acute cholangitis

background  
- Acute cholangitis is a life-threatening infection of the bile duct system, often due to obstruction.

risk factors  
- Biliary obstruction (e.g., gallstones, strictures)  
- Previous biliary surgery  
- Age over 50 years  
- Chronic liver disease  
- Immunosuppression  

causes  
- Gallstones  
- Biliary stricture  
- Tumours (e.g., pancreatic, biliary)  
- Infection (e.g., bacterial or parasitic)  
- Post-surgical complications  

pathophysiology  
- Obstruction of biliary tract leads to increased pressure and bacterial overgrowth  
- Bile stasis causes inflammation and infection  
- Bacteria enter the bloodstream, potentially leading to sepsis  
- Inflammatory mediators increase vascular permeability and promote further infection  
- Can result in systemic inflammatory response syndrome (SIRS)  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever and chills  
- Jaundice  
- Right upper quadrant pain  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Altered mental status or shock in severe cases  

investigations  
- Blood tests (LFTs, inflammatory markers, blood cultures)  
- Ultrasound scan to assess for gallstones or dilation of bile ducts  
- CT or MRI for detailed imaging of biliary anatomy  
- ERCP for diagnostic and therapeutic purposes  
- Endoscopic ultrasound (if non-invasive imaging inconclusive)  

diagnosis  
- Characteristic symptoms present with fever, jaundice, and abdominal pain  
- Laboratory confirmation: elevated liver enzymes and bilirubin  
- Imaging findings of bile duct obstruction or inflammation  
- Sepsis indicators present  
- Diagnosis of acute cholangitis is often based on the Tokyo Guidelines criteria  

management  
- Early intravenous antibiotics  
  - Cover gram-negative and anaerobic bacteria  
  - Adjust based on culture results  
- Urgent biliary decompression  
  - Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP)  
  - Percutaneous drainage if ERCP not successful  
- Supportive care and monitoring  
  - IV fluids and electrolyte management  
  - Intensive care if septic or severe  
- Surgical intervention if indicated  
  - Cholecystectomy for gallstone-related cholangitis  
  - Biliary bypass for malignancy or stricture  
- Follow-up and management of underlying conditions  
  - Regular monitoring for recurrence  
  - Address risk factors (e.g., lifestyle changes)  







Acute coronary syndromes

background  
- Acute coronary syndromes encompass a range of conditions associated with sudden reduced blood flow to the heart  

risk factors  
- Age  
- Smoking  
- Hypertension  
- High cholesterol  
- Diabetes  

causes  
- Atherosclerotic plaque rupture  
- Coronary artery spasm  
- Increased myocardial oxygen demand  
- Severe anaemia  
- Hypercoagulable states  

pathophysiology  
- Plaque rupture exposes thrombogenic material leading to thrombus formation  
- Reduced blood flow results in ischaemia and potential myocardial necrosis  
- Depending on the extent of obstruction, acute myocardial infarction or unstable angina may occur  

signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain or discomfort  
- Shortness of breath  
- Nausea or vomiting  
- Diaphoresis  
- Feeling of impending doom  

investigations  
- ECG to identify ST-segment changes or new Q waves  
- Trivial cardiac biomarkers (troponins) for myocardial injury  
- Chest X-ray to rule out other causes of chest pain  
- Echocardiography for assessing cardiac function  
- Coronary angiography for definitive diagnosis of coronary artery disease  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical presentation, ECG findings, and biomarker levels  
- Acute myocardial infarction confirmed by significant troponin elevation and associated symptoms  
- Unstable angina identified by symptoms with non-diagnostic ECG and normal troponin levels  
- Risk stratification using clinical tools   
- Consideration of patient history and assessment of pre-test probability  

management  
- Immediate initiation of aspirin and nitrates  
  - Dual antiplatelet therapy if myocardial infarction confirmed  
  - Morphine for pain relief if required  
- Early revascularisation (percutaneous coronary intervention or CABG)  
  - Based on risk assessment and severity of disease  
  - Consideration of medical management in patients unfit for surgery  
- Anticoagulation therapy to prevent thrombus reformation  
  - Use of low molecular weight heparin or fondaparinux  
  - Monitor for bleeding complications  
- Statin therapy initiated for lipid lowering  
  - Targeting LDL cholesterol reduction  
  - Consider high-intensity statin therapy  
- Cardiac rehabilitation and lifestyle modification advice  
  - Promote smoking cessation and dietary changes  
  - Encourage physical activity tailored to the patient's recovery







Acute glaucoma

background  
- Acute glaucoma is a medical emergency characterised by a rapid increase in intraocular pressure, potentially leading to permanent vision loss  

risk factors  
- Family history of glaucoma  
- Age over 40 years  
- Hyperopia (farsightedness)  
- Previous eye surgery or trauma  
- Use of medications that dilate pupils (e.g. anticholinergics)  

causes  
- Sudden blockage of the trabecular meshwork  
- Pupil dilation leading to closure of the angle  
- Inflammatory processes in the eye  
- Certain medications that increase aqueous humour production or impede drainage  
- Ocular conditions such as cataracts or lens dislocation  

pathophysiology  
- Increased intraocular pressure due to impaired aqueous humour drainage  
- Ischaemia of the optic nerve head causing damage  
- Potential for irreversible nerve loss and visual field defects  

signs and symptoms  
- Severe eye pain and headache  
- Blurred vision and visual halos  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Red and congested eye with mid-dilated pupil  
- Corneal clouding  

investigations  
- Intraocular pressure (IOP) measurement using tonometry  
- Slit-lamp examination for angle assessment  
- Fundoscopy to check optic nerve head  
- Gonioscopy to evaluate anterior chamber angle  
- Visual field testing if chronic glaucoma is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and presentation of acute symptoms  
- Elevated IOP confirmed on measurement  
- Anterior chamber angle closure observed on gonioscopy  
- Exclude other causes of eye pain and vision loss  
- Assessment of visual acuity and optic nerve status  

management  
- Immediate reduction of intraocular pressure  
  - Topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (e.g. dorzolamide)  
  - Oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (e.g. acetazolamide)  
- Topical beta-blockers (e.g. timolol) to decrease aqueous humour production  
  - Prostaglandin analogues (e.g. latanoprost) may be used  
  - Pilocarpine to induce miosis and open the angle  
- Systemic agents for pain and nausea control  
  - Analgesics for pain management  
  - Antiemetics for nausea and vomiting  
- Surgical intervention if pressure not controlled  
  - Laser peripheral iridotomy as definitive treatment  
  - Additional interventions if angle remains closed  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring of eye health  
  - Assessment of ongoing IOP control  
  - Education on glaucoma management and eye care  







Acute kidney injury

Background  
- Acute kidney injury (AKI) is a rapid decline in kidney function, often reversible with prompt treatment.

Risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Pre-existing chronic kidney disease  
- Dehydration or hypovolaemia  
- Heart failure or liver disease  
- Nephrotoxic medications  

Causes  
- Prerenal causes: reduced blood flow to kidneys  
- Intrinsic renal damage: acute tubular necrosis  
- Postrenal obstruction: urinary tract obstructions  
- Sepsis or severe infections  
- Medications: NSAIDs, ACE inhibitors, diuretics  

Pathophysiology  
- Impaired glomerular filtration rate  
- Accumulation of metabolic waste products  
- Electrolyte imbalances  
- Potential progression to chronic kidney disease  
- Vascular or tubular injury from ischaemia or nephrotoxins  

Signs and symptoms  
- Oliguria or anuria  
- Swelling due to fluid retention  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Confusion or altered mental status  

Investigations  
- Blood tests: serum creatinine, urea, electrolytes  
- Urinalysis: proteinuria, haematuria, casts  
- Renal ultrasound: assessing for obstruction  
- Urine output monitoring  
- Imaging: CT or MRI if needed for further assessment  

Diagnosis  
- Acute increase in serum creatinine by ≥26.5 µmol/L in 48 hours  
- Increase by >1.5 times baseline creatinine in the last 7 days  
- Urine output less than 0.5 mL/kg/hour for 6 hours  
- Exclusion of chronic kidney disease  
- Clinical context and underlying cause assessment  

Management  
- Assess and correct underlying causes  
  - Hydration with IV fluids for prerenal causes  
  - Discontinue nephrotoxic medications  
- Monitor kidney function and electrolytes  
  - Regular serum creatinine measurements  
  - Monitor electrolytes, particularly potassium  
- Manage fluid balance  
  - Diuretics for fluid overload if necessary  
  - Consider dialysis if severe AKI or symptomatic  
- Provide supportive care  
  - Nutritional support as needed  
  - Address complications like hyperkalemia  
- Follow up on recovery and renal function  
  - Regular follow-up appointments post-AKI  
  - Long-term monitoring for potential CKD







Acute pancreatitis

Background  
- Acute pancreatitis is a sudden inflammation of the pancreas, often causing severe abdominal pain and potentially life-threatening complications

Risk factors  
- Gallstones  
- Chronic and excessive alcohol consumption  
- Hypertriglyceridaemia  
- Certain medications  
- Abdominal trauma  

Causes  
- Biliary tract disease  
- Alcohol abuse  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Infections  
- Post-surgical complications  

Pathophysiology  
- Premature activation of pancreatic enzymes leads to autodigestion of pancreatic tissue  
- Inflammatory response and release of cytokines  
- Vascular permeability increases, potentially causing necrosis  
- Systemic inflammatory response syndrome may occur  
- Can lead to complications such as organ failure and pancreatic necrosis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Severe abdominal pain, often radiating to the back  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Abdominal tenderness and distension  
- Fever  
- Tachycardia and hypotension in severe cases  

Investigations  
- Serum amylase and lipase levels elevated  
- Abdominal ultrasound to assess for gallstones  
- CT scan if diagnosis is uncertain or complications suspected  
- MRI in certain cases for better imaging  
- Blood tests including liver function tests and electrolyte levels  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation of severe abdominal pain  
- Confirmed by elevated serum lipase or amylase  
- Imaging findings consistent with pancreatitis  
- Exclusion of other potential causes of abdominal pain  
- Assessment of severity using Ranson criteria or GGT score  

Management  
- Supportive care with hydration and pain management  
  - IV fluids to prevent dehydration  
  - Analgesia for pain control  
- Nutritional support  
  - Early oral feeding if tolerated  
  - Consider enteral feeding in severe cases  
- Treat underlying causes  
  - Surgical intervention for gallstones if necessary  
  - Management of hyperlipidaemia or discontinuing problematic medications  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Regular assessment of vital signs and lab values  
  - Imaging if complications are suspected  
- Patient education and follow-up  
  - Advise on lifestyle modifications to prevent recurrence  
  - Regular follow-up for management of underlying risk factors  







Acute stress reaction

background  
- Acute stress reaction is a psychological response to a traumatic event occurring within four weeks of the incident

risk factors  
- Previous exposure to trauma  
- Lack of social support  
- History of mental health issues  
- Severe or prolonged trauma  
- Personality traits such as neuroticism

causes  
- Natural disasters  
- Accidents or serious injuries  
- Witnessing violence or death  
- Sexual assault or other personal violation  
- Sudden loss of a loved one

pathophysiology  
- Activation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  
- Increased levels of cortisol and adrenaline  
- Altered neurotransmitter activity  
- Dysregulation of the autonomic nervous system  
- Emotional and psychological distress due to trauma

signs and symptoms  
- Intrusive thoughts and memories  
- Avoidance behaviour related to the trauma  
- Hyperarousal, such as irritability and insomnia  
- Emotional numbness or detachment  
- Physical symptoms like headaches or gastrointestinal issues

investigations  
- Clinical assessment using standardised interview tools  
- Psychological evaluation  
- Patient history and trauma details  
- Observation of symptoms duration and severity  
- Rule out other medical conditions

diagnosis  
- Fulfilment of criteria in ICD-10 or DSM-5  
- Symptoms lasting for more than three days but less than four weeks  
- Presence of distress or impairment in functioning  
- Exclusion of other mental health disorders  
- Assessment of the traumatic event context

management  
- Psychological first aid to provide immediate support  
  - Utilise reassurance and stabilisation techniques  
  - Connect patients with social support systems  
- Cognitive Behavioural Therapy for long-term coping  
  - Focus on trauma processing and symptom management  
  - Explore thoughts, feelings, and behaviours related to trauma  
- Medication if necessary for associated symptoms  
  - Consider SSRIs or anxiolytics if severe anxiety/depression  
  - Monitor for side effects and treatment response  
- Regular follow-up to monitor recovery progress  
  - Reassess psychological symptoms and functioning  
  - Adjust treatment plans as needed  
- Encourage self-care and coping strategies  
  - Promote relaxation techniques and mindfulness  
  - Facilitate engagement in supportive activities







Addison’s disease

background  
- Addison's disease is a primary adrenal insufficiency leading to insufficient production of hormones.

risk factors  
- Autoimmune disorders  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  
- Previous adrenal gland damage  
- Tuberculosis or other infections  
- Use of certain medications (e.g. anti-fungal drugs)  

causes  
- Autoimmune destruction of adrenal cortex  
- Tuberculosis infecting adrenal glands  
- Adrenal metastasis from cancers  
- Congenital adrenal hyperplasia  
- Adrenal haemorrhage  

pathophysiology  
- Insufficient glucocorticoids and mineralocorticoids leading to metabolic disturbances  
- Reduced cortisol causes decreased gluconeogenesis and impaired stress response  
- Lack of aldosterone leads to electrolyte imbalances and volume depletion  
- Increased ACTH due to negative feedback inhibition  
- Potentially life-threatening adrenal crisis during stress or illness  

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Hyperpigmentation of skin and mucous membranes  
- Weight loss and decreased appetite  
- Hypotension and dizziness  
- Salt craving  

investigations  
- Serum cortisol level (low in Addison's)  
- ACTH stimulation test (failure of cortisol increase)  
- Serum electrolytes (hyponatraemia and hyperkalaemia common)  
- Random serum ACTH level (increased in primary adrenal insufficiency)  
- Imaging studies (CT/MRI to assess adrenal size and morphology)  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed by low cortisol and high ACTH levels  
- Abnormal response in ACTH stimulation test  
- Exclusion of secondary causes of adrenal insufficiency  
- Consider adrenal autoantibodies in suspected autoimmune cases  
- Clinical features along with laboratory results  

management  
- Glucocorticoid replacement (hydrocortisone or equivalent)  
  - Adjust doses during illness or stress  
  - Regular monitoring of symptoms and hormone levels  
- Mineralocorticoid replacement (fludrocortisone)  
  - Monitor blood pressure and electrolytes  
  - Adjust dosage based on clinical response and laboratory results  
- Patient education on adrenal crisis management  
- Provide steroid emergency card and educating on stress dosing  
- Regular follow-up to monitor for complications







Adverse drug effects

background  
- Adverse drug effects are unintended and harmful reactions to medications, impacting patient safety and treatment outcomes  

risk factors  
- Age, with the elderly being more susceptible  
- Polypharmacy, particularly in individuals taking multiple medications  
- Renal or hepatic impairment, affecting drug metabolism and excretion  
- History of previous adverse drug reactions  
- Allergies or sensitivities to specific drug classes  

causes  
- Drug interactions with other medications or substances  
- Medication errors during prescribing, dispensing, or administration  
- Pharmacogenetic variations affecting drug metabolism  
- Dose-related effects due to incorrect dosing  
- Changes in health status influencing drug effects  

signs and symptoms  
- Nausea, vomiting, or gastrointestinal disturbances  
- Allergic reactions such as rash, itching, or anaphylaxis  
- Changes in mood or behaviour  
- Unexplained fatigue, malaise, or fever  
- Organ-specific symptoms like jaundice or renal impairment  

investigations  
- Review of medication history for potential interactions  
- Laboratory tests to assess organ function (e.g. liver and kidney tests)  
- Monitoring vital signs for signs of adverse reactions  
- Drug level monitoring for medications with narrow therapeutic ranges  
- Allergy testing if hypersensitivity is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Identification of the adverse effect based on clinical judgement  
- Correlation of timing of drug administration with onset of symptoms  
- Review of patient history for prior reactions or risks  
- Exclusion of other potential causes of symptoms  
- Use of established criteria for specific medication reactions  

management  
- Discontinue or adjust the offending medication  
  - Switch to an alternative if necessary  
  - Gradually taper off if avoiding withdrawal symptoms  
- Symptomatic treatment for adverse effects  
  - Antihistamines for allergic reactions  
  - Antiemetics for nausea or vomiting  
- Inform patient and document the adverse effect  
  - Include it in the patient’s medical record  
  - Report to regulatory agencies if needed  
- Educate the patient about the risk of future adverse effects  
  - Provide information on medication safety  
  - Encourage reporting of any new symptoms  
- Regular follow-up to monitor for further adverse effects  
  - Schedule appointments to assess ongoing therapy  
  - Adjust management plan as needed  







Alcoholic hepatitis

Background  
- Alcoholic hepatitis is a form of liver inflammation caused by excessive alcohol consumption

Risk factors  
- Heavy and prolonged alcohol consumption  
- History of alcohol use disorder  
- Poor nutritional status  
- Co-existing liver disease  
- Male sex

Causes  
- Chronic alcohol abuse  
- Binge drinking episodes  
- Metabolic disturbances from alcohol  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Concurrent infections

Pathophysiology  
- Ethanol metabolism leads to acetaldehyde accumulation  
- Oxidative stress and mitochondrial dysfunction  
- Inflammatory cytokine release  
- Hepatocyte injury and apoptosis  
- Fibrosis and potential for cirrhosis

Signs and symptoms  
- Jaundice  
- Abdominal pain and tenderness  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Fever  
- Altered mental status

Investigations  
- Liver function tests showing elevated liver enzymes  
- Serum bilirubin levels  
- Complete blood count for leukocytosis  
- Coagulation profile  
- Abdominal ultrasound to assess liver size and features

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history of alcohol intake  
- Evidence of liver inflammation from blood tests  
- Exclusion of other causes of liver disease  
- Assessment of severity using the Glasgow Alcoholic Hepatitis Score  
- Imaging to rule out complications  

Management  
- Abstinence from alcohol  
  - Referral to addiction services  
  - Support groups and counselling  
- Nutritional support  
  - High-calorie diet  
  - Vitamin supplementation, especially thiamine  
- Corticosteroids for severe cases  
  - Monitor for side effects  
  - Follow up on clinical improvement  
- Treat complications like infections  
  - Use of antibiotics if indicated  
  - Regular monitoring for sepsis  
- Consider liver transplantation in severe cases  
  - Multidisciplinary assessment  
  - Careful selection of candidates  







Allergic disorder

background  
- Allergic disorders are immune system reactions to typically harmless substances known as allergens

risk factors  
- Family history of allergic conditions  
- Previous allergic reactions or conditions  
- Exposure to environmental allergens  
- Age (more common in children)  
- Presence of other allergic disorders  

causes  
- Exposure to allergens like pollen, dust mites, pet dander, or certain foods  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental factors including pollution and lifestyle  
- Immune system hyperreactivity  
- Chronic inflammation due to allergens  

pathophysiology  
- Allergen exposure leads to sensitisation of T-helper cells  
- Production of specific IgE antibodies  
- Re-exposure to allergens triggers mast cell degranulation  
- Release of histamines and other inflammatory mediators  
- Results in acute inflammatory response causing allergy symptoms  

signs and symptoms  
- Common symptoms include sneezing, itching, and runny nose  
- Skin reactions such as hives or eczema  
- Respiratory symptoms like wheezing or shortness of breath  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms like nausea or diarrhoea  
- Severe reactions can lead to anaphylaxis  

investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Skin prick tests for specific allergens  
- Serum IgE testing for allergen-specific IgE  
- Challenge tests (in controlled settings)  
- Spirometry for respiratory symptoms  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history of symptoms  
- Identification of specific allergens through tests  
- Exclusion of other possible conditions  
- Assessment of severity and frequency of symptoms  
- Consideration of family history  

management  
- Allergen avoidance strategies  
  - Educate patients about triggers  
  - Implement environmental control measures  
- Pharmacological treatments  
  - Antihistamines for symptom relief  
  - Corticosteroids for inflammation control  
- Immunotherapy for long-term management  
  - Subcutaneous injections or sublingual tablets  
  - Gradual desensitisation process  
- Management of severe cases  
  - Adrenaline auto-injectors for anaphylaxis  
  - Emergency action plans in place  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring  
  - Assess treatment efficacy  
  - Adjust management plans as needed  







Anaemia

background  
- Anaemia is a condition characterized by a deficiency of red blood cells or haemoglobin, affecting oxygen transport

risk factors  
- Older age  
- Chronic diseases (e.g. renal failure, cancer)  
- Poor dietary intake of iron, vitamin B12, or folate  
- Menstrual blood loss in females  
- Family history of blood disorders  

causes  
- Iron deficiency (most common)  
- Vitamin B12 deficiency  
- Folate deficiency  
- Bone marrow disorders (e.g. aplastic anaemia)  
- Chronic inflammation or diseases  

pathophysiology  
- Decreased production of red blood cells  
- Increased destruction of red blood cells  
- Blood loss leading to decreased volume of circulating red blood cells  
- Nutritional deficiencies impacting haemoglobin synthesis  
- Disruption in erythropoiesis in the bone marrow  

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Pallor of the skin and mucous membranes  
- Shortness of breath on exertion  
- Dizziness or light-headedness  
- Heart palpitations  

investigations  
- Complete blood count (CBC)  
- Peripheral blood smear  
- Serum ferritin and iron studies  
- Vitamin B12 and folate levels  
- Bone marrow biopsy (if indicated)  

diagnosis  
- Presence of low haemoglobin level (for gender-specific cut-offs)  
- Identification of underlying cause through investigations  
- Evaluation of reticulocyte count to assess bone marrow response  
- Exclusion of other conditions causing similar symptoms  
- Clinical assessment correlating signs with laboratory findings  

management  
- Treat underlying cause (e.g. iron or vitamin deficiency)  
  - Oral or intravenous iron supplementation  
  - Vitamin B12 or folate supplementation  
- Transfusion of red blood cells for severe cases  
  - Indications include symptomatic anaemia and critical thresholds  
  - Monitor for transfusion reactions  
- Promote a balanced diet to include iron-rich foods  
  - Red meat, leafy greens, beans, and fortified cereals  
  - Education on absorption enhancers (e.g. vitamin C)  
- Address chronic diseases contributing to anaemia  
  - Optimize management of chronic renal disease  
  - Manage inflammatory conditions effectively  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring of blood levels  
  - Adjust treatment based on response to therapy  
  - Re-evaluate if symptoms persist despite treatment  







Anal fissure

Background  
- Anal fissures are common, acute or chronic tears in the anal canal lining, causing pain and bleeding during bowel movements

Risk factors  
- Constipation or diarrhoea  
- Childbirth and pregnancy  
- Anal intercourse  
- Chronic inflammatory bowel disease  
- Poor dietary habits

Causes  
- Trauma from hard stools  
- Inflammatory bowel diseases such as Crohn's disease  
- Post-surgical complications with anal procedures  
- Infection (e.g. sexually transmitted infections)  
- Ischemia (reduced blood flow)

Pathophysiology  
- Disruption of the anal epithelium due to trauma  
- Increased anal sphincter tone and spasm  
- Impaired healing due to decreased blood supply  
- Development of chronic inflammation  
- Persistent pain leading to avoidance of bowel movements

Signs and symptoms  
- Sharp pain during and after defecation  
- Bright red blood on toilet paper or stool  
- Spasms of the anal sphincter  
- Itching or irritation around the anus  
- Visible fissure may be noted on examination

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Anoscopy or proctoscopy for visualisation of the fissure  
- Stool examination for blood or infection  
- Digital rectal examination  
- Exclusion of underlying diseases (if chronic)

Diagnosis  
- History of pain during defecation and visible fissure  
- Duration and characteristics of symptoms  
- Exclusion of other conditions (e.g. haemorrhoids, malignancy)  
- Assessment of the severity of the lesion  
- Classification into acute or chronic based on duration

Management  
- Dietary modifications to increase fibre intake  
  - Increase fluid intake  
  - Consider fibre supplements  
- Analgesics and topical treatments  
  - Topical nitroglycerin or calcium channel blockers  
  - Local anaesthetics for pain relief  
- Laxatives to soften stools  
  - Osmotic laxatives recommended  
  - Avoid stimulants to prevent colonic spasm  
- Surgical interventions for chronic fissures  
  - Lateral internal sphincterotomy  
  - Advancement flap procedures if recurrent  
- Follow-up to monitor healing and recurrence  
  - Regular reassessment of symptoms  
  - Evaluate for any underlying disorders  







Anaphylaxis

Background  
- Anaphylaxis is a severe, life-threatening allergic reaction requiring immediate medical intervention  

Risk factors  
- Previous history of anaphylaxis  
- Known allergies (e.g. food, insect stings, medications)  
- Asthma or respiratory conditions  
- Family history of anaphylaxis or allergies  
- Young age, particularly children  

Causes  
- Food allergens (e.g. peanuts, tree nuts, shellfish)  
- Insect stings (e.g. bee or wasp stings)  
- Medications (e.g. penicillin, NSAIDs)  
- Latex exposure  
- Exercise-induced anaphylaxis  

Pathophysiology  
- IgE-mediated hypersensitivity reaction  
- Release of mediators like histamine from mast cells and basophils  
- Widespread vasodilation and increased vascular permeability  
- Bronchoconstriction and gut contraction  
- Decreased blood pressure leading to shock  

Signs and symptoms  
- Difficulty breathing or wheezing  
- Swelling of lips, face, or throat  
- Skin reactions (e.g. urticaria, rash)  
- Tachycardia and hypotension  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms (e.g. abdominal pain, vomiting)  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom evaluation  
- Measurement of serum tryptase levels (if within 1-2 hours post-reaction)  
- Skin prick testing or specific IgE testing (for future risk assessment)  
- Assessment for other possible causes of similar symptoms (e.g. cardiac, respiratory)  
- Monitor vital signs during acute reaction  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and symptoms  
- Must occur within minutes to hours after allergen exposure  
- One or more of the following: airway compromise, breathing difficulties, or hypotension  
- Presence of skin or mucosal involvement in conjunction  
- Consideration of differential diagnoses  

Management  
- Immediate administration of adrenaline intramuscularly (0.5 mg in adults, 0.01 mg/kg in children)  
- Respond to airway and breathing complications with oxygen therapy  
  - Establish IV access for fluids if hypotensive  
  - Prepare for advanced airway management if needed  
- Secondary medications (e.g. antihistamines, corticosteroids) after adrenaline administration  
  - Antihistamines for itching or urticaria relief  
  - Corticosteroids to reduce delayed allergic response  
- Observation in a monitored setting for at least 4-6 hours post-episode  
- Education and anaphylaxis action plan for future prevention  







Aneurysms, ischaemic limb and occlusions

background  
- Aneurysms are abnormal dilations of blood vessels that can lead to significant complications, including ischaemic limbs and occlusions

risk factors  
- Age over 60  
- Smoking history  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Family history of vascular diseases

causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Trauma  
- Genetic disorders (e.g., Marfan syndrome)  
- Infection (e.g., mycotic aneurysms)  
- Inflammatory conditions (e.g., vasculitis)

pathophysiology  
- Weakening of the arterial wall leads to localised bulging  
- Increased pressure can cause rupture or thrombus formation  
- Obstruction of blood flow may result in ischaemia of distal tissues  
- Acute ischaemia occurs when blood supply is critically reduced or stopped  
- Complications can result in limb loss if not addressed promptly

signs and symptoms  
- Pulsatile mass (in case of aneurysm)  
- Pain or discomfort in the affected limb  
- Weak or absent pulse in the affected area  
- Coldness or paleness of the limb  
- Symptoms of systemic embolisation (e.g., sudden limb pain)

investigations  
- Duplex ultrasound to assess blood flow and vessel morphology  
- CT angiography for detailed imaging of vasculature  
- MRI for evaluation of complex cases  
- Angiography for invasive assessment and possible intervention  
- Laboratory tests to assess risk factors (lipid profile, coagulation)

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and risk factors  
- Imaging findings consistent with aneurysm or occlusion  
- Assessment of limb ischaemia through Doppler studies  
- Identification of associated comorbid conditions  
- Confirmation of diagnosis through cross-sectional imaging

management  
- Control of cardiovascular risk factors  
  - Lifestyle modifications (smoking cessation, diet)  
  - Pharmacotherapy (antihypertensives, statins)  
- Surgical intervention if indicated  
  - Open surgical repair for significant aneurysms  
  - Endovascular techniques for selective cases  
- Monitoring of aneurysm size and symptoms  
  - Regular imaging follow-ups  
  - Adjustment of management based on findings  
- Management of acute limb ischaemia  
  - Urgent revascularisation (thrombectomy, bypass)  
  - Analgesia and supportive care  
- Education on warning signs and when to seek help  
  - Instruction on limb care and monitoring  
  - Emphasis on adherence to follow-up appointments







Ankylosing spondylitis

Background  
- Chronic inflammatory arthritis primarily affecting the spine and sacroiliac joints 

Risk factors  
- Male gender  
- Family history of ankylosing spondylitis  
- Presence of HLA-B27 antigen  
- Age of onset typically between 15 and 30 years  
- History of inflammatory bowel disease or psoriasis  

Causes  
- Exact cause unknown  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Potential triggers include infections or trauma  
- Altered immune response  
- Environmental factors  

Pathophysiology  
- Chronic inflammation of entheses and synovial membranes  
- Bone formation leading to fusion of vertebrae  
- Enthesitis and associated structural changes  
- Activation of T cells and elevated cytokine levels  
- Impaired spinal mobility due to calcification  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chronic lower back pain and stiffness  
- Reduced spinal mobility  
- Peripheral arthritis  
- Fatigue and generalised malaise  
- Associated conditions like uveitis or inflammatory bowel disease  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history  
- X-rays showing sacroiliac joint changes  
- MRI for early detection of inflammation  
- Blood tests for inflammatory markers (CRP, ESR)  
- HLA-B27 testing  

Diagnosis  
- Must meet modified New York criteria  
- Evidence of inflammatory back pain  
- Imaging showing sacroiliitis  
- Exclusion of other causes of back pain  
- Positive HLA-B27 can support diagnosis  

Management  
- Non-pharmacological approaches  
  - Regular exercise to maintain spinal flexibility  
  - Physiotherapy for posture and mobility improvement  
- Pharmacological treatments  
  - NSAIDs as first-line therapy  
  - Consideration of biologics (e.g. TNF inhibitors) if unresponsive  
- Monitoring and managing complications  
  - Regular eye examinations for uveitis  
  - Assessing for osteoporosis risks  
- Surgical options for severe cases  
  - Total hip replacement if hip involvement  
  - Spinal surgery for significant deformity or instability  
- Patient education and support  
  - Information about the disease and its progression  
  - Support groups for psychosocial aspects







Anxiety disorder: generalised

background  
- Generalised anxiety disorder is characterised by excessive and uncontrollable worry about various aspects of daily life

risk factors  
- Family history of anxiety or other mental health disorders  
- Personal history of other mental health conditions  
- High-stress life events or trauma  
- Chronic medical conditions  
- Substance misuse or withdrawal

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances, particularly serotonin and noradrenaline  
- Environmental stressors and lifestyle factors  
- Cognitive patterns of negative thinking  
- Developmental factors, such as early childhood experiences

pathophysiology  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis  
- Altered brain activity in the amygdala and prefrontal cortex  
- Increased sympathetic nervous system activity leading to physiological symptoms  
- Dysfunction in neurotransmitter systems

signs and symptoms  
- Persistent worry or anxiety across multiple areas  
- Restlessness or feeling on edge  
- Fatigue or difficulty concentrating  
- Irritability  
- Somatic symptoms like muscle tension, headaches, or gastrointestinal issues

investigations  
- Clinical assessment using standardised anxiety scales  
- Medical history and physical examination to rule out other conditions  
- Blood tests to rule out thyroid dysfunction or other metabolic issues  
- Psychological questionnaires, such as GAD-7  
- Consideration of substance use screening

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis made using criteria from DSM-5 or ICD-10  
- Symptoms persist for more than six months  
- Impairment in social, occupational, or other important areas of functioning  
- Symptoms cannot be attributed to another mental disorder  
- Exclusion of medical conditions that may cause anxiety symptoms

management  
- First-line treatment with cognitive behavioural therapy (CBT)  
  - Consider guided self-help or group therapy  
  - Tailor therapy to individual needs and preferences  
- Pharmacotherapy with SSRI or SNRI medications  
  - Start with low doses and titrate as needed  
  - Monitor for side effects and response  
- Consider lifestyle modifications in conjunction with treatments  
  - Encourage regular physical activity  
  - Promote healthy sleep hygiene  
- Referral to specialised mental health services if necessary  
  - For severe or treatment-resistant cases  
  - For consideration of other therapeutic modalities like psychotherapy  
- Regular follow-up to assess treatment efficacy and adjust as needed  
  - Monitor for symptom improvement and potential relapses  
  - Provide ongoing support and education







Anxiety disorder: post-traumatic stress disorder

Background  
- Post-traumatic stress disorder is a mental health condition triggered by experiencing or witnessing a traumatic event

Risk factors  
- History of trauma or abuse  
- Emotional stress during childhood  
- Family history of mental health disorders  
- Being a female  
- Lack of social support

Causes  
- Exposure to significant trauma such as war, sexual assault or natural disasters  
- Genetic predisposition to anxiety disorders  
- Neurobiological changes in response to stress  
- Psychological factors such as cognitive appraisal of threat  
- Chronic stress leading to maladaptive coping mechanisms

Pathophysiology  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  
- Alterations in neurotransmitter systems including serotonin and norepinephrine  
- Changes in brain structure, particularly the hippocampus and amygdala  
- Impaired fear extinction and heightened arousal responses  
- Persistent memory retrieval of traumatic events

Signs and symptoms  
- Intrusive memories or flashbacks of the event  
- Avoidance of reminders associated with the trauma  
- Negative alterations in mood or cognition  
- Hyperarousal symptoms such as insomnia and irritability  
- Social withdrawal and diminished interest in activities

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment using standardised questionnaires such as the PTSD Checklist for DSM-5  
- Psychological evaluation to assess history and symptoms  
- Rule out comorbid conditions like depression or anxiety disorders  
- Physical examination to assess overall health  
- Consider referral for psychological assessment if in doubt

Diagnosis  
- DSM-5 criteria including exposure to trauma and presence of symptoms for more than one month  
- Assessment of symptom severity and impairment in social or occupational functioning  
- Confirmation of avoidance, negative changes in cognition, and hyperarousal symptoms  
- Ruling out other causes for symptoms such as substance abuse  
- Multidisciplinary assessment if necessary 

Management  
- Psychological therapies such as cognitive behavioural therapy or eye movement desensitisation and reprocessing  
  - Focus on shaping cognitive patterns related to trauma  
  - Enhance coping strategies through therapeutic techniques  
- Pharmacological treatment with SSRIs or SNRIs as first-line medication  
  - Consider adjunctive medications for co-occurring disorders  
  - Monitor for treatment adherence and side effects  
- Education and support for patients and families about PTSD  
  - Encourage open discussions regarding symptoms and coping  
  - Facilitate access to support groups or community resources  
- Regular follow-up to monitor progress and adjust treatment plans  
  - Schedule consistent appointments to review symptoms and strategies  
  - Modify interventions based on patient feedback  
- Referral to specialist services if symptoms persist or worsen  
  - Consider involvement of psychiatric services for complex cases  
  - Collaborate with crisis intervention teams if needed  







Anxiety, phobias, OCD

background  
- Anxiety disorders, including phobias and OCD, are common mental health conditions that can significantly impact daily functioning  

risk factors  
- Family history of anxiety or mood disorders  
- Personal history of trauma or stressful life events  
- Chronic medical conditions  
- Substance misuse  
- Female gender  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances, particularly serotonin and norepinephrine  
- Dysfunctional thought patterns  
- Environmental influences and learned behaviours  
- Life stressors or significant changes  

pathophysiology  
- Altered activity in the amygdala affecting fear response  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  
- Hyperactivity in brain circuits involved in emotion regulation  
- Changes in serotonin pathways affecting mood and anxiety  
- Cognitive distortions leading to maladaptive behaviours  

signs and symptoms  
- Excessive worry or fear  
- Avoidance of specific situations or objects in phobias  
- Compulsive behaviours or rituals in OCD  
- Physical symptoms such as palpitations, sweating, or trembling  
- Sleep disturbances and irritability  

investigations  
- Structured clinical interviews to assess symptom severity  
- Standardised assessment tools, e.g. the Generalised Anxiety Disorder 7-item scale  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other mental health conditions  
- Screening for substance misuse or medical conditions  
- Referral for cognitive behavioural therapy evaluation if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Identification of symptoms lasting for at least 6 months  
- Symptoms causing significant distress or impairment in functioning  
- Specific criteria met for anxiety disorders as outlined in DSM-5  
- Exclusion of other mental health disorders based on clinical criteria  
- Assessment of severity to gauge treatment needs  

management  
- Cognitive behavioural therapy as first-line treatment  
  - Individual therapy sessions focusing on cognitive restructuring and exposure  
  - Consider group therapy for social phobia or OCD support  
- Pharmacological treatment with SSRIs or SNRIs if needed  
  - Start with low doses and monitor for efficacy and side effects  
  - Consider referral to a psychiatrist for medication management if symptoms persist  
- Psychoeducation for patients and families  
  - Educate about the nature of anxiety disorders and coping strategies  
  - Involve family members in support and therapy sessions  
- Lifestyle modifications to enhance general well-being  
  - Encourage regular physical activity, sleep hygiene, and healthy diet  
  - Recommend mindfulness or relaxation techniques  
- Regular follow-up to monitor progress and adjust treatment  
  - Schedule monthly reviews for the first 6 months  
  - Modify treatment plan based on patient’s response and feedback  







Aortic aneurysm

background  
- Aortic aneurysms are abnormal dilatations of the aorta, which can lead to life-threatening complications if ruptured  

risk factors  
- Smoking  
- Hypertension  
- Family history of aortic aneurysm  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Advanced age  

causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Connective tissue disorders (e.g. Marfan syndrome)  
- Trauma  
- Infections (e.g. syphilis)  
- Vasculitis  

pathophysiology  
- Weakening of the aortic wall due to degeneration of the elastic fibres and smooth muscle  
- Increase in wall tension leads to further dilatation  
- Risk of rupture increases with larger sized aneurysms  

signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic until rupture  
- Thoracic pain or back pain (in thoracic aneurysm)  
- Abdominal pain (in abdominal aortic aneurysm)  
- Pulsatile abdominal mass  
- Hypotension and shock (if ruptured)  

investigations  
- Ultrasound for initial assessment  
- CT angiography for morphology and size assessment  
- MRI for further anatomical detail when needed  
- Chest X-ray may show vascular calcification  
- Transoesophageal echocardiography in certain cases  

diagnosis  
- Clinical symptoms and history  
- Imaging confirming aneurysm presence and size  
- Exclusion of other conditions (e.g. dissection, stenosis)  
- Assessment of aneurysm growth over time  
- Consideration of family history and risk factors  

management  
- Surveillance for small, asymptomatic aneurysms (ultrasound every 6–12 months)  
- Endovascular aneurysm repair for suitable candidates  
  - Minimally invasive approach  
  - Shorter recovery time  
- Open surgical repair for larger or symptomatic aneurysms  
  - Requires general anaesthesia  
  - Longer recovery period  
- Blood pressure control with antihypertensives  
  - Beta-blockers may be preferred  
  - ACE inhibitors can be considered  
- Smoking cessation advice and support   
  - Smoking cessation programs  
  - Nicotine replacement therapy options  







Aortic dissection

Background  
- Aortic dissection is a life-threatening condition where there is a tear in the aorta's inner layer, leading to blood flow between the layers.

Risk factors  
- Hypertension  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Genetic conditions (e.g., Marfan syndrome, Ehlers-Danlos syndrome)  
- Cocaine use  
- Older age

Causes  
- Spontaneous rupture due to chronic hypertension  
- Trauma (e.g., from car accidents)  
- Pregnancy-related changes  
- Aortic valve disease  
- Inflammatory diseases (e.g., Takayasu arteritis)

Pathophysiology  
- A tear develops in the tunica intima of the aorta  
- Blood enters the media between the intima and adventitia layers  
- Formation of a false lumen that can propagate and compromise blood flow  
- Potential to lead to rupture or occlusion of arterial branches  
- Can result in organ ischaemia, cardiac tamponade, or death

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden, severe chest or back pain  
- Pain that may migrate or be felt in the abdomen  
- Signs of shock (e.g., hypotension, tachycardia)  
- Neurological deficits (if arteries supplying the brain are affected)  
- Asymmetric blood pressure or pulses in extremities

Investigations  
- Chest X-ray (may show widening of the mediastinum)  
- CT angiography (sensitivity and specificity for diagnosis)  
- MRI (gold standard for aortic imaging but less accessible)  
- Transoesophageal echocardiography  
- Blood tests (to rule out other conditions)

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed by imaging studies that identify the dissection  
- Consider Stanford classification (Type A or B) for management  
- Assess for complications (e.g., rupture or organ ischaemia)  
- Evaluate the extent of dissection  
- Review history and clinical features

Management  
- Immediate medical management (monitoring and stabilisation)  
  - Blood pressure control (target systolic BP <120 mmHg)  
  - Pain management to reduce stress response  
- Surgical intervention for Type A dissection  
  - Emergency surgical repair of the aorta  
  - Possible valve replacement if aortic valve involved  
- Medical management for Type B dissection  
  - Antihypertensive therapy (beta-blockers and vasodilators)  
  - Regular imaging follow-ups for progression  
- Long-term follow-up 
  - Cardiovascular risk factor modification  
  - Regular imaging to monitor for complications  
- Patient education and counselling  
  - Importance of adherence to treatment  
  - Recognition of symptoms of complications  







Aortic valve disease

background  
- Aortic valve disease encompasses conditions affecting the function of the aortic valve, crucial for normal cardiac output

risk factors  
- Older age  
- History of rheumatic fever  
- Congenital heart defects  
- Hypertension  
- Atherosclerosis  

causes  
- Aortic stenosis  
- Aortic regurgitation  
- Bicuspid aortic valve  
- Endocarditis  
- Degenerative calcification  

pathophysiology  
- Aortic stenosis leads to left ventricular outflow obstruction  
- Aortic regurgitation causes volume overload of the left ventricle  
- Changes in ventricular geometry and function develop over time  
- Increased myocardial oxygen demand occurs  
- Heart failure may develop if untreated  

signs and symptoms  
- Shortness of breath on exertion  
- Chest pain or angina  
- Syncope or near-syncope  
- Fatigue and reduced exercise tolerance  
- Heart murmur detected on auscultation  

investigations  
- Echocardiography to assess valve structure and function  
- Doppler ultrasound to evaluate blood flow dynamics  
- Electrocardiogram for arrhythmias and hypertrophy signs  
- Chest X-ray for cardiac silhouette and aortic calcification  
- Cardiac MRI for detailed anatomical assessment  

diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation combining history and physical examination  
- Echocardiographic findings confirming stenosis or regurgitation severity  
- Assessment of left ventricular function and dimensions  
- Consideration of symptom burden and impact on quality of life  
- Confirmatory tests as needed  

management  
- Regular monitoring for asymptomatic patients  
  - Echo every 1-2 years for stable, mild cases  
  - More frequent assessments for moderate to severe cases  
- Surgical intervention if symptomatic or severe  
  - Aortic valve replacement (surgical or transcatheter)  
  - Consider bioprosthetic or mechanical valves depending on patient's age  
- Medical therapy for heart failure management  
  - Diuretics for fluid overload  
  - Beta-blockers for rate control if indicated  
- Antibiotic prophylaxis in high-risk patients  
  - Prior to dental or surgical procedures  
  - Duration and choice based on individual case  
- Lifestyle modifications and cardiovascular risk management  
  - Smoking cessation and diet optimisation  
  - Exercise tailored to patient capacity and symptoms  







Appendicitis

background  
- Appendicitis is an inflammation of the appendix, usually requiring surgical intervention  

risk factors  
- Age between 10 and 30 years  
- Family history of appendicitis  
- Male gender  
- Low-fibre diet  
- Previous episodes of abdominal pain  

causes  
- Obstruction of the appendiceal lumen by faecaliths  
- Enlarged lymphoid follicles  
- Tumours  
- Foreign bodies  
- Infection  

pathophysiology  
- Obstruction leads to increased intraluminal pressure  
- Compromised blood supply results in ischaemia  
- Bacterial overgrowth causing inflammation  
- Progression to perforation if untreated  
- Potential for peritonitis or abscess formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Right iliac fossa pain, often initially periumbilical  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Loss of appetite  
- Fever and chills  
- Rebound tenderness and guarding on examination  

investigations  
- Abdominal ultrasound for children and pregnant women  
- CT scan with contrast for adults  
- Complete blood count showing leukocytosis  
- Urinalysis to rule out urinary tract infection  
- Pregnancy test for females to exclude ectopic pregnancy  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Imaging studies to confirm inflammation or obstruction  
- Laboratory tests supporting diagnosis of appendicitis  
- Differential diagnosis to exclude other causes of abdominal pain  
- Assessment of severity and complications  

management  
- Surgical intervention is the primary treatment  
  - Appendectomy, either open or laparoscopic  
  - Consider non-operative management for early uncomplicated cases  
- Antibiotic therapy  
  - Pre-operative antibiotics for appendectomy  
  - Continue post-operative antibiotics if complicated  









Arrhythmias

background  
- Arrhythmias are irregular heartbeats that can disrupt normal heart function and may lead to serious health issues

risk factors  
- Age over 65  
- Presence of cardiovascular diseases  
- Hypertension  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Family history of arrhythmias  

causes  
- Ischaemic heart disease  
- Electrolyte imbalances  
- Structural heart disease  
- Inflammation (e.g., myocarditis)  
- Drug effects (e.g., stimulants, antiarrhythmics)  

pathophysiology  
- Disruption of normal electrical conduction systems in the heart  
- Abnormal automaticity, triggered activity, or re-entry circuits  
- Alterations in ion channel function affecting depolarisation and repolarisation  
- Impaired myocardial perfusion leading to ischaemia  
- Other factors such as fibrosis or scar tissue formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Palpitations or irregular heartbeat  
- Dizziness or syncope  
- Chest pain or discomfort  
- Shortness of breath  
- Fatigue  

investigations  
- Electrocardiogram (ECG)  
- Holter monitoring  
- Echocardiogram  
- Blood tests for electrolytes and thyroid function  
- Exercise stress testing or electrophysiological studies  

diagnosis  
- Review of patient history and symptoms  
- ECG findings of specific arrhythmias  
- Correlation with symptoms and clinical context  
- Consideration of differential diagnoses  
- Utilisation of monitoring results for confirmation  

management  
- Lifestyle modification (diet, exercise, smoking cessation)  
  - Encourage a heart-healthy diet  
  - Suggest regular physical activity as tolerated  
- Pharmacological therapy (antiarrhythmics, beta-blockers)  
  - Consider rate or rhythm control drugs  
  - Monitor for side effects and adjust as necessary  
- Device therapy (pacemakers, ICDs)  
  - Indicated for symptomatic bradycardia  
  - Only for high-risk patients with ventricular arrhythmias  
- Catheter ablation  
  - Consider for recurrent atrial fibrillation or tachycardias  
  - Discuss risks and benefits with patient  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring  
  - Schedule for ongoing assessments  
  - Re-evaluate treatment efficacy and adjust as needed  







Arterial thrombosis

background  
- Arterial thrombosis involves the formation of a blood clot in an artery, potentially leading to serious health complications

risk factors  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Hypertension  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Smoking  
- Hyperlipidaemia  

causes  
- Plaque rupture in atherosclerosis  
- Endothelial injury  
- Increased coagulability (e.g. due to genetic disorders)  
- Stasis of blood flow  
- Trauma to the vessel  

pathophysiology  
- Disruption of the endothelial lining leads to platelet activation and coagulation cascade  
- Formation of a thrombus obstructs blood flow, causing ischaemia to the supplied tissues  
- Potential for embolisation, leading to distant complications  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of pain in the affected limb or organ  
- Pallor or cyanosis of the affected area  
- Coldness in the limb  
- Weak or absent pulses in the involved artery  
- Possible signs of systemic ischaemia, depending on the location  

investigations  
- Duplex ultrasound to assess blood flow and thrombosis  
- Computed tomography angiography (CTA) for visualisation of the vessel  
- Magnetic resonance angiography (MRA) as an alternative imaging modality  
- Blood tests including D-dimer to assess clot formation  
- ECG if myocardial involvement is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and physical examination findings  
- Confirmation of thrombosis through imaging techniques  
- Assessment of risk factors and medical history  
- Exclusion of other conditions causing similar symptoms  
- Consideration of laboratory results supporting the diagnosis  

management  
- Antiplatelet therapy (e.g. aspirin or clopidogrel)  
  - Initiate as soon as thrombosis is suspected  
  - Long-term maintenance therapy may be necessary  
- Anticoagulation (e.g. heparin followed by warfarin)  
  - Administer for venous thromboembolism prevention  
  - Monitor INR if using warfarin  
- Surgical intervention (e.g. thrombectomy or bypass)  
  - Consider in cases of limb ischaemia  
  - Evaluate based on severity and location of thrombosis  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Recommend smoking cessation  
  - Advise dietary changes to manage cholesterol and blood pressure  
- Education and follow-up  
  - Inform about symptoms of recurrence  
  - Schedule regular reviews to monitor risk factors  







Arterial ulcers

background  
- Arterial ulcers are non-healing wounds caused by inadequate blood supply due to arterial insufficiency  

risk factors  
- Peripheral artery disease  
- Smoking  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  

causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Thromboembolism  
- Vasculitis  
- Trauma or injury  
- Inflammatory conditions  

pathophysiology  
- Reduced blood flow to tissues leads to ischaemia  
- Oxygen deprivation impairs healing mechanisms  
- Accumulation of waste products and toxins in tissue  
- Breakdown of skin integrity occurs due to ischaemia  
- Ulceration develops in areas of high pressure or trauma  

signs and symptoms  
- Pain at ulcer site, especially with elevation  
- Ulcers usually located on toes, feet, or lower legs  
- Well-defined edges, often necrotic  
- Skin may appear pale, cool, or hairless  
- Delayed capillary refill time  

investigations  
- Ankle-brachial index to assess blood flow  
- Doppler ultrasound for vascular assessment  
- Arteriography may be performed if necessary  
- Serum glucose levels to evaluate diabetes  
- Wound culture if infection is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of ulcer appearance and symptoms  
- History of risk factors like peripheral artery disease  
- Ankle-brachial index <0.9 indicates arterial insufficiency  
- Doppler ultrasound confirming reduced blood flow  
- Exclusion of other ulcer types based on clinical criteria  

management  
- Optimise perfusion with revascularisation if indicated  
  - Options include angioplasty or bypass surgery  
  - Medications to improve blood flow may be prescribed  
- Wound care and prevention of infection  
  - Use of appropriate dressings to promote healing  
  - Regular cleaning and monitoring for infection  
- Smoking cessation advice  
- Manage underlying conditions like diabetes and hypertension  
- Education on foot care and ulcer prevention strategies  







Asbestos-related lung disease

background  
- Asbestos-related lung disease encompasses a range of conditions caused by the inhalation of asbestos fibres  

risk factors  
- Occupational exposure to asbestos  
- Living in close proximity to asbestos mining or manufacturing  
- Smoking history  
- Previous lung disease  
- Genetic predisposition  

causes  
- Inhalation of asbestos fibres  
- Environmental exposure to asbestos  
- Exposure during demolition or renovation work  
- Asbestos contamination in specific industries  
- Latency period of 10-50 years  

pathophysiology  
- Asbestos fibres cause inflammation and scarring in lung tissues  
- Formation of granulomas and pleural plaques  
- Development of interstitial lung disease  
- Increased risk of malignant transformation in lung cells  
- Pleural effusion due to asbestos exposure  

signs and symptoms  
- Progressive dyspnoea  
- Chronic cough  
- Chest pain  
- Weight loss  
- Digital clubbing  

investigations  
- Chest X-ray  
- CT scan of the chest  
- Pulmonary function tests  
- Bronchoscopy with biopsy if indicated  
- Serum biomarkers in certain cases  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of asbestos exposure  
- Imaging findings consistent with asbestosis or pleural disease  
- Exclusion of other causes of pulmonary symptoms  
- Histological confirmation if necessary  
- Assessment by a respiratory specialist  

management  
- Smoking cessation support  
  - Referral to cessation programmes  
  - Nickname for nicotine replacement therapy  
- Symptomatic treatment for respiratory distress  
  - Bronchodilators  
  - Corticosteroids for inflammatory conditions  
- Regular monitoring for lung function  
  - Annual check-ups  
  - Assessment of disease progression  
- Palliative care for advanced disease  
  - Referral to palliative services  
  - Pain management strategies  
- Legal advice for compensation claims  
  - Providing documentation of exposure  
  - Guidance on legal processes  







Ascites

Background  
- Ascites is the pathological accumulation of fluid in the peritoneal cavity often due to liver disease or malignancy  

Risk factors  
- Chronic liver disease, especially cirrhosis  
- Heart failure or pericardial disease  
- Malignancies such as ovarian or liver cancer  
- Kidney disease  
- Infection such as tuberculosis  

Causes  
- Portal hypertension secondary to liver cirrhosis  
- Malignancy causing peritoneal carcinomatosis  
- Infective causes like spontaneous bacterial peritonitis  
- Heart failure leading to hepatic venous congestion  
- Pancreatitis or nephrotic syndrome  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased hydrostatic pressure in portal circulation  
- Decreased oncotic pressure due to low albumin  
- Lymphatic obstruction or overproduction of peritoneal fluid  
- Inflammation or malignancy affecting fluid dynamics  
- Imbalance between fluid formation and absorption  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal swelling or distension  
- Weight gain or a sense of fullness  
- Abdominal pain or discomfort  
- Difficulty breathing due to diaphragm displacement  
- Possible signs of underlying liver disease or infection  

Investigations  
- Abdominal ultrasound for fluid detection  
- Paracentesis for fluid analysis and cytology  
- Serum-ascites albumin gradient (SAAG) measurement  
- Liver function tests and renal function tests  
- CT or MRI for underlying causes if necessary  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Paracentesis to establish etiology of ascites  
- SAAG to differentiate between portal hypertension vs other causes  
- Imaging studies to identify underlying pathology  
- Consideration of patient history and risk factors  

Management  
- Address underlying cause (e.g. treat liver disease or malignancy)  
  - Consider medications or surgical intervention  
  - Lifestyle modifications such as abstaining from alcohol  
- Diuretics (e.g. spironolactone) to manage fluid retention  
  - Monitor electrolyte levels regularly  
  - Adjust doses based on clinical response  
- Paracentesis for symptomatic relief or diagnostic purposes  
  - Evaluate fluid characteristics  
  - Repeated procedures if necessary for chronic cases  
- Consider placement of a transjugular intrahepatic portosystemic shunt for refractory ascites  
  - Evaluate candidacy based on liver function  
  - Monitor for complications post-procedure  
- Provide patient education and support for chronic management  
  - Discuss dietary modifications (low sodium)  
  - Offer resources for managing chronic conditions  







Asthma

background  
- Asthma is a chronic inflammatory disease of the airways causing recurrent breathing difficulties  

risk factors  
- Family history of asthma or atopy  
- Exposure to allergens such as pollen, dust mites, and pet dander  
- Tobacco smoke exposure during pregnancy or early childhood  
- Obesity and sedentary lifestyle  
- Respiratory infections in early childhood  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition and family history  
- Environmental factors and allergen exposure  
- Occupational exposures to irritants or allergens  
- Viral respiratory infections, particularly rhinovirus  
- Air pollution and exposure to smoke  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation and hyperresponsiveness of the bronchial tubes  
- Increased mucus production and airway obstruction  
- Bronchoconstriction in response to stimuli  
- Eosinophilic infiltration and airway remodelling  
- Mucosal oedema and disrupted epithelial integrity  

signs and symptoms  
- Episodic wheeze, particularly at night or early morning  
- Shortness of breath, especially with exertion  
- Persistent cough, often worsening at night  
- Chest tightness and discomfort  
- Symptoms vary in frequency and intensity  

investigations  
- Spirometry to measure lung function  
- Peak expiratory flow rate monitoring  
- Assessment of reversibility with bronchodilators  
- Allergy testing to identify triggers  
- Exhaled nitric oxide measurement for inflammation  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Positive response to bronchodilators in spirometry  
- Evidence of reduced lung function (FEV1/FVC ratio)  
- Exclusion of other respiratory conditions  
- Regular monitoring of symptoms  

management  
- Stepwise pharmacological approach based on severity  
  - Low-dose inhaled corticosteroids as first-line therapy  
  - Short-acting beta-agonists for quick relief  
- Patient education and self-management strategies  
  - Asthma action plan development  
  - Regular review of symptoms and medication adherence  
- Consideration of long-acting beta-agonists for persistent asthma  
  - Combine with inhaled corticosteroids when needed  
  - Review every 3-6 months for medication adjustment  
- Lifestyle modifications and avoidance of triggers  
  - Encourage weight management and smoking cessation  
  - Promote physical activity where feasible  
- Referral to specialist care for difficult-to-treat cases  
  - Consideration of biologic therapies for severe asthma  
  - Multidisciplinary team involvement for comprehensive care  







Asthma COPD overlap syndrome

background  
- Asthma COPD overlap syndrome is a condition featuring clinical and physiological characteristics of both asthma and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease.

risk factors  
- Smoking tobacco  
- Exposure to environmental pollutants  
- Occupational exposures (e.g. dust, chemicals)  
- Genetic predisposition  
- History of respiratory infections in childhood  

causes  
- Chronic inflammation of the airways  
- Airway hyperresponsiveness  
- Structural changes in bronchial walls  
- Mucus hypersecretion  
- Genetic factors affecting lung function  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation leading to airway narrowing  
- Increased mucus production contributes to airflow obstruction  
- Structural remodelling due to chronic inflammation  
- Altered airway responsiveness to stimuli  
- Impaired gas exchange due to airflow limitation  

signs and symptoms  
- Breathlessness, especially on exertion  
- Chronic cough, often with sputum production  
- Wheezing during breathing  
- Chest tightness  
- Frequent respiratory infections  

investigations  
- Spirometry to assess lung function  
- Peak flow measurement  
- Chest X-ray to exclude other conditions  
- FeNO test to quantify airway inflammation  
- Blood tests for eosinophilia  

diagnosis  
- Documented history of respiratory symptoms  
- Spirometry showing evidence of airflow limitation  
- Consideration of reversibility with bronchodilators  
- Assessment of degree of obstruction and hyperresponsiveness  
- Confirmation of overlapping features from asthma and COPD  

management  
- Smoking cessation support  
  - Referral to cessation programmes  
  - Pharmacological aids (e.g. nicotine replacement therapy)  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Inhaled corticosteroids for inflammation  
  - Long-acting bronchodilators for airflow limitation  
- Regular monitoring of lung function  
  - Scheduled follow-up appointments  
  - Self-monitoring with peak flow meters  
- Educating patient about triggers and symptom management  
  - Use of action plans  
  - Lifestyle modifications to reduce exposure to triggers  
- Vaccination against influenza and pneumonia  
  - Annual flu vaccine  
  - Pneumococcal vaccine as per guidelines  







Atopic dermatitis and eczema

background  
- Atopic dermatitis is a common chronic inflammatory skin condition often seen in children but can persist into adulthood  

risk factors  
- Family history of atopic conditions  
- Personal history of asthma or allergic rhinitis  
- Exposure to irritants and allergens  
- Young age, particularly in infants and children  
- Living in urban environments  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition affecting skin barrier function  
- Immune system dysregulation leading to inflammation  
- Environmental factors including allergens and irritants  
- Microbial factors, particularly Staphylococcus aureus colonisation  
- Climate changes, especially increased dryness  

pathophysiology  
- Dysfunction in filaggrin protein affects skin barrier integrity  
- Inflammatory response mediated by T-helper cells, particularly Th2 cells  
- Increased transepidermal water loss due to compromised barrier  
- Elevated levels of IgE and pro-inflammatory cytokines  
- Skin microbiome alterations contributing to exacerbations  

signs and symptoms  
- Intense itching often preceding visible lesions  
- Red, inflamed patches of skin  
- Dry, flaky skin that may become thickened  
- Oozing and crusting in weeping lesions  
- Commonly affected areas include folds of the elbow and knee  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of the skin and patient history  
- Patch testing to identify specific allergens  
- Skin biopsy in atypical cases to exclude other conditions  
- Measurement of serum IgE levels  
- Microbial swob for Staphylococcus aureus if infection is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination findings  
- Rule out other dermatological conditions with similar presentation  
- Assess for associated allergic conditions, like asthma or rhinitis  
- Use of diagnostic criteria including EASI score or SCORAD index  
- To classify severity, consider impact on quality of life  

management  
- Emollients as basic daily skincare  
   - Use thick, occlusive formulations  
   - Apply frequently, especially after bathing  
- Topical corticosteroids for inflammation control  
   - Use the lowest effective potency  
   - Taper usage to minimise side effects  
- Antihistamines for itch relief  
   - Consider sedating options at night  
   - Non-sedating for daytime use  
- Exclude known triggers and educate on skin care  
   - Advise on avoiding irritants and allergens  
   - Provide written action plans for flare management  
- Immunosuppressants or biologics for severe cases  
   - Consider drugs like cyclosporine or dupilumab  
   - Monitor regularly for side effects and efficacy  







Atrophic vaginitis

Background
