- Atrophic vaginitis is inflammation and tissue thinning of the vaginal walls due to oestrogen deficiency

Risk factors  
- Menopause and post-menopausal state  
- Surgical removal of ovaries  
- Hormonal therapies that reduce oestrogen  
- Certain medications (e.g., some antidepressants)  
- Radiation therapy to the pelvic area  

Causes  
- Decreased oestrogen levels due to menopause  
- Age-related changes in the vaginal tissue  
- Chronic hormonal disorders  
- Lactation and breastfeeding  
- Pelvic surgery affecting hormone production  

Pathophysiology  
- Thinning of vaginal epithelium and loss of elasticity  
- Decreased vaginal lubrication  
- Alterations in vaginal flora leading to infections  
- Increased susceptibility to trauma and irritation  
- Chronic inflammation of vaginal tissues  

Signs and symptoms  
- Vaginal dryness and discomfort  
- Burning or itching sensations  
- Dyspareunia (painful intercourse)  
- Urinary symptoms (e.g., frequency, urgency)  
- Vaginal discharge may be decreased or altered  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Gynaecological examination and visual inspection  
- pH testing of vaginal secretions  
- Microscopic examination of vaginal discharge  
- Rule out other causes of symptoms (e.g., infections)  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical findings and symptoms  
- Exclusion of other vaginal and urinary conditions  
- Assessment of menopausal status  
- Consideration of hormone levels if indicated  
- Response to topical oestrogen treatment  

Management  
- Topical oestrogen therapy  
  - Low-dose vaginal creams or rings  
  - Considered first-line treatment  
- Non-hormonal lubricants and moisturisers  
  - Use during sexual activity for comfort  
  - Regular use to alleviate dryness  
- Education and lifestyle modifications  
  - Discuss normal changes post-menopause  
  - Encourage pelvic floor exercises  
- Consider systemic hormone replacement therapy  
  - Discuss risks and benefits  
  - Individualise for severe symptoms  
- Regular follow-up and reassessment  
  - Monitor symptom improvement  
  - Adjust treatment as needed  







Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder

background  
- A neurodevelopmental disorder characterised by persistent inattention, hyperactivity, and impulsivity 

risk factors  
- Family history of ADHD or other psychiatric disorders  
- Premature birth or low birth weight  
- Maternal smoking or alcohol use during pregnancy  
- Exposure to environmental toxins (e.g., lead)  
- Neurodevelopmental disorders (e.g., autism spectrum disorder)

causes  
- Exact cause is unknown and likely multifactorial  
- Genetic predisposition influencing brain structure and function  
- Neurotransmitter dysregulation, particularly dopamine and norepinephrine  
- Brain structure abnormalities, especially in prefrontal cortex  
- Environmental factors and psychosocial stressors

pathophysiology  
- Reduced dopamine receptor levels linked to impulse control  
- Impaired executive function in brain regions responsible for attention  
- Alterations in neural pathways affecting behaviour and attention  
- Dysregulated activity in frontostriatal circuits  
- Possible alterations in sensory processing and emotional regulation 

signs and symptoms  
- Inattention: difficulty focusing, careless mistakes, losing items  
- Hyperactivity: fidgeting, excessive talking, difficulty staying seated  
- Impulsivity: interrupting others, difficulty awaiting turn, acting without thinking  
- Symptoms present in multiple settings (e.g., home, school, social)  
- Variability in symptom severity and manifestation among individuals

investigations  
- Clinical interview and assessment of history and symptoms  
- Standardised ADHD rating scales and questionnaires  
- Behavioural assessments from parents and teachers  
- Rule out other psychiatric or medical conditions  
- Developmental history and assessment of cognitive function 

diagnosis  
- DSM-5 criteria for ADHD must be met  
- Symptoms must persist for at least six months  
- Symptoms must be present before the age of 12  
- Symptoms must cause impairment in social, academic, or occupational functioning  
- Must be assessed in multiple settings (home, school, etc.) 

management  
- Behavioural therapy for children and adolescents  
  - Parent training and child-focused interventions  
  - School-based support and accommodations  
- Medication (e.g., stimulants like methylphenidate)  
  - Regular monitoring for efficacy and side effects  
  - Consideration of non-stimulant options (e.g., atomoxetine)  
- Psychoeducation for patients and families  
  - Inform about ADHD and its impact  
  - Provide coping strategies for managing symptoms  
- Collaboration with schools and teachers  
  - Develop Individual Education Plans (IEPs)  
  - Implement behavioural interventions in the classroom  
- Regular follow-up and re-evaluation of treatment effectiveness  
  - Adjust management plan as necessary  
  - Consider referral to specialists if needed  







Autism spectrum disorder

background  
- Autism spectrum disorder is a neurodevelopmental condition characterised by challenges in social communication and restricted or repetitive behaviour

risk factors  
- Family history of autism or other neurodevelopmental disorders  
- Advanced parental age at conception  
- Low birth weight or prematurity  
- Exposure to teratogens during pregnancy  
- Male sex  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition with various associated gene mutations  
- Environmental factors influencing brain development  
- Imbalances in neurotransmitters  
- Altered immune responses  
- Disruptions in early brain development  

pathophysiology  
- Abnormalities in brain structure and function observed  
- Differences in neuronal connectivity and synaptic pruning  
- Alterations in brain integration and regional function  
- Dysregulation of social communication circuits in the brain  
- Aberrant processing of sensory information  

signs and symptoms  
- Difficulty with social interactions and understanding social cues  
- Limited eye contact and facial expressions  
- Repetitive behaviours or restricted interests  
- Sensory sensitivities to light, sound, or textures  
- Delayed or absent speech and language development  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of developmental history and behaviour  
- Standardised screening tools, e.g., Autism Diagnostic Observation Schedule  
- Parent and teacher questionnaires to assess behaviour  
- Genetic testing if warranted by clinical findings  
- Multi-disciplinary assessment for comprehensive evaluation  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on the criteria from DSM-5 or ICD-10  
- Comprehensive assessment including behavioural observations and parental input  
- Exclusion of other conditions presenting similarly  
- Consideration of associated medical conditions  
- Referral for diagnostic assessment by a specialist  

management  
- Early intervention services focusing on communication and social skills  
  - Behavioural therapies such as Applied Behaviour Analysis  
  - Speech and language therapy to enhance communication skills  
- Educational support with tailored learning approaches  
  - Individualised Education Plans (IEPs) to meet educational needs  
  - Support from special educational needs coordinators  
- Medication for associated symptoms like anxiety or hyperactivity  
  - SSRIs for anxiety and depression   
  - Stimulants for attention deficits  
- Parent and family support programmes  
  - Training and resources for managing behaviours  
  - Support groups for emotional and social needs  
- Continuous monitoring and review of progress and management  
  - Regular follow-ups with healthcare providers  
  - Adjustment of interventions as necessary  







Bacterial vaginosis

Background  
- Bacterial vaginosis is a common vaginal condition characterised by an imbalance in the normal bacterial flora  

Risk factors  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- New sexual partner  
- Douching  
- Smoking  
- Lack of proper hygiene  

Causes  
- Overgrowth of anaerobic bacteria, mainly *Gardnerella vaginalis*  
- Disruption of normal vaginal flora  
- Associated with high levels of sexual activity  
- Hormonal changes  
- Certain medications  

Pathophysiology  
- Decrease in Lactobacillus species  
- Increase in diverse anaerobic bacteria  
- Metabolic by-products leading to inflammation  
- May alter the local immune response  
- Increased pH of the vaginal environment  

Signs and symptoms  
- Thin, greyish-white vaginal discharge  
- Fishy odour, especially after sexual intercourse  
- Itching or irritation in the vaginal area  
- Burning sensation during urination  
- Asymptomatic in some cases  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination and history  
- pH testing of vaginal secretions  
- Microscopic examination of discharge  
- Whiff test with KOH for fishy odour  
- Nucleic acid amplification tests  

Diagnosis  
- Amsel's criteria (at least three of the four criteria)  
- Elevated vaginal pH (>4.5)  
- Characteristic discharge (thin, grey)  
- Positive whiff test  
- Microscopic evidence of clue cells  

Management  
- First-line treatment with metronidazole (oral or topical)  
  - 500mg orally twice daily for 7 days  
  - 0.75% gel applied intravaginally for 5 nights  
- Alternative treatment with clindamycin  
  - 300mg orally twice daily for 7 days  
  - 2% cream applied intravaginally for 7 nights  
- Advise on abstaining from sexual intercourse during treatment  
- Offer education regarding BV and potential recurrence  
- Consider follow-up if symptoms persist after treatment  







Basal cell carcinoma

background  
- Most common type of skin cancer, arising from basal cells in the epidermis

risk factors  
- UV radiation exposure  
- Fair skin and freckling  
- History of sunburns  
- Immunosuppression  
- Genetic predisposition, such as Gorlin syndrome  

causes  
- Prolonged exposure to ultraviolet light  
- Ionising radiation  
- Chronic skin conditions or inflammatory skin disorders  
- Exposure to carcinogenic substances  
- Rarely, genetic mutations  

pathophysiology  
- Abnormal proliferation of basal keratinocytes  
- Local invasion without significant metastatic potential  
- Destruction of surrounding tissue due to uncontrolled growth  
- Potential for multifocality  
- Genetic mutations in the hedgehog signalling pathway  

signs and symptoms  
- Pearly or waxy nodules with translucent appearance  
- Ulcerated or crusted lesions  
- Scaly patches or elevated growths  
- Possible itching or bleeding  
- Frequently found on sun-exposed areas of skin  

investigations  
- Clinical examination of the skin  
- Dermatoscopy for detailed imaging  
- Biopsy (shave or excisional) for histological diagnosis  
- Imaging studies if invasion is suspected  
- Referral to dermatology for complex cases  

diagnosis  
- Presence of characteristic lesions on examination  
- Histological confirmation via biopsy showing basal cell features  
- Exclusion of other skin malignancies  
- Assessment of previous skin cancer history  
- Evaluation of lesion behaviour, such as growth rate  

management  
- Surgical excision for primary tumours  
  - Mohs micrographic surgery for high-risk cases  
  - Standard excisional surgery for low-risk tumours  
- Non-surgical options for superficial BCCs  
  - Topical chemotherapy (e.g. 5-fluorouracil)  
  - Cryotherapy or photodynamic therapy for superficial lesions  
- Regular surveillance for recurrence  
  - Follow-up appointments every 6 to 12 months  
  - Educate on skin self-examination  
- Consideration of radiotherapy for non-surgical candidates  
  - Palliative care for advanced cases  
  - Referral to oncology for treatment options  
- Addressing risk factor modification  
  - Sun protection advice and education  
  - Smoking cessation support if applicable  







Bell’s palsy

Background  
- A common, acute, unilateral facial nerve paralysis with sudden onset  

Risk factors  
- Viral infections, particularly herpes simplex virus  
- Recent upper respiratory tract infections  
- Stress or emotional disturbance  
- Pregnancy, especially in the third trimester  
- Family history of Bell's palsy  

Causes  
- Likely due to viral reactivation causing inflammation of the facial nerve  
- Idiopathic in nature, with no definitive cause in most cases  
- Potential association with Lyme disease and other infections  
- Facial trauma or surgery may contribute  
- Environmental factors such as exposure to cold drafts  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the facial nerve leading to demyelination  
- Disruption of nerve transmission resulting in muscle weakness  
- Edema in the facial nerve canal causing compression  
- Possible hypoperfusion of the nerve due to inflammatory mediators  
- Regeneration of the nerve may occur, but could lead to aberrant reinnervation  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of unilateral facial weakness  
- Inability to close the eye or lift the eyebrow on the affected side  
- Changes in taste sensation on the anterior two-thirds of the tongue  
- Hyperacusis or increased sensitivity to sound  
- Possible pain or discomfort around the jaw or behind the ear  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment of facial movement  
- Rule out other causes with clinical features  
- MRI or CT if atypical features are present  
- Blood tests to check for Lyme disease or other infections  
- Electromyography may be used if needed  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on unilateral facial weakness  
- Exclusion of other causes such as stroke or tumour  
- Appraisal of associated symptoms like ear pain or taste changes  
- Consideration of patient history and risk factors  
- Diagnosis should meet the NICE criteria for Bell's palsy  

Management  
- Most cases recover spontaneously within weeks  
- Consider corticosteroids to reduce inflammation  
  - Prednisolone at 25mg twice daily for 10 days  
  - Initiate treatment within 72 hours of onset  
- Analgesics for pain relief if required  
- Eye care to protect cornea if unable to close eye  
  - Lubricating eye drops or ointment  
  - Eyepatch at night for protection  
- Follow-up for reassessment and support  
  - Monitor progress to identify complications  
  - Referral for physiotherapy if necessary  







Benign eyelid disorders

background  
- Benign eyelid disorders encompass a range of non-cancerous conditions affecting the eyelids

risk factors  
- Age-related skin changes  
- Chronic sun exposure  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Poor hygiene or skin care  
- Genetic predisposition  

causes  
- Seborrheic keratosis  
- Chalazion  
- Eyelid cysts (e.g. meibomian cyst)  
- Papilloma  
- Contact dermatitis  

pathophysiology  
- Abnormal proliferation of skin cells in lesions  
- Blockage of meibomian glands leading to chalazion  
- Inflammatory response due to irritants or allergens  

signs and symptoms  
- Swelling or lump on eyelid  
- Redness or irritation of the eyelid   
- Discharge or crusting  
- Pain or tenderness in some cases  
- Changes in eyelid appearance  

investigations  
- Clinical examination to assess the eyelid and surrounding structures  
- Visual acuity test if vision is affected  
- Dermatoscopy for further evaluation of lesions  
- Biopsy if malignancy is suspected  
- Swab for culture if infection is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on patient history and physical examination  
- Differentiation from malignant lesions through characteristics of the lesion  
- Exclusion of other conditions like styes or infections  
- Evaluation of associated symptoms and duration of symptoms  
- Consideration of patient demographics and risk factors  

management  
- Reassurance and monitoring in asymptomatic cases  
  - Regular follow-ups to check for changes  
  - Patient education regarding potential complications  
- Surgical excision for symptomatic or cosmetically concerning lesions  
  - Referral to ophthalmic specialist if needed  
  - Consideration for cryotherapy or laser treatment if appropriate  
- Topical treatments for inflammation or irritation  
  - Use of antibiotic ointments if bacterial infection is suspected  
  - Regular eyelid hygiene to prevent recurrence  
- Intralesional steroid injection for chalazia  
  - Reduces inflammation and promotes drainage  
  - May require multiple sessions for effectiveness  
- Avoidance of known irritants and allergens  
  - Patch testing for dermatitis  
  - Use of hypoallergenic products where possible  







Benign paroxysmal positional vertigo

background  
- Benign paroxysmal positional vertigo is a common vestibular disorder characterised by brief episodes of dizziness related to changes in head position  

risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Previous head trauma  
- Prolonged bed rest  
- Migraine history  
- Inner ear disorders  

causes  
- Displacement of otoliths within the semicircular canals  
- Degeneration of vestibular system  
- Viral infections of the inner ear  
- Meniere's disease  
- Idiopathic  

pathophysiology  
- Otoliths detached from the utricle become embedded in the semicircular canals  
- Movement of these particles causes abnormal stimulation of hair cells, resulting in vertigo  
- Changes in head position lead to fluid movement in the canals  

signs and symptoms  
- Short episodes of vertigo triggered by head movements  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Unsteadiness or loss of balance  
- Nystagmus observed during episodes  
- Symptoms may resolve spontaneously  

investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom description  
- Neurological examination  
- Dix-Hallpike manoeuvre to provoke symptoms  
- Videonystagmography (VNG) or electronystagmography (ENG) for confirmation  
- CT or MRI in atypical cases to rule out other conditions  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on history of symptoms and physical examination  
- Positive findings during the Dix-Hallpike manoeuvre confirm BPPV  
- Excluded other causes of vertigo  

management  
- Particle repositioning manoeuvres such as Epley manoeuvre  
  - Repeat as necessary for persistent symptoms  
  - Consider vestibular rehabilitation if symptoms are chronic  
- Education on avoiding provocative head positions  
  - Provide written information for patient reference  
  - Advise on gradual return to normal activities  









Benign prostatic hyperplasia

Background  
- Benign prostatic hyperplasia is a common condition in older men characterised by enlargement of the prostate gland

Risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Family history of BPH  
- Obesity  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hormonal changes, particularly testosterone

Causes  
- Hyperplasia of prostatic stromal and epithelial cells  
- Hormonal imbalance, primarily involving androgens and oestrogens  
- Inflammatory processes  
- Growth factor influence  
- Increased cell proliferation

Pathophysiology  
- Prostate enlargement leads to urethral obstruction  
- Results in bladder outlet obstruction  
- Altered bladder function due to increased pressure  
- Detrusor muscle hypertrophy  
- Possible development of urinary retention and infections

Signs and symptoms  
- Increased urinary frequency  
- Nocturia  
- Urgency to urinate  
- Weak urine stream  
- Incomplete bladder emptying

Investigations  
- Digital rectal examination  
- Urinalysis to rule out infection or blood  
- Serum prostate-specific antigen (PSA) testing  
- Post-void residual urine measurement  
- Uroflowmetry to assess urine flow rate

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and symptoms  
- Digital rectal examination findings  
- Positive PSA tests in context (not definitive)  
- Results from uroflowmetry and residual volume measurement  
- Exclusion of other causes of LUTS

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Fluid management  
  - Dietary changes to reduce irritative symptoms  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Alpha-blockers (e.g. tamsulosin)  
  - 5-alpha-reductase inhibitors (e.g. finasteride)  
- Surgical options if indicated  
  - Transurethral resection of the prostate (TURP)  
  - Holmium laser enucleation of the prostate (HoLEP)  
- Monitoring and follow-up  
  - Regular assessments of symptoms and quality of life  
  - Repeat PSA testing  
- Discuss potential complications with patients  
  - Risk of urinary incontinence  
  - Sexual dysfunction following surgery







Biliary atresia

background  
- Biliary atresia is a rare congenital disorder affecting the biliary system in infants, leading to liver damage and cirrhosis

risk factors  
- Female sex 
- Premature birth 
- Maternal infections during pregnancy 
- Genetic syndromes (e.g., Down syndrome, Alagille syndrome) 
- Environmental factors (e.g., exposure to certain toxins)

causes  
- Developmental defect in biliary tree formation 
- Unknown aetiology in most cases 
- Possible autoimmune mechanisms 
- Viral infections (e.g., cytomegalovirus) may play a role 
- Associated with other congenital anomalies

pathophysiology  
- Progressive obstruction of bile ducts leads to bile accumulation  
- Cholestasis results in liver injury and fibrosis  
- Cirrhosis develops if untreated, resulting in portal hypertension and liver failure  
- Malabsorption of fats and fat-soluble vitamins occurs  
- Potential for hepatocellular carcinoma in later life  

signs and symptoms  
- Neonatal jaundice, usually appearing after two weeks of age  
- Pale or clay-coloured stools  
- Dark urine  
- Abdominal distension or hepatomegaly  
- Poor weight gain and growth

investigations  
- Blood tests (LFTs, bilirubin levels, coagulation profile)  
- Ultrasound to assess biliary tree and liver structure  
- Hepatobiliary scan (HIDA) for bile excretion evaluation  
- Liver biopsy to assess for fibrosis or cirrhosis  
- Genetic testing for associated conditions if indicated   

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation (jaundice, pale stools)  
- Fulfilling the criteria from imaging findings  
- Exclusion of other causes of cholestasis  
- Confirmation via operative cholangiography or liver biopsy  
- Early diagnosis is crucial for improving outcomes 

management  
- Kasai portoenterostomy as the first-line treatment  
  - Performed ideally before 3 months of age  
  - Aims to restore bile flow to the intestine  
- Liver transplantation for end-stage liver disease  
  - Considered in patients with progressive liver failure  
  - Important to manage complications of cirrhosis pre-transplant  
- Nutritional support to address malabsorption  
  - Supplement fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K)  
  - High-calorie diet as needed  
- Regular monitoring for complications  
  - Surveillance for portal hypertension and liver function  
  - Assessment for hepatocellular carcinoma in long-term follow-up  
- Multidisciplinary approach involving paediatricians, surgeons, and dietitians  








Bipolar affective disorder

background  
- Bipolar affective disorder is a mental health condition characterised by extreme mood swings between depressive and manic episodes  

risk factors  
- Family history of bipolar disorder  
- Personal history of depression or anxiety  
- Substance misuse  
- Major life stressors  
- Trauma or abuse during childhood  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurochemical imbalances in the brain  
- Environmental triggers  
- Sleep disturbances  
- Hormonal changes  

pathophysiology  
- Alterations in neurotransmitter systems (serotonin, dopamine, norepinephrine)  
- Dysregulation of circadian rhythms  
- Structural brain changes (e.g. volume changes in specific regions)  
- Dysfunctional neural circuitry  
- Inflammation and oxidative stress  

signs and symptoms  
- Manic phase: elevated mood, increased energy, decreased need for sleep, impulsivity  
- Depressive phase: low mood, loss of interest, fatigue, feelings of worthlessness  
- Mixed episodes: features of both mania and depression occurring simultaneously  
- Psychotic symptoms may occur (e.g. hallucinations, delusions)  
- Functional impairment in personal and professional life  

investigations  
- Clinical interview and psychiatric history  
- Mood charting  
- Standardised assessment tools (e.g. Hamilton Depression Rating Scale)  
- Physical examination to rule out other conditions  
- Blood tests to assess hormone levels and exclude other causes  

diagnosis  
- Based on criteria from DSM-5 or ICD-10  
- Identification of one or more manic or hypomanic episodes  
- History of depressive episodes is also necessary for bipolar I or II diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other mental health disorders  
- Assessment of functional impact  

management  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Mood stabilisers (e.g. lithium)  
  - Atypical antipsychotics (e.g. quetiapine, olanzapine)  
- Psychotherapy  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy (CBT)  
  - Psychoeducation and support groups  
- Regular monitoring of medication adherence and side effects  
- Lifestyle interventions (e.g. sleep hygiene, exercise)  
- Crisis management and safety planning 







Bladder cancer

Background  
- Bladder cancer is a common urological malignancy affecting mainly older adults

Risk factors  
- Age over 55  
- Male gender  
- Smoking  
- Exposure to certain chemicals (e.g., aniline dyes, benzidine)  
- Schistosomiasis infection  

Causes  
- Genomic mutations in bladder epithelium  
- Chronic irritation of the bladder mucosa  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Infection with *Schistosoma haematobium*  
- Environmental exposures  

Pathophysiology  
- Malignant transformation of urothelial cells  
- Tumour invasion through bladder wall layers  
- Potential metastasis to lymph nodes and distant organs  
- Angiogenesis to support tumour growth  
- Evasion of immune response  

Signs and symptoms  
- Painless haematuria  
- Increased urinary frequency  
- Urgency  
- Dysuria  
- Pelvic pain or discomfort  

Investigations  
- Urinalysis for blood or abnormal cells  
- Urine cytology  
- Cystoscopy for direct visualisation  
- Biopsy of bladder lesions  
- Imaging studies (CT or MRI) for staging  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed through histopathological evaluation of biopsy  
- Staging according to the TNM classification  
- Exclusion of other causes of haematuria  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Multidisciplinary team discussion for complex cases  

Management  
- Transurethral resection of bladder tumour (TURBT)  
  - Used for non-muscle invasive bladder cancer  
  - May involve fulguration of remaining tumour  
- Intravesical therapy (e.g., Bacillus Calmette-Guérin)  
  - Recommended for high-risk non-muscle invasive disease  
  - Administered post-TURBT  
- Radical cystectomy with urinary diversion  
  - Indicated for muscle-invasive bladder cancer  
  - May involve lymph node dissection  
- Chemotherapy for advanced disease  
  - Neoadjuvant before radical surgery or for palliative care  
  - Regimens may include cisplatin-based combinations  
- Follow-up with cystoscopy and imaging  
  - Regular check-ups to monitor for recurrence  
  - Individualised based on risk stratification  







Blepharitis

background  
- Blepharitis is a common inflammatory condition affecting the eyelid margins  

risk factors  
- Seborrhoeic dermatitis  
- Allergies or sensitivities to cosmetics  
- Chronic skin conditions such as rosacea  
- Poor eyelid hygiene  
- Contact lens wear  

causes  
- Bacterial infections, often due to *Staphylococcus* species  
- Malfunctioning Meibomian glands  
- Skin conditions affecting eyelids  
- Allergic reactions to substances  
- Trauma or irritation to the eyelids  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the eyelid margins  
- Accumulation of debris and bacteria in the eyelid glands  
- Altered tear film stability  
- Compromised eyelid barrier leading to irritation  
- Potential for secondary infections  

signs and symptoms  
- Red, swollen eyelid margins  
- Itching or burning sensation in the eyes  
- Crusting or flaking at the eyelid margins  
- Dry eyes or a gritty feeling  
- Intermittent blurry vision  

investigations  
- Clinical examination of eyelids and eyes  
- Assessment of eyelid hygiene practices  
- Culture of eyelid margins if infection is suspected  
- Tear film evaluation if dry eyes are present  
- Consideration of underlying skin conditions  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and examination findings  
- Rule out other eyelid conditions such as chalazia or hordeola  
- Documentation of symptoms and duration  
- Identification of contributing factors  
- Assessment of response to initial treatment  

management  
- Maintain eyelid hygiene  
  - Warm compresses to loosen crusts and debris  
  - Cleansing eyelids with diluted baby shampoo or eyelid wipes  
- Topical antibiotic therapy if bacterial infection is suspected  
  - Consider antibiotics such as fusidic acid  
  - Short courses of topical corticosteroids for inflammation  
- Assess and manage underlying conditions, if present  
  - Treat seborrhoeic dermatitis or rosacea if diagnosed  
  - Review contact lens hygiene practices  
- Advise on the use of artificial tears for comfort  
  - Preservative-free options are preferred  
  - Regular application to maintain moisture  
- Follow-up for persistent or recurrent cases  
  - Regular review to adjust treatment as necessary  
  - Referral to an ophthalmologist for severe cases  







Brain abscess

background  
- Brain abscess is a focal suppurative infection within the brain tissue.

risk factors  
- Recent history of intracranial surgery or trauma  
- Immunocompromised status (e.g., HIV, diabetes)  
- Pre-existing infections (e.g., endocarditis, sinusitis)  
- Presence of foreign bodies (e.g., shunts, prosthetic devices)  
- Substance abuse (e.g., intravenous drugs)

causes  
- Bacterial (e.g., *Staphylococcus aureus*, *Streptococcus*)  
- Fungal (e.g., *Aspergillus*, *Candida*)  
- Parasitic infections (e.g., *Toxoplasma gondii*)  
- Complications from neighbouring infections (e.g., otitis media)  
- Haematogenous spread from distant sites (e.g., lung infections)

pathophysiology  
- Initial infection leads to inflammatory response  
- Localised area of necrosis and pus formation  
- Formation of a capsule around the abscess  
- Increased intracranial pressure can occur  
- Surrounding brain tissue may become oedematous

signs and symptoms  
- Headache (often progressively worsening)  
- Fever and chills  
- Neurological deficits (e.g., weakness, seizures)  
- Altered consciousness or behaviour changes  
- Nausea and vomiting

investigations  
- CT scan of the head to detect abscess formation  
- MRI for better definition and localisation of abscess  
- Blood cultures to identify causative organisms  
- Complete blood count for leukocytosis  
- Lumbar puncture may be avoided if increased intracranial pressure is suspected

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination consistent with brain abscess  
- Imaging studies showing characteristic features of abscess  
- Microbiological identification if possible  
- Exclusion of other intracranial pathologies (e.g., tumours)  
- Identification of predisposing factors or sources of infection 

management  
- Empirical antibiotic therapy initiated promptly  
  - Consideration of specific antibiotic coverage based on suspected organism  
  - Adjust based on culture and sensitivity results  
- Surgical intervention may be required  
  - Drainage of abscess through aspiration or craniotomy  
  - Monitor for complications such as secondary infections  
- Manage associated symptoms  
  - Control seizures with antiepileptics  
  - Manage raised intracranial pressure if present  
- Follow-up with imaging to assess treatment efficacy  
  - Repeat imaging to evaluate changes in abscess size  
  - Long-term follow-up to monitor for recurrence  
- Patient education and supportive care  
  - Discuss compliance with medications  
  - Ensure regular neurology check-ups and symptom monitoring  







Brain metastases

background  
- Brain metastases occur when cancer cells spread to the brain from primary tumours in other parts of the body  

risk factors  
- History of cancer  
- Advanced age  
- Immunosuppression  
- Female gender  
- Genetic predisposition  

causes  
- Lung cancer  
- Breast cancer  
- Melanoma  
- Renal cell carcinoma  
- Colorectal cancer  

pathophysiology  
- Cancer cells spread to the brain via the bloodstream or lymphatic system  
- Cells can form multiple lesions  
- Growth typically occurs in the cerebral hemispheres  
- Disruption of normal brain function due to mass effect  
- Induction of local inflammation and increased intracranial pressure  

signs and symptoms  
- Headaches  
- Seizures  
- Cognitive changes  
- Focal neurological deficits  
- Nausea and vomiting  

investigations  
- MRI of the brain  
- CT scan as an alternative  
- Neurological examination  
- Biopsy if needed  
- Blood tests to assess overall health  

diagnosis  
- Confirm presence of one or more lesions on imaging  
- Histological confirmation if uncertain  
- Patient history indicating previous cancer diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other causes of neurological symptoms  
- Assessment of clinical features consistent with brain metastases  

management  
- Surgical intervention  
  - Resection of accessible metastases  
  - Consideration of patient performance status  
- Radiotherapy  
  - Whole brain radiotherapy for multiple lesions  
  - Stereotactic radio-surgery for single or few lesions  
- Systemic therapy  
  - Targeted therapy depending on primary cancer type  
  - Chemotherapy if indicated  
- Palliative care  
  - Symptom management for quality of life  
  - Psychological support for patients and families  
- Clinical trials  
  - Consider participation in research studies  
  - Access to novel therapies and approaches  







Breast abscess/ mastitis

Background  
- Breast abscess and mastitis are inflammatory conditions affecting breast tissue, commonly seen in lactating women

Risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Recent childbirth or breastfeeding  
- Poor breastfeeding technique  
- Blocked milk ducts  
- Smoking  

Causes  
- Staphylococcus aureus infection  
- Bacterial infection from skin flora  
- Milk stasis leading to duct obstruction  
- Trauma or nipple fissures  
- Diabetes mellitus  

Pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to inflammation of breast tissue  
- Accumulation of pus within the breast  
- Breakdown of tissue structure as abscess develops  
- Possible obstructed drainage causing secondary infection  
- Surrounding tissue may become involved

Signs and symptoms  
- Localised breast pain or tenderness  
- Swelling and redness over the affected area  
- Heat and increased warmth of the breast  
- Fever and malaise in more severe cases  
- Fluctuant mass indicating abscess formation  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination for signs of inflammation  
- Ultrasound to identify abscess or fluid collection  
- Microbiological culture of breast milk if indicated  
- Fine needle aspiration for cytology  
- Blood tests for signs of infection or other underlying issues  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and clinical examination  
- Ultrasound confirmation for abscess presence  
- Bacteriological confirmation if necessary  
- Rule out other conditions like breast cancer  
- Ensure no underlying systemic illness exists  

Management  
- Antibiotic therapy targeting the identified bacteria  
  - Consider empirical treatment based on clinical guidelines  
  - Adjust antibiotics based on culture results if available  
- Surgical intervention for abscess drainage if required  
  - Incision and drainage may be necessary for large abscesses  
  - Monitoring for recurrence following drainage  
- Supportive care including pain management  
  - Analgesics for pain relief  
  - Warm compresses to assist drainage  
- Education on breastfeeding techniques  
  - Importance of proper latching  
  - Strategies to prevent recurrence such as frequent feeding  
- Referral to a specialist if complications arise  
  - Obstetrician or breast surgeon for persistent cases  
  - Consider referral for recurrent issues or significant concerns  







Breast cancer

Background  
- Breast cancer is one of the most common cancers affecting women in the UK and is associated with significant morbidity and mortality  

Risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Increasing age  
- Family history of breast cancer  
- Genetic mutations (e.g. BRCA1, BRCA2)  
- Lifestyle factors (e.g. obesity, alcohol consumption)  

Causes  
- Genetic mutations  
- Hormonal factors (e.g. prolonged exposure to oestrogen)  
- Environmental influences (e.g. radiation exposure)  
- Age-related cellular changes  
- Family history of breast and other cancers  

Pathophysiology  
- Malignant transformation of breast epithelial cells  
- Invasion of surrounding tissues  
- Angiogenesis promoting tumour growth  
- Potential metastasis to lymph nodes and distant organs  
- Tumour heterogeneity affecting treatment response  

Signs and symptoms  
- Breast lump or mass  
- Changes in breast size or shape  
- Skin changes (dimpling, redness)  
- Nipple discharge or retraction  
- Lymphadenopathy  

Investigations  
- Clinical breast examination  
- Mammography  
- Ultrasound of the breast  
- Biopsy (fine needle aspiration, core biopsy)  
- MRI of the breast (if indicated)  

Diagnosis  
- Imaging findings consistent with malignancy  
- Histopathological confirmation from biopsy  
- Staging of cancer (TNM classification)  
- Assessment of hormone receptor status  
- Evaluation for HER2/neu overexpression  

Management  
- Surgery (lumpectomy or mastectomy)  
  - Sentinel lymph node biopsy may be performed  
  - Consider reconstructive options post-mastectomy  
- Adjuvant therapy  
  - Radiotherapy post-surgery  
  - Hormonal therapy if hormone receptor positive  
- Chemotherapy  
  - Neoadjuvant or adjuvant based on staging  
  - Regimens tailored to individual patient profiles  
- Targeted therapy  
  - HER2-targeted treatment if positive  
  - Consideration of emerging therapies based on genetic profiling  
- Palliative care  
  - Symptom management and support  
  - Psychological support for patients and families  







Breast cysts

background  
- Breast cysts are fluid-filled sacs within breast tissue, commonly found in premenopausal women  

risk factors  
- Age between 35 to 50 years  
- Hormonal changes related to menstrual cycle  
- Family history of breast cysts or breast disease  
- Exogenous hormone use  
- High caffeine intake  

causes  
- Hormonal fluctuations, especially oestrogen and progesterone  
- Fibrocystic changes in breast tissue  
- Leakage or obstruction of ducts  
- Age-related changes in breast tissue  
- Genetic predisposition  

pathophysiology  
- Occurs when a duct becomes blocked or dilated  
- Fluid accumulates leading to cyst formation  
- Surrounding tissue may react, causing tenderness or inflammation  
- Cysts can vary in size and may change with menstrual cycle  
- Can be solitary or multiple  

signs and symptoms  
- Palpable, smooth, well-defined lumps in the breast  
- Tenderness or pain in the affected area  
- Fluctuation in size with menstrual cycle  
- May feel softer or fluctuant compared to surrounding tissue  
- Possible associated nipple discharge in some cases  

investigations  
- Clinical breast examination  
- Ultrasound to assess characteristics of the cyst  
- Fine needle aspiration if the cyst is suspected to be symptomatic  
- Mammography if suspicion of malignancy exists  
- Biopsy may be considered if there are concerning features  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical findings and imaging results  
- Diagnosis confirmed if cyst appears simple and benign on ultrasound  
- Consider differential diagnoses such as solid masses or infections  
- Repeat imaging may be required if cysts persist or change  
- Follow-up consultations advised for symptom changes  

management  
- Observation for asymptomatic simple cysts  
  - Reassess if symptoms develop or cyst changes  
  - Regular monitoring may be suggested  
- Fine needle aspiration for symptomatic or complex cysts  
  - Relieves discomfort and confirms diagnosis  
  - May require repeat aspiration if symptoms recur  
- Patient education on normal breast changes  
  - Discuss lifestyle modifications, e.g., caffeine reduction  
  - Encourage regular breast self-examination  
- Referral to specialist services if concerns arise  
  - For persistent symptoms or difficult cases  
  - To discuss possible surgical options if indicated  
- Follow NICE guidelines for breast cyst management  
  - Individualised care based on patient needs  
  - Promote shared decision-making regarding interventions  







Bronchiectasis

Background  
- Bronchiectasis is a chronic lung condition characterised by abnormal dilation of the bronchi and recurrent respiratory infections  

Risk factors  
- History of respiratory infections such as pneumonia or tuberculosis  
- Genetic conditions like cystic fibrosis  
- Autoimmune diseases such as rheumatoid arthritis  
- Exposure to environmental toxins or pollutants  
- Smoking history  

Causes  
- Post-infectious damage from pneumonia or viral infections  
- Congenital abnormalities including cystic fibrosis  
- Obstruction from tumours or foreign bodies  
- Immune system deficiencies  
- Idiopathic causes  

Pathophysiology  
- Chronic inflammation leading to damage of the bronchial walls  
- Impaired mucociliary clearance resulting in mucus accumulation  
- Increased susceptibility to infections and further inflammation  
- Structural changes in the airways that become dilated and distorted  
- Development of lung scarring and potential fibrosis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chronic cough producing sputum  
- Recurrent respiratory infections  
- Shortness of breath with exertion  
- Wheezing  
- Chest pain  

Investigations  
- High-resolution computed tomography (HRCT) scan of the chest  
- Sputum culture to identify pathogens  
- Pulmonary function tests to assess lung function  
- Blood tests checking for immunodeficiencies  
- Genetic testing for cystic fibrosis if indicated  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings of a chronic productive cough  
- Confirmed by HRCT showing bronchial dilation and thickening  
- Exclusion of other causes of chronic cough or lung disease  
- Microbiological confirmation from sputum cultures  
- Assessment of associated conditions or infections  

Management  
- Airway clearance techniques  
   - Chest physiotherapy  
   - Use of devices such as flutter valves or positive expiratory pressure devices  
- Antibiotics for exacerbations and prophylaxis  
   - Choose based on sputum culture results  
   - Long-term macrolide therapy for some patients  
- Bronchodilators if wheezing or airflow obstruction is present  
   - Beta-agonists or anticholinergics as needed  
   - Regular monitoring for efficacy  
- Management of comorbidities and underlying causes  
   - Treat autoimmune diseases or address allergies  
   - Regular follow-up with respiratory specialists  
- Vaccinations to prevent respiratory infections  
   - Influenza and pneumococcal vaccines recommended  
   - Ensure patient is up to date with vaccinations  







Bronchiolitis

background  
- Bronchiolitis is a common viral respiratory infection in infants and young children characterised by inflammation of the bronchioles

risk factors  
- Age under 2 years  
- Prematurity  
- Underlying respiratory conditions  
- Exposure to tobacco smoke  
- Attendance at childcare settings  

causes  
- Respiratory syncytial virus (RSV)  
- Human rhinovirus  
- Influenza virus  
- Parainfluenza virus  
- Adenovirus  

pathophysiology  
- Viral invasion leads to inflammation and obstruction of small airways  
- Increased mucus production and airway hyperreactivity  
- Air trapping and atelectasis may occur  

signs and symptoms  
- Cough and wheezing  
- Tachypnoea and increased work of breathing  
- Nasal congestion and rhinorrhoea  
- Cyanosis in severe cases  
- Poor feeding and irritability  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of respiratory status  
- Pulse oximetry for oxygen saturation levels  
- Chest X-ray if severe disease or complications suspected  
- Nasopharyngeal aspirate for viral testing if indicated  
- Blood tests generally not required  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and examination  
- Presence of wheezing and respiratory distress in infants under 2 years  
- Exclusion of other conditions with similar symptoms  
- Diagnosis usually primary and clinical without laboratory confirmation  
- Clinical severity may guide further management  

management  
- Supportive care for mild cases includes  
  - Maintaining hydration  
  - Regular suctioning of nasal secretions  
- For moderate to severe cases includes  
  - Oxygen therapy to maintain saturation  
  - Admission for continuous monitoring if needed  
- Consideration of bronchodilators in some cases  
  - More effective in patients with underlying wheezing  
- Corticosteroids not routinely recommended  
  - Reserved for cases with significant wheezing  
- Prevention through palivizumab in high-risk infants  
  - For those with congenital heart disease or chronic lung disease  







Bursitis

Background  
- Bursitis is the inflammation of a bursa, a fluid-filled sac that reduces friction between tissues.

Risk factors  
- Repetitive motions or prolonged pressure on joints  
- Age-related degeneration  
- Previous joint injury  
- Systemic conditions (e.g. rheumatoid arthritis)  
- Infections or gout

Causes  
- Overuse or strain on a joint  
- Trauma or direct blow to a bursa  
- Infection (e.g. septic bursitis)  
- Inflammatory diseases  
- Crystal deposits (e.g. gout)

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the bursa causes swelling and pain  
- Excess synovial fluid production in response to irritation  
- Potential for calcification or chronic changes with repeated episodes  

Signs and symptoms  
- Localised swelling and tenderness  
- Pain during movement or pressure  
- Redness and warmth over the affected area  
- Stiffness in the joint  
- Limited range of motion

Investigations  
- Clinical examination and history  
- Ultrasound to assess fluid collection  
- MRI for deeper structures if needed  
- Aspiration of bursal fluid for analysis  
- Blood tests to rule out systemic conditions

Diagnosis  
- Clinical criteria based on symptoms and examination findings  
- Imaging studies to confirm the diagnosis if required  
- Aspiration results in cases of infection or gout detection  
- Ruling out other causes of joint pain  
- Responsiveness to conservative treatment as a diagnostic factor

Management  
- Rest and modification of activity  
  - Avoiding aggravating activities  
  - Use of braces or supports  
- Anti-inflammatory medications  
  - NSAIDs for pain and inflammation  
  - Corticosteroid injections if severe  
- Physiotherapy for strength and mobility  
  - Guided exercises to improve joint function  
  - Manual therapy techniques  
- Aspiration of the bursa  
  - Remove excess fluid to relieve pressure  
  - Send fluid for analysis if infection suspected  
- Treatment of underlying causes  
  - Manage any inflammatory conditions  
  - Lifestyle changes to reduce risk factors  







Candidiasis

background  
- A fungal infection caused by overgrowth of *Candida* species, commonly affecting oral, vaginal, and systemic sites

risk factors  
- Antibiotic use disrupting normal flora  
- Diabetes mellitus compromising immune response  
- Immunosuppression from medications or diseases  
- Hormonal changes associated with pregnancy or contraceptives  
- High carbohydrate diet promoting fungal growth  

causes  
- Overgrowth of *Candida albicans* as the primary pathogen  
- Other *Candida* species such as *Candida glabrata* and *Candida tropicalis*  
- Environmental factors like moisture and warmth  
- Disruption of normal microbial flora  
- Presence of foreign bodies (e.g. catheters) facilitating infection  

pathophysiology  
- Imbalance of host and commensal relationship leading to fungal overgrowth  
- Inflammatory response resulting from tissue invasion  
- Biofilm formation on mucosal surfaces or implanted devices  
- Immune evasion mechanisms employed by *Candida*  
- Systemic dissemination in immunocompromised patients  

signs and symptoms  
- Oral thrush presenting as white patches in the mouth  
- Vaginal candidiasis with itching, burning, and discharge  
- Skin infections with red, itchy rashes in moist areas  
- Systemic infections may present with fever and malaise  
- Chronic mucocutaneous candidiasis in immunocompromised individuals  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of signs and symptoms  
- Microscopy and culture of affected tissue or fluid  
- KOH preparation for skin scrapings to identify hyphae  
- Blood cultures if systemic candidiasis is suspected  
- Serological tests for specific *Candida* species 

diagnosis  
- Clinical features consistent with candidiasis  
- Positive culture from appropriate samples  
- Identification of *Candida* species if necessary  
- KOH positivity from scrapings confirming fungal elements  
- Exclusion of other differential diagnoses  

management  
- Topical antifungal agents for superficial infections  
  - Clotrimazole or miconazole for oral/vaginal infections  
  - Nystatin for oral thrush treatment  
- Systemic antifungal therapy for severe or disseminated infections  
  - Fluconazole or itraconazole for non-albicans infections  
  - Amphotericin B for life-threatening cases  
- Management of underlying conditions contributing to infection  
  - Control of diabetes and optimisation of immune status  
  - Review and adjust antibiotic or immunosuppressive therapy  
- Education on lifestyle modifications to reduce recurrence  
  - Importance of hygiene and moisture control  
  - Dietary advice to limit sugar intake  
- Regular follow-up to monitor treatment response and prevention of relapse  
  - Assessment of clinical signs and symptoms  
  - Repeat investigations if needed for persistent symptoms  







Cardiac arrest

Background  
- Cardiac arrest is a clinical emergency characterised by the sudden cessation of effective blood circulation due to the heart's failure to pump effectively  

Risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Pre-existing cardiovascular disease  
- Family history of sudden cardiac death  
- Substance abuse, such as cocaine  
- Sedentary lifestyle or poor physical fitness  

Causes  
- Ventricular fibrillation  
- Myocardial infarction  
- Severe electrolyte imbalances  
- Respiratory failure  
- Cardiac tamponade  

Pathophysiology  
- Electrical disturbance leading to ineffective heart contractions  
- Loss of circulation results in decreased oxygen delivery to tissues  
- Cellular and organ damage due to prolonged ischaemia  
- Possible return of spontaneous circulation leading to post-cardiac arrest syndrome  
- Risk of secondary brain injury from hypoxia  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden collapse or loss of consciousness  
- Absence of pulse and breathing  
- Cyanosis or pallor  
- Agonal gasps  
- Unresponsive to stimuli  

Investigations  
- Electrocardiogram to assess rhythm  
- Chest X-ray to evaluate cardiac silhouette and lung pathology  
- Blood tests including electrolytes, troponin, and lactate levels  
- Echocardiography to assess cardiac function if stabilised  
- Advanced cardiac life support protocols  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed by the absence of effective circulation and respiration  
- Assessment of rhythm via ECG is critical in differentiating causes  
- Clinical history and circumstances surrounding collapse  
- Identification of reversible causes during resuscitation efforts  
- Collaboration with emergency services for pre-hospital assessment  

Management  
- Immediate initiation of cardiopulmonary resuscitation  
  - High-quality chest compressions and ventilation  
  - Early defibrillation if indicated by rhythm analysis  
- Advanced life support interventions  
  - Consider airway management options  
  - Administer appropriate medications like adrenaline as per guidelines  
- Post-cardiac arrest care  
  - Targeted temperature management in suitable patients  
  - Intensive monitoring and treatment of underlying causes  
- Referral to cardiology for further evaluation and intervention  
  - Consider implantable cardioverter-defibrillator for secondary prevention  
  - Comprehensive lifestyle and medication management post-event  
- Education on risk factor modification  
  - Promote physical activity and smoking cessation  
  - Encourage adherence to prescribed medications and regular check-ups  







Cardiac failure

Background  
- Cardiac failure is a clinical syndrome resulting from the heart's inability to pump sufficient blood to meet the body's needs

Risk factors  
- Age over 65  
- Hypertension  
- Coronary artery disease  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Obesity  

Causes  
- Ischaemic heart disease  
- Hypertensive heart disease  
- Valvular heart disease  
- Cardiomyopathy  
- Arrhythmias  

Pathophysiology  
- Impaired cardiac function leads to reduced cardiac output  
- Compensatory mechanisms (e.g., neurohormonal activation) may exacerbate heart failure  
- Increased preload and afterload contribute to heart failure progression  
- Remodelling of cardiac tissue occurs over time  
- Congestion in pulmonary and systemic circulation results from fluid retention  

Signs and symptoms  
- Dyspnoea on exertion or at rest  
- Fatigue and reduced exercise tolerance  
- Peripheral oedema  
- Ascites  
- Pulmonary crackles  

Investigations  
- Chest X-ray to assess heart size and pulmonary congestion  
- ECG to identify rhythm abnormalities and ischaemia  
- Echocardiography for assessing cardiac function and structure  
- BNP or NT-proBNP levels to aid diagnosis  
- Blood tests (renal function, electrolytes, and thyroid function)  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of history and physical examination  
- Consideration of investigations (ECG, echocardiography)  
- Use of BNP/NT-proBNP levels alongside clinical judgement  
- Stage classification (NYHA classification)  
- Exclusion of other causes of symptoms  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Dietary salt restriction  
  - Regular physical activity as tolerated  
- Pharmacological therapy  
  - Initiation of ACE inhibitors or ARBs  
  - Consider beta-blockers and diuretics as needed  
- Device therapy  
  - Consider implantable cardioverter defibrillator for high-risk patients  
  - Referral for cardiac resynchronisation therapy if indicated  
- Management of comorbidities  
  - Control of hypertension and diabetes  
  - Address sleep apnoea  
- Palliative care and advanced planning  
  - Discussion of prognosis and treatment options  
  - Support for advanced heart failure management decisions  







Cataracts

background  
- Cataracts are a clouding of the lens of the eye, leading to impaired vision.

risk factors  
- Age  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Smoking  
- Prolonged exposure to UV light  
- Family history of cataracts  

causes  
- Age-related changes in lens proteins  
- Trauma to the eye  
- Certain medications, e.g., corticosteroids  
- Systemic illnesses  
- Congenital factors  

pathophysiology  
- Accumulation of lens proteins leading to opacity  
- Disruption of lens transparency and refraction  
- Formation of reactive oxygen species  
- Interaction between water content and lens proteins  

signs and symptoms  
- Blurred or cloudy vision  
- Difficulty with night vision  
- Sensitivity to glare  
- Fading or yellowing of colours  
- Double vision  

investigations  
- Visual acuity test  
- Slit-lamp examination  
- Retinal exam  
- Tonometry for intraocular pressure  
- Amsler grid for macular function  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on symptoms  
- Confirmation through ophthalmic examination  
- Visual acuity testing  
- Slit-lamp findings  
- Assessment of lens opacity  

management  
- Regular monitoring if mild symptoms  
  - Follow-up every 1-2 years  
  - Educating about lifestyle modifications  
- Surgical intervention for severe cases  
  - Phacoemulsification with intraocular lens implantation  
  - Post-operative follow-up for complications  







Cellulitis

Background  
- Cellulitis is a common bacterial skin infection that affects the deeper layers of the skin and underlying tissues 

Risk factors  
- Age over 50 years  
- Previous history of cellulitis  
- Immunocompromised state  
- Chronic skin conditions (e.g., eczema, psoriasis)  
- Venous insufficiency or lymphedema  

Causes  
- Most commonly caused by *Staphylococcus aureus* and *Streptococcus pyogenes*  
- Open wounds or breaks in the skin  
- Insect bites or animal bites  
- Surgical incisions  
- Associated with diabetes or other systemic diseases  

Pathophysiology  
- Bacteria enter through breaks in the skin  
- Inflammatory response leads to swelling, redness, and pain  
- Can progress to abscess formation and systemic infection   

Signs and symptoms  
- Localised swelling and redness  
- Warmth and tenderness in the affected area  
- Fever and chills  
- Blisters or ulceration possible  
- Lymphangitis and lymphadenopathy  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history-taking  
- Blood tests to assess white cell count and inflammation markers  
- Blood cultures if systemic symptoms are present  
- Wound culture if primary lesion is identified  
- Ultrasound may be used to identify abscess  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical assessment of signs and symptoms  
- Consider differential diagnoses (e.g., deep vein thrombosis, necrotising fasciitis)  
- Assess for systemic involvement (e.g., fever, tachycardia)  
- Consider laboratory investigations if atypical presentation  
- Use scoring systems when appropriate (e.g., Cellulitis Severity Scale)  

Management  
- Broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotics for severe cases   
   - Options include benzylpenicillin or ceftriaxone  
   - Adjust antibiotic therapy based on culture results  
- Oral antibiotics for mild to moderate cases  
   - Options include flucloxacillin or clindamycin  
   - Review treatment after 48-72 hours for effectiveness  
- Elevation of the affected limb and analgesia for pain relief  
   - Immobilise the area to reduce swelling  
   - Use non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs as needed  
- Monitor for complications such as abscess formation  
   - Surgical intervention may be necessary for abscess drainage  
   - Educate on proper wound care to prevent recurrence  
- Follow-up within 1-2 weeks to assess treatment response  
   - Repeat blood tests if initial presentation was severe  
   - Referral to specialist if recurrent or complicated cellulitis







Central retinal arterial occlusion

Background  
- Central retinal arterial occlusion is an acute ophthalmic emergency that can lead to sudden vision loss

Risk factors  
- Age over 60 years  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Smoking  

Causes  
- Embolic occlusion from cardiac sources  
- Cholesterol plaques  
- Thrombotic occlusion  
- Vasculitis  
- Systemic diseases (e.g. giant cell arteritis)  

Pathophysiology  
- A blockage in the central retinal artery leads to reduced blood flow to the retina  
- Resulting in ischaemia and potential irreversible vision loss  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden, painless loss of vision in one eye  
- Possible field defects (e.g. altitudinal field loss)  
- Retinal findings like a pale retina and a "cherry-red spot"  

Investigations  
- Fundoscopy to assess retinal changes  
- Optical coherence tomography  
- Fluorescein angiography if necessary  
- Blood tests for underlying conditions (e.g. lipid profile, inflammatory markers)  
- Carotid Doppler ultrasound  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on history and ocular findings  
- Fundoscopic examination confirming occlusion  
- Consideration of vascular risk factors  

Management  
- Urgent referral to ophthalmology  
  - Assessment for potential treatments  
  - Monitor for complications  
- Treat underlying conditions  
  - Control blood pressure and cholesterol  
  - Manage diabetes  
- Consider anterior chamber paracentesis for refractory cases  
  - Reduces intraocular pressure  
  - May improve retinal perfusion  
- Discuss possible surgical options  
  - Vitrectomy or retinal revascularisation  
  - Consult specialist services  
- Provide legal and occupational advice  
  - Discuss implications of vision loss  
  - Consider rehabilitation services  







Cerebral palsy and hypoxic-ischaemic encephalopathy

Cerebral palsy

Background  
- A group of permanent movement disorders attributed to non-progressive disturbances in the developing brain

Risk factors  
- Premature birth  
- Low birth weight  
- Multiple gestations  
- Maternal infections during pregnancy  
- Birth complications such as asphyxia  

Causes  
- Hypoxic-ischaemic injury  
- Infections such as meningitis or encephalitis  
- Genetic disorders  
- Toxins and teratogens during pregnancy  
- Traumatic brain injury during or after birth  

Pathophysiology  
- Damage to motor areas of the developing brain  
- Disruption of normal neuronal development and myelination  
- Resulting in abnormal muscle tone and movement control 

Signs and symptoms  
- Muscle stiffness or spasticity  
- Poor coordination and balance  
- Difficulty with fine motor skills  
- Associated conditions such as mental retardation or epilepsy  
- Impaired speech and swallowing  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment and developmental history  
- Neuroimaging including MRI or CT scans  
- Genetic testing if a hereditary condition is suspected  
- Blood tests to rule out metabolic disorders  
- Assessment of milestones and functionality  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- Exclusion of other conditions with similar presentations  
- Utilisation of standardised growth and development assessment tools  

Management  
- Multidisciplinary approach  
  - Physiotherapy and occupational therapy to improve functional mobility  
  - Speech and language therapy for communication assistance  
- Pharmacological options  
  - Muscle relaxants such as baclofen for spasticity  
  - Antiepileptic medications if seizures are present  
- Surgical options  
  - Orthopaedic surgery for hip dislocations or severe contractures  
  - Selective dorsal rhizotomy for spasticity in specific muscles  
- Assistive devices  
  - Aids to support mobility and daily activities  
  - Customised seating systems for posture support  
- Family support and education  
  - Resources and training for caregivers  
  - Connection to support groups and counselling services  

Hypoxic-ischaemic encephalopathy

Background  
- A clinical syndrome resulting from inadequate oxygen supply to the brain, leading to neural damage 

Risk factors  
- Birth asphyxia  
- Umbilical cord issues such as prolapse or tight knot  
- Maternal hypotension during labour  
- Chorioamnionitis  
- Intrauterine growth restriction  

Causes  
- Perinatal asphyxia  
- Severe maternal hypotension  
- Cardiac arrest or severe maternal illness  
- Traumatic delivery  
- Congenital abnormalities affecting respiration  

Pathophysiology  
- Reduction in cerebral blood flow leads to neuronal injury  
- Cellular energy failure and apoptosis  
- Subsequent inflammatory response exacerbating the injury  

Signs and symptoms  
- Altered consciousness or responsiveness  
- Seizures or decreased motor activity  
- Hypotonia or hypertonicity  
- Abnormal respiratory patterns  
- Poor feeding and difficulty with suck  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment of neurological status  
- Neuroimaging such as MRI to assess brain injury extent  
- Blood gas analysis for metabolic disturbances  
- Cardiac monitoring to detect arrhythmias  
- EEG to evaluate for seizure activity  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- Assessment of Apgar scores and neurological status  
- Neuroimaging findings to assess extent of injury  
- Exclusion of other neurological conditions  

Management  
- Immediate resuscitation efforts  
  - Airway management and oxygenation support  
  - Cardiopulmonary resuscitation if necessary  
- Therapeutic hypothermia  
  - Cooling to 33.5°C for 72 hours to reduce neuronal injury  
  - Monitoring for potential complications during cooling  
- Control of seizures  
  - Use of antiepileptic drugs if seizures occur  
  - Continuous EEG monitoring in critically ill infants  
- Supportive care  
  - Nutritional support and fluid management  
  - Maintenance of normothermia and electrolyte balance  
- Long-term follow-up  
  - Monitoring developmental progress  
  - Early intervention services for rehabilitation and support  







Cervical cancer

Background  
- Cervical cancer primarily arises from the cervix and is linked to persistent infection with high-risk *Human Papillomavirus* (HPV) types

Risk factors  
- Persistent infection with high-risk HPV  
- Smoking  
- Immunosuppression  
- Long-term use of oral contraceptives  
- Having multiple sexual partners  

Causes  
- High-risk HPV infection  
- Co-infection with other sexually transmitted infections  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Previous dysplastic lesions  
- Prolonged exposure to oestrogen  

Pathophysiology  
- HPV infects cervical epithelial cells, leading to disruption of normal cell cycle  
- Oncogenic HPV types produce proteins (E6 and E7) that inhibit tumour suppressor genes (p53 and Rb)  
- Persistent viral infection results in cellular changes and progression to precancerous lesions  
- Further progression can lead to invasion of surrounding tissues and metastasis  
- Immune evasion mechanisms help the cancer to grow unchecked  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abnormal vaginal bleeding  
- Postcoital bleeding  
- Vaginal discharge that may be watery, bloody, or foul-smelling  
- Pelvic pain or discomfort  
- Urinary symptoms, such as haematuria  

Investigations  
- Cervical screening (Pap smear)  
- HPV testing  
- Colposcopy  
- Biopsy of abnormal tissue  
- Imaging (e.g. MRI, CT scan) for staging  

Diagnosis  
- Histological confirmation via biopsy of cervical tissue  
- Diagnosis of cervical intraepithelial neoplasia (CIN) based on histological grading  
- Staging according to FIGO criteria  
- Incorporation of imaging findings for staging  
- Exclusion of other conditions presenting similarly  

Management  
- Follow NICE guidelines for cervical cancer management  
- Surgery for early-stage disease  
  - Radical hysterectomy  
  - Trachelectomy in select cases  
- Radiation therapy for local control  
  - External beam radiotherapy  
  - Intracavitary brachytherapy in advanced cases  
- Chemotherapy for advanced disease  
  - Platinum-based regimens with radiation  
  - Consideration of targeted therapies in recurrent cases  
- Palliative care for advanced disease  
  - Symptom management  
  - Psychological support for patients and families  
- Regular follow-up for surveillance and management of recurrence  
  - Cervical screening post-treatment  
  - Clinical evaluations at designated intervals  







Cervical screening (HPV)

background  
- Cervical screening aims to detect pre-cancerous changes in the cervix and prevent cervical cancer

risk factors  
- Human papillomavirus (HPV) infection  
- Early sexual activity  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Smoking  
- Immunosuppression  

causes  
- Persistent infection with high-risk HPV types  
- Sexual transmission of HPV  
- Hormonal changes influencing cervical cells  
- Chronic inflammation of the cervix  
- Genetic predisposition  

pathophysiology  
- HPV infects cervical epithelial cells  
- Viral persistence leads to cellular changes  
- Dysplastic changes can progress to cervical intraepithelial neoplasia (CIN)  
- CIN can progress to invasive cervical cancer if untreated  
- The immune response plays a critical role in clearance and progression  

signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic in early stages  
- Abnormal vaginal bleeding  
- Pelvic pain  
- Watery or bloody vaginal discharge  
- Pain during intercourse  

investigations  
- Cervical cytology (Pap smear)  
- HPV testing  
- Colposcopy with biopsy if abnormal results  
- Histopathological examination of biopsy samples  
- Follow-up screens as per guidelines  

diagnosis  
- Abnormal cervical cytology result  
- Positive HPV test indicating high-risk HPV types  
- Diagnosis of CIN via biopsy  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms  
- Exclusion of other gynaecological conditions  

management  
- Routine monitoring if low-grade abnormalities  
  - Repeat cytology and HPV testing at specified intervals  
  - Lifestyle modifications to reduce risk factors  
- Treatment for high-grade lesions or invasive disease  
  - LEEP or cone biopsy for CIN  
  - Referral to oncology for invasive cancer management  
- Vaccination for HPV prevention in eligible groups  
  - Offer HPV vaccine to pre-teens and young adults  
  - Educate about safe sexual practices  
- Follow-up protocols for previous abnormal results  
  - Adhere to NHS screening programme guidelines  
  - Regular communication with patients  
- Provide psychological support if required  
  - Refer to counselling services if needed  
  - Information on support groups and resources  







Chlamydia

background  
- Chlamydia is a common sexually transmitted infection caused by *Chlamydia trachomatis*

risk factors  
- Young age (15-24 years)  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Lack of barrier contraceptive use  
- Previous history of STIs  
- High prevalence in local community  

causes  
- Infection with *Chlamydia trachomatis*  
- Unprotected vaginal, anal, or oral sex  
- Vertical transmission from mother to infant  
- Autosomal recessive disorders affecting immune response  
- Diminished access to sexual health services  

pathophysiology  
- Intracellular bacterium leading to localised infection  
- Host immune response causes inflammation  
- Can ascend to reproductive organs causing complications  
- Possible long-term sequelae without treatment  
- Serovar variation contributes to differing disease severity  

signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic in both men and women  
- Genital discharge or abnormal bleeding  
- Dysuria (painful urination)  
- Pelvic pain or discomfort  
- Peritonitis in cases of PID (Pelvic Inflammatory Disease)  

investigations  
- Nucleic acid amplification tests (NAATs)  
- Urine tests for men and vaginal swabs for women  
- Serology for specific cases  
- Testing for other STIs concurrently  
- Follow-up tests to confirm treatment success  

diagnosis  
- Based on NAAT results or clinical presentation  
- Asymptomatic individuals should be screened regularly  
- Clinically assess for pelvic inflammatory disease in women  
- Consider risk factors and exposure history  
- Exclude other causes of symptoms  

management  
- First-line treatment: Azithromycin or Doxycycline  
   - Single dose of Azithromycin (1g orally)  
   - Doxycycline 100mg twice daily for 7 days  
- Advise partner notification and treatment  
   - Encourage sexual abstinence during treatment  
   - Recommend retesting after 3 months  
- Screen for other STIs if diagnosed  
   - Provide information about safe sex practices  
   - Follow up as needed based on clinical judgment  







Cholecystitis

background  
- Cholecystitis is inflammation of the gallbladder, commonly due to gallstones.

risk factors  
- Obesity  
- Female gender  
- Age over 40  
- Rapid weight loss  
- Family history of gallstones  

causes  
- Gallstones obstructing the cystic duct  
- Bacterial infection  
- Ischaemia due to compromised blood supply  
- Alcohol-related liver disease  
- Post-operative complications  

pathophysiology  
- Obstruction of cystic duct leads to bile accumulation  
- Increased pressure within the gallbladder causes inflammation  
- Bacterial overgrowth may occur due to stagnant bile  
- Edema and necrosis of gallbladder wall can occur  
- Perforation may lead to peritonitis if untreated  

signs and symptoms  
- Right upper quadrant pain  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Fever  
- Cholecystitis sign (Murphy's sign)  
- Jaundice in cases of bile duct obstruction  

investigations  
- Abdominal ultrasound to assess gallbladder and stones  
- Blood tests (LFTs, WBC count, CRP)  
- HIDA scan for cystic duct obstruction  
- CT scan if complications are suspected  
- MRCP for evaluating the biliary tree  

diagnosis  
- Presence of acute right upper quadrant pain  
- Imaging evidence of gallbladder inflammation or stones  
- Positive Murphy's sign  
- Elevated inflammatory markers  
- Ruling out other conditions (e.g., pancreatitis)  

management  
- Initial supportive care (hydration, pain control)  
  - Intravenous fluids  
  - Analgesics  
- Surgical intervention (laparoscopic cholecystectomy)  
  - Preferred within 24-48 hours  
  - Consider open cholecystectomy for complicated cases  
- Antibiotics for suspected infection  
  - Piperacillin-tazobactam or equivalent  
  - Continue until surgery is performed  
- Monitor for complications such as perforation  
  - Regular imaging as needed  
  - Surgical intervention if complications arise  
- Dietary advice post-surgery  
  - Low-fat diet initially  
  - Gradual reintroduction of normal diet  







Chronic fatigue syndrome

background  
- Chronic fatigue syndrome is a debilitating condition characterised by extreme fatigue that does not improve with rest  

risk factors  
- Age between 30 and 50 years  
- Female gender  
- Presence of stress or trauma  
- Underlying physical or mental health conditions  
- Sedentary lifestyle  

causes  
- Viral infections such as Epstein-Barr virus  
- Immune system dysfunction  
- Hormonal imbalances  
- Genetic predispositions  
- Psychological stressors  

pathophysiology  
- Altered cytokine profiles and immune responses  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  
- Mitochondrial dysfunction leading to energy depletion  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances  
- Chronic inflammation  

signs and symptoms  
- Persistent fatigue lasting more than six months  
- Post-exertional malaise  
- Unrefreshing sleep  
- Cognitive difficulties or 'brain fog'  
- Muscle pain and joint pain without swelling  

investigations  
- Comprehensive medical history and physical examination  
- Exclusion of other medical conditions  
- Blood tests to rule out anaemia, thyroid issues, and infections  
- Sleep studies if sleep disorders are suspected  
- Mental health assessments to evaluate for co-morbidities  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis primarily based on symptoms  
- Must meet criteria from NICE guidelines for chronic fatigue syndrome  
- Exclude other possible conditions  
- Use of patient-reported outcome measures  
- Consider presence of accompanying symptoms  

management  
- Educate the patient about the condition  
   - Discuss expected outcomes and coping strategies  
   - Encourage patient involvement in their care plan  
- Gradual increase in physical activity  
   - Tailored exercise programmes guided by a physiotherapist  
   - Monitor progress and adjust as required  
- Cognitive behavioural therapy for addressing psychological impacts  
   - Provide access to trained therapists  
   - Incorporate self-management strategies  
- Symptomatic treatment for associated symptoms  
   - Pain management with NSAIDs or analgesics  
   - Sleep hygiene education and guidance  
- Consider referral to specialist services if condition persists  
   - Referral to a chronic fatigue service  
   - Interdisciplinary approach involving different healthcare professionals  







Chronic glaucoma

Background  
- Chronic glaucoma is a group of eye conditions that damage the optic nerve, often associated with increased intraocular pressure

Risk factors  
- Age over 40 years  
- Family history of glaucoma  
- High intraocular pressure  
- Myopia or hyperopia  
- Diabetes mellitus  

Causes  
- Primary open-angle glaucoma is often idiopathic  
- Secondary glaucoma may result from eye injuries or diseases  
- Corticosteroid use can increase risk  
- Ocular hypertension predisposes individuals  
- Anatomical factors like narrow angles  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased intraocular pressure leads to optic nerve damage  
- Chronic pressure causes loss of retinal ganglion cells  
- Reduced blood flow to optic nerve exacerbates damage  
- Neural apoptosis occurs in response to stress  
- Changes in extracellular matrix affect nerve function  

Signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic in early stages  
- Peripheral vision loss  
- Difficulty adjusting to dark environments  
- Tunnel vision in advanced cases  
- Eye pain or headache in some patients  

Investigations  
- Intraocular pressure measurement via tonometry  
- Visual field testing to assess peripheral vision  
- Fundoscopy to examine the optic nerve head  
- Optical coherence tomography (OCT) for nerve fibre layer thickness  
- Gonioscopy for angle assessment  

Diagnosis  
- Sustained elevated intraocular pressure over 21 mmHg  
- Abnormal visual field test results  
- Characteristic optic nerve head changes  
- Assess family history for hereditary factors  
- Rule out secondary causes of glaucoma  

Management  
- Prostaglandin analogues as first-line treatment  
  - Examples include latanoprost or bimatoprost  
  - Administer once daily for efficacy  
- Beta-blockers if further reduction needed  
  - Timolol maleate or betaxolol prescribed  
  - Monitor for systemic side effects  
- Laser treatments like selective laser trabeculoplasty  
  - Effective for lowering intraocular pressure  
  - May reduce the need for medications  
- Surgical options for refractory cases  
  - Trabeculectomy or tube-shunt surgery  
  - Indicated when medications are insufficient  
- Regular follow-up to monitor disease progression  
  - Adjust treatment based on intraocular pressure and visual field  
  - Educate patients about adherence to therapy  







Chronic kidney disease

Background  
Chronic kidney disease is a progressive condition that affects kidney function over time  

Risk factors  
Ageing  
Diabetes mellitus  
Hypertension  
Obesity  
Cardiovascular disease  

Causes  
Diabetic nephropathy  
Hypertensive nephrosclerosis  
Chronic glomerulonephritis  
Polycystic kidney disease  
Prolonged urinary obstruction  

Pathophysiology  
Progressive nephron loss leads to reduced glomerular filtration rate  
Compensatory hyperfiltration may occur in remaining nephrons  
Accumulation of waste products and toxins results in metabolic disturbances  
Fluid overload and electrolyte imbalances develop as kidney function declines  
End-stage renal disease may develop without appropriate management  

Signs and symptoms  
Fatigue and weakness  
Swelling (oedema) in legs and ankles  
Changes in urination frequency or volume  
Hypertension  
Nausea and vomiting  

Investigations  
Serum creatinine and estimated glomerular filtration rate  
Urinalysis for proteinuria or haematuria  
Blood tests for electrolytes and metabolic panels  
Ultrasound of the kidneys to assess structure  
24-hour urine collection for creatinine clearance  

Diagnosis  
Confirmed by persistently elevated serum creatinine and reduced eGFR  
Assessment of kidney damage through urinalysis and imaging  
Classification based on eGFR stages (1-5)  
Evaluation of underlying causes of kidney dysfunction
