Consideration of associated conditions like hypertension and diabetes  

Management  
Control of blood pressure with ACE inhibitors or ARBs  
- Aim for BP target of <140/90 mmHg  
- Monitor renal function and electrolytes regularly  
Management of diabetes if present  
- Individualised glycaemic targets with appropriate medications  
- Regular monitoring of HbA1c  
Use of statins for cardiovascular risk reduction  
- Aim for a target LDL cholesterol level  
- Discuss lifestyle modifications like diet and exercise  
Referral to nephrology if rapidly progressive or advanced CKD  
- Evaluate options for dialysis or transplant if indicated  
- Supportive care and patient education on CKD management  







Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease

background  
- Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease is a progressive lung disease characterised by persistent respiratory symptoms and airflow limitation  

risk factors  
- Smoking, including passive smoking  
- Occupational dust and chemicals  
- Air pollution, both indoor and outdoor  
- Genetic factors, such as alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency  
- History of childhood respiratory infections  

causes  
- Long-term exposure to harmful particulates and gases  
- Chronic bronchitis leading to airway inflammation and obstruction  
- Emphysema causing alveolar destruction and loss of elasticity  
- Asthma contributing to fixed airflow limitation  
- Rare genetic conditions affecting lung function  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the airways leads to mucus hypersecretion  
- Progressive destruction of alveoli results in impaired gas exchange  
- Airflow limitation caused by airway narrowing and loss of lung elasticity  
- Systemic effects including muscle wasting and cardiovascular complications  
- Exacerbations caused by infections, pollutants, or non-compliance with treatment  

signs and symptoms  
- Chronic cough and sputum production  
- Dyspnoea on exertion and at rest  
- Wheezing and chest tightness  
- Frequent respiratory infections and exacerbations  
- Cyanosis and signs of respiratory failure in advanced cases  

investigations  
- Spirometry to measure airflow obstruction  
- Chest X-ray to rule out other conditions  
- CT scan of the chest for detailed lung structure  
- Arterial blood gas analysis for oxygen and carbon dioxide levels  
- Six-minute walk test to assess exercise tolerance  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed by post-bronchodilator spirometry showing FEV1/FVC ratio < 0.70  
- Consider symptoms and risk factors in the diagnostic process  
- Use of validated questionnaires for symptom assessment  
- Assessment of severity based on airflow limitation  
- Exclusions of other conditions with similar symptoms  

management  
- Smoking cessation support and counselling  
  - Pharmacotherapy with nicotine replacement and medications  
  - Behavioural therapies and support groups  
- Long-acting bronchodilator therapy  
  - Use of beta-2 agonists or anticholinergics  
  - Assessing individual response to optimise therapy  
- Inhaled corticosteroids for frequent exacerbations  
  - Combination therapy if symptoms persist  
  - Regular review to minimise side effects  
- Pulmonary rehabilitation programme  
  - Exercise training and education on disease management  
  - Improvement of quality of life and exercise capacity  
- Oxygen therapy for patients with severe hypoxaemia  
  - Long-term oxygen therapy if indicated  
  - Monitoring of oxygen saturation and clinical status  







Cirrhosis

background  
- Advanced scarring of the liver resulting from chronic liver disease

risk factors  
- Chronic viral hepatitis (B or C)  
- Alcohol misuse  
- Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease  
- Autoimmune liver diseases  
- Genetic disorders (Wilson's disease, haemochromatosis)

causes  
- Chronic viral infections  
- Alcohol-related liver disease  
- Metabolic liver disorders  
- Drug-induced liver injury  
- Biliary obstruction or disease

pathophysiology  
- Repeated liver injury leads to inflammation and activation of stellate cells  
- Fibrosis occurs as a repair response, resulting in scarring  
- Disrupted hepatic architecture impairs blood flow and liver function  
- Formation of regenerative nodules within fibrotic tissue  
- Complications arise from portal hypertension and liver dysfunction

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Jaundice  
- Ascites  
- Encephalopathy  
- Spider angiomata and palmar erythema

investigations  
- Liver function tests  
- Viral hepatitis serologies  
- Imaging (ultrasound, CT, or MRI)  
- Liver biopsy (in select cases)  
- Coagulation profile

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings  
- Imaging studies showing nodular liver and portal hypertension  
- Laboratory tests indicating impaired liver function  
- Histological confirmation in uncertain cases  
- Exclusion of alternative diagnoses

management  
- Lifestyle modifications (abstinence from alcohol, weight management)  
  - Educate on dietary needs  
  - Promote exercise  
- Pharmacological treatments (diuretics for ascites, lactulose for hepatic encephalopathy)  
  - Adjust medications based on liver function  
  - Monitor for side effects  
- Screening for hepatocellular carcinoma  
  - Regular ultrasound and AFP testing  
  - Follow NICE guidelines on surveillance  
- Management of complications (variceal bleeding, prevention of infections)  
  - Beta-blockers for portal hypertension  
  - Antibiotics for spontaneous bacterial peritonitis prophylaxis  
- Consideration for liver transplantation  
  - Assess candidacy based on overall health  
  - Multidisciplinary team involvement for evaluation







Coeliac disease

Background  
- Coeliac disease is an autoimmune disorder triggered by the ingestion of gluten in genetically predisposed individuals  

Risk factors  
- First-degree relatives with coeliac disease  
- Other autoimmune conditions (e.g. type 1 diabetes, thyroid disease)  
- Specific genetic markers (HLA-DQ2 and HLA-DQ8)  
- Age of introduction of gluten in infancy  
- Female sex  

Causes  
- Ingestion of gluten (wheat, barley, rye)  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental factors (e.g. infections)  
- Dysbiosis of gut microbiota  
- Immune system dysregulation  

Pathophysiology  
- Gluten peptides are deamidated by tissue transglutaminase   
- Presentation of deamidated gliadin to T cells activates an immune response  
- Inflammation leads to damage of the small intestinal villi  
- Resulting malabsorption of nutrients  
- Development of various gastrointestinal and extraintestinal symptoms  

Signs and symptoms  
- Diarrhoea, abdominal pain, and bloating  
- Weight loss and growth failure in children  
- Fatigue and nutritional deficiencies  
- Dermatitis herpetiformis  
- Osteoporosis and neurological symptoms (e.g. ataxia)  

Investigations  
- Serological tests for anti-tissue transglutaminase antibodies (tTG-IgA)  
- Total IgA levels to rule out IgA deficiency  
- Biopsy of the small intestine (duodenum) to assess villous atrophy  
- Genetic testing for HLA-DQ2/DQ8 if clinically indicated  
- Response to a gluten-free diet as a clinical investigation  

Diagnosis  
- Positive serological tests in conjunction with symptoms  
- Duodenal biopsy showing villous atrophy and intraepithelial lymphocytes  
- Exclusion of other conditions with similar presentations  
- Confirmation by clinical response to a gluten-free diet  
- Monitoring for complications (e.g. nutritional deficiencies)  

Management  
- Strict adherence to a lifelong gluten-free diet  
  - Education on reading labels and avoiding gluten-containing foods  
  - Consider referral to a dietitian for specialised dietary advice  
- Nutritional supplementation if deficiencies are identified  
  - Iron and vitamin D supplementation for deficiencies  
  - Regular follow-up to monitor nutritional status  
- Management of associated conditions (e.g. osteoporosis)  
- Regular monitoring of antibodies and intestinal health  
  - Follow-up serological testing annually  
  - Reassess biopsy if symptoms return  
- Consider screening for related conditions (e.g. thyroid disorders)  







Colorectal tumours

background  
- Colorectal tumours are abnormal growths in the colon or rectum, which can be benign or malignant.  

risk factors  
- Age over 50 years  
- Family history of colorectal cancer or polyps  
- Inflammatory bowel disease such as Crohn's disease or ulcerative colitis  
- Lifestyle factors like obesity, smoking, and low physical activity  
- Diet high in red or processed meats  

causes  
- Genetic mutations (e.g., APC, MLH1, MSH2)  
- Familial adenomatous polyposis  
- Lynch syndrome (hereditary non-polyposis colorectal cancer)  
- Diet and environmental influences  
- Previous history of colorectal polyps or cancer  

pathophysiology  
- Disruption of normal cellular regulation leading to uncontrolled cell growth  
- Adenoma-carcinoma sequence involving dysplasia and genetic alterations  
- Tumour microenvironment influencing cancer progression  
- Metastasis potential through lymphatic and vascular systems  
- Tumour heterogeneity impacting treatment response  

signs and symptoms  
- Changes in bowel habits (diarrhoea or constipation)  
- Rectal bleeding or blood in stool  
- Abdominal pain or discomfort  
- Unexplained weight loss  
- Anaemia from chronic blood loss  

investigations  
- Colonoscopy for direct visualisation and biopsy  
- CT colonography as a non-invasive imaging alternative  
- Fecal occult blood test for screening  
- Blood tests for complete blood count and tumour markers (e.g., CEA)  
- MRI for local staging and assessing metastasis  

diagnosis  
- Histological confirmation of malignancy via biopsy  
- Staging with imaging studies (CT or MRI) to assess tumour spread  
- Classification based on histopathological features (e.g., adenocarcinoma)  
- Evaluate for associated genetic syndromes if clinically indicated  
- Multidisciplinary team discussion for holistic management planning  

management  
- Surgical resection for curative intent in localized disease  
  - Consideration of minimally invasive techniques (laparoscopic)  
  - Adjuvant chemotherapy for high-risk patient groups  
- Chemotherapy for metastatic disease or non-resectable tumours  
  - Regimens often include FOLFOX or FOLFIRI  
  - Targeted therapies (e.g., anti-EGFR) for specific mutations  
- Regular surveillance post-treatment to monitor for recurrence  
  - Colonoscopy follow-up based on risk stratification  
  - Blood tests for tumour markers periodically  
- Palliative care for advanced disease to manage symptoms  
  - Access to multidisciplinary palliative teams  
  - Focus on quality of life and symptom control  
- Patient education on lifestyle modifications to reduce risk  
  - Promotion of a balanced diet and physical activity  
  - Smoking cessation support programs  







Compartment syndrome

background  
- Compartment syndrome is a condition involving increased pressure within a closed muscle compartment, leading to tissue ischaemia 

risk factors  
- Fractures or trauma to the limbs  
- Vascular injuries  
- Burns or crush injuries  
- Vigorous exercise or extreme exertion  
- Tight casts or dressings 

causes  
- Increased intracompartmental pressure due to swelling  
- Muscle or vascular injury  
- Venous obstruction  
- External compression from tight dressings or swelling  
- Ischaemic reperfusion injury 

pathophysiology  
- Elevated pressure impairs blood flow to nerves and muscles  
- Ischaemia leads to cellular injury and necrosis if untreated  
- Release of inflammatory mediators exacerbates tissue damage  
- Nerve dysfunction occurs before irreversible muscle injury  
- Possible loss of limb function if pressure not relieved

signs and symptoms  
- Severe pain that is disproportionate to the injury  
- Swelling or tenderness in the affected area  
- Decreased sensation or paresthesia  
- Weakness or paralysis of the affected muscles  
- Hard or tense compartment on examination  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Measurement of intracompartmental pressure (considering >30 mmHg as abnormal)  
- Non-invasive imaging (e.g., ultrasound) if necessary  
- Assessment of blood supply (e.g., Doppler studies)  
- Laboratory tests to evaluate muscle enzyme levels  

diagnosis  
- Strong clinical suspicion based on history and assessment  
- Confirmatory measurement of intracompartmental pressure  
- Exclude other conditions (e.g., venous thromboembolism)  
- Consider MRI or ultrasound if differential diagnosis is required  
- Early recognition is crucial for effective management  

management  
- Surgical fasciotomy to relieve pressure  
  - Immediate intervention recommended for confirmed cases  
  - Delayed intervention increases risk of irreversible damage  
- Supportive care and monitoring post-surgery  
  - Assessment of muscle viability and function  
  - Pain management and rehabilitation  









Conjunctivitis

Background  
- Conjunctivitis is the inflammation of the conjunctiva, often leading to redness and discharge

Risk factors  
- Contact lens use  
- Recent upper respiratory tract infections  
- Allergies  
- Exposure to irritants such as smoke or chemicals  
- Viral infections in the community  

Causes  
- Viral infections (e.g. adenovirus)  
- Bacterial infections (e.g. *Staphylococcus aureus*, *Haemophilus influenzae*)  
- Allergic reactions (e.g. pollen, dust mites)  
- Irritants (e.g. chlorine, soaps)  
- Autoimmune conditions  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of conjunctival tissues  
- Increased vascular permeability leading to redness  
- Exudate production, which can be watery or purulent  
- Conjunctival epithelial cell infiltration and hyperplasia  
- T-helper cell activation in allergic conjunctivitis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Redness of the eye  
- Itching or burning sensation  
- Discharge (watery or purulent)  
- Swollen eyelids  
- Sensitivity to light  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and examination  
- Conjunctival swab for culture (if bacterial infection suspected)  
- Allergens testing (if allergic conjunctivitis suspected)  
- Visual acuity assessment  
- Slit-lamp examination if complications are considered  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on symptoms and signs  
- Differentiate between viral, bacterial, allergic, and irritative causes  
- Consider culture results if bacterial conjunctivitis is suspected  
- History of contact lens use or recent infections  
- Referral if severe symptoms or complications arise  

Management  
- Viral conjunctivitis  
  - Symptomatic relief with cool compresses  
  - Artificial tears to alleviate discomfort  
- Bacterial conjunctivitis  
  - Topical antibiotic drops (e.g. chloramphenicol)  
  - Ensure compliance with treatment duration  
- Allergic conjunctivitis  
  - Avoidance of known allergens  
  - Topical antihistamines or mast cell stabilisers  
- Irritative conjunctivitis  
  - Rinse eyes with saline to remove irritant  
  - Avoid exposure to irritants  
- Unresolved or severe cases  
  - Referral to an ophthalmologist  
  - Consideration of other underlying conditions  







Constipation

Background  
- Constipation is a common gastrointestinal condition characterised by infrequent bowel movements and difficulty in passing stool  

Risk factors  
- Age over 65  
- Sedentary lifestyle  
- Low fibre diet  
- Medications (e.g. opioids, antacids)  
- Psychological factors (e.g. stress, depression)  

Causes  
- Dietary factors (low fluid and fibre intake)  
- Lack of physical activity  
- Medications affecting bowel motility  
- Underlying health conditions (e.g. hypothyroidism, diabetes)  
- Structural abnormalities (e.g. strictures, tumours)  

Pathophysiology  
- Reduced colonic motility leading to prolonged stool transit time  
- Increased water absorption from stool, resulting in hard, dry stools  
- Impaired rectal sensation or function contributing to difficulties in defecation  

Signs and symptoms  
- Infrequent bowel movements (less than three times a week)  
- Straining or difficulty during bowel movements  
- Hard or lumpy stools  
- Abdominal discomfort or bloating  
- Feeling of incomplete evacuation  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Abdominal X-ray (to check for stool retention)  
- Blood tests (to assess for underlying conditions)  
- Anorectal manometry (to evaluate rectal function)  
- Colonoscopy if alarm symptoms present  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria (e.g. Rome IV criteria)  
- Exclusion of secondary causes via investigations if indicated  
- Consideration of history of symptoms and dietary habits  
- Assessment of alarm symptoms like weight loss or rectal bleeding  
- Diagnosis of functional constipation if no secondary cause found  

Management  
- Dietary modifications to increase fibre intake  
  - Aim for 30g of fibre per day  
  - Increase fluid intake to ensure hydration  
- Laxative use as first-line pharmacological treatment  
  - Start with osmotic laxatives (e.g. polyethylene glycol)  
  - Consider stimulant laxatives if no response  
- Behavioural modifications (e.g. regular bowel routines)  
  - Encourage physical activity  
  - Recommend responding to bowel urges promptly  
- Treatment of underlying causes if identified  
  - Manage medications contributing to constipation  
  - Address any psychological factors present  
- Referral to specialist if symptoms persist or complicate  
  - Gastroenterology assessment for further evaluation  
  - Consideration of surgical options for obstructive cases  







Contact dermatitis

background  
- Contact dermatitis is a common inflammatory skin condition caused by exposure to irritants or allergens

risk factors  
- Occupational exposure to irritants or allergens  
- Atopic dermatitis or a personal history of allergies  
- Family history of allergic conditions  
- Frequent handwashing or wet work exposure  
- Use of harsh soaps or chemicals  

causes  
- Irritant contact dermatitis from friction, chemicals, or extreme temperatures  
- Allergic contact dermatitis from allergens like nickel, latex, or fragrances  
- Sunlight exposure leading to photoallergic reactions  
- Plants, such as *Toxicodendron radicans* (poison ivy) causing allergic reactions  
- Preservatives or additives in topical products  

pathophysiology  
- Involves immune-mediated responses in allergic cases  
- Damaged skin barrier in irritant contact dermatitis  
- Sensitisation phase in allergic types leading to T-cell activation  
- Re-exposure causes an inflammatory response  
- Cytokines and immune cells contribute to inflammation  

signs and symptoms  
- Redness, itching, and swelling of affected areas  
- Vesicles or blisters in allergic cases  
- Dry, cracked, or flaky skin in irritant cases  
- Localised or generalised rash depending on exposure  
- Possible secondary infections due to scratching  

investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Patch testing for suspected allergens in allergic cases  
- Skin biopsy if diagnosis is unclear  
- Elimination of potential irritants for differentiation  
- Assessment of work and lifestyle factors  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical history and symptoms  
- Patch testing confirms allergic contact dermatitis  
- Identification of irritants through thorough patient history  
- Exclusion of other dermatological conditions  
- Clinical response to avoidance of suspected triggers  

management  
- Avoidance of identified irritants and allergens  
  - Provide education on avoiding triggers  
  - Recommend protective clothing and skin care  
- Symptomatic treatment for itching and inflammation  
  - Topical corticosteroids for inflammation  
  - Antihistamines for severe itching  
- Moisturisers to restore skin barrier  
  - Regular application of emollients  
  - Avoidance of soaps and detergents  
- Referral for dermatology if persistent or severe  
  - Consider dermatological therapies  
  - Review management plan  
- Patient education on condition management  
  - Inform about the nature of the condition  
  - Discuss lifestyle changes to prevent flare-ups  







Cord prolapse

background  
- Cord prolapse occurs when the umbilical cord slips ahead of the presenting part during labour, risking fetal hypoxia  

risk factors  
- Presenting part not engaged  
- Multiple gestation  
- Polyhydramnios  
- Preterm delivery  
- Abnormal fetal or maternal pelvic shape  

causes  
- Excessive fetal movement  
- Non-cephalic presentation  
- Premature rupture of membranes  
- Uterine anomalies or abnormalities  
- Intrauterine tumours  

pathophysiology  
- Compression of the umbilical cord leads to reduced blood flow  
- Can cause fetal distress due to hypoxia and potential acidosis  
- May result in cord ischaemia if severe and prolonged  
- Occurs with loss of protective cushioning from amniotic fluid  
- Associated with abnormal fetal heart rate patterns  

signs and symptoms  
- Fetal heart rate abnormalities, e.g., variable decelerations  
- Palpable cord through the cervix during vaginal examination  
- Maternal reporting of decreased fetal movements  
- Signs of fetal distress or metabolic acidosis in severe cases  
- Examination may reveal abnormal auscultation patterns  

investigations  
- Continuous fetal heart rate monitoring  
- Ultrasound for assessment of fetal position and cord  
- Analysis of maternal vital signs for signs of distress  
- Amniotic fluid assessment if membrane rupture occurs  
- Clinical examination during labour  

diagnosis  
- Identification of cord via vaginal examination  
- Fetal heart rate monitoring identifying variable decelerations  
- Clinical judgement based on maternal-fetal status  
- Assessment of maternal contractions and fetal presentation  
- Confirmation through obstetric imaging if required  

management  
- Immediate delivery, either vaginal or via caesarean section  
  - Depends on fetal condition and stage of labour  
  - Surgery if necessary for delivery  
- Positioning mother in a knee-chest or lateral position  
  - Aims to relieve cord compression  
  - Use of tocolytics may assist in some cases  
- Continuous fetal monitoring for signs of distress  
  - Allow for rapid intervention as needed  
  - Regular assessment of maternal condition  
- Ensure preparation for potential neonatal resuscitation  
  - Staff trained for immediate newborn care  
  - Equipment ready for possible complications  
- Address underlying causes and provide supportive care  
  - Manage fluid and electrolyte balance  
  - Emotional support and counselling for parents  







Covid-19

background  
- Covid-19 is a highly infectious respiratory illness caused by the novel coronavirus SARS-CoV-2

risk factors  
- Older age  
- Chronic respiratory or cardiovascular diseases  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Obesity  
- Immunocompromised status  

causes  
- Infection with SARS-CoV-2 virus  
- Community transmission  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Contaminated surfaces  
- Airborne spread in enclosed spaces  

pathophysiology  
- Virus enters host cells via ACE2 receptors  
- Triggers inflammatory response leading to cytokine storm  
- Impairment of alveolar capillary membrane  
- Development of acute respiratory distress syndrome  
- Potential multi-organ involvement  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever or chills  
- Cough  
- Difficulty breathing  
- Fatigue  
- Loss of taste or smell  

investigations  
- RT-PCR for viral detection  
- Lateral flow antigen tests  
- Imaging (e.g., chest X-ray or CT) for pneumonia  
- Blood tests for inflammatory markers  
- Serology for antibody detection post-infection  

diagnosis  
- Positive RT-PCR or antigen test  
- Clinical presentation consistent with Covid-19  
- Imaging confirming pneumonia in symptomatic patients  
- History of contact with a confirmed case  
- Exclusion of other respiratory pathogens  

management  
- Supportive care, including oxygen therapy  
  - Administer supplemental oxygen as needed  
  - Monitor respiratory status continuously  
- Antiviral medications (e.g., remdesivir) in severe cases  
  - Consider for hospitalised patients with severe disease  
  - Initiate within 10 days of symptom onset  
- Corticosteroids (e.g., dexamethasone) for severe cases  
  - Administer to patients requiring oxygen  
  - Avoid in mild or moderate cases  
- Monoclonal antibody therapies for high-risk patients  
  - Use in outpatients within 10 days of symptom onset  
  - Requires specific eligibility criteria  
- Vaccination for prevention  
  - Administer according to NHS guidelines  
  - Encourage uptake in vulnerable populations  







Croup

Background  
- Croup is a common respiratory condition in young children characterised by a barking cough and stridor

Risk factors  
- Age between 6 months and 3 years  
- Recent upper respiratory tract infection  
- Exposure to second-hand smoke  
- Allergies or atopic conditions  
- Male gender  

Causes  
- Viral infections, particularly by *Parainfluenza* virus  
- *Influenza* and *Rhinovirus* infections  
- Non-infectious causes such as allergens or irritants  
- Less common bacterial infections  
- Gastroesophageal reflux in some cases  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation and swelling of the larynx, trachea, and bronchi  
- Increased mucus production  
- Narrowing of the airway leading to stridor and respiratory distress  
- Increased effort required for breathing

Signs and symptoms  
- Barking cough  
- Stridor on inspiration  
- Hoarseness  
- Fever  
- Respiratory distress, including retractions  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment based on history and physical examination  
- Pulse oximetry to assess oxygen saturation  
- Blood tests if secondary bacterial infection is suspected  
- Chest X-ray rarely needed unless complications suspected  
- Parental report of symptoms for context and severity  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation of symptoms  
- Modified Wood's clinical score to assess severity  
- Exclude other diagnoses like epiglottitis or foreign body aspiration  
- Consider a history of illness leading to current symptoms  
- Follow NICE guidelines for categorising mild, moderate, and severe croup  

Management  
- Mild croup: reassurance and supportive care  
  - Encourage fluid intake  
  - Use of a humidifier   
- Moderate to severe croup: corticosteroids and nebulised adrenaline  
  - Dexamethasone as first-line treatment  
  - Monitor for response and oxygen levels  
- Hospital admission for severe cases or respiratory distress  
  - Continuous monitoring of airway and saturation  
  - Prepare for possible intubation  
- Parental education on warning signs and follow-up care  
  - Provide written information  
  - Plan for follow-up appointments  
- Avoid unnecessary antibiotics unless secondary bacterial infection suspected  
  - Review clinical signs daily  
  - Ensure an appropriate timeline for any further investigations  







Crystal arthropathy

background  
- A group of conditions caused by the deposition of crystals in joints and surrounding tissues 

risk factors  
- Age over 40  
- Obesity  
- Family history of crystal arthropathies  
- Chronic kidney disease  
- Certain metabolic disorders (e.g., hyperuricaemia)  

causes  
- Monosodium urate crystals leading to gout  
- Calcium pyrophosphate dihydrate crystals causing pseudogout  
- Other less common crystals (e.g., hydroxyapatite)  
- Trauma or joint surgery  
- Genetic predisposition  

pathophysiology  
- Crystal deposition triggers inflammatory response  
- Neutrophil migration and activation in joint space  
- Release of pro-inflammatory cytokines  
- Chondrocyte damage and synovial inflammation  
- Recurrent attacks leading to joint damage  

signs and symptoms  
- Intense joint pain, often sudden in onset  
- Swelling and tenderness in the affected joint  
- Erythema over the joint  
- Limited range of motion  
- Possible fever during acute attacks  

investigations  
- Joint aspiration and synovial fluid analysis  
- Polarised light microscopy to identify crystals  
- Serum urate levels (not definitive for acute gout)  
- X-rays to assess joint damage in chronic cases  
- Ultrasound for detecting crystal deposits  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and examination findings  
- Synovial fluid microscopy confirming presence of crystals  
- Serum urate levels to support diagnosis of gout  
- Exclusion of other causes of arthritis  
- Diagnosis of pseudogout confirmed through identification of calcium pyrophosphate crystals  

management  
- Acute attack management:  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
  - Colchicine for severe pain relief  
- Long-term management:  
  - Uric acid-lowering therapy (e.g., allopurinol)  
  - Lifestyle modifications (diet, exercise, weight management)  
- Joint aspirations for symptomatic relief in acute arthritis  
- Patient education on risk factor modification  
- Regular monitoring of serum urate levels for treatment efficacy  







Cushing’s syndrome

background  
- Cushing's syndrome is a hormonal disorder caused by prolonged exposure to high levels of cortisol 

risk factors  
- Female sex  
- Age between 30 and 50  
- Obesity  
- Use of corticosteroid medications  
- History of pituitary or adrenal tumours  

causes  
- Pituitary adenoma secreting adrenocorticotropic hormone  
- Adrenal adenomas or carcinomas  
- Ectopic ACTH production from tumours  
- Chronic use of glucocorticoids  
- Cushing's disease (specifically the pituitary cause)  

pathophysiology  
- Excess cortisol disrupts normal metabolism  
- Causes abnormalities in carbohydrate, protein, and fat metabolism  
- Impairs immune response  
- Leads to increased blood pressure  
- Results in increased central obesity and skin changes  

signs and symptoms  
- Weight gain, particularly central obesity  
- Moon facies and facial plethora  
- Easy bruising and skin thinning  
- Hypertension  
- Muscle weakness and fatigue  

investigations  
- 24-hour urinary free cortisol test  
- Late-night salivary cortisol test  
- Low-dose dexamethasone suppression test  
- Plasma ACTH levels  
- Imaging studies (CT or MRI) to identify tumours  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed if cortisol excess is demonstrated  
- Differential diagnosis to differentiate between ACTH-dependent and independent causes  
- Evaluate adrenal function  
- Consider patient's history and medication use  
- Consider genetic testing if indicated  

management  
- Surgical resection of adenoma or tumour  
  - Transsphenoidal surgery for pituitary adenomas  
  - Laparoscopic adrenalectomy for adrenal tumours  
- Medical therapy in inoperable cases  
  - Mifepristone for hyperglycaemia  
  - Ketoconazole or metyrapone to control cortisol production  
- Radiotherapy for residual tumours  
  - Stereotactic radiosurgery for pituitary adenomas  
  - External beam radiotherapy if surgery is not feasible  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Dietary advice for weight management  
  - Exercise regimen for physical fitness  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Regular screening for diabetes, hypertension, and osteoporosis  
  - Continuous assessment of cortisol levels and symptoms  







Cutaneous fungal infection

background  
- Cutaneous fungal infections are common skin conditions caused by various fungal species affecting the skin, hair, and nails 

risk factors  
- Immunosuppression  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Obesity  
- Poor hygiene  
- Use of broad-spectrum antibiotics  

causes  
- Dermatophytes such as *Trichophyton*, *Microsporum*, and *Epidermophyton*  
- Yeasts, particularly *Candida* species  
- Moulds, such as *Aspergillus*  
- Environmental exposure, particularly in warm and humid conditions  
- Contact with infected individuals or animals  

pathophysiology  
- Fungal invasion of keratinised tissues leads to tissue damage  
- Increased inflammatory response causing erythema and pruritus  
- Fungal elements can spread laterally and deeper into the dermis  
- Chronic infections may lead to secondary bacterial infections  
- Immune response variability among individuals affects the severity of symptoms  

signs and symptoms  
- Erythematous, scaly lesions  
- Pruritus or itchiness  
- Hair loss in cases of scalp involvement  
- Nail discolouration and distortion  
- Possible formation of vesicles or pustules  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of skin lesions  
- KOH (potassium hydroxide) preparation of skin or nail samples  
- Fungal culture from skin scraping or nail clipping  
- Wood's lamp examination for certain fungal infections  
- Biopsy may be considered in atypical cases  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and physical examination  
- Confirmatory tests like KOH preparation or culture  
- Rule out other skin conditions such as eczema or psoriasis  
- Consider history of exposure to known infections  
- Monitor for response to treatment in suspected cases  

management  
- Topical antifungals (e.g. clotrimazole, miconazole)  
  - Apply as directed until lesions resolve  
  - Continue for 1-2 weeks after symptoms resolve  
- Oral antifungals for extensive or resistant cases (e.g. terbinafine, fluconazole)  
  - Initiate based on severity and type of infection  
  - Monitor for potential side effects and drug interactions  
- Adjunctive measures like maintaining skin dryness  
  - Educate on hygiene and care of affected areas  
  - Advise on avoiding sharing personal items  
- Follow-up to assess treatment effectiveness  
  - Repeat investigations if symptoms persist  
  - Adjust management based on response  
- Consider referral to dermatology for resistant or complicated cases  
  - Involve specialists if systemic involvement is suspected  
  - Coordinate care for immunocompromised patients







Cutaneous warts

Background  
- Cutaneous warts are benign epithelial tumours caused by human papillomavirus (HPV) infection

Risk factors  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Weakened immune system  
- Skin injury or abrasion  
- Sharing personal items like towels or razors  
- Age, most common in children and adolescents  

Causes  
- Infection with human papillomavirus types 1, 2, 4, 27, or 57  
- Skin-to-skin contact with an infected person  
- Autoinoculation from other areas of the body  
- Environmental factors such as warmth and moisture  
- High-risk HPV types can cause different wart presentations  

Pathophysiology  
- HPV invades the keratinocytes in the epidermis  
- Viral replication leads to hyperproliferation of the infected skin cells  
- Resulting in the characteristic appearance of warts  
- Immune response may vary leading to persistence or clearance of the warts  
- Can spread to neighbouring skin cells due to superficial nature  

Signs and symptoms  
- Raised, rough lesions on the skin  
- Can be single or multiple  
- Typically painless but can be tender, especially on pressure points  
- May have black dots (thrombosed capillaries) in the centre  
- Variation in appearance based on wart type (e.g., common, plantar, flat)  

Investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on appearance  
- Dermatoscopy may be used to examine lesions  
- Exclusion of other skin conditions such as seborrheic keratosis  
- Biopsy rarely needed, generally only to rule out malignancy  
- PCR or HPV typing not routinely performed  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmatory clinical assessment based on wart appearance  
- Consideration of patient history and risk factors  
- Exclude differential diagnoses based on clinical features  
- Assessing any symptoms of pain or discomfort  
- Monitor for changes in size, colour, or appearance  

Management  
- Watchful waiting as many warts resolve spontaneously  
  - Advise on self-care and hygiene practices  
- Topical treatments such as salicylic acid or cryotherapy  
  - Consider weekly treatments for 4-12 weeks  
- Seminal treatments include laser therapy for resistant warts  
  - Referral to a dermatologist if extensive or painful  
- Surgical options for large or symptomatic warts  
  - Often requires local anaesthesia  
- Education on preventing transmission and recurrence  
  - Encourage use of personal items and protective measures  







Cystic fibrosis

Background  
- Cystic fibrosis is a genetic disorder affecting the respiratory and digestive systems due to defective ion transport.

Risk factors  
- Family history of cystic fibrosis  
- Inherited mutations in the CFTR gene  
- Ethnicity (more common in Caucasians)  
- Male sex predilection  
- History of meconium ileus at birth  

Causes  
- Mutations in the CFTR gene located on chromosome 7  
- Over 2000 identified mutations, with F508del being the most common  
- Dysfunctional chloride ion transport across epithelial cells  
- Results in thick, sticky mucus production  
- Impaired function of exocrine glands  

Pathophysiology  
- Thick mucus obstructs airways, leading to respiratory infections  
- Pancreatic ducts blockage causes malabsorption of nutrients  
- Decreased chloride and bicarbonate ion secretion in sweat glands  
- Chronic inflammation and lung damage due to recurrent infections  
- Formation of cysts and fibrotic tissue in affected organs  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chronic productive cough with sputum  
- Recurrent lung infections and nasal polyps  
- Digestive issues, including steatorrhoea and poor growth  
- Salty sweat leading to dehydration  
- Clubbing of fingers and toes  

Investigations  
- Newborn screening for immunoreactive trypsinogen  
- Sweat test to measure chloride levels  
- Genetic testing for CFTR mutations  
- Chest X-ray to assess lung condition  
- Spirometry to evaluate lung function  

Diagnosis  
- Confirm diagnosis with sweat test showing elevated chloride levels  
- Genetic testing to identify CFTR mutations for definitive diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and family history  
- Use of imaging and lung function tests to support diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other conditions with similar symptoms  

Management  
- Multidisciplinary care involving respiratory and gastroenterology specialists  
  - Regular monitoring of lung function and nutritional status  
  - Genetic counselling and support for patients and families  
- Airway clearance techniques to reduce mucus buildup  
  - Chest physiotherapy and devices to aid mucus clearance  
  - Use of bronchodilators and mucolytics as needed  
- Nutritional support to address malabsorption issues  
  - High-calorie diet with enzyme replacement therapy  
  - Vitamin supplementation, especially fat-soluble vitamins  
- Management of respiratory infections  
  - Use of antibiotics for acute exacerbations  
  - Long-term prophylactic antibiotics for Pseudomonas aeruginosa  
- Consideration of lung transplantation for end-stage lung disease  
  - Referral to transplant centres for evaluated candidates  
  - Collaboration with transplant teams for comprehensive care







Deep vein thrombosis

Background  
- Deep vein thrombosis is a condition where a blood clot forms in a deep vein, usually in the legs  

Risk factors  
- Prolonged immobility  
- Recent surgery or trauma  
- Active cancer or cancer treatments  
- Obesity  
- Hormonal therapy or pregnancy  

Causes  
- Venous stasis due to immobility  
- Endothelial injury from trauma or surgery  
- Hypercoagulability from genetic or acquired conditions  
- Inflammatory states  
- Certain medications  

Pathophysiology  
- Clot formation occurs in response to the Virchow's triad  
- Leads to blockage of venous return  
- Possible embolisation to pulmonary circulation  

Signs and symptoms  
- Swelling in the affected leg  
- Pain or tenderness in the leg  
- Red or discoloured skin over the vein  
- Warmth in the affected area  
- Prominent superficial veins  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history  
- D-dimer testing to rule out thrombosis  
- Compression ultrasound of the affected limb  
- MRI or CT venography if needed  
- Venous Doppler examination  

Diagnosis  
- Use of the Wells Score to assess probability  
- D-dimer levels if suspected  
- Confirmation through imaging (ultrasound primarily)  
- Rule out alternative diagnoses  
- Ensure clinical criteria are met  

Management  
- Pharmacological anticoagulation therapy  
  - Initiate with low molecular weight heparin or fondaparinux  
  - Transition to oral anticoagulation after initial treatment  
- Thrombolysis for severe cases  
  - Consider in cases with massive DVT  
  - Assess risk of bleeding before proceeding  
- Compression stockings for symptom relief  
  - Use graduated compression stockings  
  - Advise usage for at least 2 years post-DVT  
- Education and prevention strategies  
  - Lifestyle modifications to reduce recurrence  
  - Discuss risks and benefits of prescribed anticoagulants  
- Routine follow-up and reassessment  
  - Monitor for complications  
  - Adjust management based on symptoms and risk factors







Dehydration

Background  
- Dehydration is a clinical condition resulting from excessive loss of body fluids.

Risk factors  
- Inadequate fluid intake  
- Increased fluid loss (e.g., vomiting, diarrhoea, sweating)  
- Age (especially children and elderly)  
- Chronic illnesses (e.g., diabetes)  
- Hot weather conditions  

Causes  
- Gastroenteritis  
- Excessive sweating during exercise  
- Fever  
- Increased urination (e.g., due to diabetes or diuretics)  
- Inadequate access to fluids  

Pathophysiology  
- Fluid loss leads to decreased plasma volume  
- Results in reduced renal perfusion and possible acute kidney injury  
- Activation of compensatory mechanisms (e.g., vasoconstriction, increased thirst)  
- Electrolyte imbalances may occur (e.g., hypernatremia, hypokalemia)  
- Severity depends on the degree and duration of fluid loss  

Signs and symptoms  
- Thirst  
- Dry mouth and mucous membranes  
- Decreased urine output or concentrated urine  
- Dizziness or light-headedness  
- Fatigue and weakness  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment (history and physical examination)  
- Serum electrolytes (sodium, potassium)  
- Blood urea and creatinine  
- Urine specific gravity  
- Complete blood count (CBC)  

Diagnosis  
- Consider clinical signs and symptoms  
- Assess fluid balance and risk factors  
- Lab tests to confirm dehydration severity  
- Ruling out other potential causes of symptoms  
- Classify as mild, moderate, or severe dehydration  

Management  
- Oral rehydration solutions for mild to moderate dehydration  
  - Encourage gradual intake of fluids  
  - Monitor fluid intake and symptoms  
- Intravenous fluids for severe dehydration  
  - Administer isotonic solutions (e.g., saline, lactated Ringer's)  
  - Monitor vital signs and renal function  
- Treat underlying cause (e.g., antiemetics for nausea)  
  - Manage diarrhoea with appropriate medications  
  - Adjust anticoagulant or diuretic medications if necessary  
- Educate on hydration strategies  
  - Advise on fluid intake during hot weather or illness  
  - Promote awareness of dehydration signs and symptoms  
- Consider ongoing monitoring  
  - Regularly assess hydration status  
  - Adjust fluid replacement therapy as required  







Delirium

background  
- Delirium is an acute, fluctuating disturbance in attention and cognition, often reversible.

risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Pre-existing cognitive impairment  
- Severe illness or comorbidity  
- Polypharmacy  
- Recent surgery or hospitalisation  

causes  
- Infections (e.g., urinary tract infections, pneumonia)  
- Metabolic disturbances (e.g., hyponatraemia, hypercalcaemia)  
- Substance withdrawal or intoxication  
- Medications (e.g., anticholinergics, opioids)  
- Neurological conditions (e.g., stroke, Parkinson's disease)  

pathophysiology  
- Neurotransmitter imbalance (including acetylcholine deficiency)  
- Inflammatory cytokine release  
- Disruption of neural circuits involved in attention and perception  
- Hyperactivity of the autonomic nervous system  
- Impaired blood-brain barrier function  

signs and symptoms  
- Fluctuating levels of consciousness  
- Reduced attention span  
- Disorientation to time and place  
- Cognitive changes (e.g., memory disturbances)  
- Hallucinations or delusions  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment (including history and examination)  
- Blood tests (e.g., electrolytes, renal function, infection markers)  
- Urinalysis  
- Imaging (e.g., CT or MRI if neurological cause suspected)  
- Review of medications  

diagnosis  
- Assess mental status using standardised tools (e.g., Confusion Assessment Method)  
- Rule out acute medical causes  
- Determine presence of core features (disturbance in attention and cognition)  
- Evaluate for fluctuating course of symptoms  
- Exclude other potential diagnoses (e.g., dementia, psychiatric disorders)  

management  
- Identify and treat underlying causes  
  - Adjust or discontinue causative medications  
  - Address metabolic or infectious issues  
- Supportive care and environment modification  
  - Maintain a calm and quiet setting  
  - Encourage orientation and familiarity with surroundings  
- Hydration and nutrition management  
  - Ensure adequate fluid intake  
  - Provide nutritional support if needed  
- Pharmacological management if necessary  
  - Use antipsychotics (e.g., haloperidol) for severe agitation  
  - Limit use to short-term as needed  
- Family involvement and education  
  - Inform family about delirium and its management  
  - Involve family in care and orientation efforts  







Dementias

Background  
Dementia is a syndrome characterized by progressive cognitive decline affecting daily functioning and behaviour

Risk factors  
Older age  
Family history of dementia  
Cardiovascular disease  
Low educational attainment  
Head injuries  

Causes  
Alzheimer's disease  
Vascular dementia  
Lewy body dementia  
Frontotemporal dementia  
Mixed dementia  

Pathophysiology  
Neurodegeneration leading to neuronal cell death  
Disruption of neurotransmitter systems  
Accumulation of pathological proteins (e.g., amyloid plaques, tau tangles)  
Vascular damage in cases of vascular dementia  
Chronic inflammation in the central nervous system  

Signs and symptoms  
Memory loss, especially recent memories  
Difficulty in problem-solving and planning  
Changes in mood and behaviour  
Disorientation to time and place  
Impairment in language and communication  

Investigations  
Detailed medical history and cognitive assessment  
Physical examination  
Diagnostic imaging (e.g., MRI, CT scans)  
Laboratory tests to exclude other conditions  
Neuropsychological testing  

Diagnosis  
Clinical assessment based on history and symptoms  
Cognitive testing using standardised tools  
Imaging studies to identify specific types of dementia  
Exclusion of reversible causes of cognitive impairment  
Collaboration with multidisciplinary teams  

Management  
Pharmacological treatment with cholinesterase inhibitors for Alzheimer's disease  
  May also consider memantine for moderate to severe cases  
Non-pharmacological interventions, including cognitive stimulation therapy  
  Environment modification to enhance daily living skills  
Support for caregivers and families through education and resources  
  Referral to dementia support services and local resources  
Advanced care planning and consideration of palliative care when appropriate  
  Regular review of the care plan to meet changing needs  







Depression

background  
- Depression is a common mental health disorder characterised by persistent sadness and loss of interest  

risk factors  
- Family history of depression  
- Previous episodes of depression  
- Chronic medical conditions  
- Substance misuse  
- Major life events or trauma  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances  
- Environmental stressors  
- Psychological factors such as cognitive distortions  
- Hormonal changes  

pathophysiology  
- Dysregulation of serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine pathways  
- Impaired neuroplasticity and neurogenesis  
- Alterations in brain structure and function  
- Inflammation and immune system involvement  
- Dysregulation of the HPA axis  

signs and symptoms  
- Persistent low mood  
- Anhedonia or loss of pleasure  
- Changes in appetite and weight  
- Sleep disturbances  
- Fatigue and decreased energy  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Standardised screening tools such as PHQ-9  
- Exclusion of physical causes (routine blood tests)  
- Mental state examination  
- Assessment of functional impairment  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on DSM-5 criteria or ICD-10  
- At least five symptoms present for two weeks  
- Symptoms cause significant distress or impairment  
- Not attributable to substance use or other medical condition  
- Rule out other mental health disorders  

management  
- First-line: Antidepressant medications  
  - SSRIs as preferred first-line agents  
  - Consider dosage adjustments based on response and side effects  
- Psychotherapy options, including CBT  
  - Tailor therapy type based on patient preference  
  - Engage in a number of sessions depending on the severity  
- Combination of medication and therapy for moderate to severe depression  
  - Monitor closely for effectiveness  
  - Adjust treatment as necessary  
- Consider referrals to secondary care for treatment-resistant cases  
  - Explore alternative treatments such as psychotherapy and antidepressants  
  - Evaluate for ECT in severe cases  
- Monitor and support patient’s safety and risk of self-harm  
  - Conduct regular follow-up assessments  
  - Engage family support when appropriate  







Developmental delay

background  
- Developmental delay refers to a significant lag in a child's physical, cognitive, emotional, or social development compared to peers

risk factors  
- Premature birth or low birth weight  
- Maternal drug or alcohol use during pregnancy  
- Genetic disorders  
- Environmental factors, such as poor socio-economic status  
- Lack of early intervention or stimulation

causes  
- Chromosomal abnormalities, e.g. Down syndrome  
- Neurodevelopmental disorders, e.g. autism spectrum disorders  
- Congenital infections, e.g. cytomegalovirus  
- Metabolic disorders, e.g. phenylketonuria  
- Traumatic brain injury or oxygen deprivation at birth

pathophysiology  
- Disruption in normal development pathways  
- Alterations in neuroanatomical structure and function  
- Impact of genetic and environmental interactions  
- Inflammation or injury affecting brain development  
- Impairment in neural connectivity and synaptic pruning

signs and symptoms  
- Delays in speech and language skills  
- Difficulty with motor skills or coordination  
- Challenges in social interactions or emotional regulation  
- Learning difficulties in educational settings  
- Variability in developmental milestones compared to peers

investigations  
- Comprehensive developmental assessment  
- Genetic testing if a hereditary condition is suspected  
- Neuroimaging (MRI or CT) to assess brain structure  
- Developmental screening tools, e.g. ASQ or Denver II  
- Blood tests to rule out metabolic or infectious causes

diagnosis  
- Identification of developmental delays in specific areas  
- Assessment of overall functioning via multidisciplinary evaluation  
- Consideration of family and medical history  
- Use of standardised assessment tools for consistency  
- Collaboration with paediatric specialists as required

management  
- Individualised intervention plans tailored to the child's needs  
  - Early intervention services, including speech and occupational therapy  
  - Educational support through special education needs provisions  
- Family support and counselling services  
  - Parent training programmes to enhance developmental support  
  - Resources for coping and advocacy in educational settings  
- Regular monitoring and re-assessment of developmental progress  
  - Follow-up appointments to adjust interventions as necessary  
  - Integration of services from healthcare, education, and social care







Diabetes in pregnancy (gestational and pre-existing)

background  
- Diabetes in pregnancy can complicate maternal and fetal health, necessitating careful management to reduce risks

risk factors  
- Obesity  
- Previous gestational diabetes  
- Family history of diabetes  
- Advanced maternal age  
- Ethnic background (higher prevalence in South Asian, Afro-Caribbean, and Middle Eastern populations)

causes  
- Insulin resistance increases during pregnancy  
- Hormonal changes affecting glucose metabolism  
- Genetic predisposition in pre-existing diabetes  
- Autoimmune factors in type 1 diabetes  
- Uncontrolled pre-existing diabetes leading to gestational diabetes

pathophysiology  
- Increased placental hormones lead to insulin resistance  
- Elevated maternal glucose levels cross the placenta  
- Fetal pancreas responds with increased insulin production  
- Risks of macrosomia and other complications associated with high maternal glucose  
- Potential for long-term metabolic effects on offspring

signs and symptoms  
- Polyuria  
- Polydipsia  
- Fatigue  
- Uncontrolled blood glucose levels  
- Symptoms of hyperglycaemia or hypoglycaemia

investigations  
- Blood glucose monitoring (self-monitoring and HbA1c)  
- Oral glucose tolerance test for gestational diabetes  
- Urinalysis for glucose and ketones  
- Ultrasound for fetal growth assessment  
- Screening for other pregnancy-related complications

diagnosis  
- Confirmed gestational diabetes by OGTT (2-hour plasma glucose of ≥ 7.8 mmol/L)  
- HbA1c ≥ 48 mmol/mol (6.5%) indicates pre-existing diabetes  
- Symptoms of diabetes with random plasma glucose ≥ 11.1 mmol/L  
- Persistent hyperglycaemia findings on subsequent tests  
- Possible referral to diabetes specialist services

management  
- Dietary modification with emphasis on balanced nutrition  
  - Education on carbohydrate counting  
  - Meal planning to stabilise blood glucose levels  
- Regular physical activity tailored to maternal fitness  
  - Encourage safe, moderate exercise  
  - Monitor exertion levels for safety  
- Blood glucose monitoring to ensure targets are met  
  - Self-monitoring protocols for patient empowerment  
  - Adjustments based on individual responses  
- Pharmacological therapy if blood glucose targets not met  
  - Insulin as first-line for gestational diabetes not controlled by diet  
  - Metformin may be considered for some women  
- Close monitoring throughout pregnancy  
  - Regular check-ups for glycaemic control  
  - Assess fetal growth and wellbeing via ultrasound







Diabetes insipidus

background  
- Diabetes insipidus is a condition characterised by excessive thirst and urination due to a deficiency of vasopressin 

risk factors  
- Head trauma or neurosurgery  
- Central nervous system disorders  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Chronic kidney disease  
- Certain medications (e.g. lithium)  

causes  
- Central diabetes insipidus due to insufficient vasopressin production  
- Nephrogenic diabetes insipidus from renal insensitivity to vasopressin  
- Dipsogenic diabetes insipidus due to excessive fluid intake  
- Gestational diabetes insipidus related to pregnancy  
- Congenital forms associated with genetic mutations  

pathophysiology  
- Impaired secretion or action of antidiuretic hormone (ADH)  
- Leads to increased urine output and decreased urine concentration  
- Electrolyte imbalances can occur due to excessive diuresis  
- Compensatory thirst mechanism activated  
- Risk of dehydration due to fluid loss  

signs and symptoms  
- Polyuria with dilute urine  
- Polydipsia, often with preference for cold drinks  
- Nocturia  
- Dehydration symptoms (dry mucous membranes, skin turgor)  
- Fatigue or weakness  

investigations  
- Urinalysis showing low specific gravity  
- Serum osmolality increased  
- Urine osmolality decreased  
- Water deprivation test to differentiate types  
- Vasopressin stimulation test if central diabetes insipidus suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination to assess symptoms  
- Urine and serum osmolality measured  
- Water deprivation test results analysed for differential diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other causes of polydipsia and polyuria  
- Diagnosis of nephrogenic diabetes insipidus confirmed with genetic testing if applicable  

management  
- Central diabetes insipidus: desmopressin prescribed  
   - Monitor for response and adjust dosage  
   - Educate about potential side effects  
- Nephrogenic diabetes insipidus: dietary measures, thiazide diuretics  
   - Maintain adequate hydration  
   - Monitor electrolyte balance  
- Psychological support for dipsogenic diabetes insipidus  
   - Engage with mental health services if needed  
   - Provide counselling on fluid intake regulation  
- Regular follow-up appointments for monitoring  
   - Assess treatment effectiveness  
   - Screening for complications  
- Patient education on recognition of dehydration and management  
   - Discuss when to seek medical attention  
   - Encourage hydration strategies  







Diabetes mellitus

Background  
- A chronic metabolic disorder characterised by hyperglycaemia due to insulin deficiency or resistance

Risk factors  
- Family history of diabetes  
- Obesity or overweight  
- Sedentary lifestyle   
- Unhealthy diet with high sugar intake  
- Age over 45 years

Causes  
- Type 1: autoimmune destruction of pancreatic beta cells  
- Type 2: insulin resistance and progressive beta-cell dysfunction  
- Gestational: hormonal changes during pregnancy affecting insulin function  
- Genetic predisposition associated with certain HLA genotypes  
- Environmental triggers such as viral infections

Pathophysiology  
- Impaired insulin secretion in Type 1 and progressive loss in Type 2  
- Increased hepatic glucose production  
- Altered lipid metabolism leading to dyslipidaemia  
- Increased glucose reabsorption in kidneys  
- Development of microvascular and macrovascular complications over time

Signs and symptoms  
- Polyuria  
- Polydipsia  
- Polyphagia  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Blurred vision

Investigations  
- Fasting plasma glucose test  
- Oral glucose tolerance test  
- HbA1c level  
- Random plasma glucose  
- Urinalysis for ketones

Diagnosis  
- HbA1c ≥ 48 mmol/mol (6.5%)  
- Fasting plasma glucose ≥ 7.0 mmol/L  
- 2-hour plasma glucose ≥ 11.1 mmol/L during OGTT  
- Symptoms of hyperglycaemia with random plasma glucose ≥ 11.1 mmol/L  
- Confirm diagnosis with a repeat test if asymptomatic  

Management  
- Lifestyle modification with dietary changes and increased physical activity  
  - Aim for weight loss if overweight  
  - Promote a balanced diet low in sugar and saturated fats  
- Pharmacotherapy as appropriate  
  - Metformin as first-line for Type 2 diabetes  
  - Insulin therapy for Type 1 diabetes or advanced Type 2 diabetes  
- Regular monitoring of blood glucose levels  
  - Self-monitoring for Type 1 patients  
  - Routine check-ups for Type 2 patients  
- Education on diabetes self-management and recognition of complications  
  - Diabetes education programmes  
  - Support from healthcare professionals  
- Screening for complications  
  - Annual eye exams for retinopathy  
  - Regular foot assessments to prevent ulcers  







Diabetic eye disease

Background  
- Diabetic eye disease encompasses a range of complications arising from diabetes that affect vision

Risk factors  
- Prolonged duration of diabetes  
- Poor glycaemic control  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Pregnancy in women with diabetes  

Causes  
- Diabetic retinopathy due to vascular changes  
- Diabetic macular oedema from fluid accumulation  
- Cataracts from lens opacification  
- Glaucoma due to increased intraocular pressure  
- Vitreous haemorrhage from retinal damage  

Pathophysiology  
- Hyperglycaemia leads to microvascular damage  
- Increased vascular permeability causes exudates and oedema  
- Ischaemia results in neovascularisation  
- Accumulation of sorbitol and fructose via the polyol pathway  
- Inflammatory mediators contribute to retinal damage  

Signs and symptoms  
- Blurred or fluctuating vision  
- Dark spots or floaters in vision  
- Impaired colour vision  
- Eye pain or pressure in cases of glaucoma  
- Sudden vision loss in advanced disease  

Investigations  
- Fundoscopy for retinal examination  
- Optical coherence tomography for macular assessment  
- Fluorescein angiography to assess retinal circulation  
- Visual acuity testing to evaluate vision  
- Blood glucose and HbA1c monitoring  

Diagnosis  
- Presence of diabetic retinopathy on examination  
- Visual impairment correlating with retinal changes  
- Diagnosis of diabetes mellitus as a precondition  
- Classification of retinopathy severity based on imaging  
- Exclusion of other ocular conditions  

Management  
- Optimise blood glucose control  
  - Lifestyle modifications  
  - Antidiabetic medications  
- Manage hypertension and dyslipidaemia  
  - Antihypertensive medications  
  - Statins as needed  
- Regular eye examinations  
  - Annual screening for those with diabetes  
  - Referral to ophthalmology for significant findings  
- Laser therapy for diabetic retinopathy  
  - Panretinal photocoagulation for proliferative retinopathy  
  - Focal laser for macular oedema  
- Intravitreal injections of anti-VEGF agents  
  - For diabetic macular oedema  
  - Repeated treatments may be necessary







Diabetic ketoacidosis

background  
- Diabetic ketoacidosis is a life-threatening complication of diabetes characterised by hyperglycaemia, ketosis, and metabolic acidosis

risk factors  
- Type 1 diabetes mellitus  
- Inadequate insulin administration  
- Infection or illness  
- Stress or trauma  
- Substance misuse (e.g., alcohol or drugs)

causes  
- Insulin deficiency  
- Increased counter-regulatory hormones (glucagon, cortisol)  
- Glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis  
- Lipolysis leading to ketone body production  
- Infection, pancreatitis, myocardial infarction, or trauma

pathophysiology  
- Insulin deficiency leads to increased blood glucose levels  
- Excess fat breakdown results in ketone formation  
- Accumulation of ketoacids causes metabolic acidosis  
- Electrolyte imbalances due to osmotic diuresis  
- Hypovolaemia and dehydration from polyuria 

signs and symptoms  
- Polyuria and polydipsia  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Abdominal pain  
- Kussmaul respirations (rapid, deep breathing)  
- Altered consciousness or coma

investigations  
- Blood glucose levels (often >11mmol/L)  
- Arterial blood gas analysis (acidic pH, low bicarbonate)  
- Urinalysis for ketones and glucose  
- Serum electrolytes (especially sodium and potassium)  
- Full blood count and blood cultures to identify infection

diagnosis  
- Hyperglycaemia (blood glucose >11mmol/L)  
- Metabolic acidosis (pH <7.3, bicarbonate <15mmol/L)  
- Presence of ketones in blood or urine  
- Exclusion of other causes of metabolic acidosis  
- Clinical presentation consistent with DKA  

management  
- Administer intravenous fluid replacement  
  - Use isotonic crystalloids (e.g., 0.9% sodium chloride)  
  - Monitor fluid status and adjust accordingly  
- Initiate insulin therapy  
  - Continuous intravenous insulin infusion  
  - Aim for gradual decrease in blood glucose (around 5 mmol/L/hour)  
- Correct electrolyte imbalances  
  - Monitor and replace potassium to maintain levels  
  - Address sodium levels as required  
- Identify and treat precipitating factors  
  - Investigate and manage infections promptly  
  - Address any other underlying issues  
- Monitor clinical status and adjust treatment  
  - Regularly assess vital signs and blood gases  
  - Re-evaluate treatment effectiveness and patient response  







Diabetic nephropathy

background  
- A common complication of diabetes leading to progressive kidney dysfunction 

risk factors  
- Poor glycaemic control  
- Hypertension  
- Duration of diabetes  
- Family history of kidney disease  
- Obesity 

causes  
- Chronic hyperglycaemia  
- Increased glomerular filtration pressure  
- Proteinuria resulting from glomerulosclerosis  
- Inflammatory mediators and cytokines  
- Activation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system 

pathophysiology  
- Initial hyperglycaemia leads to glycation of proteins and formation of advanced glycation end-products  
- Increased renal blood flow and hyperfiltration affect glomeruli  
- Progressive damage leads to glomerulosclerosis and tubular atrophy  
- Proteinuria results from increased permeability of glomerular capillaries  
- Progressive decline in renal function leading to end-stage renal disease 

signs and symptoms  
- Microalbuminuria to macroalbuminuria  
- Hypertension  
- Swelling in the legs and ankles  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Nausea and decreased appetite  

investigations  
- Urine dipstick testing for protein  
- 24-hour urine collection for albumin excretion  
- Serum creatinine and estimated glomerular filtration rate (eGFR)  
- Blood glucose levels and HbA1c  
- Imaging studies as needed (e.g. ultrasound) 

diagnosis  
- Presence of proteinuria or albuminuria  
- Decreased eGFR  
- Evidence of diabetic retinopathy  
- Symptoms of kidney impairment  
- Exclusion of other causes of renal impairment 

management  
- Optimise glycaemic control  
  - Use of metformin and/or insulin  
  - Continuous monitoring of HbA1c  
- Control hypertension  
  - Use of ACE inhibitors or ARBs  
  - Lifestyle modifications including diet and exercise  
- Monitor renal function regularly  
  - Periodic eGFR and urine tests  
  - Adjust medications based on renal function  
- Educate patients on dietary modifications  
  - Low-protein diet if indicated  
  - Adequate hydration and avoidance of nephrotoxins  
- Consider referral to nephrology  
  - For advanced cases or rapid progression  
  - Management of potential dialysis or transplantation options  







Diabetic neuropathy

Background  
- Diabetic neuropathy is a common complication of diabetes affecting peripheral and autonomic nerves

Risk factors  
- Poor glycaemic control  
- Duration of diabetes  
- Presence of diabetic nephropathy  
- Hypertension  
- Smoking

Causes  
- Chronic hyperglycaemia leading to nerve damage  
- Accumulation of advanced glycation end products  
- Ischaemia due to microvascular complications  
- Oxidative stress and inflammation  
- Genetic predisposition

Pathophysiology  
- Axonal degeneration and demyelination of peripheral nerves  
- Disruption of neuronal energy metabolism  
- Impaired neuronal repair mechanisms  
- Inflammatory responses contributing to nerve damage  
- Altered blood flow affecting nerve function  

Signs and symptoms  
- Numbness, tingling or burning sensations in extremities  
- Loss of sensation and reflexes  
- Pain, often described as shooting or stabbing  
- Muscle weakness and atrophy  
- Autonomic symptoms such as orthostatic hypotension

Investigations  
- Clinical examination including sensory and motor tests  
- Monofilament testing for neuropathy assessment  
- Nerve conduction studies to evaluate nerve function  
- Blood glucose monitoring and HbA1c levels  
- Imaging studies in severe cases to rule out other conditions

Diagnosis  
- Clinical criteria based on symptoms and physical examination  
- Confirmation through nerve conduction studies, if necessary  
- Exclusion of other causes of neuropathy  
- Assessment of diabetes control  
- Evaluation for other complications of diabetes  

Management  
- Optimisation of glycaemic control  
   - Individualised dietary modifications  
   - Considerant insulin therapy or oral hypoglycaemics  
- Pharmacological treatment for neuropathic pain  
   - Tricyclic antidepressants or duloxetine  
   - Gabapentin or pregabalin  
- Regular foot care and education  
   - Monitoring for foot ulcers and deformities  
   - Referral to podiatry if necessary  
- Management of cardiovascular risk factors  
   - Control of blood pressure and lipids  
   - Smoking cessation support  
- Psychological support as needed  
   - Counselling or cognitive behavioural therapy  
   - Support groups for chronic pain management  







Disease prevention/screening

background  
- Disease prevention and screening aim to reduce disease incidence and enhance early detection

risk factors  
- Age, with older adults at higher risk for many conditions  
- Family history of disease increases susceptibility  
- Lifestyle factors such as smoking, poor diet, and physical inactivity  
- Environmental exposures including pollutants and toxins  
- Socioeconomic status affecting access to healthcare and education

causes  
- Genetic predisposition leading to hereditary diseases  
- Lifestyle choices contributing to non-communicable diseases  
- Infectious agents causing communicable diseases  
- Environmental risk factors such as poor living conditions  
- Lack of immunisation leading to vaccine-preventable diseases

pathophysiology  
- Disease mechanisms vary widely, often involving inflammation, genetic mutations, or pathogen interactions  
- Chronic diseases may involve metabolic dysregulation or immune system dysfunction  
- Infectious diseases can result from pathogen entry, replication, and immune response  
- Multifactorial aetiology for many conditions entails interplay of genetics and environment  
- Early detection improves prognosis by preventing disease progression

signs and symptoms  
- Varies widely depending on specific disease or condition  
- Often asymptomatic in early stages, emphasising the need for screening  
- Clinical signs can include changes in physiological parameters  
- Symptoms may arise due to advanced disease or complications  
- Screening can identify signs before symptoms develop

investigations  
- Blood tests to assess risk factors like cholesterol and glucose levels  
- Imaging techniques such as X-rays or MRIs for early disease detection  
- Biopsy or cytology for tissue diagnosis in suspected malignancies  
- Screening questionnaires for risk factor assessment and symptom identification  
- Genetic testing for hereditary conditions in at-risk individuals

diagnosis  
- Holistic assessment including medical history and physical examination  
- Risk factor identification through screening tools and questionnaires  
- Lab tests confirming specific disease markers or indicators  
- Radiological findings correlating with clinical signs and symptoms  
- Multidisciplinary discussion when dealing with complex cases

management  
- Lifestyle modification support including diet and exercise counselling  
  - Referral to dietitians or physiotherapists as needed  
  - Behavioural therapy or support groups for smoking cessation  
- Preventative measures such as immunisation and prophylactic treatments  
  - Schedule vaccinations according to national protocols  
  - Educate patients on the importance of regular screenings  
- Pharmacological interventions for high-risk patients  
  - Statins for hyperlipidaemia  
  - Antihypertensives for uncontrolled blood pressure  
- Education and empowerment of patients regarding their health  
  - Provide access to resources for self-management  
  - Encourage participation in preventive health initiatives  
- Regular follow-up and reassessment of risk factors  
  - Schedule periodic screenings as recommended by guidelines  
  - Adjust management plans based on individual progress and needs







Disseminated intravascular coagulation

background  
- Disseminated intravascular coagulation is a serious disorder leading to abnormal blood clotting throughout the body's small blood vessels  

risk factors  
- Sepsis and severe infections  
- Trauma or surgery  
- Obstetric complications  
- Malignancies  
- Severe liver disease  

causes  
- Sepsis from bacterial infections  
- Moderate to severe tissue damage  
- Release of thromboplastic substances  
- Obstetric events like placental abruption  
- Malignancies leading to hypercoagulable state  

pathophysiology  
- Overactivation of coagulation cascade  
- Formation of microthrombi leading to ischaemia  
- Consumption of clotting factors and platelets  
- Activation of fibrinolysis  
- Results in bleeding due to depletion of coagulation factors  

signs and symptoms  
- Bleeding tendencies such as bruising and petechiae  
- Organ dysfunction due to ischaemia  
- Thrombosis and associated complications  
- Symptoms of underlying condition contributing to DIC  
- Laboratory findings of thrombocytopenia and prolonged PT/aPTT  

investigations  
- Complete blood count for platelet levels  
- Coagulation profile (PT, aPTT, fibrinogen levels)  
- Peripheral blood smear for schistocytes  
- D-dimer levels  
- Assess underlying cause with appropriate imaging or cultures  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of bleeding and thrombosis  
- Laboratory findings consistent with coagulation abnormalities  
- Evidence of an initiating event such as sepsis or trauma  
- Rule out other causes of coagulopathy  
- Confirmatory tests as indicated based on clinical scenario  

management  
- Address underlying cause urgently  
  - Treat infection or remove the source of sepsis  
  - Stabilise any underlying medical or surgical condition  
- Supportive care for bleeding  
  - Transfusion of platelets if needed  
  - Administer coagulation factor replacement based on findings  
- Consider anticoagulation in certain scenarios  
  - Use with caution if systemic bleeding risk is low  
  - Monitor closely for any signs of bleeding  
- Use of antifibrinolytic agents  
  - Administer in cases of excessive bleeding  
  - Monitor for potential thromboembolic events  
- Multidisciplinary approach for severe cases  
  - Involvement of haematology and critical care teams  
  - Regular assessment of coagulation status and organ function  







Diverticular disease

background  
- Diverticular disease encompasses diverticulosis and diverticulitis, affecting the colon and its diverticula  

risk factors  
- Age over 40  
- Low-fibre diet  
- Sedentary lifestyle  
- Obesity  
- Smoking  

causes  
- Increased intra-colon pressure leading to diverticulum formation  
- Dietary factors, particularly low fibre intake  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Colonic motility disturbances  
- Alterations in gut microbiota  

pathophysiology  
- Formation of diverticula due to pressure and herniation of the colonic mucosa  
- Inflammation of diverticula resulting in diverticulitis  
- Complications include abscess, perforation, and fistula  

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain, often in the left lower quadrant  
- Altered bowel habits, such as constipation or diarrhoea  
- Nausea and vomiting in severe cases  
- Fever and chills  
- Rectal bleeding  

investigations  
- CT scan of the abdomen for suspected diverticulitis  
- Colonoscopy for diverticulosis and assessment of complications  
- Blood tests including full blood count to check for infection  
- Ultrasound may be used in some cases  
- Stool tests to rule out other conditions  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on symptoms and history  
- Confirmation through imaging studies like CT scan  
- Exclusion of other gastrointestinal conditions  
- Endoscopy for further evaluation if needed  
- Consideration of recurrent episodes for chronic cases  

management  
- Dietary modifications to increase fibre intake  
  - Recommend high-fibre foods and hydration  
  - Consider fibre supplements if needed  
- Antibiotics for diverticulitis if moderate to severe  
  - Ciprofloxacin with metronidazole as first-line treatment  
  - Adjust based on local resistance patterns  
- Surgical intervention for complications  
  - Consider resection for recurrent diverticulitis  
  - Assess for abscess drainage if present  
- Pain management with analgesics  
  - Use paracetamol or NSAIDs judiciously  
  - Avoid opioid use if possible due to constipation risks  
- Patient education on lifestyle modifications  
  - Promote regular exercise and healthy weight maintenance  
  - Discourage smoking and encourage cessation efforts  







Down’s syndrome

background  
- Down's syndrome is a genetic condition caused by the presence of an extra chromosome 21  

risk factors  
- Advanced maternal age  
- Family history of chromosomal abnormalities  
- Previous child with Down's syndrome  
- Certain genetic conditions in parents  
- Environmental factors during conception  

causes  
- Trisomy 21 (nondisjunction during meiosis)  
- Translocation Down syndrome  
- Mosaic Down syndrome  
- Failure of chromosome separation  
- Genetic anomalies leading to an extra chromosome  

pathophysiology  
- Extra chromosome 21 causes overexpression of genes  
- Disruption in normal development and function of various tissues  
- Associated with numerous organ system abnormalities  
- Impaired cognitive function due to altered neurodevelopment  
- Increased risk of congenital anomalies  

signs and symptoms  
- Distinctive facial features such as flat facial profile  
- Hypotonia (reduced muscle tone)  
- Short stature and limbs  
- Increased flexibility of joints  
- Learning difficulties and developmental delays  

investigations  
- Prenatal screening (e.g. nuchal translucency scan)  
- Non-invasive prenatal testing (NIPT)  
- Karyotype analysis of fetal cells  
- Postnatal chromosome analysis  
- Assessment of congenital anomalies through imaging  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis confirmed by karyotyping  
- Clinical assessment of characteristic features  
- Prenatal diagnosis using amniocentesis or CVS  
- Family history evaluation  
- Exclusion of other genetic syndromes  

management  
- Regular health check-ups and screenings  
  - Monitor for congenital heart defects and other health issues  
  - Routine assessments for growth and development  
- Early intervention services  
  - Speech therapy and occupational therapy  
  - Special education resources and support  
- Cardiovascular care if congenital heart defects present  
  - Surgical intervention as needed  
  - Ongoing cardiac monitoring  
- Support for families  
  - Genetic counselling  
  - Access to Down syndrome support organisations  
- Promotion of independence and self-care skills  
  - Life skills training  
  - Vocational training opportunities  







Drug overdose

background  
- Drug overdose is a critical condition resulting from the excessive intake of substances, leading to life-threatening effects.

risk factors  
- History of substance abuse  
- Co-existing mental health disorders  
- Polypharmacy  
- Lack of social support  
- High-risk environments

causes  
- Intentional self-harm  
- Accidental overdose  
- Drug interactions  
- High potency of substances  
- Withdrawal from substances

pathophysiology  
- Overactivation of neurotransmitter systems  
- Cellular injury and necrosis  
- Acidosis or alkalosis due to altered metabolism  
- Organ dysfunction, particularly liver and kidneys  
- Electrolyte imbalances

signs and symptoms  
- Altered mental status  
- Respiratory depression or hyperventilation  
- Abnormal heart rate and blood pressure  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Seizures or loss of consciousness

investigations  
- Toxicology screening  
- Blood tests (liver function, electrolytes)  
- ECG to assess cardiac effects  
- Chest X-ray for aspiration or other complications  
- Urine analysis

diagnosis
