- Clinical assessment of symptoms  
- Establishing a timeline of substance use  
- Consideration of potential co-ingestants  
- Assessment of vital signs and level of consciousness  
- Identification of any relevant medical history

management  
- Airway protection and respiratory support  
  - Intubation if necessary  
  - Use of supplemental oxygen if hypoxic  
- Administration of antidotes if available  
  - Naloxone for opioid overdose  
  - Activated charcoal for certain substances  
- Supportive care in a monitored environment  
  - IV fluids to maintain hydration  
  - Correction of electrolyte imbalances  
- Observation for potential complications  
  - Continuous monitoring of vitals  
  - Repeat toxicology screens as needed  
- Referral to mental health services if indicated  
  - Assessment for substance use disorders  
  - Access to counselling or rehabilitation programmes  







Eating disorders

background  
- Eating disorders are severe mental health conditions characterised by disturbed eating behaviours and related distress  

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Adolescence or young adulthood  
- History of trauma or abuse  
- Family history of eating disorders or mental illness  
- Socio-cultural pressures and ideal body image  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Psychological factors such as low self-esteem or anxiety  
- Environmental influences including family dynamics  
- Sociocultural factors like media portrayal of thinness  
- Neurobiological factors affecting appetite and mood regulation  

pathophysiology  
- Dysregulation of hunger and satiety hormones  
- Altered brain reward pathways related to food  
- Emotional regulation impairment leading to unhealthy coping mechanisms  
- Changes in metabolism and energy balance  
- Nutritional deficiencies impacting physical and mental health  

signs and symptoms  
- Significant weight loss or fluctuations  
- Preoccupation with food, dieting, and body image  
- Restrictive eating or binge-eating behaviours  
- Changes in physical appearance such as hair loss or skin issues  
- Social withdrawal and mood disturbances  

investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Blood tests to assess electrolytes and nutritional status  
- Psychological assessments to evaluate eating behaviours  
- BMI calculation and dietary history analysis  
- Assessment for co-morbid psychiatric conditions  

diagnosis  
- Use DSM-5 criteria for specific eating disorder diagnoses  
- Assessment of severity based on physical and psychological health  
- Rule out other medical conditions causing similar symptoms  
- Consider duration and impact of symptoms on daily functioning  
- Involve multidisciplinary team for comprehensive evaluation  

management  
- Cognitive behavioural therapy tailored for eating disorders  
  - Focus on changing unhealthy eating patterns  
  - Address underlying psychological issues  
- Nutritional rehabilitation and education  
  - Develop a structured meal plan  
  - Monitor and support gradual weight restoration  
- Family-based therapy for adolescents  
  - Involve family in the treatment process  
  - Strengthen familial support systems  
- Pharmacotherapy for co-morbid conditions  
  - Consider antidepressants for comorbid depression or anxiety  
  - Regularly review medication efficacy and side effects  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring of progress  
  - Adjust treatment based on response and needs  
  - Support ongoing recovery and relapse prevention  







Ectopic pregnancy

background  
- Ectopic pregnancy occurs when a fertilised egg implants outside the uterus, commonly in a fallopian tube.

risk factors  
- Previous ectopic pregnancy  
- Pelvic inflammatory disease  
- Endometriosis  
- Surgical sterilisation  
- Use of assisted reproductive technology

causes  
- Abnormalities in the fallopian tubes  
- Hormonal factors  
- Infections causing scarring  
- Prior pelvic surgery  
- Genetic factors

pathophysiology  
- Implantation of the embryo in non-uterine tissue  
- Inadequate blood supply to the implanted tissue  
- Expansion leading to tubal rupture  
- Potential for significant internal bleeding  
- Hormonal changes impacting the pregnancy process

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain, often unilateral  
- Vaginal bleeding  
- Shoulder tip pain due to diaphragmatic irritation  
- Signs of shock in severe cases  
- Positive pregnancy test

investigations  
- Serum β-hCG levels  
- Transvaginal ultrasound  
- Pelvic examination  
- Complete blood count  
- Urinalysis

diagnosis  
- Confirming an empty uterus on ultrasound with elevated β-hCG  
- Detecting an adnexal mass on ultrasound  
- Ruling out normal intrauterine pregnancies  
- Assessing for signs of ruptured ectopic pregnancy  
- Clinical history and physical examination findings

management  
- Medical management with methotrexate for unruptured ectopic pregnancy  
   - Monitor β-hCG levels  
   - Provide counselling on potential side effects  
- Surgical management if rupture or significant complications are present  
   - Laparoscopy with salpingectomy or salpingostomy  
   - Blood transfusion if necessary in cases of significant bleeding







Encephalitis

Background  
- Encephalitis is an inflammation of the brain, often caused by infection or autoimmune processes  

Risk factors  
- Younger age  
- Immunocompromised status  
- Previous viral infections  
- Travel history to endemic areas  
- Exposure to vectors like mosquitoes  

Causes  
- Viral infections such as *Herpes simplex virus* and *Varicella-zoster virus*  
- Bacterial infections like *Listeria monocytogenes*  
- Autoimmune encephalitis  
- Post-infectious immune-mediated reactions  
- Metabolic disturbances  

Pathophysiology  
- Direct viral invasion of brain tissue  
- Immune-mediated damage to neurones and glial cells  
- Cytokine release leading to inflammation  
- Disruption of the blood-brain barrier  
- Neuronal apoptosis and necrosis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fever  
- Headache  
- Seizures  
- Altered mental status or confusion  
- Focal neurological deficits  

Investigations  
- MRI or CT brain imaging  
- Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis  
- Polymerase chain reaction testing for viral DNA/RNA  
- Serological tests for specific infections  
- Electroencephalogram (EEG) to assess for abnormal brain activity  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms  
- CSF analysis showing pleocytosis and elevated protein  
- Positive PCR or serological tests for pathogens  
- Neuroimaging showing inflammation or lesions  
- Exclusion of other causes of acute neurological dysfunction  

Management  
- Admit to hospital for monitoring and supportive care  
  - IV fluids and electrolyte management  
  - Regular neurological assessments  
- Start antiviral treatment if viral aetiology suspected  
  - Acyclovir for suspected *Herpes simplex*  
  - Consider adjunctive therapies if autoimmune encephalitis is indicated  
- Treat seizures with appropriate antiepileptic medication  
  - Initiate as needed based on clinical judgement  
  - Continuously monitor for seizure activity  
- Consider corticosteroids for autoimmune encephalitis  
  - Taper dosing based on clinical response  
  - Monitor for adverse effects  
- Provide rehabilitation and supportive therapies  
  - Occupational and physiotherapy as required  
  - Psychological support for cognitive and emotional recovery







Endometrial cancer

Background  
- Endometrial cancer is the most common gynaecological malignancy in the UK.

Risk factors  
- Obesity or high body mass index  
- Age over 50 years  
- History of hormonal therapy or oestrogen exposure  
- Family history of endometrial or colorectal cancer  
- Diabetes mellitus  

Causes  
- Unopposed oestrogen exposure  
- Genetic mutations (e.g. Lynch syndrome)  
- Chronic anovulation  
- Endometrial hyperplasia  
- Previous pelvic radiation  

Pathophysiology  
- Hyperplasia of the endometrial lining due to excess oestrogen  
- Transformation to atypical cells leading to malignancy  
- Invasion of myometrium and potential spread to lymph nodes and distant sites

Signs and symptoms  
- Abnormal vaginal bleeding (postmenopausal or intermenstrual)  
- Pelvic pain or pressure  
- Abdominal distension or bloating  
- Unexplained weight loss  
- Fatigue  

Investigations  
- Transvaginal ultrasound to assess endometrial thickness  
- Endometrial biopsy for histopathological examination  
- Hysteroscopy for direct visualisation if required  
- MRI or CT scan for staging  
- Blood tests including full blood count and tumour markers  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmatory histological diagnosis from biopsy  
- Staging based on FIGO criteria  
- Assessing for metastasis via imaging studies  
- Rule out differential diagnoses of abnormal bleeding  
- Evaluate for associated genetic syndromes if indicated  

Management  
- Surgical intervention (hysterectomy with bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy)  
  - Consider lymphadenectomy if high risk  
  - Assess need for sentinel node biopsy  
- Adjuvant therapy (radiotherapy and/or chemotherapy)  
  - Use radiotherapy for high-risk patients post-surgery  
  - Consider hormonal therapies for specific subtypes  
- Follow-up with regular surveillance for recurrence  
  - Monitor symptoms and imaging based on risk assessment  
  - Educate patients on signs of recurrence  
- Consider referral to genetic counselling if hereditary risk factors are present  
  - Discuss implications for family members  
  - Assess eligibility for surveillance or preventive measures  
- Palliative care for advanced disease  
  - Symptom control and psychosocial support  
  - Involve multidisciplinary teams for holistic management  







Endometriosis

Background  
- Endometriosis is a chronic inflammatory condition where endometrial-like tissue grows outside the uterus  

Risk factors  
- Family history of endometriosis  
- Early menarche or prolonged menstrual periods  
- Nulliparity or infertility  
- High body mass index  
- Immunological disorders  

Causes  
- Retrograde menstruation  
- Embryonic cell transformation  
- Surgical scarring or complications  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental toxins  

Pathophysiology  
- Ectopic endometrial tissue responds to hormonal fluctuations  
- Inflammation and fibrogenesis occur around ectopic lesions  
- Formation of adhesions and scar tissue  
- Ovarian cysts (endometriomas) can develop  
- Chronic pelvic pain results from tissue irritation  

Signs and symptoms  
- Dysmenorrhoea (painful periods)  
- Dyspareunia (pain during intercourse)  
- Chronic pelvic pain  
- Infertility or difficulty conceiving  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms (e.g. dyschezia, bloating)  

Investigations  
- Pelvic ultrasound to assess ovarian cysts  
- Transvaginal ultrasound for better imaging details  
- MRI for detailed pelvic anatomy  
- Laparoscopy for direct visualisation and biopsy  
- CA-125 blood test (though not diagnostic)  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and symptoms assessment  
- Transvaginal ultrasound findings  
- Confirmation via laparoscopy with biopsy  
- Exclusion of other pelvic pathologies  
- NICE criteria for laparoscopic confirmation  

Management  
- Pain management with NSAIDs or analgesics  
  - Review dosage and frequency  
  - Consider alternative analgesics if necessary  
- Hormonal therapies (e.g. contraceptive pills)  
  - Discuss potential side effects  
  - Monitor response to treatment  
- Surgical intervention for severe cases  
  - Conservative surgery to remove lesions  
  - Hysterectomy as a last resort  
- Lifestyle modifications (diet, exercise)  
  - Encourage regular physical activity  
  - Suggest dietary changes to reduce symptoms  
- Psychological support and counselling  
  - Referral to psychological services if needed  
  - Explore support groups for ongoing support  







Epididymitis and orchitis

background  
- Epididymitis and orchitis refer to inflamed conditions of the epididymis and testis, often presenting together  

risk factors  
- Younger age, particularly between 14 and 35 years  
- Recent urogenital procedures or trauma  
- Sexually transmitted infections  
- Urinary tract infections  
- Immunosuppression  

causes  
- Bacterial infections, particularly from *Chlamydia trachomatis* and *Neisseria gonorrhoeae*  
- Viral infections, such as mumps or Coxsackie virus  
- Non-infectious causes like trauma or drug reactions  
- Urinary reflux  
- Testicular torsion during the acute phase  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the epididymis or testis leads to swelling and pain  
- Increased vascular permeability and infiltration of immune cells  
- Possible obstruction of the spermatic duct due to swelling  
- Vascular congestion can lead to ischemia if untreated  
- Chronic inflammation may result in fibrosis and fertility issues  

signs and symptoms  
- Unilateral scrotal pain and swelling  
- Tenderness in the affected area  
- Possible fever and chills  
- Dysuria or urethral discharge  
- Redness and warmth over the affected testicle  

investigations  
- Clinical examination including scrotal ultrasound  
- Urinalysis for signs of infection  
- Urethral swab for STI screening  
- Serum inflammatory markers, such as CRP  
- Consider tests for viral infections if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and examination findings  
- Positive urine cultures or swabs indicating bacterial infection  
- Ultrasound showing inflammation or abscess formation  
- Exclusion of testicular torsion and other differential diagnoses  
- Serological tests confirming viral causes  

management  
- Antibiotics for bacterial infections  
  - Empirical treatment targeting common causative organisms  
  - Tailored therapy following culture results  
- Analgesics for pain relief  
  - NSAIDs for pain management  
  - Ice packs may help reduce swelling  
- Supportive care with scrotal elevation  
  - Encouragement of rest and avoiding strenuous activities  
  - Use of scrotal support garments  
- Management of underlying causes  
  - Treat STIs or urinary tract infections as necessary  
  - Address any anatomical abnormalities if present  
- Referral to specialist if complications arise  
  - Surgical intervention for abscess drainage  
  - Further assessment for chronic cases  









Epiglottitis

background  
- Epiglottitis is a potentially life-threatening inflammation of the epiglottis, often caused by infection  

risk factors  
- Age (commonly seen in children, particularly those unvaccinated)  
- Incomplete vaccination against *Haemophilus influenzae* type b (Hib)  
- Immunocompromised state  
- Presence of foreign body in the airway  
- History of upper respiratory tract infections  

causes  
- *Haemophilus influenzae* type b  
- Streptococcus pneumoniae  
- Staphylococcus aureus  
- Viruses (e.g., adenovirus, herpes simplex virus)  
- Mycobacterium tuberculosis  

pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to inflammation and swelling of the epiglottis  
- Inflammation can obstruct the airway  
- Possible formation of an abscess  
- Increased vascular permeability leading to oedema  
- Cellular injury and necrosis of epiglottic tissue  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset fever  
- Severe sore throat  
- Difficulty swallowing (dysphagia)  
- Stridor (noisy breathing)  
- Drooling and sitting in a tripod position  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history  
- Throat examination (may show swelling and redness)  
- Lateral neck X-ray (to assess airway and epiglottis size)  
- Blood tests (to identify infection markers)  
- Direct laryngoscopy (definitive diagnosis if safe)  

diagnosis  
- Clinical suspicion based on symptoms and signs  
- Radiological confirmation via lateral neck X-ray  
- Throat culture if applicable  
- Consideration of age and vaccination history  
- Ruling out other causes of airway obstruction  

management  
- Airway management (intubation if severe)  
  - Assess need for emergency intervention  
  - Ensure close monitoring for respiratory distress  
- Intravenous antibiotics  
  - Ceftriaxone or cefotaxime as first-line  
  - Consideration for airway support if unable to maintain patent airway  
- Corticosteroids (to reduce inflammation)  
  - Dexamethasone recommended in severe cases  
  - Monitor for side effects through treatment  
- Supportive care (hydration and fever management)  
  - Encourage oral intake if possible  
  - Administer antipyretics for fever relief  
- Hospital admission (for observation and treatment)  
  - Monitor respiratory status frequently  
  - Duration based on clinical response and improvement  







Epilepsy

background  
- A neurological disorder characterised by recurrent seizures due to abnormal electrical activity in the brain  

risk factors  
- Family history of epilepsy  
- Head injuries or trauma  
- Stroke or brain damage  
- Infections affecting the brain, such as meningitis  
- Alcohol or drug misuse  

causes  
- Idiopathic (unknown cause)  
- Structural lesions (tumours, scars)  
- Genetic factors (channelopathies)  
- Metabolic disturbances (hypoglycaemia)  
- Cerebral dysgenesis (congenital malformations)  

pathophysiology  
- Imbalance between excitatory and inhibitory neurotransmitters  
- Hyperexcitability of neuronal networks  
- Synchronised electrical discharges leading to seizures  

signs and symptoms  
- Different types of seizures (tonic-clonic, absence, focal)  
- Postictal confusion or fatigue  
- Aura (warning signs) before a seizure  
- Loss of consciousness or awareness  
- Possible physical injuries during seizures  

investigations  
- Electroencephalogram (EEG) to assess electrical activity  
- MRI or CT scan of the brain for structural abnormalities  
- Blood tests to rule out metabolic causes  
- Video-EEG monitoring for seizure characterisation  
- Genetic testing in specific cases  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and seizure type classification  
- Exclusion of secondary causes of seizures  
- EEG findings consistent with epilepsy  
- Confirmation by specialists if needed  
- Assessment of functionality and impact on daily life  

management  
- Antiepileptic drugs (AEDs) as first-line treatment  
  - Individualised therapy based on seizure type  
  - Regular review for efficacy and side effects  
- Surgical intervention for refractory epilepsy  
  - Consideration for patients unresponsive to multiple AEDs  
  - Involves resection of epileptogenic tissue  
- Lifestyle modifications to avoid triggers  
  - Stress management techniques  
  - Regular sleep and exercise patterns  
- Patient education and counselling  
  - Awareness of seizure first aid  
  - Support for mental health and wellbeing  
- Consideration of other therapies if needed  
  - Ketogenic diet for drug-resistant cases  
  - Vagus nerve stimulation (VNS) in specific populations  







Epistaxis

background  
- Epistaxis, or nosebleed, is a common condition often self-limiting but can indicate underlying pathology  

risk factors  
- Trauma to the nasal mucosa  
- Dry air or low humidity environments  
- Use of anticoagulant medications  
- Nasal conditions such as rhinitis or polyps  
- Alcohol or drug use  

causes  
- Local trauma or injury  
- Infection such as sinusitis  
- Vascular abnormalities like arteriovenous malformations  
- Systemic conditions such as hypertension or coagulopathies  
- Tumours in the nasal cavity or sinuses  

pathophysiology  
- Disruption of the nasal mucosal blood vessels  
- Inflammation and irritation of the nasal lining  
- Increased blood pressure leading to vessel rupture  

signs and symptoms  
- Bleeding from one or both nostrils  
- Possible associated symptoms such as nasal congestion or pain  
- Clots or continuous trickle of blood  
- May experience dizziness if severe and prolonged  

investigations  
- History and physical examination focusing on bleeding history  
- Nasal endoscopy for direct visualisation of bleeding source  
- Coagulation profile to assess for coagulopathies  
- Imaging if a mass or structural anomaly is suspected  
- Blood tests to evaluate for anaemia if necessary  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination findings  
- Identify underlying causes through appropriate investigations  
- Document duration and severity of bleeding episodes  
- Assess for any associated symptoms suggesting systemic illness  
- Evaluate response to initial treatment  

management  
- Direct pressure application to the nose for at least 10-15 minutes  
  - Sitting forward to avoid swallowing blood  
  - Pinching the soft part of the nose  
- Nasal packing if bleeding persists  
  - Anterior or posterior nasal packing based on severity  
  - Consider referral to ENT for severe cases  
- Cautery for localised bleeding sites  
  - Chemical cautery with silver nitrate or electrocautery  
  - Ensure adequate pre-treatment anaesthesia  
- Management of underlying causes such as hypertension  
  - Optimisation of anticoagulation therapy if applicable  
  - Address contributing factors like nasal dryness  
- Consider patient education on nasal care  
  - Recommendations for humidifiers in dry environments  
  - Avoidance of habitual nose picking or vigorous nose blowing  







Essential or secondary hypertension

Background  
- Hypertension is a common condition characterised by persistently high blood pressure with various underlying causes.  

Risk factors  
- Age  
- Family history of hypertension  
- High salt intake  
- Obesity  
- Sedentary lifestyle  

Causes  
- Primary hypertension with no identifiable cause  
- Secondary hypertension due to identifiable conditions such as renal disease  
- Endocrine disorders like hyperaldosteronism or Cushing's syndrome  
- Medications like NSAIDs or certain antidepressants  
- Sleep apnoea  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased vascular resistance leading to elevated blood pressure  
- Overactivity of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system  
- Endothelial dysfunction contributing to arterial stiffness  
- Increased sympathetic nervous system activity  
- Role of genetic and environmental factors in pathogenesis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic  
- Headaches  
- Dizziness  
- Blurred vision  
- Shortness of breath  

Investigations  
- Blood pressure measurement (office and home monitoring)  
- Urinalysis for kidney function  
- Blood tests including serum electrolytes and renal function  
- ECG to assess for left ventricular hypertrophy  
- Echocardiogram if indicated  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed by two or more readings of elevated blood pressure  
- Assess for secondary causes if hypertension is stage 2 or resistant  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications such as diet and exercise  
  - Reduce salt intake  
  - Increase physical activity  
- Initiate antihypertensive medication if required  
  - First-line agents include ACE inhibitors, calcium channel blockers, or thiazides  
- Regular monitoring of blood pressure and medication adherence  
  - Schedule follow-up appointments to assess control  
- Consider referral to specialist if treatment goals are not met  
  - Evaluate for secondary causes or resistant hypertension  
- Address associated cardiovascular risk factors  
  - Manage dyslipidaemia and diabetes if present  







Essential tremor

background  
- Essential tremor is a common neurological movement disorder characterised by involuntary shaking, often affecting the hands and arms.

risk factors  
- Family history of essential tremor  
- Age over 40 years  
- Alcohol consumption may temporarily improve symptoms  
- Increased caffeine intake  
- Stress and fatigue can exacerbate tremors

causes  
- Genetic mutations (e.g. in the *ETM* gene)  
- Abnormalities in the cerebellum and related pathways  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances, particularly involving GABA  
- Progressive neurodegeneration  
- Environmental factors may play a role

pathophysiology  
- Altered communication in the central nervous system  
- Dysfunction in the basal ganglia and cerebellar circuits  
- Increased excitability of motor neurons  
- Synergistic activity of tremor-generating circuits  
- Interference in motor control leads to tremor

signs and symptoms  
- Rhythmic, oscillatory movements, primarily in hands and arms  
- Tremor worsens with movement (action tremor)  
- May affect head, voice, and other body parts  
- Symptoms may improve with alcohol consumption temporarily  
- Impairment of fine motor skills, impacting daily activities

investigations  
- Clinical assessment including family history and symptom evaluation  
- Neurological examination to assess tremor type  
- Blood tests to exclude secondary causes  
- Neuroimaging (MRI or CT) if atypical symptoms are present  
- Neurophysiological tests may be considered in complex cases

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination findings  
- Identification of action tremor characteristics  
- Exclusion of other conditions (e.g. Parkinson's disease)  
- Response to alcohol or beta-blockers may support diagnosis  
- Consider family history indicating genetic predisposition

management  
- Education and reassurance about the condition  
  - Discuss the benign nature of essential tremor  
  - Provide strategies for coping with symptoms  
- Pharmacological treatment with beta-blockers (propranolol)  
  - Starting dose titrated based on response  
  - Consider primidone if beta-blockers are ineffective  
- Consider deep brain stimulation for refractory cases  
  - Typically reserved for severe tremors impacting quality of life  
  - Interdisciplinary evaluation required before surgery  
- Occupational therapy to assist with activities of daily living  
  - Adaptive devices may be recommended  
  - Techniques to improve fine motor tasks  
- Lifestyle modifications such as reducing caffeine and alcohol  
  - Encourage stress management techniques  
  - Recommend regular exercise to improve overall function  







Extradural haemorrhage

Background  
- Extradural haemorrhage is a collection of blood between the outer membrane of the brain and the skull, often resulting from traumatic injury 

Risk factors  
- History of head trauma  
- Anticoagulant medication use  
- Alcohol abuse  
- Young age (particularly in children due to accidents)  
- Elderly individuals 

Causes  
- Skull fractures, often linear  
- Trauma from accidents or falls  
- Baseball bat or similar blunt force injury  
- Penetrating injuries  
- Spontaneous bleeding disorders (rare) 

Pathophysiology  
- Arterial bleeding typically occurs from meningeal arteries  
- Blood accumulation leads to increased intracranial pressure  
- Brain tissue may be displaced, potentially causing herniation  
- Symptoms may develop rapidly post-injury due to volume of blood  
- Can lead to secondary brain injury if not treated promptly 

Signs and symptoms  
- Loss of consciousness, often brief  
- Lucid interval followed by deterioration  
- Headache  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Focal neurological deficits 

Investigations  
- Non-contrast CT scan of the head is the imaging modality of choice  
- MRI may be used in specific cases if required  
- Neurological examination to assess function  
- Blood tests to evaluate coagulopathy  
- Observation of vital signs  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical correlation with mechanism of injury  
- Imaging findings of convexity-based blood collection on CT  
- Identification of midline shift or skull fracture  
- Presence of symptoms suggestive of increased intracranial pressure  
- Assessment of neurological status 

Management  
- Immediate airway management if indicated  
  - Intubation may be required for respiratory support  
  - Ensure cervical spine protection if trauma suspected  
- Surgical intervention for decompression if significant mass effect  
  - Craniotomy or burr hole evacuation may be necessary  
  - Monitor for recurrent bleeding or complications  
- Close monitoring in a clinical setting  
  - Regular neurological assessments  
  - Control of intracranial pressure as needed  
- Supportive care for associated injuries  
  - Pain management and fluid resuscitation  
  - Rehabilitation services for long-term recovery  
- Education for patient's family regarding potential complications  
  - Discuss follow-up appointments and neurological evaluations  
  - Provide information on signs of deterioration to watch for  







Febrile convulsion

Background  
- Febrile convulsions are seizures occurring in young children due to fever, commonly resolving without long-term effects  

Risk factors  
- Family history of febrile seizures  
- Age between 6 months and 5 years  
- Viral infections such as roseola or influenza  
- Rapid increase in body temperature  
- Developmental and neurological disorders  

Causes  
- Common viral infections leading to fever  
- Bacterial infections like otitis media or pneumonia  
- Immunisations causing mild fever  
- Subtle unrecognised illnesses causing fever  
- Genetic predisposition  

Pathophysiology  
- Potentially related to immature central nervous system  
- Fever-induced hyperexcitability in the brain  
- Role of neuroinflammation during febrile episodes  
- Immature cerebral cortex may be more susceptible to convulsive activity  
- Short-lived alterations in neuronal signalling  

Signs and symptoms  
- Generalised tonic-clonic movements  
- Lasting less than 15 minutes  
- Occurs in the context of fever  
- Typically self-limiting with postictal confusion  
- May include loss of consciousness  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Blood tests to assess for infection  
- Lumbar puncture if meningitis is suspected  
- EEG rarely performed unless atypical features present  
- Imaging studies not usually indicated unless focal signs  

Diagnosis  
- Febrile convulsion occurring in a child aged 6 months to 5 years  
- Fever presence during the event  
- No history of afebrile seizures  
- Short duration, resolving without intervention  
- Exclusion of other causes for the seizure  

Management  
- Reassurance to parents and caregivers  
- First-line treatment with rectal diazepam or buccal midazolam for ongoing seizures  
  - If seizures are prolonged or recurrent  
  - Consider emergency care if status epilepticus occurs  
- Safety measures post-convulsion  
  - Position child on their side  
  - Monitor breathing and recovery  

- Long-term management not generally required  
  - Antiepileptics not indicated after a simple febrile convulsion  
  - Education on fever management and warning signs for complications  
- Referral to a paediatrician for complex cases  
  - Persistent atypical seizure features  
  - Significant underlying health concerns  








Fibroadenoma

background  
- Fibroadenoma is a benign breast tumour commonly found in young women

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Age between 15 and 35 years  
- Family history of breast diseases  
- Hormonal factors such as Oestrogen exposure  
- Race, more common in Black women  

causes  
- Exact cause unknown  
- Hormonal influences, particularly Oestrogen  
- Genetic predispositions in some cases  
- Factors related to breast tissue composition  
- Benign proliferation of glandular and stromal tissue  

pathophysiology  
- Fibroadenomas consist of both glandular and fibrous stromal tissue  
- They arise from lobular units of breast tissue  
- They may respond to hormonal changes in the body  
- Growth is usually slow and may vary with menstrual cycles  
- Generally well-circumscribed and mobile on examination  

signs and symptoms  
- Painless lump in the breast  
- Firm and rubbery texture  
- Well-defined borders on examination  
- Typically mobile within the breast tissue  
- May undergo changes in size during menstrual cycles  

investigations  
- Clinical breast examination  
- Ultrasound scan to assess lump characteristics  
- Mammography for women over 40 years  
- Fine needle aspiration cytology if uncertain  
- Core needle biopsy for definitive diagnosis if required  

diagnosis  
- Clinical examination findings  
- Imaging results consistent with fibroadenoma  
- Aspiration or biopsy confirms benign nature  
- Monitoring if the lump is stable and asymptomatic  
- Exclusion of other breast pathologies   

management  
- Observation for small, asymptomatic fibroadenomas  
  - Regular follow-ups and self-examination  
  - Consider ultrasound monitoring  
- Surgical excision if the lesion is large or symptomatic  
  - Educate the patient on procedure and recovery  
  - Pathological examination of excised tissue  
- Fine needle aspiration for symptomatic lumps  
  - Provides symptom relief  
  - Minimal complications associated  
- Hormonal therapy may be considered in some cases  
  - Selective Oestrogen receptor modulators  
  - Monitor for response   
- Reassurance and education about breast changes  
  - Addressing anxiety regarding breast lumps  
  - Providing lifestyle modification advice if indicated  







Fibroids

background  
- Uterine fibroids are benign smooth muscle tumours affecting many women of reproductive age

risk factors  
- Family history of fibroids  
- Early onset of menstruation  
- Obesity  
- African-Caribbean ethnicity  
- Nulliparity  

causes  
- Exact cause unknown  
- Hormonal influence, particularly oestrogen and progesterone  
- Genetic mutations  
- Growth factors  
- Extrinsic factors like diet and environment  

pathophysiology  
- Arise from myometrial smooth muscle cells  
- Uncontrolled proliferation of these cells leading to mass formation  
- May induce localised changes in tissue architecture  
- Can be responsive to hormonal changes  
- Associated with increased angiogenesis  

signs and symptoms  
- Heavy menstrual bleeding  
- Pelvic pain or pressure  
- Urinary frequency  
- Constipation  
- Abdominal enlargement  

investigations  
- Pelvic ultrasound (transabdominal or transvaginal)  
- MRI of the pelvis for detailed imaging  
- Hysteroscopy for direct visualisation if indicated  
- Complete blood count to assess anaemia  
- Pregnancy test to rule out complicating factors  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Imaging findings consistent with fibroids  
- Exclusion of other pelvic masses  
- Assessment of symptom severity  
- Confirmation via histological examination if surgery performed  

management  
- Watchful waiting in asymptomatic cases  
- Medical management:  
  - Tranexamic acid for heavy bleeding  
  - Hormonal therapy (e.g. GnRH agonists, combined oral contraceptives)  
- Surgical options:  
  - Myomectomy for symptomatic women wishing to preserve fertility  
  - Hysterectomy for definitive treatment or severe cases  
- Uterine artery embolisation for symptom control  
- MRI-guided focused ultrasound for targeted fibroid treatment  







Fibromyalgia

background  
- Fibromyalgia is a chronic condition characterised by widespread musculoskeletal pain and fatigue

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Age between 30 and 60 years  
- Family history of fibromyalgia  
- History of traumatic events  
- Coexisting psychological conditions such as anxiety or depression  

causes  
- Exact cause is unknown  
- Abnormal pain processing in the central nervous system  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances  
- Environmental factors such as stress or infections  

pathophysiology  
- Increased sensitivity to pain through alterations in pain pathways  
- Dysregulation of neurotransmitters affecting mood and pain perception  
- Abnormal levels of cytokines contributing to inflammation  
- Disruption of sleep patterns impacting recovery and pain perception  
- Impaired function of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  

signs and symptoms  
- Widespread pain lasting more than three months  
- Fatigue and sleep disturbances  
- Cognitive disturbances referred to as "fibro fog"  
- Morning stiffness  
- Tender points on examination  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment including history and physical examination  
- No specific laboratory tests for diagnosis  
- Blood tests to rule out other conditions  
- Physical function assessment tools  
- Pain scales to quantify symptom severity  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria from the American College of Rheumatology  
- Presence of widespread pain for more than three months  
- Tenderness in specific areas  
- Exclusion of other medical conditions  
- Multidisciplinary approach to consider psychological and physical health  

management  
- Patient education about fibromyalgia  
  - Encourage self-management techniques  
  - Provide resources for support groups  
- Pharmacological treatment options  
  - Use of analgesics such as paracetamol or NSAIDs  
  - Antidepressants like duloxetine or amitriptyline for pain management  
- Non-pharmacological therapies  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy for pain coping skills  
  - Physical activity and exercise for symptom relief  
- Alternative therapies  
  - Acupuncture or massage for pain management  
  - Mindfulness and relaxation techniques  
- Regular follow-up and reassessment of treatment plans  
  - Monitor for medication side effects  
  - Adjust management based on patient response and preferences  







Fibrotic lung disease

background  
- A group of lung diseases characterised by the thickening and stiffening of lung tissue

risk factors  
- Smoking history  
- Exposure to environmental pollutants  
- Occupational exposures (e.g. asbestos, silica)  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Previous lung infections

causes  
- Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis  
- Interstitial lung diseases (e.g. sarcoidosis, hypersensitivity pneumonitis)  
- Drug-induced lung injury (e.g. certain chemotherapeutics)  
- Connective tissue diseases (e.g. rheumatoid arthritis, scleroderma)  
- Radiation therapy to the chest

pathophysiology  
- Injury to alveolar epithelium leads to inflammation  
- Release of inflammatory mediators causes fibroblast proliferation  
- Accumulation of extracellular matrix proteins  
- Altered gas exchange and lung compliance  
- Progressive fibrosis leads to reduced lung function

signs and symptoms  
- Progressive dyspnoea on exertion  
- Chronic dry cough  
- Clubbing of fingers or toes  
- Fatigue and weight loss  
- Inspiratory crackles on auscultation

investigations  
- Chest X-ray showing reticular patterns  
- High-resolution CT scan revealing ground-glass opacities  
- Pulmonary function tests demonstrating restrictive lung disease  
- Serological tests for connective tissue diseases  
- Lung biopsy for definitive diagnosis (if needed)

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and risk factors  
- Diagnosis based on evidence of fibrotic lung disease on imaging  
- Exclusion of other causes of lung fibrosis  
- Multidisciplinary team review for complex cases  
- Consideration of lung biopsy in atypical presentations

management  
- Referral to specialist respiratory services  
  - Evaluate for lung transplantation candidacy  
  - Consider pulmonary rehabilitation programme  
- Antifibrotic therapy (e.g. pirfenidone, nintedanib)  
  - Initiate treatment to slow disease progression  
  - Monitor for side effects   
- Symptomatic management  
  - Use bronchodilators for symptom relief if indicated  
  - Provide oxygen therapy for hypoxaemia  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring  
  - Assess disease progression and treatment response  
  - Adjust treatment as necessary  
- Palliative care approach  
  - Ensure support for end-of-life issues  
  - Address psychological and emotional aspects of care







Folliculitis

background  
- Folliculitis is an inflammation of hair follicles, commonly presenting as pustules or red bumps on the skin 

risk factors  
- Poor hygiene  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Obesity  
- Certain medical therapies (e.g. immunosuppressants)  
- Friction from clothing or equipment  

causes  
- Bacterial infection (e.g. *Staphylococcus aureus*)  
- Fungal infection (e.g. *Malassezia*, dermatophytes)  
- Viral infection (e.g. herpes simplex)  
- Irritation from shaving or waxing  
- Occlusion from oils or creams  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the hair follicle  
- Infection often leads to pustule formation  
- Can involve the dermis in more severe cases  
- Immune response contributes to redness and swelling  
- Chronic cases may lead to scarring or keloid formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Itchy, painful bumps around hair follicles  
- Pustules filled with pus  
- Erythema and inflammation  
- Possible crusting and oozing  
- Can occur anywhere on the body with hair  

investigations  
- Clinical examination  
- Skin swab for culture if bacterial infection is suspected  
- Fungal scraping for suspected fungal infections  
- Histopathology in chronic or atypical cases  
- Consideration of underlying conditions if recurrent  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and history  
- Exclusion of other dermatological conditions  
- Confirm diagnosis with culture or scrapes if necessary  
- Evaluate for underlying systemic conditions if recurrent  
- Assess severity and extent of infection  

management  
- I. Topical treatments  
  - Antibacterial ointments (e.g. mupirocin)  
  - Antifungal creams if fungal aetiology is established  
- II. Systemic treatments  
  - Oral antibiotics for extensive bacterial infections  
  - Antifungal medications for confirmed fungal infections  
- III. Hygiene measures  
  - Advise on proper washing and skin care  
  - Recommend avoiding tight clothing and irritants  
- IV. Behavioural modifications  
  - Educate on proper shaving techniques  
  - Suggest non-irritating shaving equipment  
- V. Follow-up  
  - Monitor for response to treatment  
  - Refer to a dermatologist if persistent or severe







Gallstones and biliary colic

background  
- Gallstones are solid particles that form in the gallbladder, causing biliary colic when they obstruct the bile duct.  

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Obesity or rapid weight loss  
- Age over 40  
- Family history of gallstones  
- Pregnancy  

causes  
- Supersaturation of bile with cholesterol or bilirubin  
- Incomplete emptying of the gallbladder  
- Changes in bile salts  
- Inflammation of the gallbladder  
- Haemolysis leading to increased bilirubin  

pathophysiology  
- Formation of gallstones due to imbalances in bile components  
- Stone movement can lead to biliary colic when obstructing bile flow  
- Inflammation occurs if obstruction leads to infection or cholecystitis  

signs and symptoms  
- Severe abdominal pain in the right upper quadrant  
- Pain radiating to the back or right shoulder  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Symptoms often occur after fatty meals  
- Jaundice may indicate cholangitis if stone blocks the bile duct  

investigations  
- Ultrasound abdomen to detect gallstones  
- CT scan if complications are suspected  
- LFTs to assess liver function  
- MRCP for non-invasive imaging of the biliary tree  
- Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP) for therapeutic intervention  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on history and physical examination  
- Confirmatory imaging (usually ultrasound) showing gallstones  
- Assessment for complications like cholecystitis or pancreatitis  
- Consideration of symptoms timing and pattern  
- Exclusion of other abdominal conditions  

management  
- Dietary modifications to manage symptoms  
  - Low-fat diet to prevent biliary colic episodes  
  - Encourage weight loss if overweight  
- Analgesia for pain relief  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
  - Opioids for severe pain if necessary  
- Surgical intervention (cholecystectomy) for symptomatic gallstones  
  - Laparoscopic approach preferred for quicker recovery  
  - Open surgery may be indicated in certain cases  
- ERCP for acute complications such as choledocholithiasis  
  - Can provide stone removal and relieve obstruction  
  - May be followed by cholecystectomy  
- Patient education on recognising symptoms and when to seek care  
  - Advise on potential complications of untreated gallstones  
  - Regular follow-up if surgery is not immediately planned  







Gangrene

Background  
- Gangrene is a severe condition resulting from tissue death due to inadequate blood supply, infection or both  

Risk factors  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Peripheral arterial disease  
- Trauma or injury  
- Immunosuppression  
- Obesity  

Causes  
- Ischaemia due to vascular occlusion  
- Infection, particularly with *Clostridium* species  
- Trauma or surgical complications  
- Frostbite  
- Chronic wounds  

Pathophysiology  
- Decreased blood flow leads to tissue hypoxia  
- Anaerobic metabolism generates toxins  
- Bacterial infection promotes necrosis  
- Inflammatory response exacerbates tissue damage  
- Surrounding tissue may also become affected  

Signs and symptoms  
- Dark discolouration of skin  
- Swelling and pain  
- Blisters or ulcers  
- Foul-smelling discharge  
- Systemic symptoms like fever  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination  
- Blood tests including full blood count  
- Imaging studies (X-ray, CT, MRI)  
- Wound cultures  
- Doppler ultrasound to assess blood flow  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical features and medical history  
- Imaging findings consistent with tissue necrosis  
- Cultures identifying causative organisms  
- Assessment of blood supply  
- Exclusion of other conditions  

Management  
- Surgical debridement of necrotic tissue  
  - May require amputation for severe cases  
  - Focus on removing infected tissue  
- Antibiotic therapy based on cultures  
  - Broad-spectrum antibiotics initially  
  - Adjust according to sensitivity results  
- Supportive care for systemic symptoms  
  - Fluid resuscitation if necessary  
  - Analgesia for pain management  
- Hyperbaric oxygen therapy in selected cases  
  - Promotes healing and reduces infection  
  - Consider in cases of gas gangrene  
- Manage underlying conditions (e.g., diabetes)  
  - Optimise blood glucose levels  
  - Improve vascular supply if possible  







Gastric cancer

background  
- Gastric cancer is a malignant condition affecting the stomach, with increasing incidence in certain populations  

risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Higher prevalence in men  
- Family history of gastric cancer  
- Chronic gastritis and Helicobacter pylori infection  
- Smoking and high-salt diet  

causes  
- Genetic mutations such as CDH1 and TP53  
- Infection with *Helicobacter pylori*  
- Autoimmune gastritis  
- Environmental factors including diet and smoking  
- Gastroesophageal reflux disease  

pathophysiology  
- Initiation often involves chronic gastric inflammation  
- Progression can lead to dysplasia and carcinoma  
- Tumours commonly arise from the gastric epithelium  
- The presence of H. pylori influences tumour development  
- Metastasis commonly occurs to lymph nodes and surrounding organs  

signs and symptoms  
- Unintentional weight loss  
- Early satiety and loss of appetite  
- Abdominal pain or discomfort  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Presence of melaena or fresh blood in stools  

investigations  
- Upper gastrointestinal endoscopy with biopsy  
- CT scans for staging and metastasis assessment  
- Blood tests for anaemia and tumour markers (e.g., CEA)  
- Endoscopic ultrasound for local invasion assessment  
- Barium swallow studies can demonstrate structural changes  

diagnosis  
- Histopathological confirmation through biopsy  
- Classification based on Lauren classification (intestinal vs. diffuse)  
- Staging via TNM system to determine extent of disease  
- Consideration of clinical presentation and risk factors  
- Exclusion of other gastric pathologies  

management  
- Surgical resection (subtotal or total gastrectomy)  
  - Node dissection may be included  
  - Palliative approaches if unresectable  
- Chemotherapy as neoadjuvant or adjuvant treatment  
  - Consideration of regimens including cisplatin and fluorouracil  
  - Targeted therapy if HER2 positive (e.g., trastuzumab)  
- Symptomatic management for advanced disease  
  - Pain management strategies  
  - Nutritional support and counselling  
- Regular follow-up and surveillance  
  - Monitoring for recurrence or metastasis  
  - Blood tests for tumour markers  
- Referral to specialist palliative care  
  - Input on managing symptoms and quality of life  
  - Support for patient and caregivers  







Gastrointestinal perforation

Background  
- Gastrointestinal perforation is a life-threatening condition where a hole forms in the wall of the gastrointestinal tract

Risk factors  
- Peptic ulcer disease  
- Inflammatory bowel disease  
- Trauma or injury to the abdomen  
- Advanced malignancy  
- Foreign body ingestion  

Causes  
- Perforated ulcer  
- Diverticulitis  
- Appendicitis  
- Colorectal cancer  
- Ischaemic bowel disease  

Pathophysiology  
- Escape of gastrointestinal contents into the peritoneal cavity  
- Leads to peritonitis and systemic inflammatory response  
- May result in sepsis if untreated  
- Can cause fluid and electrolyte imbalances  
- Requires rapid intervention to prevent complications  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset abdominal pain  
- Rigidity and tenderness in the abdomen  
- Rebound tenderness  
- Fever and chills  
- Nausea and vomiting  

Investigations  
- Abdominal X-ray to identify free air  
- CT scan for precise localisation and cause  
- Blood tests including white cell count and inflammatory markers  
- Urinalysis to rule out other causes  
- Ultrasound in specific cases  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and physical examination  
- Imaging studies confirming perforation  
- Laboratory tests indicating infection or perforation  
- Consideration of patient history and risk factors  
- Multidisciplinary team discussion in complex cases  

Management  
- Immediate surgical intervention for perforation  
  - Laparotomy or laparoscopy depending on the situation  
  - Resection or repair of the affected segment  
- Antibiotic therapy to treat and prevent infection  
  - Broad-spectrum antibiotics initiated immediately  
  - Tailored antibiotics based on culture results  
- Fluid resuscitation and electrolyte management  
  - IV fluids to restore hydration  
  - Monitoring electrolyte levels and correcting abnormalities  
- Post-operative care and monitoring  
  - Analgesia and wound care  
  - Assessment for complications such as abscess formation  
- Referral to specialist teams if necessary  
  - Gastroenterology for underlying conditions  
  - Nutrition teams for post-operative care and feeding recommendations  







GORD

Background  
- Gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (GORD) is a chronic condition characterised by the reflux of gastric contents into the oesophagus 

Risk factors  
- Obesity  
- Smoking  
- Pregnancy  
- Hiatal hernia  
- Certain medications (e.g. NSAIDs, beta-blockers)

Causes  
- Reduced lower oesophageal sphincter (LOS) tone  
- Delayed gastric emptying  
- Increased intra-abdominal pressure  
- Oesophageal motility disorders  
- Food and beverage triggers (e.g. spicy foods, caffeine)

Pathophysiology  
- Reflux of stomach acid leads to oesophageal mucosal injury  
- Inflammation (oesophagitis) may occur  
- Barret's oesophagus can develop as a complication, increasing the risk of adenocarcinoma

Signs and symptoms  
- Heartburn  
- Regurgitation  
- Dysphagia  
- Chest pain  
- Chronic cough

Investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Oesophagogastroduodenoscopy (OGD) for direct visualisation  
- Oesophageal pH monitoring  
- Manometry to assess motility  
- Barium swallow study 

Diagnosis  
- Based on symptom history and physical examination  
- Consider OGD if alarm symptoms present or symptoms persist despite treatment  
- Use 24-hour pH monitoring to confirm diagnosis in atypical cases  
- Evaluate for complications (e.g., strictures, Barrett's oesophagus)  
- Consider response to proton pump inhibitors in diagnosis

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Weight loss if overweight  
  - Elevation of head during sleep  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Proton pump inhibitors (PPIs) as first-line therapy  
  - H2-receptor antagonists for milder cases  
- Surgical options  
  - Fundoplication for refractory cases  
  - Consider anti-reflux surgery if medication fails  
- Endoscopic therapies  
  - Less common but can be used for specific patients  
  - Not widely available or standardised  
- Patient education  
  - Inform about diet and lifestyle changes  
  - Explain the nature and chronicity of the disease  







Gonorrhoea

Background  
- Gonorrhoea is a sexually transmitted infection caused by the bacterium *Neisseria gonorrhoeae*

Risk Factors  
- Unprotected sexual intercourse  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Previous history of sexually transmitted infections  
- Inconsistent condom use  
- Young age (especially those under 25)

Causes  
- Infection with *Neisseria gonorrhoeae*  
- Spread through sexual contact (oral, vaginal, anal)  
- Vertical transmission from mother to child during childbirth  
- Co-infection with other STIs  
- Lack of vaccination since no effective vaccine exists

Pathophysiology  
- The bacterium infects mucous membranes of the urogenital tract, rectum, or throat  
- It attaches to epithelial cells and elicits an inflammatory response  
- Can lead to purulent discharge and complications if untreated  
- Potential for systemic spread, causing disseminated gonococcal infection  
- Long-term complications include pelvic inflammatory disease and infertility

Signs and Symptoms  
- Urethral discharge (whitish or yellow) in males  
- Dysuria (painful urination)  
- Increased vaginal discharge in females  
- Pelvic pain, especially if complications develop  
- Asymptomatic cases are common, particularly in females

Investigations  
- Nucleic acid amplification tests (NAATs) for urine and swabs  
- Culture of discharge from affected sites  
- Microscopy of discharge (gram stain)  
- Testing for co-existing STIs  
- Pharyngeal and rectal swabs if symptoms in those areas

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed through NAAT or culture  
- Positive test for *Neisseria gonorrhoeae* from appropriate sites  
- Clinical assessment considering symptoms and epidemiological factors  
- Exclude other STIs and non-infective causes  
- Presence of symptoms with positive tests in asymptomatic individuals requires consideration

Management  
- First-line treatment with dual therapy (Ceftriaxone and Azithromycin)  
  - Ceftriaxone 500 mg IM stat  
  - Azithromycin 1 g orally stat  
- Advise on safe sex practices and partner notification  
  - Offer STI screening for partners  
  - Counsel regarding the importance of treatment adherence  
- Follow-up appointments if symptoms persist or in complicated cases  
  - Re-test for reinfection after 3 months  
  - Clinical review for ongoing symptoms or complications  
- Consider referral to specialist services if complications arise  
  - Discuss potential long-term effects  
  - Provide support for management of chronic conditions if necessary  
- Provide additional information regarding reproductive health and future STI risks  
  - Educate on prevention strategies  
  - Discuss contraceptive options as needed  







Haemochromatosis

Background  
- A genetic disorder causing excessive iron accumulation in the body 

Risk factors  
- Genetic predisposition (HFE mutations)  
- Family history of haemochromatosis  
- Male gender  
- Age over 40  
- Alcohol consumption 

Causes  
- Primary (hereditary) haemochromatosis due to HFE gene mutations  
- Secondary haemochromatosis arising from repeated blood transfusions  
- Excessive oral iron intake  
- Chronic liver disease  
- Certain blood disorders (e.g., thalassaemia)  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased intestinal iron absorption leading to iron overload  
- Iron deposition in organs (liver, heart, pancreas)  
- Organ damage resulting from oxidative stress  
- Development of conditions like cirrhosis, diabetes, and cardiomyopathy  
- Inflammation and fibrosis in affected organs  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Joint pain or arthritis  
- Skin changes (bronzing)  
- Abdominal pain and hepatomegaly  
- Diabetes mellitus  

Investigations  
- Serum ferritin levels  
- Transferrin saturation percentage  
- Genetic testing for HFE mutations  
- Liver function tests  
- MRI for liver iron concentration  

Diagnosis  
- Elevated ferritin and transferrin saturation in conjunction with clinical findings  
- HFE genetic testing confirming hereditary haemochromatosis  
- Liver biopsy if necessary to assess fibrosis or cirrhosis  
- Exclusion of secondary causes of iron overload  
- Clinical correlation of signs and symptoms  

Management  
- Phlebotomy for iron removal  
  - Regular venesection to reduce iron stores  
  - Target ferritin levels below 50 µg/L  
- Dietary modifications  
  - Avoidance of iron-rich foods and vitamin C supplements  
  - Limit alcohol intake  
- Monitoring  
  - Regular blood tests for ferritin and transferrin saturation  
  - Liver function tests to monitor liver health  
- Treatment of complications  
  - Management of diabetes, liver disease, or heart issues  
  - Referral to specialists as required  
- Genetic counselling for affected families  
  - Discuss inheritance patterns and screening of relatives  
  - Provide support and information on disease management  







Haemoglobinopathies

Background  
- Haemoglobinopathies are inherited blood disorders affecting haemoglobin structure and function  

Risk factors  
- Family history of haemoglobinopathy  
- Ethnicity (higher prevalence in African, Mediterranean, Middle Eastern, and Southeast Asian populations)  
- Consanguineous marriage  
- Presence of sickle cell trait  
- Exposure to environmental factors (e.g., exposure to toxins)  

Causes  
- Genetic mutations affecting globin chains  
- Sickle cell disease from mutation in HBB gene  
- Thalassaemias from deletions or point mutations in globin genes  
- Structural variants from amino acid substitutions in globin chains  
- Coinheritance of different haemoglobinopathies  

Pathophysiology  
- Altered haemoglobin production leading to ineffective erythropoiesis  
- Abnormal haemoglobin polymerisation (e.g., in sickle cell disease)  
- Increased red blood cell destruction (haemolysis)  
- Complications from vaso-occlusion and chronic inflammation  
- Organ damage due to chronic hypoxia  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Pallor and jaundice  
- Recurrent infections  
- Pain episodes (vaso-occlusive crisis in sickle cell disease)  
- Splenic sequestration and crisis   

Investigations  
- Complete blood count (CBC)  
- Peripheral blood smear  
- Haemoglobin electrophoresis  
- Genetic testing for specific mutations  
- Additional tests for iron studies and reticulocyte count  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmation of abnormal haemoglobin through electrophoresis  
- Presence of clinical signs consistent with type of haemoglobinopathy  
- Genetic testing to confirm diagnosis and assess severity  
- Exclusion of other causes of anaemia  
- Family testing for carrier status  

Management  
- Supportive care: pain management for crisis  
  - Analgesics and hydration  
- Regular monitoring: routine blood tests  
  - Manage complications (e.g., transfusions, hydroxycarbamide)  
- Vaccination and prophylaxis: infection prevention  
  - Pneumococcal, meningococcal, and influenza vaccinations  
- Education and counselling: genetic counselling for families  
  - Psychological support for affected individuals  
- Specific therapies: blood transfusions, chelation therapy in thalassaemia  
  - Bone marrow/stem cell transplant for severe cases  







Haemophilia

background  
- Haemophilia is a hereditary bleeding disorder due to deficiency of clotting factors 

risk factors  
- Family history of haemophilia  
- Male sex  
- Genetic mutations  
- Maternal carrier status  
- Severity determined by factor levels  

causes  
- Haemophilia A due to factor VIII deficiency  
- Haemophilia B due to factor IX deficiency  
- Most cases are inherited in an X-linked recessive manner  
- Spontaneous mutations may occur  
- Less common forms can arise from factor deficiencies  

pathophysiology  
- Impaired blood coagulation due to low clotting factor levels  
- Prolonged bleeding time in response to injury  
- Risk of spontaneous internal bleeds, particularly in joints and muscles  
- Synthesis of factor proteins occurs in the liver  
- Genetic mutations disrupt normal factor production  

signs and symptoms  
- Frequent nosebleeds or bleeding gums  
- Prolonged bleeding after cuts or injuries  
- Bruising easily  
- Joint pain and swelling from bleeding (haemarthrosis)  
- Blood in urine or stool  

investigations  
- Prolonged activated partial thromboplastin time (aPTT)  
- Normal prothrombin time (PT)  
- Measurement of factor VIII or IX activity  
- Genetic testing for mutation identification  
- Complete blood count to assess for anaemia  

diagnosis  
- Identification of prolonged aPTT in haemophilia symptoms  
- Confirmation of factor deficiency through specific factor assays  
- Family history assessment to evaluate inheritance patterns  
- Genetic testing for definitive diagnosis in difficult cases  
- Exclude other coagulation disorders  

management  
- Factor replacement therapy for bleeding episodes  
  - Use factor VIII for Haemophilia A  
  - Use factor IX for Haemophilia B  
- Prophylaxis to prevent bleeds in severe cases  
  - Regular factor infusions  
  - Tailored treatment regimen based on clinical needs  
- Management of bleeding episodes with antifibrinolytics  
  - Tranexamic acid for mucosal bleeding  
  - Desmopressin for mild Haemophilia A  
- Education on avoidance of injury and self-management  
  - Informational resources for patients and families  
  - Training on factor administration techniques  
- Psychological support and counselling as needed  
  - Addressing psychosocial effects of chronic condition  
  - Support groups for patients and families  







Haemorrhoids

Background  
- Haemorrhoids are swollen blood vessels in the rectal area causing pain, itching, and bleeding

Risk factors  
- Ageing  
- Pregnancy  
- Chronic constipation or diarrhoea  
- Prolonged sitting or standing  
- Obesity  

Causes  
- Increased pressure in the pelvic and rectal area  
- Straining during bowel movements  
- Low-fibre diet  
- Heavy lifting  
- Anal intercourse  

Pathophysiology  
- Dilation and engorgement of rectal veins  
- Formation of internal and external haemorrhoids  
- Inflammation and possible thrombosis of external haemorrhoids  

Signs and symptoms  
- Rectal bleeding during bowel movements  
- Pain and discomfort in the anal area  
- Itching or irritation around the anus  
- Swelling around the anus  
- A lump near the anus, possibly painful  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination including anal inspection  
- Digital rectal examination  
- Flexible sigmoidoscopy if necessary  
- External examination for thrombosed external haemorrhoids  
- Consideration of colonoscopy for differing diagnoses  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed through symptoms and physical examination  
- Exclusion of other causes of rectal bleeding  
- Assessment of the location and type of haemorrhoids  
- Severity of symptoms influences classification  
- Use of endoscopy if significant symptoms or concerning findings  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications including increased dietary fibre  
   - Encourage fluid intake  
   - Advise regular exercise  
- Topical treatments such as creams or suppositories  
   - Consider hydrocortisone for inflammation  
   - Use local anaesthetics for pain relief  
- Banding or sclerotherapy for symptomatic internal haemorrhoids  
   - Rubber band ligation for grade 1-3   
   - Sclerotherapy for patients who cannot undergo banding  
- Surgical options for severe or prolapsed cases  
   - Haemorrhoidectomy for third-degree or fourth-degree haemorrhoids  
   - Stapled haemorrhoidopexy for selected patients  
- Follow-up care to monitor symptoms and recurrence  
   - Education on proper bowel habits  
   - Regular assessment to ensure management efficacy  







Henoch-Schonlein purpura

background  
- Henoch-Schonlein purpura is a small-vessel vasculitis primarily affecting children, characterised by a tetrad of symptoms.

risk factors  
- Age between 2 and 6 years  
- Recent infections (especially respiratory)  
- Positive family history of vasculitis  
- Exposure to certain medications  
- Possible food allergies  

causes  
- Immune-mediated response  
- Post-infectious hypersensitivity   
- Associated with viral infections (e.g., **Epstein-Barr virus**)  
- In rare cases, associated with connective tissue disorders  
- Environmental triggers  

pathophysiology  
- Deposition of immunoglobulin A (IgA) in small blood vessels  
- Activation of the complement cascade  
- Resultant inflammation and vascular damage  
- Increased vascular permeability leading to tissue oedema  
- Activation of platelets contributing to capillary leakage  

signs and symptoms  
- Palpable purpura typically on buttocks and legs  
- Abdominal pain and gastrointestinal bleeding  
- Arthralgia or arthritis  
- Renal involvement (haematuria and proteinuria)  
- Fever and malaise  

investigations  
- Clinical examination and history taking  
- Urinalysis for haematuria and proteinuria  
- Blood tests (full blood count, renal function tests, inflammatory markers)  
- Skin biopsy may show IgA deposition  
- Imaging (abdominal ultrasound if gastrointestinal complications)  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on the classic tetrad of symptoms  
- Exclusion of other causes of vasculitis  
- Consideration of age and recent infections  
- Renal involvement assessed with urine tests  
- Confirmation through skin biopsy if necessary  

management  
- Supportive care including hydration  
  - Encourage fluid intake  
  - Monitor for renal function  
- Pain management with analgesics  
  - Use paracetamol or ibuprofen  
  - Avoid aspirin in children  
- Corticosteroids for severe abdominal/pulmonary symptoms  
  - Consider high-dose steroids for significant renal involvement  
  - Taper once symptoms improve  
- Management of renal complications if present  
  - Refer to nephrology for significant impairment  
  - Monitor urine output and electrolytes  
- Follow-up appointments to assess ongoing symptoms  
  - Monitor for resolution of purpura  
  - Regular renal function tests  







Hepatitis

Background  
- Hepatitis is an inflammation of the liver often caused by viral infections, alcohol, or autoimmune diseases.

Risk factors  
- Unprotected sexual contact  
- Sharing needles or drug paraphernalia  
- Travel to endemic areas  
- Blood transfusions before 1991  
- Chronic liver disease history 

Causes  
- Hepatitis A virus (HAV)  
- Hepatitis B virus (HBV)  
- Hepatitis C virus (HCV)  
- Alcoholic hepatitis  
- Autoimmune hepatitis 

Pathophysiology  
- Viral invasion leads to hepatocyte damage  
- Immune response causes inflammation  
- Fibrosis develops with chronic inflammation  
- Cirrhosis may occur over time  
- Liver cancer risk increases with chronic hepatitis

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue  
- Jaundice  
- Dark urine and pale stools  
- Abdominal pain or discomfort  
- Loss of appetite and nausea 

Investigations  
- Serology for hepatitis viruses  
- Liver function tests (LFTs)  
- Ultrasound of the liver  
- Hepatitis B viral load (if HBV)  
- Liver biopsy (if indicated) 

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination  
- Positive serology for specific hepatitis virus  
- Elevated LFTs consistent with hepatitis  
- Exclusion of other liver diseases  
- Assessment of chronicity if applicable 

Management  
- Supportive care for acute hepatitis  
  - Adequate hydration and rest  
  - Avoidance of alcohol and hepatotoxic drugs  
- Antiviral therapy for chronic hepatitis B and C  
  - Nucleos(t)ide analogues for HBV  
  - Direct-acting antivirals for HCV  
- Vaccination for hepatitis A and B  
  - Administered according to risk  
  - Monitor for antibody response  
- Monitor liver function and complications  
  - Regular follow-up appointments  
  - Screening for hepatocellular carcinoma in high-risk patients  
- Referral to a specialist if severe or chronic  
  - Hepatology or liver transplant unit  
  - Consideration of liver transplant in advanced cases







Hernias

Background  
- Hernias occur when an organ or tissue protrudes through a weak spot in the abdominal wall

Risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Obesity  
- Chronic cough or straining during bowel movements  
- Previous abdominal surgery  
- Family history of hernias  

Causes  
- Congenital weakness in the abdominal wall  
- Increased abdominal pressure due to heavy lifting  
- Ascites or pregnancy   
- Connective tissue disorders  
- Trauma or injury to the abdomen  

Pathophysiology  
- Protrusion of an organ or tissue through the fascia or muscle of the abdominal wall  
- Weakness in tissue allows for abdominal contents to bulge out  
- Can lead to incarceration or strangulation if blood supply is compromised  

Signs and symptoms  
- Visible bulge in the abdominal area  
- Pain or discomfort, especially when bending over or lifting  
- Symptoms may worsen with activity or straining  
- Tenderness at the site of the bulge  
- Nausea or vomiting in cases of incarceration or strangulation  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination to identify the bulge and assess symptoms  
- Ultrasound to evaluate the hernia size and contents  
- CT scan for complex or recurrent cases  
- MRI if soft tissue differentiation is needed  
- Blood tests in cases of complications such as strangulation  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical findings and patient history  
- Confirmed with imaging if necessary  
- Assess for complications like incarceration or strangulation  
- Differentiate between inguinal, femoral, umbilical, and incisional hernias  
- Consider differential diagnoses such as lymphadenopathy or abscess  

Management  
- Surgical intervention is the definitive treatment  
  - Open or laparoscopic repair depending on the hernia type and patient factors  
  - Mesh repair may be used to strengthen the abdominal wall  
- Conservative management for asymptomatic hernias  
  - Watchful waiting with regular monitoring  
  - Patient education on symptoms to watch for indicating complications







Herpes simplex virus

background  
- Herpes simplex virus (HSV) is a common viral infection causing oral and genital lesions

risk factors  
- Previous HSV infection  
- Immunocompromised state  
- Unprotected sexual activity  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- History of sexually transmitted infections

causes  
- HSV type 1 primarily causes oral herpes  
- HSV type 2 primarily causes genital herpes  
- Viral transmission through direct contact with lesions or mucosal surfaces  
- Asymptomatic shedding of the virus  
- Reactivation from latent infection

pathophysiology  
- Virus enters through broken skin or mucous membranes  
- Travels along sensory nerves to sensory ganglia  
- Establishes latency, reactivating during stress or immunosuppression  
- Causes local tissue inflammation and vesicular lesions  
- Can spread to adjacent tissues and cause systemic symptoms

signs and symptoms  
- Painful vesicular lesions on erythematous base  
- Itching or burning sensation prior to lesion appearance  
- Tender lymphadenopathy in regional lymph nodes  
- Flu-like symptoms during primary infection  
- Recurrent episodes may have milder symptoms

investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- PCR testing of lesion swabs for confirmation  
- Serological testing for IgG/IgM antibodies  
- Viral culture from lesions  
- Swab taken from mucosal surfaces or vesicles for direct testing

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation of lesions  
- PCR testing to confirm HSV type  
- Serology to assess for past infections  
- Examination for recurrent episodes with classic presentation  
- Consider differential diagnoses if atypical symptoms are present

management  
- Antiviral medications to reduce severity and duration  
  - Aciclovir, Valaciclovir, or Famciclovir  
- Pain relief using analgesics or topical treatments  
  - Paracetamol or topical lidocaine  
- Counsel on transmission risk and safe practices  
  - Use of condoms and avoidance during outbreaks  
- Provide education on recognising prodromal symptoms  
  - Guidance on early management of recurrences  
- Regular follow-up for those with frequent recurrences  
  - Consider suppressive therapy in severe cases







Hospital acquired infections

background  
- Hospital-acquired infections, also known as nosocomial infections, are infections developed during hospital stays that were not present upon admission

risk factors  
- Prolonged hospital stay  
- Invasive procedures or devices such as catheters  
- Immunocompromised status  
- Increased age  
- Previous antibiotic therapy

causes  
- Bacteria such as *Staphylococcus aureus* and *Escherichia coli*  
- Viruses including influenza and norovirus  
- Fungi like *Candida* species  
- Multi-drug resistant organisms  
- Poor hand hygiene practices

pathophysiology  
- Microbial colonisation of the host's epithelial surfaces  
- Breach of natural barriers (skin, mucosal membranes)  
- Immune system evasion by pathogens  
- Pathogen dissemination within healthcare settings  
- Development of biofilms on medical devices

signs and symptoms  
- Fever and chills  
- Increased heart rate  
- Localised redness, swelling, or pain at the site of infection  
- Respiratory distress (in pneumonia)  
- Changes in white blood cell count 

investigations  
- Blood cultures to identify pathogens  
- Imaging studies (X-ray, CT scan) for abscess or pneumonia  
- Urinalysis and cultures for urinary tract infections  
- Swabs from infected sites (wounds, respiratory samples)  
- Full blood count and inflammatory markers (CRP, ESR)

diagnosis  
- Confirm infection with laboratory results  
- Evaluate clinical signs and symptoms  
- Rule out other possible causes of symptoms  
- Assess timing of infection relative to hospital admission  
- Use scoring systems (e.g., NHSN criteria) for classification

management  
- Start appropriate empirical antibiotic therapy  
  - Adjust based on microbiological results  
  - Consider local resistance patterns  
- Implement infection control measures  
  - Hand hygiene protocols  
  - Isolation of infected patients  
- Monitor clinical response  
  - Regular assessments of infection markers  
  - Adjust treatment based on patient progress  
- Educate healthcare staff on prevention  
  - Training on aseptic techniques  
  - Promotion of vaccination where applicable  
- Coordinate with multidisciplinary teams  
  - Involve infection prevention specialists  
  - Regularly review treatment plans







Human immunodeficiency virus

Background  
- Human immunodeficiency virus is a virus that attacks the immune system, potentially leading to acquired immunodeficiency syndrome

Risk factors  
- Unprotected sexual intercourse with HIV-positive individuals  
- Sharing needles or syringes  
- Receiving contaminated blood products  
- Mother-to-child transmission during childbirth or breastfeeding  
- Having multiple sexual partners

Causes  
- Infection with HIV-1 or HIV-2  
- The virus is primarily transmitted through bodily fluids  
- High viral load increases transmission risk  
- Certain sexually transmitted infections can facilitate HIV infection  
- Lack of access to preventive measures increases vulnerability

Pathophysiology  
- HIV infects CD4 T-cells, leading to their depletion  
- Reduction in CD4 T-cell count impairs immune response  
- Results in opportunistic infections and malignancies  
- HIV integrates into the host genome, establishing chronic infection  
- Immune activation and hyper-inflammation occur, damaging tissues

Signs and symptoms  
- Acute HIV infection may present with flu-like symptoms  
- Chronic HIV infection can be asymptomatic for years  
- Symptoms may include weight loss, night sweats, and diarrhoea  
- Opportunistic infections manifest as recurrent pneumonia or candidiasis  
- Late-stage may present with opportunistic cancers or severe infections

Investigations  
- HIV antibody test (initial screening)  
- HIV antigen test (p24 antigen detection)  
- CD4 count to assess immune function  
- HIV viral load test to monitor active infection  
- Genotypic resistance testing for treatment-naïve patients

Diagnosis  
- Positive HIV antibody or antigen test confirms infection  
- Confirmatory testing with a second test if initial is reactive  
- CD4 count determines the need for ART  
- Full health assessment for opportunistic infections  
- Consider risk factors and symptoms in diagnosis

Management  
- Initiate antiretroviral therapy (ART) for all HIV-positive patients  
  - Medication adherence is critical for viral suppression  
  - Regular monitoring of viral load and CD4 count  
- Monitor for and treat opportunistic infections as needed  
  - Prophylactic treatment for specific opportunistic infections  
  - Address any other co-morbid health conditions  
- Provide counselling and support for mental health  
  - Assess and support psychosocial needs  
  - Encourage participation in support groups  
- Encourage safer sex practices to prevent transmission  
  - Use of condoms and regular testing for partners  
  - Pre-exposure prophylaxis (PrEP) for high-risk individuals  
- Consider additional vaccinations as indicated  
  - Hepatitis B and influenza vaccinations recommended  
  - Assess vaccination status in line with NICE guidelines







Human papilloma virus infection

Background  
- Human papillomavirus (HPV) is a group of viruses linked to various benign and malignant lesions, notably cervical cancer  

Risk factors  
- Early onset of sexual activity  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Immunocompromised status  
- Smoking  
- Co-infection with other sexually transmitted infections  

Causes  
- Transmission through skin-to-skin contact during sexual activity  
- Infection from mother to child during childbirth  
- HPV types 16 and 18 linked to cervical and other cancers  
- Low-risk types associated with genital warts  
- High-risk types implicated in anogenital and oropharyngeal cancers  

Pathophysiology  
- HPV infects keratinocytes in the epithelium  
- Integration of viral DNA into host cell genome may lead to dysregulation of cell cycle  
- Oncogenes E6 and E7 promote cellular proliferation and inhibit apoptosis  
- Persistent infection can lead to precancerous lesions  
- Immune evasion contributes to carcinogenesis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic in early stages  
- Genital warts may appear as small lumps or lesions  
- Cervical dysplasia can be detected via screening  
- Advanced cancers may present with abnormal bleeding or pain  
- Oropharyngeal cancer may cause dysphagia or sore throat  

Investigations  
- Pap smear for cervical screening  
- HPV DNA testing for high-risk types  
- Colposcopy for abnormal cervical findings  
- Biopsy of suspicious lesions  
- Visual inspection for genital warts  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and presenting signs  
- Confirmation through laboratory testing (Pap smear, HPV typing)  
- Follow guidelines for screening and management based on risk
