- Assess for co-existing sexually transmitted infections  
- Evaluate severity of lesions through biopsies  

Management  
- Offer HPV vaccination (Gardasil, Cervarix) to eligible age groups  
  - Administer before the onset of sexual activity for best efficacy  
  - Recommend catch-up vaccination for those missed  
- Treat genital warts with topical agents (e.g., podophyllotoxin, imiquimod)  
  - Cryotherapy or surgical options for larger warts  
  - Monitor for recurrence after treatment  
- Perform excisional treatment for high-grade squamous intraepithelial lesions (HSIL)  
  - Loop electrosurgical excision procedure (LEEP) as a common choice  
  - Follow up with regular cervical screening after treatment  
- Provide education on safe sex practices to reduce transmission  
  - Discuss importance of barrier methods and regular screenings  
  - Encourage open communication with partners  
- Monitor and manage any identified dysplasia or malignancy accordingly  
  - Refer to oncology for advanced cases  
  - Follow-up protocols in place for abnormal findings  







Hypercalcaemia of malignancy

background  
- Hypercalcaemia of malignancy is an elevated calcium level due to cancer-related processes.

risk factors  
- Malignancies such as lung, breast, and multiple myeloma  
- Older age  
- Previous history of malignancy  
- Extended immobilisation  
- Dehydration 

causes  
- Osteolytic metastasis leading to increased bone resorption  
- Paraneoplastic syndromes producing parathyroid hormone-related peptide  
- Secretion of cytokines stimulating bone resorption  
- Tumour-induced hypercalcaemia due to calcitriol production  
- Renal impairment exacerbating calcium retention

pathophysiology  
- Increased calcium release from bones  
- Elevated levels of parathyroid hormone-related peptide or calcitriol  
- Decreased renal excretion of calcium  
- Volume depletion affecting calcium homeostasis  
- Disruption of normal calcium metabolism

signs and symptoms  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Weakness and fatigue  
- Confusion or altered mental status  
- Polyuria and dehydration  
- Constipation

investigations  
- Serum calcium and albumin levels  
- Parathyroid hormone and parathyroid hormone-related peptide levels  
- Renal function tests  
- Serum phosphate and magnesium levels  
- Urinary calcium excretion

diagnosis  
- Persistent hypercalcaemia greater than 2.6 mmol/L  
- Exclusion of other causes of hypercalcaemia  
- Correlation with malignancy type  
- Assessment of renal function and hydration status  
- Clinical history and physical examination

management  
- Hydration with intravenous fluids  
   - Saline infusion to correct dehydration  
   - Monitor electrolyte balance  
- Bisphosphonates for long-term management  
   - Zoledronic acid as first-line agent  
   - Consider pamidronate if renal impairment present  
- Calcitonin for rapid calcium reduction  
   - Administer in conjunction with bisphosphonates  
   - Monitor response and adjust treatment  
- Corticosteroids for specific malignancies  
   - Particularly effective in lymphoma  
   - Dose adjusted based on clinical response  
- Dialysis in severe cases  
   - Reserved for acute renal failure  
   - Manage severe symptomatic hypercalcaemia







Hyperlipidemia

background  
- Hyperlipidemia is an abnormal elevation of lipids in the blood, increasing cardiovascular disease risk

risk factors  
- Obesity and overweight  
- Physical inactivity  
- Unhealthy diet high in saturated fats and refined carbohydrates  
- Family history of hyperlipidemia  
- Conditions such as diabetes and hypothyroidism  

causes  
- Genetics (e.g. familial hypercholesterolaemia)  
- Dietary factors (e.g. high cholesterol intake)  
- Sedentary lifestyle  
- Metabolic disorders  
- Medications (e.g. steroids, thiazides)  

pathophysiology  
- Excess lipids accumulate in blood leading to plaque formation in arteries  
- Endothelial dysfunction and inflammation occur due to lipid deposits  
- Increased LDL and decreased HDL levels contribute to atherogenesis  
- Risk of ischaemic cardiovascular events increases  
- Chronic condition leading to potential complications like atherosclerosis  

signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic until complications develop  
- Xanthomas (fatty deposits on skin)  
- Corneal arcus (white ring around cornea)  
- Chest pain or angina in advanced cases  
- Symptoms of cardiovascular disease (e.g. myocardial infarction, stroke)  

investigations  
- Lipid profile blood test to measure cholesterol and triglycerides  
- Liver function tests to assess metabolic health  
- Thyroid function tests to rule out hypothyroidism  
- High-sensitivity C-reactive protein (hs-CRP) to assess inflammation risk  
- Electrocardiogram (ECG) if cardiovascular symptoms present  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis of hyperlipidemia based on elevated total cholesterol, LDL-C, or triglycerides  
- Confirm diagnosis through repeat lipid profile if initial levels are borderline  
- Assess for underlying causes via history and lab tests  
- Classify into primary and secondary types for better management  
- Risk assessment for cardiovascular disease using QRISK score  

management  
- Lifestyle modifications including diet and exercise  
  - Encourage a Mediterranean diet rich in unsaturated fats  
  - Recommend at least 150 minutes of moderate aerobic exercise weekly  
- Pharmacotherapy for high-risk patients or those not reaching targets  
  - Statins as first-line treatment for lowering LDL-C  
  - Consider additional drugs (e.g. ezetimibe, PCSK9 inhibitors) if necessary  
- Regular follow-up every 3-12 months to monitor lipid levels  
  - Adjust therapy based on lipid profile and side effects  
  - Reinforce adherence to lifestyle changes  
- Education on cardiovascular risk factors and lipid management  
  - Provide resources for patient support and information  
  - Encourage screening for family members if hereditary condition suspected  
- Manage comorbidities like hypertension and diabetes simultaneously  
  - Ensure blood pressure and glucose levels are well controlled  
  - Coordinate care with primary healthcare team for holistic management  







Hyperosmolar hyperglycaemic state

Background  
- A serious acute complication of diabetes characterised by extreme hyperglycaemia and dehydration

Risk factors  
- Type 2 diabetes mellitus  
- Older age  
- Insulin deficiency or resistance  
- Infection or illness  
- Poor adherence to diabetes management

Causes  
- Uncontrolled diabetes  
- New diagnosis of diabetes  
- Illness or infection triggering stress response  
- Medications that raise blood sugar  
- Inadequate fluid intake

Pathophysiology  
- Insulin deficiency leads to increased blood glucose  
- Glucose spills into urine causing osmotic diuresis  
- Severe dehydration occurs from fluid loss  
- Electrolyte imbalances develop  
- High plasma osmolality results in cellular dehydration

Signs and symptoms  
- Extreme thirst  
- Frequent urination  
- Dry mouth and skin  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Altered mental status or confusion

Investigations  
- Blood glucose levels significantly elevated  
- Serum osmolarity increased  
- Arterial blood gases showing no acidosis  
- Electrolyte imbalances, particularly hypernatremia  
- Urinalysis revealing glucosuria and ketonuria

Diagnosis  
- Hyperglycaemia > 30 mmol/L  
- Serum osmolality > 320 mOsm/kg  
- No significant ketoacidosis (pH > 7.3)  
- Clinical features of dehydration  
- Exclusion of other conditions  

Management  
- Initiate fluid replacement  
  - Isotonic saline until volume repletion  
  - Switch to hypotonic fluids if osmolality normalises  
- Insulin therapy to reduce blood glucose  
  - Start low-dose continuous intravenous insulin  
  - Monitor blood glucose regularly  
- Electrolyte management  
  - Assess and correct potassium levels  
  - Monitor sodium and magnesium as needed  
- Identify and treat underlying causes  
  - Investigate infection or other precipitating factors  
  - Modify medications that exacerbate hyperglycaemia  
- Education and follow-up  
  - Reinforce diabetes management strategies  
  - Arrange regular follow-up to prevent recurrence  







Hyperparathyroidism

Background  
- Hyperparathyroidism is a condition characterised by excessive secretion of parathyroid hormone, leading to increased calcium levels in the blood

Risk factors  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Age over 50  
- Female gender  
- History of neck radiation  
- Vitamin D deficiency  

Causes  
- Primary hyperparathyroidism due to adenoma or hyperplasia  
- Secondary hyperparathyroidism often due to chronic kidney disease  
- Tertiary hyperparathyroidism following long-standing secondary hyperparathyroidism  
- Parathyroid carcinoma (rare)  
- Familial syndromes such as multiple endocrine neoplasia  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased parathyroid hormone secretion causes increased renal tubular reabsorption of calcium  
- Stimulation of osteoclast activity leads to bone resorption  
- Increased intestinal absorption of calcium due to elevated vitamin D levels  
- Hypercalcaemia results in symptoms of calcium toxicity  
- Can lead to complications like osteoporosis and nephrolithiasis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Asymptomatic in many cases  
- Kidney stones  
- Bone pain and fractures  
- Abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting  
- Neuropsychiatric symptoms such as depression and confusion  

Investigations  
- Serum calcium elevated  
- Parathyroid hormone levels elevated  
- Serum phosphate may be low or normal  
- Vitamin D levels assessed  
- Urinary calcium excretion measured  

Diagnosis  
- Based on elevated serum calcium and parathyroid hormone  
- Exclude secondary causes  
- Imaging studies for parathyroid adenoma if needed  
- Family history and genetic testing for hereditary forms  
- Confirm diagnosis by correlating clinical symptoms with lab findings  

Management  
- Surgical excision for primary hyperparathyroidism with symptomatic disease  
  - Parathyroidectomy is the treatment of choice  
  - Pre-operative localisation studies may assist  
- Monitoring in asymptomatic primary hyperparathyroidism  
  - Regular follow-up and serum calcium checks  
  - Targeted lifestyle changes and hydration  
- Treatment of secondary hyperparathyroidism focuses on underlying cause  
  - Phosphate binders and vitamin D supplementation if needed  
  - Renal dialysis for advanced kidney disease  
- Consideration of bisphosphonates in severe cases  
  - Effective in reducing fracture risk  
  - Can help manage hypercalcaemia  
- Management of complications such as osteoporosis  
  - Lifestyle modification including diet and exercise  
  - Pharmacotherapy with calcium and vitamin D supplementation  







Hyperthermia and hypothermia

background  
- Hyperthermia and hypothermia are significant disturbances in body temperature regulation, leading to potentially life-threatening conditions.

risk factors for hyperthermia  
- High ambient temperatures  
- Vigorous physical activity  
- Certain medications (e.g., diuretics, anticholinergics)  
- Dehydration  
- Pre-existing health conditions (e.g., cardiovascular disease)

causes of hyperthermia  
- Heat exposure (heat stroke)  
- Malignant hyperthermia  
- Neuroleptic malignant syndrome  
- Fever (secondary to infection)  
- Drug-induced syndromes

pathophysiology of hyperthermia  
- Impaired thermoregulation leads to increased core temperature  
- Cellular damage due to protein denaturation  
- Compromised cardiovascular function and circulation  
- Increased metabolic demand and respiratory rate  
- Risk of multi-organ failure

signs and symptoms of hyperthermia  
- Elevated body temperature above 38.5°C  
- Altered mental status (confusion, delirium)  
- Sweating (in heat exhaustion) or dry skin (in heat stroke)  
- Rapid pulse and respiration  
- Nausea and vomiting

investigations for hyperthermia  
- Core temperature measurement  
- Blood tests (electrolytes, renal function, lactate)  
- Blood gas analysis  
- Complete blood count (to assess for infection)  
- Toxicology screen if drug-induced etiology is suspected

diagnosis of hyperthermia  
- Clinical history with emphasis on temperature regulation environment  
- Exclusion of other underlying processes (e.g., sepsis)  
- Assessment of clinical signs and symptoms  
- Consideration of risk factors present  
- Use of diagnostic imaging if necessary for complications

management of hyperthermia  
- Immediate removal from hot environment  
   - Use of cool fluids for hydration  
   - Application of cool packs to major vessels  
- Pharmacological treatment if indicated  
   - Diazepam for seizures  
   - Dantrolene for malignant hyperthermia

background  
- Hypothermia occurs when body temperature drops below the normal threshold, leading to serious complications if untreated.

risk factors for hypothermia  
- Cold weather exposure  
- Wet or windy conditions  
- Immersion in cold water  
- Alcohol or drug use  
- Older age or chronic illness

causes of hypothermia  
- Environmental exposure  
- Submersion in cold water  
- Hypoglycaemia or sepsis  
- Endocrine disorders (e.g., adrenal insufficiency)  
- Neurological conditions affecting thermoregulation

pathophysiology of hypothermia  
- Decreased body heat production due to shivering and metabolic decline  
- Impaired coagulation and increased risk of arrhythmias  
- Vasoconstriction leading to peripheral circulatory failure  
- Potential for organ dysfunction with prolonged exposure  
- Risk of frostbite in extreme cases

signs and symptoms of hypothermia  
- Core temperature below 35°C  
- Shivering (may cease in severe cases)  
- Slurred speech and confusion  
- Weak pulse and respiratory rate  
- Muscle rigidity and cold extremities

investigations for hypothermia  
- Core temperature measurement  
- Electrolyte levels to assess for abnormalities  
- Blood gases to evaluate acid-base status  
- ECG for cardiac conduction abnormalities  
- Imaging if trauma is suspected

diagnosis of hypothermia  
- Clinical evaluation of temperature and symptoms  
- Confirmation of environmental exposure risk factors  
- Exclusion of alternative diagnoses  
- Assessment of dehydration and electrolyte status  
- Utilisation of history to determine severity and risk

management of hypothermia  
- Gentle rewarming with blankets and clothing  
   - Administer warm, sweetened fluids if alert  
   - Avoid hot water immersion due to shock risk  
- Advanced treatments in severe cases  
   - Active rewarming using warmed IV fluids  
   - Consideration of extracorporeal rewarming methods if necessary







Hypoglycaemia

background  
- Hypoglycaemia is a condition characterised by abnormally low blood glucose levels, often associated with diabetes management.

risk factors  
- Insulin therapy in diabetes  
- Certain oral hypoglycaemic agents  
- Inadequate food intake  
- Alcohol consumption  
- Intense physical activity  

causes  
- Excessive insulin production  
- Insulin overdose  
- Reactive hypoglycaemia  
- Adrenal insufficiency  
- Alcohol-induced hypoglycaemia  

pathophysiology  
- Decreased blood glucose levels impair carbohydrate metabolism  
- Release of counter-regulatory hormones like glucagon and adrenaline  
- Activation of gluconeogenesis and glycogenolysis in the liver  
- Failure of these mechanisms in cases of prolonged hypoglycaemia  
- Symptoms arise due to insufficient glucose for brain metabolism  

signs and symptoms  
- Sweating and palpitations  
- Tremors  
- Confusion and irritability  
- Blurred vision  
- In severe cases, loss of consciousness or seizures  

investigations  
- Blood glucose measurement  
- Continuous glucose monitoring  
- Assessment of insulin and C-peptide levels  
- Evaluation for underlying adrenal or hormonal disorders  
- Review of medication history  

diagnosis  
- Documented blood glucose level < 4 mmol/L during symptomatic episodes  
- Exclusion of other causes for symptoms  
- Positive response to carbohydrate intake  
- Consideration of the Whipple triad in recurrent cases  
- If necessary, glucagon response test  

management  
- Immediate administration of fast-acting carbohydrates  
  - Glucose tablets or sugary drinks  
  - Intravenous dextrose in severe cases  
- Long-term management strategies  
  - Review and adjust diabetes medication  
  - Education on recognising early symptoms and self-management plans  







Hypoparathyroidism

background  
- Hypoparathyroidism is a rare endocrine disorder characterised by insufficient production of parathyroid hormone leading to hypocalcaemia  

risk factors  
- Previous neck surgery or radiation  
- Autoimmune conditions  
- Congenital hypoparathyroidism  
- Family history of thyroid or parathyroid disorders  
- DiGeorge syndrome  

causes  
- Surgical removal of parathyroid glands  
- Autoimmune destruction of parathyroid glands  
- Genetic mutations  
- Magnesium deficiency  
- Infiltrative diseases (e.g., hemochromatosis)  

pathophysiology  
- Decreased parathyroid hormone leads to reduced calcium levels  
- Impaired renal calcium reabsorption and intestinal calcium absorption  
- Increased phosphate levels due to decreased renal excretion  
- Impaired bone resorption of calcium  

signs and symptoms  
- Tetany or muscle cramps  
- Numbness and tingling in extremities  
- Seizures  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Mood changes or depression  

investigations  
- Serum calcium levels (low)  
- Serum phosphate levels (high)  
- Serum parathyroid hormone levels (low or inappropriately normal)  
- Magnesium levels (may be low)  
- 24-hour urine calcium excretion (may be low)  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Laboratory tests confirming hypocalcaemia and low parathyroid hormone  
- Exclude other causes of hypocalcaemia  
- Genetic testing if congenital hypoparathyroidism is suspected  
- Imaging if structural abnormalities are suspected  

management  
- Calcium supplementation  
  - Oral calcium salts or intravenous calcium in severe cases  
  - Monitor serum calcium regularly  
- Vitamin D supplementation  
  - Active forms like calcitriol for better absorption  
  - Adjust dosage based on calcium levels  
- Magnesium supplementation if deficient  
- Educational support for patients regarding hypocalcaemia symptoms  
- Regular follow-up to monitor calcium and parathyroid hormone levels  







Hyposplenism/splenectomy

background  
- Hyposplenism refers to reduced splenic function, often due to splenectomy or functional failure.

risk factors  
- History of splenectomy  
- Haematological disorders  
- Autoimmune diseases  
- Infection-related conditions  
- Certain genetic syndromes  

causes  
- Surgical removal of the spleen  
- Trauma leading to splenic damage  
- Congenital asplenia or hyposplenism  
- Infiltrative disorders (e.g. lymphoma)  
- Infection (e.g. HIV, malaria)  

pathophysiology  
- Reduced clearance of encapsulated bacteria from circulation  
- Impaired antibody production against polysaccharide antigens  
- Increased risk of infections and sepsis  
- Altered immune response  
- Increased risk of thrombosis in the absence of splenic filtering  

signs and symptoms  
- Recurrent infections, particularly with encapsulated organisms  
- Fever  
- Fatigue  
- Splenomegaly (if functional splenic tissue remains)  
- Thrombosis-related symptoms  

investigations  
- Full blood count (FBC)  
- Blood film for abnormalities  
- Vaccination history assessment  
- Blood cultures if infection is suspected  
- Immunoglobulin levels  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of asplenia or hyposplenism  
- Identification of recurrent infections or associated risk factors  
- Laboratory findings suggestive of hyposplenism  
- Response to vaccination tests  
- Exclusion of other causes of immunodeficiency  

management  
- Vaccination against encapsulated organisms (pneumococcal, meningococcal, Hib)  
  - Administer prior to splenectomy where possible  
  - Revaccinate every 5 years for pneumococcal vaccine  
- Prophylactic antibiotics in children and some adults  
  - Penicillin V for children under 5  
  - Consider for high-risk adults  
- Education on signs of infection and need for prompt medical attention  
  - Advise on maintaining a supply of emergency antibiotics  
  - Encourage wearing medical alert identification  
- Consider screening for other complications like thrombosis  
  - Regular monitoring in patients with history of venous thromboembolism  
  - Referral to a haematologist for high-risk individuals  
- Individualised follow-up plan based on underlying conditions  
  - Regular assessments for infection susceptibility  
  - Care coordination with primary and specialist care providers  







Hypothyroidism

Background  
- A common endocrine disorder characterised by insufficient thyroid hormone production

Risk factors  
- Female sex  
- Age over 60 years  
- Family history of thyroid disease  
- Previous thyroid surgery or radiation  
- Autoimmune conditions such as type 1 diabetes

Causes  
- Hashimoto's thyroiditis  
- Iodine deficiency  
- Thyroidectomy or radioactive iodine treatment  
- Congenital hypothyroidism  
- Medications such as lithium or amiodarone

Pathophysiology  
- Decreased thyroid hormone leads to reduced metabolic rate  
- Impaired feedback mechanism results in elevated TSH levels  
- Accumulation of glycosaminoglycans causing myxoedema  
- Affects multiple organ systems causing systemic manifestations  
- Influence on lipid metabolism leading to dyslipidaemia

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and lethargy  
- Weight gain and reduced appetite  
- Cold intolerance and dry skin  
- Hair loss and hoarseness  
- Depression and cognitive impairment

Investigations  
- Serum TSH measurement  
- Serum free T4 level  
- Anti-thyroid peroxidase (anti-TPO) antibodies  
- Ultrasound of thyroid if structural abnormalities suspected  
- Radioactive iodine uptake if hyperthyroidism is considered

Diagnosis  
- Elevated TSH and low free T4 confirm diagnosis  
- Exclude secondary causes of hypothyroidism  
- Clinical and biochemical features consistent with primary hypothyroidism  
- Rule out other relevant conditions  
- Monitor for pituitary disease if TSH is normal or low

Management  
- Levothyroxine as first-line treatment  
  - Adjust dosage based on TSH levels  
  - Regular monitoring every 6-12 months  
- Patient education on medication adherence  
  - Advise on timing of levothyroxine intake  
  - Discuss potential side effects and when to seek help  
- Address lifestyle modifications  
  - Nutritional counselling for a balanced diet  
  - Encourage regular physical activity  
- Treatment of associated symptoms  
  - Management of dyslipidaemia if present  
  - Psychological support for mood disorders  
- Continuous follow-up and adjustment of therapy  
  - Regular TSH monitoring after dosage changes  
  - Assess for complications associated with hypothyroidism  







Idiopathic arthritis

Background  
- Idiopathic arthritis encompasses a group of inflammatory joint diseases with no identifiable cause 

Risk factors  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  
- Female gender  
- Age at onset typically between 1 and 10 years  
- Presence of other autoimmune conditions  
- Environmental triggers such as infections  

Causes  
- Unknown etiology; believed to involve genetic and environmental factors  
- Possible autoimmune mechanisms  
- Potential role of infections in triggering inflammation  
- Dysregulation of immune response  
- Genetic mutations linked to increased susceptibility  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of synovial membranes leading to joint damage  
- Proliferation of synoviocytes and increased production of inflammatory mediators  
- Cartilage destruction and bone erosion due to chronic inflammation  
- Formation of pannus which invades and erodes adjacent structures  
- Potential systemic effects including extra-articular manifestations  

Signs and symptoms  
- Persistent joint pain and swelling  
- Morning stiffness lasting over one hour  
- Reduced range of motion in affected joints  
- Presence of joint deformities over time  
- Extra-articular manifestations such as fever, rash, and fatigue  

Investigations  
- Complete blood count and inflammatory markers (ESR, CRP)  
- Rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP antibodies screening  
- Imaging studies (X-rays, ultrasound, MRI) to assess joint damage  
- Joint aspiration for synovial fluid analysis  
- Genetic testing for susceptibility markers in specific cases  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria and classification of idiopathic arthritis  
- Exclusion of other conditions causing arthritis  
- Consideration of joint count, duration of symptoms, and systemic features  
- Assessment of inflammatory markers and radiological findings  
- Use of juvenile idiopathic arthritis criteria for paediatric patients  

Management  
- Disease-modifying anti-rheumatic drugs (DMARDs)  
  - Methotrexate as first-line treatment  
  - Consider biological agents for refractory cases  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
  - Use for symptom relief and inflammation control  
  - Monitor for gastrointestinal side effects  
- Corticosteroids  
  - Short-term use for acute exacerbations  
  - Tapering strategy to minimise side effects  
- Physical therapy and exercise  
  - Tailored exercise programmes to maintain joint function  
  - Occupational therapy for daily living activities  
- Regular monitoring and follow-up  
  - Assess disease activity and treatment efficacy  
  - Adjust management based on clinical response and development of complications  







Impetigo

background  
- Impetigo is a highly contagious bacterial skin infection predominantly affecting children.  

risk factors  
- Age (most common in children aged 2-6 years)  
- Close contact in schools or childcare settings  
- Warm weather and high humidity  
- Skin injuries (cuts, insect bites, or dermatitis)  
- Poor hygiene  

causes  
- Most commonly caused by *Staphylococcus aureus*  
- Also can be caused by *Streptococcus pyogenes*  
- Secondary infection following other skin conditions  
- Impaired skin barrier function  
- Direct transmission from person to person  

pathophysiology  
- Bacteria enter through breaches in the skin   
- Local inflammatory response causes vesicle and crust formation  
- Toxins produced by bacteria can cause tissue damage  
- Spread can occur through autoinoculation or contact with contaminated surfaces  
- Can progress to more extensive infections if untreated  

signs and symptoms  
- Honey-coloured crusted lesions typically on the face and extremities  
- Vesicles that may rupture and ooze  
- Itching or discomfort in the affected area  
- Redness and swelling surrounding lesions  
- Possible systemic symptoms like fever in extensive cases  

investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on appearance of lesions  
- Swabs from lesions for culture and sensitivity if atypical or severe  
- Consider blood tests if systemic symptoms present  
- Dermatological referral in resistant or complex cases  
- Consider differential diagnoses such as eczema or herpes simplex  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of characteristic skin lesions  
- Rule out differential diagnoses based on symptoms  
- Consider invading organisms if high-risk or atypical  
- Confirmatory tests rarely needed in classical cases  
- Monitor for complications or non-response  

management  
- Topical antibiotics (e.g. fusidic acid or mupirocin) for localised infections  
   - Apply twice daily for 5-7 days  
   - Ensure good skin hygiene and care  
- Oral antibiotics (e.g. flucloxacillin or phenoxymethylpenicillin) for extensive or recurrent cases  
   - Course typically lasts 7 days  
   - Assess for allergies and resistance  
- Advise on hygiene measures to prevent spread  
   - Wash hands frequently  
   - Avoid close contact until lesions are healed  
- Educate caregivers about recognition of complications  
   - Seek medical advice for worsening symptoms  
   - Understand when to seek further medical help  
- Follow-up if lesions do not improve after initial treatment  
   - Reassess for potential treatment failure or alternative diagnoses  
   - Ensure adequate follow-up for recurrent cases







Infectious colitis

background  
- Infectious colitis is inflammation of the colon caused by infectious agents, leading to diarrhoea and abdominal discomfort  

risk factors  
- Recent travel to endemic areas  
- Consumption of contaminated food or water  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Immunocompromised status  
- Use of antibiotics  

causes  
- Bacterial infections (e.g., *Salmonella*, *Shigella*, *Campylobacter*, *Escherichia coli*)  
- Viral infections (e.g., Norovirus, Rotavirus)  
- Parasitic infections (e.g., *Entamoeba histolytica*)  
- Clostridium difficile infection, often related to antibiotic use  
- Chemical irritants or non-infectious causes  

pathophysiology  
- Pathogens invade the colonic mucosa, resulting in inflammation  
- Production of toxins can damage epithelial cells  
- Dysregulation of gut motility and secretion occurs  
- Inflammatory mediators contribute to symptoms and tissue damage  
- Severe cases may lead to ulceration and colonic perforation  

signs and symptoms  
- Watery or bloody diarrhoea  
- Abdominal pain and cramping  
- Fever and chills  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Tenesmus (feeling of incomplete bowel evacuation)  

investigations  
- Stool sample analysis for pathogens  
- Blood tests for signs of infection and inflammation  
- Flexible sigmoidoscopy if indicated  
- Imaging studies (e.g., abdominal X-ray, CT scan) for complications  
- PCR testing for specific viral and bacterial pathogens  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings  
- Positive stool culture or PCR for infectious agents  
- Evidence of inflammation from blood tests (e.g., elevated CRP, WBC)  
- Exclusion of non-infectious causes  
- Assessment of severity based on clinical features  

management  
- Rehydration and electrolyte replacement   
  - Oral rehydration solutions for mild cases  
  - Intravenous fluids for severe dehydration  
- Antibiotics for bacterial infections   
  - Based on organism and severity  
  - Caution in cases of *C. difficile* to avoid complications  
- Antimotility agents only if non-invasive pathogens  
  - Not recommended for dysentery or severe colitis  
  - Use with discretion for symptom relief  
- Nutritional support if needed   
  - Maintain fluid and electrolyte balance  
  - Gradual reintroduction of normal diet  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Observe for signs of severe disease  
  - Refer to specialist if required  







Infectious diarrhoea

Background  
- Infectious diarrhoea is a common condition caused by pathogens leading to inflammation of the gastrointestinal tract

Risk factors  
- Young age, particularly in children  
- Recent travel to endemic areas  
- Consumption of contaminated food or water  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Compromised immune system  

Causes  
- Bacterial infections (e.g., *Salmonella*, *Campylobacter*)  
- Viral infections (e.g., Norovirus, Rotavirus)  
- Parasitic infections (e.g., *Giardia lamblia*, *Entamoeba histolytica*)  
- Antibiotic-associated diarrhoea (e.g., *Clostridium difficile*)  
- Foodborne illnesses  

Pathophysiology  
- Invasion and damage of intestinal mucosa by pathogens  
- Increased secretion of electrolytes and water into the bowel  
- Malabsorption of nutrients leading to dehydration  
- Activation of inflammatory response causing symptoms  

Signs and symptoms  
- Watery or bloody diarrhoea  
- Abdominal cramps and pain  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Fever and malaise  
- Dehydration (thirst, dry mouth, decreased urine output)  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Stool sample analysis for pathogens  
- Blood tests to assess hydration status and infection parameters  
- Abdominal imaging if complications are suspected  
- Serology in specific cases (e.g., *E. histolytica*)  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical features and stool test results  
- Consideration of travel history and exposure  
- Assess for dehydration and severity of illness  
- Identify specific pathogens if necessary  
- Exclude other causes of diarrhoea  

Management  
- Rehydration therapy (oral rehydration solutions or IV fluids)  
   - Administer ORS for mild to moderate dehydration  
   - Provide IV fluids for severe dehydration or inability to tolerate oral intake  
- Symptomatic treatment (antidiarrhoeals, where appropriate)  
   - Avoid in cases of bacterial infection to prevent complications  
   - Use cautiously in mild non-bacterial infections  
- Antibiotics for specific bacterial causes (e.g., *Salmonella*, *Shigella*)  
   - Follow NICE recommendations based on pathogen and clinical judgement  
   - Consider resistance patterns before prescribing  
- Antiparasitic treatment for protozoan infections (e.g., metronidazole for *Giardia*)  
   - Confirm diagnosis before treatment initiation  
   - Monitor for treatment response  
- Education on hygiene and food safety to prevent recurrence  
   - Advise handwashing practices  
   - Promote safe food-handling methods  







Infectious mononucleosis

Background  
- Infectious mononucleosis is a viral infection, commonly caused by Epstein-Barr virus (EBV), leading to lymphadenopathy, fatigue, and fever

Risk factors  
- Adolescents and young adults, especially 15-24 years old  
- Close personal contact, such as kissing  
- Immunocompromised individuals  
- Presence of other viral infections  
- Lack of previous exposure to EBV  

Causes  
- Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) is the primary causative agent  
- Cytomegalovirus (CMV) occasionally causes similar symptoms  
- Human herpesvirus type 6 (HHV-6) can also be implicated  
- Toxoplasma gondii infections in rare cases  
- Other respiratory viruses may contribute  

Pathophysiology  
- EBV infects B-lymphocytes, leading to their activation and proliferation  
- Causes atypical T-lymphocyte response, leading to lymphadenopathy  
- Induces a polyclonal response of the immune system  
- Results in the production of heterophile antibodies  
- Immune-mediated symptoms due to cytokine release and lymphocyte infiltration  

Signs and symptoms  
- Severe fatigue and malaise  
- Sore throat and lymphadenopathy  
- Fever and headache  
- Splenomegaly and occasionally hepatomegaly  
- Rash in some cases  

Investigations  
- Complete blood count showing lymphocytosis with atypical lymphocytes  
- Heterophile antibody test (Monospot test)  
- Specific EBV serology (VCA IgM, VCA IgG, EBNA)  
- Liver function tests may show mild transaminitis  
- Ultrasound may assess splenomegaly  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on symptoms and examination findings  
- Positive heterophile antibody test supporting the diagnosis  
- Serology confirming EBV infection if heterophile test is negative  
- Exclusion of other causes for symptoms  
- Follow-up serology if atypical features are present  

Management  
- Symptomatic relief with analgesics and antipyretics  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen for pain and fever  
  - Ensure adequate hydration  
- Corticosteroids for severe cases with significant airway obstruction  
  - Short course of prednisolone for extensive lymphadenopathy  
  - Monitor for potential complications  
- Activity modification to avoid splenic rupture  
  - Rest during acute phase of illness  
  - Avoid contact sports until fully recovered  
- Education on the self-limiting nature of the condition  
  - Importance of monitoring for complications  
  - Awareness of the potential for fatigue lasting weeks to months  
- Follow-up only if symptoms worsen or persist beyond expected timeframe  
  - Consider specialist referral for persistent symptoms  
  - Regular reassessment for splenic enlargement or jaundice  







Infective endocarditis

background  
- Infective endocarditis is an infection of the heart valves or endocardial surface

risk factors  
- Pre-existing heart conditions  
- Prosthetic heart valves  
- Intravenous drug use  
- Dental procedures  
- Immunosuppression

causes  
- *Staphylococcus aureus*  
- *Streptococcus viridans*  
- *Enterococcus* species  
- Fungal infections  
- HACEK organisms

pathophysiology  
- Bacteria adhere to damaged endocardial surfaces  
- Formation of vegetations containing bacteria and clotting factors  
- Resultant inflammatory response and valve destruction  
- Potential for embolisation to distant organs  
- Triggering of immune complex formation leading to further damage

signs and symptoms  
- Fever and chills  
- Night sweats  
- New or changing heart murmur  
- Petechiae and splinter haemorrhages  
- Symptoms of embolic events (e.g. stroke)

investigations  
- Blood cultures to identify causative organism  
- Echocardiography to visualise vegetations  
- Complete blood count for leukocytosis or anaemia  
- Urinalysis for haematuria or proteinuria  
- Imaging studies for embolic complications

diagnosis  
- Positive blood cultures from two or more sites  
- Echocardiographic evidence of vegetation or abscess  
- New murmur or evidence of embolic phenomena  
- Meets modified Duke criteria  
- Clinical signs aligned with typical infection presentation

management  
- Initiate intravenous antibiotics based on organism sensitivity  
  - Consider empirical therapy initially  
  - Tailor antibiotics after culture results  
- Surgical intervention for valve replacement or repair  
  - Indicated if there is heart failure or persistent infection  
  - Perform in presence of large vegetations or abscess  
- Prophylaxis for high-risk individuals prior to procedures  
- Regular monitoring for complications during treatment  
- Multidisciplinary team involvement for comprehensive care  







Infective keratitis

background  
- Infective keratitis is a severe inflammation of the cornea due to infection, potentially leading to vision loss  

risk factors  
- Contact lens wear  
- Previous ocular surgery or trauma  
- Ocular surface disease  
- Immune suppression  
- Diabetes mellitus  

causes  
- Bacterial infections, e.g., *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*, *Staphylococcus aureus*  
- Viral infections, e.g., herpes simplex virus  
- Fungal infections, e.g., *Candida*, *Aspergillus*  
- Acanthamoeba infection  
- Non-infectious keratitis exacerbated by infectious agents  

pathophysiology  
- Microbial invasion of corneal epithelium  
- Inflammatory response leading to neutrophil infiltration  
- Disruption of corneal integrity  
- Formation of a corneal ulcer  
- Potential scarring and visual impairment  

signs and symptoms  
- Pain and redness of the eye  
- Photophobia and tearing  
- Blurred vision or loss of vision  
- Corneal opacity or ulceration  
- Eye discharge  

investigations  
- Slit lamp examination  
- Corneal scraping for microbiological cultures  
- Fluorescein staining to assess corneal defects  
- Intraocular pressure measurement  
- Imaging studies when indicated  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings  
- Positive cultures from corneal scrapings  
- Identification of infectious agents using PCR or specific stains  
- Assessment of corneal involvement and extent of disease  
- Exclusion of other causes of keratitis  

management  
- Initiate broad-spectrum topical antibiotics  
  - Adjust based on culture results  
  - Consider antifungals or antivirals if indicated  
- Provide symptomatic relief with topical lubricants  
  - Atropine drops to relieve pain and reduce ciliary spasm  
  - Analgesics for pain management  
- Possible surgical intervention for corneal ulcers or perforations  
  - Therapeutic keratoplasty in advanced cases  
  - Amniotic membrane grafting for severe surface disease  
- Educate on proper contact lens hygiene  
  - Advise against overnight wear  
  - Promote regular lens cleaning  
- Follow-up to monitor treatment response  
  - Assess for complications such as scarring or persistent epithelial defect  
  - Regular slit lamp examinations to evaluate healing







Inflammatory bowel disease

Background  
- Inflammatory bowel disease encompasses chronic conditions like Crohn's disease and ulcerative colitis, causing inflammation of the gastrointestinal tract  

Risk factors  
- Family history of inflammatory bowel disease  
- Smoking, especially with Crohn's disease  
- Prior gastrointestinal infections  
- Young adulthood or teenage onset  
- High socio-economic status  

Causes  
- Exact cause unknown, involves immune response to environmental triggers  
- Genetic predisposition implicated  
- Dysbiosis of intestinal microbiota  
- Mucosal barrier dysfunction  
- Autoimmune mechanisms  

Pathophysiology  
- Chronic inflammation leads to eroded mucosa and ulcers  
- Altered immune signalling results in ongoing tissue damage  
- Fibrosis and strictures in Crohn's disease  
- Continuous inflammation in ulcerative colitis  
- Potential for dysplasia and neoplasia  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain and cramping  
- Diarrhoea, often bloody in ulcerative colitis  
- Weight loss and malnutrition  
- Fatigue and fever  
- Extraintestinal manifestations such as arthritis or skin rashes  

Investigations  
- Blood tests for inflammatory markers and anaemia  
- Stool tests to rule out infections and assess inflammation  
- Colonoscopy with biopsy for definitive diagnosis  
- Imaging studies like MRI or CT for complications  
- Endoscopy may be used in specific cases  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation of symptoms and history  
- Confirm diagnosis through endoscopy and biopsy findings  
- Exclude infectious causes of gastrointestinal symptoms  
- Use of scoring systems to assess disease activity  
- Referral to a specialist if necessary  

Management  
- Medical therapy including aminosalicylates, corticosteroids, and immunomodulators  
  - Consider biological therapies for refractory cases  
  - Monitor for side effects and efficacy regularly  
- Surgical intervention for complications or refractory symptoms  
  - Resection for Crohn's disease with strictures  
  - Colectomy for severe ulcerative colitis cases  







Influenza

background  
- Influenza is a highly contagious viral respiratory infection caused by influenza viruses 

risk factors  
- Young children and elderly populations  
- Chronic respiratory or cardiovascular diseases  
- Immunocompromised individuals  
- Pregnant women  
- Obesity  

causes  
- Influenza A and B viruses as main causes  
- Influenza C virus linked to mild respiratory illness  
- Antigenic drift and shift contributing to recurring epidemics  
- Transmission via respiratory droplets and fomites  
- Close contact in crowded settings facilitating spread  

pathophysiology  
- Virus infects respiratory epithelial cells  
- Host immune response causes inflammation and cell damage  
- Release of cytokines and chemokines leading to systemic symptoms  
- Secondary bacterial infections may occur  
- Symptoms typically result from both viral invasion and immune response  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of fever and chills  
- Cough, sore throat, and nasal congestion  
- Myalgia and fatigue  
- Headache and muscle ache  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms (more common in children)  

investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- Rapid influenza diagnostic tests (RIDTs) if needed  
- RT-PCR for more accurate viral identification  
- Serological tests for specific influenza viruses  
- Chest X-ray if pneumonia is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Positive RIDT or RT-PCR confirms influenza  
- Clinical findings consistent with influenza  
- Exclusion of other respiratory infections  
- Assessment of seasonal outbreak patterns  
- Consideration of exposure history  

management  
- Supportive care including hydration and rest  
  - Antipyretics for fever and body aches  
  - Cough suppressants if needed  
- Antiviral medications for high-risk patients  
  - Oseltamivir or zanamivir within 48 hours of symptom onset  
  - Consider duration and dosing as per NICE guidelines  
- Vaccination for prevention in at-risk groups  
  - Annual seasonal flu vaccine as recommended  
  - Encourage vaccination in healthcare workers  
- Education on hygiene practices to reduce spread  
  - Handwashing and respiratory hygiene  
  - Stay home if symptomatic to prevent transmission  
- Monitor for complications requiring further intervention  
  - Seek medical attention for worsening symptoms  
  - Consider hospital admission for severe cases or at-risk populations  







Intestinal ischaemia

Background  
- Intestinal ischaemia is a condition caused by reduced blood flow to the intestines, leading to tissue damage 

Risk factors  
- Age over 65 years  
- Atherosclerosis or cardiovascular diseases  
- History of thromboembolic events  
- Smoking  
- Hypercoagulable states  

Causes  
- Mesenteric artery occlusion (thrombus or embolus)  
- Mesenteric venous occlusion  
- Non-occlusive ischaemia due to low perfusion pressure  
- Vasculitis  
- Intestinal volvulus or hernias  

Pathophysiology  
- Reduced blood supply leads to hypoxia and subsequent cell injury  
- Release of inflammatory mediators causing further damage  
- Progression to necrosis if ischaemia persists  
- Potential for perforation and peritonitis  
- Systemic effects due to sepsis and multi-organ failure  

Signs and symptoms  
- Severe abdominal pain out of proportion to examination findings  
- Diarrhoea or bloody stools  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Abdominal distension  
- Fever and signs of sepsis in advanced cases  

Investigations  
- Abdominal X-ray to detect bowel obstruction or perforation  
- CT angiography to visualise mesenteric vessels  
- MRI or Doppler ultrasound for blood flow assessment  
- Serum lactate levels to assess tissue hypoxia  
- Endoscopy for direct visualisation of intestinal mucosa  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation considering risk factors and presentation  
- Imaging studies confirming vascular occlusion or bowel ischaemia  
- Laboratory tests indicating metabolic acidosis or elevated lactate  
- Assessment of bowel viability if surgery is planned  
- Distinguishing between acute and chronic ischaemia  

Management  
- Immediate resuscitation with fluids and electrolyte correction  
  - Administer IV fluids and monitor vital signs  
  - Assess and manage electrolyte imbalances  
- Surgical intervention for reversible causes  
  - Embolectomy or thrombectomy for occlusions  
  - Resection of non-viable bowel  
- Anticoagulation therapy in specific scenarios  
  - Consider post-surgery for thromboembolic prevention  
  - Monitor coagulation profile regularly  
- Supportive care including pain management  
  - Use of analgesics and monitoring for sepsis  
  - Nutritional support as intestines recover  
- Follow-up assessment to monitor for complications  
  - Schedule regular check-ups after discharge  
  - Screen for recurrence of symptoms or ischaemia  







Intestinal obstruction and ileus

Background  
- Intestinal obstruction and ileus are critical conditions where there is a blockage in the intestines, affecting digestive function  

Risk factors  
- Previous abdominal surgery  
- Inflammatory bowel disease  
- Tumours or malignancies  
- Hernias or adhesions  
- Electrolyte imbalances  

Causes  
- Mechanical obstruction (e.g., tumours, hernias)  
- Functional obstruction (e.g., paralytic ileus, post-surgical)  
- Intussusception  
- Volvulus  
- Strictures  

Pathophysiology  
- Distension of the intestine due to fluid, gas, and contents accumulation  
- Bowel ischaemia and perforation risk increases  
- Electrolyte and fluid imbalance occurs  
- Inflammatory response may be triggered  
- Bacterial translocation may lead to sepsis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain and distension  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Constipation or lack of flatus  
- Bowel sounds may be hyperactive or absent  
- Dehydration  

Investigations  
- Abdominal X-ray to identify air-fluid levels  
- Ultrasound for assessing bowel activity  
- CT abdomen for detailed imaging  
- Blood tests (CBC, electrolytes)  
- Abdominal MRI if further imaging is needed  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of signs and symptoms  
- Confirmation through imaging studies  
- Differential diagnosis from other causes of abdominal pain  
- Assess for bowel function (strangulation or perforation)  
- Continuous monitoring for complications  

Management  
- Conservative (initially NPO, IV fluids)  
  - Monitor vital signs  
  - Provide electrolyte replacement  
- Surgical intervention if mechanical obstruction is confirmed  
  - Laparotomy for resection or decompression  
  - Address underlying causes (tumours, adhesions)  
- Treat underlying causes (medications for inflammatory bowel disease)  
  - Monitor for complications  
  - Long-term management as needed  
- Supportive care (pain management, nutritional support)  
  - Gradual reintroduction of diet  
  - Psychological support if needed  
- Preventative measures post-surgery (mobility exercises)  
  - Education on signs of recurrence  
  - Regular follow-up care  







Intussusception

background  
- Intussusception is a medical condition where a part of the intestine telescopes into an adjacent segment, causing obstruction.

risk factors  
- Age under 2 years  
- Recent viral infections  
- Cystic fibrosis  
- Presence of anatomical abnormalities  
- Intestinal polyps or tumours  

causes  
- Idiopathic in most childhood cases  
- Viral infections leading to lymphoid hyperplasia  
- Meckel's diverticulum  
- Intestinal tumours in older children  
- Post-surgical adhesions in adults  

pathophysiology  
- Invagination of a segment of bowel leads to obstruction  
- Compromised blood supply can cause ischaemia and necrosis  
- Increased pressure within the bowel results in potential perforation  
- Lymphoid hyperplasia in response to infection may precipitate intussusception  
- Inflammatory mediators contribute to bowel edema  

signs and symptoms  
- Severe abdominal pain that comes and goes  
- Abdominal distension  
- Vomiting, often bilious  
- "Currant jelly" stools indicating blood and mucus  
- Abdominal tenderness and a palpable mass  

investigations  
- Abdominal ultrasound to confirm diagnosis  
- Abdominal X-rays to identify obstruction  
- Contrast enema for therapeutic and diagnostic purposes  
- CT scan for detailed imagery (in older children/adults)  
- Blood tests for signs of infection or dehydration  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Confirmation with ultrasound as first-line investigation  
- Consideration of additional imaging if symptoms persist  
- Assessment of bowel status to rule out perforation  
- Diagnosis may be guided by clinical scoring systems  

management  
- Immediate resuscitation with IV fluids and electrolyte correction  
  - Monitor for signs of dehydration  
  - Administer analgesia for pain management  
- Non-operative reduction via air contrast or saline enema  
  - Successful reduction may negate need for surgery  
  - Surgical intervention for failed reduction or complications  
- Surgical resection if ischaemia, necrosis, or other lesions are identified  
  - Consideration of bowel viability  
  - Careful management of bowel continuity  
- Post-operative care for bowel function and complications  
  - Monitor for return of bowel function  
  - Manage potential infection or abscess formation  
- Follow-up with paediatric gastroenterology if recurrent cases  
  - Assess for underlying conditions  
  - Educate parents on recognising signs of recurrence  







Iritis

Background  
Iritis is inflammation of the iris and is a form of anterior uveitis 

Risk factors  
Previous episodes of uveitis  
Autoimmune disorders such as rheumatoid arthritis  
Infectious diseases like tuberculosis and syphilis  
Trauma to the eye  
Certain medications (e.g. bisphosphonates)  

Causes  
Idiopathic in many cases  
Infections such as herpes simplex or cytomegalovirus  
Systemic inflammatory conditions like Behçet's disease  
Trauma or injury to the eye  
Toxic exposure  

Pathophysiology  
Inflammation of iris leads to immune response  
Increased permeability of blood vessels in uvea  
Mediators such as cytokines attract inflammatory cells  
Accumulation of inflammatory cells in the anterior chamber  
Leads to pain and visual impairment  

Signs and symptoms  
Redness of the eye  
Pain and photophobia  
Decreased vision  
Pupil may be irregular or constricted  
Possible floaters in the field of vision  

Investigations  
Slit-lamp examination to assess iris and anterior chamber  
Intraocular pressure measurement  
Fundoscopy to look for complications like synechiae  
Blood tests for systemic causes (e.g. autoimmune markers)  
Ultrasonography if retinal detachment is suspected  

Diagnosis  
Clinical findings based on symptoms and exam  
Exclusion of secondary causes  
Consideration of patient's medical history  
Referral to ophthalmology if necessary  
Documenting the presence of iris inflammation  

Management  
Topical corticosteroids to reduce inflammation  
  Prescribed based on severity and response  
  Patient education on administration  
Cycloplegic agents to relieve pain and photophobia  
  Use of medications like tropicamide or cyclopentolate  
  Monitoring for response to treatment  
Address underlying causes if identified  
Referral to specialists for systemic disease management  
Regular follow-up to monitor for complications  







Irritable bowel syndrome

Background   
- Irritable bowel syndrome is a common functional gastrointestinal disorder affecting the large intestine

Risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Age between 20 and 40 years  
- Family history of IBS  
- Mental health disorders such as anxiety or depression  
- History of gastrointestinal infections  

Causes  
- Exact cause is unknown  
- Abnormal gut motility  
- Gut-brain axis dysfunction  
- Low-grade inflammation in the gut  
- Altered gut microbiome  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased visceral sensitivity leads to pain perception  
- Dysregulation of gut motility results in altered bowel habits  
- Serotonin dysfunction affects gut function and sensation  
- Immune activation may play a role in symptom exacerbation  
- Changes in gut microbiota could influence digestive processes  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain relieved by defecation  
- Bloating and distension  
- Altered bowel habits (diarrhoea and/or constipation)  
- Mucus in stools  
- Symptoms vary over time and may be triggered by stress or diet  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Exclusion of organic causes via blood tests  
- Stool tests for infection and inflammatory markers  
- Colonoscopy if alarm features are present  
- Hydrogen breath tests for lactose or fructose intolerance  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on symptom criteria (Rome IV)  
- Consider exclusion of other gastrointestinal disorders  
- May require negative testing for alarm symptoms  
- Evaluate response to initial dietary and lifestyle changes  
- Follow NICE guidelines for assessment 

Management  
- Dietary modifications (e.g. low FODMAP diet)  
  - Individualised dietary advice  
  - Consider referral to a dietitian  
- Pharmacological treatments  
  - Antispasmodics for symptom relief  
  - Laxatives or anti-diarrhoeal agents for bowel regulation  
- Psychological therapies  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy for anxiety management  
  - Relaxation techniques to reduce stress  
- Probiotics for symptomatic relief  
  - Specific strains may benefit IBS symptoms  
  - Monitor response to treatment  
- Regular review of treatment efficacy and symptom progression  
  - Adjust management plan as needed  
  - Encourage patient education and self-management strategies  







Ischaemic heart disease

Background  
- Ischaemic heart disease is a condition characterised by reduced blood flow to the heart muscle, leading to angina and myocardial infarction  

Risk factors  
- Smoking  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Family history of cardiovascular disease  

Causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Coronary artery spasm  
- Thrombosis  
- Embolism  
- Vasculitis  

Pathophysiology  
- Plaque formation leads to narrowing of coronary arteries  
- Decreased oxygen delivery to myocardial tissue during increased demand  
- Potential for plaque rupture causing acute thrombus formation  
- Myocardial ischaemia leads to cellular damage and tissue necrosis  
- Angina occurs due to transient ischaemia under exertion or stress  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain or discomfort (angina)  
- Shortness of breath  
- Fatigue with exertion  
- Palpitations  
- Nausea or sweating  

Investigations  
- Electrocardiogram (ECG)  
- Exercise treadmill test or stress testing  
- Coronary angiography  
- Echocardiography  
- Blood tests (e.g., troponins, lipid profile)  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and symptom evaluation  
- Confirmation through ECG changes during angina episodes  
- Use of non-invasive stress tests to assess for ischaemia  
- Coronary angiography in high-risk cases  
- Consider a combination of clinical, ECG, and imaging findings  

Management  
- Lifestyle modification  
  - Smoking cessation  
  - Dietary changes and exercise  
- Pharmacological therapy  
  - Antiplatelet agents (aspirin, clopidogrel)  
  - Beta-blockers and statins  
- Revascularisation procedures  
  - Percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI)  
  - Coronary artery bypass grafting (CABG)  
- Management of comorbidities  
  - Control of hypertension and diabetes  
  - Regular monitoring of lipid levels  
- Patient education  
  - Importance of adherence to medications  
  - Recognition of symptoms and when to seek help  







Kawasaki disease

background  
- Kawasaki disease is a vasculitis affecting children and can lead to coronary artery abnormalities

risk factors  
- Children under 5 years of age  
- Male gender  
- Asian or Pacific Islander ethnicity  
- Family history of Kawasaki disease  
- Seasonality, with increased incidence in winter and spring  

causes  
- Exact cause unknown  
- Possible infectious triggers, such as viruses or bacteria  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Immune system response abnormalities  
- Environmental factors  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of medium-sized blood vessels  
- Disruption of endothelial cells and smooth muscle  
- Vasculitis primarily affecting the coronary arteries  
- Formation of aneurysms in advanced cases  
- Immune-mediated damage causing systemic effects  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever lasting more than 5 days  
- Rash, often polymorphic  
- Conjunctivitis without discharge  
- Changes in lips and oral cavity, such as red and fissured lips  
- Cervical lymphadenopathy  

investigations  
- Blood tests showing elevated inflammatory markers  
- Echocardiography to assess coronary artery involvement  
- Chest X-ray to evaluate for cardiac complications  
- Urinalysis for proteinuria or sterile pyuria  
- Electrocardiogram may show arrhythmias  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria of fever and at least 4 of 5 principal features  
- Consider alternative diagnoses to rule out mimicking conditions  
- Use of laboratory tests to support diagnosis  
- Assess echocardiography findings for coronary artery changes  
- Diagnosis should be made promptly to initiate management  

management  
- Initiate high-dose intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG)  
  - Administer within the first 10 days of illness  
  - Aim for reduction of coronary artery damage  
- Administer high-dose aspirin  
  - Used initially for anti-inflammatory effect  
  - Transition to low-dose aspirin for antiplatelet effect after fever resolution  
- Monitor for cardiac complications  
  - Regular echocardiograms to detect aneurysms  
  - Referral to cardiology if significant abnormalities found  
- Offer supportive care  
  - Manage symptoms such as fever and irritability  
  - Ensure fluid balance and nutritional needs  
- Educate and involve parents in care plan  
  - Provide information on signs of potential complications  
  - Encourage regular follow-up appointments for monitoring







Leukaemia

Background  
- Leukaemia is a group of cancers that affect the blood and bone marrow, leading to the overproduction of abnormal blood cells  

Risk factors  
- Family history of leukaemia  
- Exposure to radiation  
- Certain genetic disorders (e.g., Down syndrome)  
- Previous chemotherapy or cancer treatment  
- Exposure to certain chemicals (e.g., benzene)  

Causes  
- Genetic mutations in blood cell precursors  
- Environmental factors contributing to genetic changes  
- Viral infections (e.g., human T-cell leukaemia virus)  
- Chronic exposure to carcinogens  
- Age-related changes in DNA  

Pathophysiology  
- Abnormal proliferation of immature white blood cells  
- Disruption in normal blood cell production in bone marrow  
- The crowding out of healthy cells, leading to anaemia and infection risk  
- Accumulation of malignant cells in peripheral blood and organs  
- Impaired immune response and potential for organ infiltration  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Frequent infections  
- Easy bruising or bleeding  
- Bone pain  
- Weight loss or loss of appetite  

Investigations  
- Complete blood count with differential  
- Bone marrow biopsy  
- Flow cytometry for immunophenotyping  
- Imaging studies (e.g., CT scan)  
- Cytogenetic analysis for chromosomal abnormalities  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation of symptoms and blood tests  
- Confirmatory diagnostics through bone marrow examination  
- Classification of leukaemia type (acute vs chronic)  
- Identification of specific subtypes (e.g., Acute Myeloid Leukaemia)  
- Assessment of cytogenetic abnormalities  

Management  
- Chemotherapy regimens specific to leukaemia type  
  - Combination of different drugs  
  - Supportive care to manage side effects  
- Targeted therapy for specific genetic mutations  
  - Tyrosine kinase inhibitors for Chronic Myeloid Leukaemia  
  - Immunotherapy for some acute leukaemias  
- Stem cell transplant for eligible patients  
  - Allogeneic or autologous depending on circumstances  
  - Risk assessment for complications  
- Supportive care and management of complications  
  - Transfusions for anaemia and thrombocytopenia  
  - Antibiotics for infections  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring for recurrence  
  - Blood tests and periodic imaging as indicated  
  - Psychosocial support for patients and families  







Liver failure

Background  
- Liver failure is a critical condition in which the liver loses its ability to function adequately

Risk factors  
- Chronic liver disease such as cirrhosis  
- Alcohol abuse  
- Viral hepatitis infections  
- Autoimmune liver diseases  
- Certain medications and toxins  

Causes  
- Hepatitis A, B, C and E virus infections  
- Alcoholic liver disease  
- Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease  
- Drug-induced liver injury  
- Metabolic disorders  

Pathophysiology  
- Impaired hepatic function leads to a buildup of toxins  
- Disruption of metabolic processes affecting glucose, protein, and fat metabolism  
- Failure of synthetic function impacting coagulation factors  
- Portal hypertension resulting from hepatic scarring  
- Increased risk of infection due to immune dysregulation  

Signs and symptoms  
- Jaundice  
- Abdominal swelling (ascites)  
- Easy bruising and bleeding  
- Confusion (hepatic encephalopathy)  
- Fatigue and weakness  

Investigations  
- Liver function tests to assess enzyme levels  
- Coagulation profile to evaluate clotting status  
- Ultrasound or CT scan for liver imaging  
- Viral serologies for hepatitis infection  
- Liver biopsy in select cases for histological diagnosis  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and history  
- Laboratory tests confirming liver dysfunction  
- Imaging studies to rule out obstructions  
- Evaluation of coagulation status  
- Consideration of differential diagnoses  

Management  
- Supportive care and monitoring in hospital  
  - Monitor vital signs and liver function  
  - Assess for signs of complications  
- Specific treatment based on underlying cause  
  - Antiviral therapy for hepatitis infections  
  - Avoidance of alcohol and hepatotoxic substances  
- Manage complications  
  - Diuretics for ascites  
  - Lactulose and rifaximin for hepatic encephalopathy  
- Consider referrals for specialist care  
  - Liver transplant evaluation for eligible patients  
  - Palliative care options for advanced disease  
- Patient and family education on liver health  
  - Nutritional support and lifestyle modifications  
  - Regular follow-up to monitor liver function  







Lower limb fractures

background  
- Lower limb fractures are common injuries often resulting from trauma, impacting mobility and requiring timely management

risk factors  
- High-impact activities such as sports or motor vehicle accidents  
- Older age, leading to increased fragility fractures  
- Osteoporosis or other conditions leading to decreased bone density  
- History of previous fractures  
- Poor balance or coordination

causes  
- Falls from a height  
- Direct blow to the limb  
- Twisting injuries during activities  
- Pathological fractures from underlying disease  
- Stress fractures from repetitive loading

pathophysiology  
- Bone is subjected to force exceeding its strength leading to disruption  
- Fractures can be classified as open or closed  
- Healing involves inflammatory, reparative, and remodelling phases  
- Complications can arise such as non-union or malunion  
- Vascular and nerve injuries may occur with severe fractures  

signs and symptoms  
- Localised pain and tenderness  
- Swelling and bruising in the affected area  
- Obvious deformity or abnormal positioning of the limb  
- Inability to bear weight or move the limb  
- Crepitus or grinding sensation at the injury site  

investigations  
- X-ray imaging to assess fracture type and location  
- CT scan for complex fractures or joint involvement  
- MRI for suspected occult fractures or soft tissue injuries  
- Ultrasound in paediatric cases or for soft tissue evaluation  
- Bone density scans if osteoporosis is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of pain, swelling, and function  
- Confirmation via imaging studies such as X-rays  
- Classification of fracture based on location and type  
- Assessment of neurovascular status of the limb  
- Consideration of patient history and mechanism of injury  

management  
- Immediate first aid: immobilisation and elevation  
- Pain management with analgesics  
  - Consider NSAIDs for mild to moderate pain  
  - Opioids for severe pain or initial management  
- Definitive treatment options: surgical or conservative  
  - Surgery for displaced fractures or those involving joints  
  - Conservative management for non-displaced fractures with splinting  
- Rehabilitation and physiotherapy for function restoration  
  - Gradual mobilisation to prevent stiffness  
  - Strengthening exercises post-healing  
- Follow-up for monitoring healing and complications  
  - Regular X-rays to assess bone healing  
  - Assess for functional recovery and rehabilitation needs  







Lower limb soft tissue injury

background  
- Lower limb soft tissue injuries are common in sports and can involve muscles, tendons, ligaments or fascia

risk factors  
- Participation in high-impact sports  
- Poor conditioning or training  
- Previous injuries  
- Inadequate warm-up or stretching  
- Misuse of equipment or footwear  

causes  
- Acute trauma or falls  
- Overuse or repetitive strain  
- Poor biomechanics or posture  
- Direct impact  
- Inadequate rehabilitation from previous injuries  

pathophysiology  
- Tissue damage leads to inflammation and pain  
- Disruption of blood supply can affect healing  
- Swelling results from fluid accumulation  
- Muscle fibres may rupture or tear  
- Ligament or tendon strains alter joint stability  

signs and symptoms  
- Localised pain or tenderness  
- Swelling and bruising  
- Reduced range of motion  
- Muscle weakness or spasm  
- Instability in affected joints  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Plain X-rays to rule out fractures  
- Ultrasound for soft tissue evaluation  
- MRI for complex injuries or to assess severity  
- Functional tests to evaluate mobility  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and physical examination  
- Use of imaging studies for confirmation if needed  
- Classification based on type and severity of injury  
- Assessment of functional impact on movement  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other conditions  

management  
- Rest and activity modification  
  - Advise avoidance of aggravating activities  
  - Use of crutches if necessary  
- Ice application and elevation  
  - Reduce swelling and pain  
  - Apply ice for 15-20 minutes several times daily  
- Compression bandaging  
  - Use elasticated bandages to limit swelling  
  - Ensure bandage is not too tight  
- Physiotherapy referral  
  - Rehabilitation programmes to restore function  
  - Focus on strengthening and flexibility exercises  
- Pain management  
  - Paracetamol or NSAIDs for pain relief  
  - Monitor for side effects and adjust as necessary







Lower respiratory tract infection

Background  
- Lower respiratory tract infections include infections of the trachea, bronchi, and lungs, commonly presenting as pneumonia or bronchitis

Risk factors  
- Age (very young or elderly)  
- Smoking history  
- Chronic respiratory diseases (e.g. COPD, asthma)  
- Immunocompromised states (e.g. diabetes, HIV)  
- Recent respiratory infections  

Causes  
- Bacterial pathogens (e.g. *Streptococcus pneumoniae*, *Haemophilus influenzae*)  
- Viral agents (e.g. influenza virus, respiratory syncytial virus)  
- Fungal organisms (e.g. *Histoplasma*, *Coccidioides*)  
- Aspiration of food or liquids  
- Environmental exposures (e.g. air pollution, occupational hazards)  

Pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to inflammation of the airways and alveoli  
- Increased mucus production and bronchoconstriction occurs  
- Impaired gas exchange results in hypoxia  
- Alveolar consolidation may develop  
- Inflammatory mediators contribute to systemic symptoms  

Signs and symptoms  
- Cough (productive or non-productive)  
- Fever and chills  
- Dyspnoea and chest pain  
- Sputum production (colour can vary)  
- General malaise and fatigue  

Investigations  
- Chest X-ray to assess for pneumonia or other complications  
- Sputum culture and sensitivity for bacterial identification  
- Blood cultures if severe infection is suspected  
- Complete blood count to check for leucocytosis  
- Pulse oximetry to assess oxygen saturation  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and history  
- Chest X-ray findings consistent with pneumonia   
- Confirmatory laboratory tests (sputum or blood cultures)  
- Consideration of age and risk factors in diagnosis  
- Adherence to NICE guidelines for diagnostic approach  

Management  
- Antibiotic therapy for bacterial infections  
  - Choice depends on severity and bacterial etiology  
  - Consider local guidelines or resistance patterns  
- Supportive measures (hydration, rest)  
  - Analgesia for fever and discomfort  
  - Bronchodilators if wheezing or asthma exacerbation  
- Hospital admission for severe cases  
  - Monitoring and intravenous antibiotics if needed  
  - Consider respiratory support if oxygen levels are low  
- Education on smoking cessation and vaccination  
  - Influenza and pneumococcal vaccines recommended  
  - Encourage adherence to treatment plans  
- Follow-up to monitor recovery and prevent recurrence  
  - Assess for resolution of symptoms  
  - Consider pulmonary rehabilitation if applicable  







Lung cancer

Background  
- Lung cancer is a leading cause of cancer-related morbidity and mortality globally.

Risk factors  
- Tobacco smoking  
- Exposure to secondhand smoke  
- Occupational exposure to carcinogens (e.g., asbestos, radon)  
- Family history of lung cancer  
- Previous lung disease (e.g., COPD, tuberculosis)  

Causes  
- Genetic mutations (e.g., EGFR, KRAS)  
- Environmental factors (e.g., air pollution)  
- Infections (e.g., chronic inflammation due to infections)  
- Ionising radiation  
- Smoking-related carcinogens  

Pathophysiology  
- Affects pulmonary epithelium leading to uncontrolled cell growth  
- May develop as non-small cell lung cancer or small cell lung cancer  
- Local invasion and metastasis to lymph nodes and distant organs  
- Associated with inflammatory responses in the lung tissue  
- Tumour microenvironment influences disease progression  

Signs and symptoms  
- Persistent cough  
- Unexplained weight loss  
- Chest pain  
- Haemoptysis  
- Dyspnoea  

Investigations  
- Chest X-ray  
- CT scan of the chest  
- Histological examination via biopsy  
- PET scan for staging  
- Bronchoscopy for visualisation and sampling  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed through histological examination of tissue  
- Staging using TNM classification  
- Assessment of performance status  
- Consideration of molecular testing for targeted therapies  
- Evaluation of metastatic spread  

Management  
- Surgical resection for early-stage disease  
  - Lobectomy, pneumonectomy, or wedge resection  
  - Adjuvant chemotherapy or radiotherapy if indicated  
- Systemic therapy for advanced disease  
  - Chemotherapy with platinum-based regimens  
  - Targeted therapy based on genetic mutations  
- Radiotherapy for local control or palliation  
  - Stereotactic body radiotherapy for specific cases  
  - Palliative radiotherapy for symptomatic relief  
- Supportive care  
  - Smoking cessation programmes  
  - Symptom management and palliative care approaches  
- Clinical trials  
  - Consider participation in ongoing research studies  
  - Access to novel therapies and treatments  







Lyme disease

Background  
- Lyme disease is a tick-borne infectious disease caused by *Borrelia* bacteria

Risk factors  
- Outdoor activities in wooded or grassy areas  
- Living or travelling in endemic regions  
- Direct exposure to ticks  
- Seasonality, primarily in late spring and summer  
- Weakened immune system

Causes  
- Infection with *Borrelia burgdorferi* in North America  
- Infection with *Borrelia afzelii* and *Borrelia garinii* in Europe  
- Tick bites, primarily from Ixodes ricinus or Ixodes scapularis  
- Transmission through tick saliva  
- Co-infection with other tick-borne pathogens

Pathophysiology  
- Immune response to *Borrelia* antigens  
- Inflammation causing tissue damage  
- Possible immune-mediated damage after initial infection  
- Dysregulation of inflammatory pathways  
- Chronic infection may lead to persistent symptoms

Signs and symptoms  
- Erythema migrans (target-like rash)  
- Flu-like symptoms including fever, headache, and fatigue  
- Joint pain and swelling (Lyme arthritis)  
- Neurological symptoms such as lymphocytic meningitis or Bell's palsy  
- Cardiac manifestations like Lyme carditis

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms  
- Serological testing for antibodies (ELISA)  
- Western blot confirmation for specific antibodies  
- Polymerase chain reaction (PCR) for detecting *Borrelia* DNA  
- Evaluation of joint fluid in cases of Lyme arthritis  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis with erythema migrans or relevant exposure  
- Positive serology with compatible symptoms  
- Negative results do not exclude Lyme disease  
- Consider differential diagnoses in atypical presentations  
- Early-stage diagnosed clinically, later stages require serology  

Management  
- Early stage: Doxycycline for 14-21 days  
  - Alternatives include amoxicillin or cefuroxime  
- Late stage: Doxycycline for 28 days for Lyme arthritis  
  - Referral for potentially complex cases like Lyme carditis or neurological manifestations  
- Discuss preventive measures against tick bites  
  - Education on tick removal and hygiene after outdoor activities  
- Monitor for post-Lyme disease symptoms  
  - Offer support for ongoing symptoms where necessary  
- Consider referral to specialists for persistent symptoms  
  - Involve rheumatology or infectious disease specialists as appropriate







Lymphoma

background  
- Lymphoma is a cancer of the lymphatic system affecting lymphocytes and lymph nodes  

risk factors  
- Age over 60  
- Family history of lymphoma  
- HIV or other immunodeficiencies  
- Exposure to certain chemicals (e.g., benzene)  
- Previous treatment for other cancers  

causes  
- Genetic mutations in lymphocytes  
- Viral infections (e.g., Epstein-Barr virus, HIV)  
- Autoimmune diseases (e.g., rheumatoid arthritis)  
- Environmental factors (e.g., pesticides)  
- Chronic inflammation or infection  

pathophysiology  
- Malignant transformation of lymphocytes  
- Proliferation of abnormal lymphocytes in lymphoid tissues  
- Disruption of normal lymphocyte function  
- Infiltration of organs and tissues  
- Formation of lymphoid tumours  

signs and symptoms  
- Painless lymphadenopathy  
- Fever and night sweats  
- Unexplained weight loss  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Pruritus or rash in some cases  

investigations  
- Complete blood count and differential  
- Imaging studies (CT, PET scan)  
- Lymph node biopsy for histopathology  
- Immunophenotyping and genetic testing  
- Bone marrow biopsy if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Histological confirmation through biopsy  
- Classification as Hodgkin or non-Hodgkin lymphoma  
- Assessment of stage and prognostic factors  
- Clinical evaluation of symptoms  
- Exclusion of other malignancies  

management  
- Chemotherapy for most types  
  - ABVD for Hodgkin lymphoma  
  - R-CHOP for non-Hodgkin lymphoma  
- Radiotherapy for localised disease  
  - Combined with chemotherapy in some cases  
  - Palliative radiotherapy for advanced stages  
- Stem cell transplant for refractory cases  
  - Autologous or allogeneic transplant considerations  
  - Patient selection based on age and health status  
- Targeted therapies and immunotherapy  
  - Consideration of CD20 monoclonal antibodies  
  - Checkpoint inhibitors for relapsed cases  
- Symptom management and supportive care  
  - Pain management and palliative services  
  - Regular follow-up for early detection of relapse  







Macular degeneration

Background  
- Macular degeneration is a leading cause of vision loss in older adults, affecting the central part of the retina

Risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Family history of macular degeneration  
- Smoking  
- Obesity  
- Cardiovascular disease  

Causes  
- Age-related changes to the retina  
- Accumulation of drusen (yellow deposits)  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Oxidative stress  
- Inflammation  

Pathophysiology  
- Degeneration of photoreceptors in the macula  
- Altered functioning of retinal pigment epithelium  
- Choroidal neovascularisation in wet macular degeneration  
- Loss of central vision due to retinal cell death  
- Accumulation of cellular waste products  

Signs and symptoms  
- Blurred or distorted central vision  
- Difficulty recognising faces  
- Dark or empty areas in central vision  
- Changes in colour perception  
- Straight lines appearing wavy  

Investigations  
- Amsler grid testing
