- Fundoscopy for retinal examination  
- Optical coherence tomography (OCT)  
- Fluorescein angiography  
- Visual acuity testing  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical findings and patient history  
- Confirmation via imaging techniques (e.g., OCT)  
- Distinction between dry and wet macular degeneration  
- Assessment of visual acuity  
- Documentation of drusen presence and size  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications (e.g., smoking cessation, diet)  
  - Encouragement of a diet rich in antioxidants  
  - Weight management advice  
- Pharmacological interventions for wet AMD  
  - Anti-VEGF injections (e.g., ranibizumab, aflibercept)  
  - Monitoring for treatment response  
- Referral to Low Vision Services  
  - Use of visual aids  
  - Rehabilitation services  
- Regular monitoring and follow-up appointments  
  - Assessment of progression  
  - Ensuring early intervention  
- Patient education and support  
  - Information on the condition  
  - Emotional support resources







Malabsorption

Background  
- Malabsorption is a condition where the intestine does not absorb nutrients properly.

Risk factors  
- Age (elderly population)  
- Chronic gastrointestinal diseases (e.g. Crohn's disease)  
- Surgical history (bariatric surgery)  
- Alcoholism  
- Medications (e.g. metformin, proton pump inhibitors)  

Causes  
- Celiac disease  
- Lactose intolerance  
- Pancreatic insufficiency  
- Bacterial overgrowth  
- Inflammatory bowel disease  

Pathophysiology  
- Damage to intestinal mucosa reduces surface area for absorption  
- Impaired secretion of digestive enzymes affects nutrient breakdown  
- Increased intestinal permeability allows toxins to enter circulation  
- Structural abnormalities obstruct nutrient flow  
- Altered gut microbiota disrupts nutrient availability  

Signs and symptoms  
- Diarrhoea  
- Weight loss  
- Bloating and flatulence  
- Fatigue  
- Nutritional deficiencies (e.g. anaemia, osteoporosis)  

Investigations  
- Stool analysis (for fat content and pathogens)  
- Blood tests (checking for deficiencies and inflammatory markers)  
- Endoscopy with biopsy (to assess mucosal damage)  
- Imaging studies (e.g. CT scan for anatomical abnormalities)  
- Breath tests (for lactose intolerance or bacterial overgrowth)  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and dietary history  
- Confirmatory blood tests and stool analysis  
- Endoscopic biopsy if celiac disease is suspected  
- Exclusion of other causes of gastrointestinal symptoms  
- Multidisciplinary team assessment if complications arise  

Management  
- Dietary modifications to avoid trigger foods  
   - Gluten-free diet for celiac disease  
   - Lactose-free diet for lactose intolerance  
- Supplementation of deficient nutrients  
   - Oral or intravenous supplements as necessary  
   - Regular monitoring of nutritional status  
- Enzyme replacement therapy for pancreatic insufficiency  
   - Pancreatic enzymes with meals  
   - Adjust dosage based on symptom control  
- Treatment of underlying conditions  
   - Medications for Crohn's or IBS  
   - Surgery if significant structural issues present  
- Patient education and support  
   - Involvement of dietitians for tailored plans  
   - Support groups for chronic condition management  







Malaria

background  
- Malaria is a life-threatening disease caused by protozoan parasites transmitted through the bite of infected female *Anopheles* mosquitoes 

risk factors  
- Living in or travelling to endemic areas  
- Not taking prophylactic antimalarial medication  
- Young age  
- Weakened immune system  
- Pregnant women  

causes  
- Infection with *Plasmodium* spp. (most commonly *P. falciparum*, *P. vivax*, *P. malariae*, *P. ovale*)  
- Transmission primarily through mosquito bites  
- Blood transfusion or sharing needles  
- Congenital transmission from mother to child  
- Inhalation of infected respiratory droplets in rare cases  

pathophysiology  
- Parasites invade red blood cells, multiply and cause haemolysis  
- Immune response leads to fever and other systemic symptoms  
- Severe cases can lead to organ failure, anaemia, and death  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever and chills  
- Headache  
- Muscle pain and fatigue  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Jaundice and splenomegaly in severe cases  

investigations  
- Blood smear (thick and thin) for parasite detection  
- Rapid diagnostic tests (RDTs) for *Plasmodium* antigen  
- Complete blood count for anaemia and leukopenia  
- Liver function tests to assess organ function  
- PCR for species identification in complicated cases  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed by microscopy or RDT for *Plasmodium* species  
- Clinical diagnosis supported by travel history and symptomatology  
- Severe malaria identified by clinical criteria and lab findings  
- Monitor for complications such as severe anaemia or respiratory distress  
- Differential diagnosis includes other febrile illnesses  

management  
- Initiate antimalarial treatment based on *P. falciparum* or other species  
  - Artemisinin-based combination therapies (ACTs) for uncomplicated cases  
  - Quinine-based regimens for severe malaria  
- Supportive care and management of complications  
  - Fluid resuscitation for hydration and electrolyte balance  
  - Blood transfusion for severe anaemia if indicated  
- Prophylactic antimalarial medication for travellers to endemic areas  
- Educate on mosquito bite prevention measures  
- Monitor for treatment response and side effects







Malignant melanoma

Background  
- Malignant melanoma is a type of skin cancer originating from melanocytes, with increasing incidence in recent years

Risk factors  
- Ultraviolet (UV) radiation exposure  
- Fair skin, hair, and eye colour  
- Family history of melanoma  
- Presence of multiple or atypical moles  
- Weakened immune system  

Causes  
- Genetic mutations in melanocytes  
- Environmental factors such as UV radiation  
- Dysregulation of cell growth due to oncogenes and tumour suppressor genes  
- Familial syndromes like familial atypical multiple mole melanoma (FAMMM)  
- Previous skin cancers or non-melanoma skin cancers  

Pathophysiology  
- Malignant transformation of melanocytes leads to uncontrolled proliferation  
- Invasion of surrounding tissues and potential metastasis  
- Alterations in cell signalling pathways that regulate growth and apoptosis  
- Inflammatory response associated with tumour growth  
- Tumour microenvironment influencing behaviour and spread of melanoma  

Signs and symptoms  
- New or changing moles or skin lesions  
- Irregular borders or colour variations in moles  
- Itchiness, ulceration, or bleeding of lesions  
- Symptoms of metastasis such as weight loss or lymphadenopathy  
- Secondary lesions in other organs, e.g., lungs, liver  

Investigations  
- Full skin examination  
- Dermatoscopy for in-depth visualisation of lesions  
- Biopsy of suspect lesions for histological analysis  
- Sentinel lymph node biopsy if indicated  
- Imaging studies such as CT or MRI for metastatic assessment  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation with consideration of family and personal history  
- Histological confirmation from biopsy  
- Assessment using the ABCDE criteria (Asymmetry, Border, Colour, Diameter, Evolving)  
- Staging based on tumour thickness and metastasis  
- Multidisciplinary team discussion  

Management  
- Surgical excision of primary melanoma  
  - Ensure clear margins of resection  
  - Consideration for local anaesthetic or general anaesthetic  
- Sentinel lymph node biopsy and possible lymphadenectomy  
  - If sentinel node is involved, consider further intervention  
  - Follow-up for lymphatic spread monitoring  
- Targeted therapy for advanced melanoma  
  - BRAF or MEK inhibitors for BRAF-mutant tumours  
  - Combination therapy may be necessary  
- Immunotherapy options  
  - Checkpoint inhibitors like ipilimumab or pembrolizumab  
  - Monitor for immune-related adverse events  
- Regular follow-up and surveillance  
  - Skin checks for new lesions or changes  
  - Patient education on self-examination and sun protection  







Malnutrition

background  
- Malnutrition is a condition resulting from inadequate intake or absorption of nutrients, affecting health and well-being  

risk factors  
- Poverty and low socioeconomic status  
- Chronic illness or disease  
- Elderly population  
- Limited access to or knowledge about nutritious food  
- Mental health issues such as depression or anorexia  

causes  
- Insufficient dietary intake of macronutrients and micronutrients  
- Malabsorption syndromes like coeliac disease or Crohn's disease  
- Increased nutritional requirements due to illness or infection  
- Medications that affect appetite or nutrient absorption  
- Eating disorders  

pathophysiology  
- Impaired energy metabolism leading to muscle wasting and fat loss  
- Dysfunction in immune response due to nutrient deficiencies  
- Hormonal imbalances affecting metabolism  
- Altered gastrointestinal function and absorption capacity  
- Impact on cellular and tissue integrity  

signs and symptoms  
- Weight loss and muscle wasting  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Delayed wound healing  
- Changes in hair and skin condition  
- Increased susceptibility to infections  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment including history and physical examination  
- Anthropometric measurements (BMI, waist circumference)  
- Blood tests (albumin, electrolytes, vitamin levels)  
- Dietary assessment (24-hour recall or food frequency questionnaire)  
- Imaging studies if indicated (e.g. x-rays for osteoporosis)  

diagnosis  
- Identify signs and symptoms consistent with malnutrition  
- Confirm with objective measurements (weight loss, BMI)  
- Evaluate dietary intake patterns and nutrient deficiencies  
- Consider presence of underlying illness or psychosocial factors  
- Use criteria from the Malnutrition Universal Screening Tool (MUST)  

management  
- Dietetic referral for personalised nutrition plan  
  - Focus on increasing calorie and nutrient intake  
  - Use oral nutritional supplements if necessary  
- Address underlying causes (e.g. treat digestive disorders)  
  - Manage coexisting health conditions  
  - Provide mental health support if applicable  
- Educate patients about nutrition and healthy eating  
  - Encourage local resources for food access  
  - Promote cooking skills and meal planning  
- Monitor progress and modify interventions as needed  
  - Regular follow-ups to reassess nutritional status  
  - Adjust diet plans based on changing needs  
- Collaborate with multidisciplinary teams for comprehensive care  
  - Involve GPs, nurses, social workers  
  - Include supports like community nutrition programmes  







Measles

Background  
- Measles is a highly infectious viral illness caused by the measles virus

Risk factors  
- Unvaccinated individuals  
- International travel  
- Crowded living conditions  
- Young age, particularly infants  
- Immunocompromised status  

Causes  
- Infection with the measles virus, an RNA virus of the Paramyxoviridae family  
- Transmission via respiratory droplets  
- Contagious from four days before to four days after the rash appears  
- Can survive on surfaces for up to 2 hours  
- Vaccination status significantly affects incidence  

Pathophysiology  
- Viral replication occurs in the nasopharynx and regional lymph nodes  
- Immune response leads to systemic spread  
- Immunosuppression due to lymphopenia and T-cell dysfunction  
- Rash results from delayed-type hypersensitivity reaction  
- Long-term complications can arise from immune suppression  

Signs and symptoms  
- Initial: high fever, cough, coryza, conjunctivitis  
- Koplik spots: small white lesions in the mouth  
- Rash: maculopapular, appears 3-5 days after onset of fever  
- Often accompanied by malaise and photophobia  
- Complications include diarrhoea, otitis media, and pneumonia  

Investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- Serology: measles IgM (indicates recent infection)  
- PCR: polymerase chain reaction can confirm diagnosis from throat swab or urine  
- Lymphopenia may be seen in full blood count  
- Chest X-ray if pneumonia is suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed case: clinical features + serology or PCR positive  
- Suspected case: clinical features in unvaccinated individuals or recent travel history  
- Differential diagnoses include rubella and roseola  
- Consider other viral exanthems in differential diagnosis  
- Monitor for potential complications  

Management  
- Supportive care: hydration, antipyretics for fever  
- Vitamin A supplementation for severe cases or high-risk populations  
  - Reduces morbidity and mortality  
- Isolation to prevent spread until four days after rash onset  
- Vaccination of close contacts; MMR vaccination for prevention  
  - Post-exposure prophylaxis recommended within 72 hours of exposure  
- Hospital management for complications such as pneumonia or encephalitis  







Ménière’s disease

Background  
- Ménière’s disease is a chronic inner ear disorder that affects balance and hearing.

Risk factors  
- Family history of Ménière's disease  
- Age between 20 and 50 years  
- Previous viral infections  
- Allergies  
- Head trauma  

Causes  
- Abnormal fluid accumulation in the inner ear  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Autoimmune processes  
- Viral infections affecting the inner ear  
- Structural abnormalities in the inner ear  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased endolymphatic fluid pressure in the cochlea and vestibular system  
- Distortion of the inner ear structures  
- Damage to hair cells in the cochlea and vestibular apparatus  
- Altered neurotransmitter release affecting auditory and vestibular pathways  
- Episodes of vertigo, tinnitus, and hearing loss  

Signs and symptoms  
- Episodic vertigo lasting minutes to hours  
- Fluctuating hearing loss  
- Tinnitus present as ringing or buzzing  
- A feeling of fullness or pressure in the ear  
- Progressive hearing loss over time  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Audiometry to assess hearing function  
- Electronystagmography (ENG) or videonystagmography (VNG) for balance function  
- MRI to rule out other causes  
- CT imaging if anatomical abnormalities are suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical criteria of episodic vertigo, fluctuating hearing loss, tinnitus, and aural fullness  
- Exclusion of other vestibular disorders  
- Audiometric confirmation of sensorineural hearing loss  
- Consideration of symptom patterns over time  
- Documentation of episode frequency and duration  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications including reduced salt intake and hydration  
  - Implement dietary changes to manage fluid retention  
  - Encourage regular exercise and stress management  
- Medications for acute attacks  
  - Use of antiemetics for nausea and vomiting  
  - Consider vestibular suppressants for vertigo relief  
- Intratympanic injections (e.g., corticosteroids) to reduce inflammation  
  - Can improve vestibular function  
  - May assist in hearing stabilization  
- Surgical options for refractory cases  
  - Endolymphatic sac decompression  
  - Vestibular nerve section  
- Referral to audiology for hearing rehabilitation  
  - Hearing aids for auditory compensation  
  - Assistive listening devices for improved communication







Meningitis

Background  
Meningitis is an inflammation of the protective membranes covering the brain and spinal cord, often due to infection  

Risk factors  
Young age (particularly infants and young children)  
Close living conditions (e.g. students in halls of residence)  
Immunosuppression (e.g. HIV, corticosteroids)  
Travel to endemic areas (e.g. meningococcal disease)  
Lack of vaccination (e.g. against Haemophilus influenzae type b, meningococcal disease)  

Causes  
Bacterial infections (e.g. *Neisseria meningitidis*, *Streptococcus pneumoniae*)  
Viral infections (e.g. enteroviruses, herpes simplex virus)  
Fungal infections (e.g. *Cryptococcus neoformans*)  
Parasitic infections (less common, e.g. *Acanthamoeba*)  
Non-infectious causes (e.g. autoimmune conditions, certain medications)  

Pathophysiology  
Infection leads to inflammation of the meninges  
Increased intracranial pressure caused by exudate and swelling  
Cerebral blood flow may be compromised  
Risk of neurological complications due to inflammation  
Potential for systemic infection and sepsis  

Signs and symptoms  
Fever  
Headache  
Nuchal rigidity (stiff neck)  
Altered mental status (confusion, drowsiness)  
Photophobia (sensitivity to light)  

Investigations  
Complete blood count (to check for infection)  
Blood cultures (to identify causative organism)  
Lumbar puncture (to analyse cerebrospinal fluid)  
CT or MRI brain (to rule out space-occupying lesions)  
PCR tests (to detect specific pathogens in CSF)  

Diagnosis  
Clinical assessment (history and physical examination)  
Lumbar puncture with analysis of CSF (to confirm diagnosis)  
Blood tests and cultures to identify pathogen  
Consider imaging if neurological signs present  
NICE guidelines recommend differentiating between viral and bacterial meningitis  

Management  
Immediate referral to hospital for suspected cases  
Empirical antibiotic therapy (based on age and risk factors)  
Supportive care (hydration, pain management)  
Vaccination for prevention (especially in high-risk groups)  
Consider adjunctive corticosteroids (for bacterial meningitis to reduce complications)  







Menopause

background  
- Transition marking the end of a woman's reproductive years, usually occurring between 45 and 55 years of age

risk factors  
- Age at onset of menopause  
- Family history of early menopause  
- Smoking  
- Certain medical conditions (e.g., autoimmune diseases)  
- Surgical removal of ovaries

causes  
- Decreased production of oestrogen and progesterone by ovaries  
- Natural aging process  
- Genetic factors  
- Autoimmune destruction of ovarian tissue  
- Chemotherapy or radiation therapy

pathophysiology  
- Follicular depletion leads to decreased hormone levels  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis  
- Increased follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH) and luteinising hormone (LH) levels  
- Resulting hormonal imbalance causing various systemic effects  
- Symptoms arise due to oestrogen deficiency affecting multiple organ systems

signs and symptoms  
- Irregular menstrual cycles leading to cessation  
- Hot flushes and night sweats  
- Sleep disturbances  
- Mood changes and irritability  
- Vaginal dryness and urogenital symptoms

investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Serum FSH and oestradiol levels  
- Thyroid function tests if indicated  
- Bone mineral density measurement for osteoporosis risk  
- Consider menopausal symptom questionnaires

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on clinical symptoms and menstrual history  
- Elevated FSH levels in the postmenopausal range  
- Exclusion of other conditions causing symptoms  
- Consideration of age-related factors  
- Diagnosis of premature menopause if occurs before age 40

management  
- Lifestyle advice on diet, exercise, and smoking cessation  
  - Weight management strategies  
  - Regular physical activity recommendations  
- Hormone replacement therapy (HRT) for symptom relief  
  - Individualise dosage and formulation  
  - Discuss risks and benefits with the patient  
- Non-hormonal options for hot flushes (e.g., SSRIs, SNRIs)  
  - Gabapentin as a consideration  
  - Clonidine for specific cases  
- Vaginal lubricants or oestrogens for vaginal dryness  
  - Topical oestrogen for local treatment  
  - Systemic methods for broader symptoms  
- Regular follow-up and re-evaluation of management plan  
  - Monitor for ongoing symptoms and side effects  
  - Update risk assessments for osteoporosis and cardiovascular health







Mesenteric adenitis

background  
- Mesenteric adenitis is the inflammation of lymph nodes in the mesentery, often mimicking appendicitis in children.

risk factors  
- Common in children and adolescents  
- Recent viral infections  
- Gastroenteritis episodes  
- Exposure to other individuals with gastrointestinal infections  
- Age between 5 and 15 years  

causes  
- Viral infections such as adenovirus or cytomegalovirus  
- Bacterial infections, frequently due to *Yersinia enterocolitica*  
- Inflammatory bowel disease  
- Trauma or abdominal surgery  
- Non-infectious causes such as malignancy  

pathophysiology  
- Activation of the immune system leads to lymphadenopathy  
- Infiltration of inflammatory cells into lymph nodes  
- Increase in lymphatic drainage and fluid accumulation  
- Possible secondary bacterial infection following primary viral infection  
- Mesenteric fat involvement and potential necrosis  

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain, often in the right lower quadrant  
- Tenderness upon palpation of the abdomen  
- Fever and malaise  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Diarrhoea may be present  

investigations  
- Blood tests including complete blood count and inflammatory markers  
- Ultrasound abdomen to assess lymph node enlargement  
- CT scan if appendicitis is suspected  
- Stool cultures in the presence of diarrhoea  
- Consideration of serological tests for specific infections  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on history and physical examination  
- Exclusion of other acute abdomen causes, especially appendicitis  
- Imaging studies confirming lymphadenopathy without evidence of appendicitis  
- Laboratory findings supporting infectious or inflammatory processes  
- Assessment of symptom duration and associated gastrointestinal symptoms  

management  
- Symptomatic relief with analgesia and antipyretics  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen as needed  
  - Maintain hydration  
- Observational management in mild cases  
  - Monitor for signs of complications  
  - Reassess if symptoms worsen or fail to improve  
- Antibiotics in case of confirmed bacterial cause  
  - Consider broad-spectrum antibiotics if severe  
  - Tailor treatment based on culture results  
- Referral for surgical evaluation if diagnosis is uncertain  
  - Surgical intervention for confirmed appendicitis  
  - Additional evaluation for atypical presentations  
- Education and reassurance for families  
  - Explain self-limiting nature of the condition  
  - Advise on warning signs for further evaluation  







Migraine

background  
- A common neurological condition characterised by recurrent, severe headaches often accompanied by other symptoms  

risk factors  
- Family history of migraines  
- Female gender  
- Age (more common in younger adults)  
- Certain lifestyle factors (stress, lack of sleep)  
- Hormonal changes (menstruation, pregnancy, menopause)  

causes  
- Exact cause is unclear  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental triggers (food, weather, stress)  
- Neurovascular imbalance (serotonin dysregulation)  
- Cortical spreading depression   

pathophysiology  
- Activation of the trigeminovascular system  
- Release of neuropeptides (CGRP, substance P)  
- Vasodilation of meningeal blood vessels  
- Activation of cortical and subcortical brain regions  
- Sensitisation of pain pathways  

signs and symptoms  
- Throbbing headache, usually unilateral  
- Can be accompanied by nausea and vomiting  
- Light and sound sensitivity (photophobia, phonophobia)  
- Aura (visual disturbances, sensory changes)  
- Duration typically ranges from 4 to 72 hours  

investigations  
- Clinical history and symptom description  
- Physical and neurological examination  
- MRI or CT if atypical features or other concerns  
- Blood tests if secondary causes suspected  
- Consider headache diaries for pattern recognition  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria from the International Classification of Headache Disorders  
- At least 5 attacks meeting criteria  
- Duration of headache 4-72 hours  
- At least two of the following: unilateral, pulsating, moderate-severe intensity, aggravated by routine physical activity  
- Nausea/vomiting or photophobia/phonophobia present  

management  
- Identify and avoid triggers  
  - Keep a headache diary  
  - Implement lifestyle modifications  
- Acute treatment options  
  - Analgesics (e.g. paracetamol, NSAIDs)  
  - Triptans (e.g. sumatriptan)  
- Preventive treatment for frequent migraines  
  - Beta-blockers (e.g. propranolol)  
  - Antidepressants (e.g. amitriptyline)  
- Consideration of non-pharmacological therapies  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy  
  - Acupuncture  
- Follow-up and reassessment of treatment efficacy  
  - Regular consultations to adjust management  
  - Monitor for side effects of medications  







Motor neurone disease

background  
- Motor neurone disease is a progressive neurodegenerative condition affecting motor neurons in the brain and spinal cord

risk factors  
- Age, typically 50-70 years old  
- Family history of motor neurone disease  
- Male gender  
- Exposure to environmental toxins  
- History of vigorous physical activity  

causes  
- Unknown aetiology in most cases  
- Genetic mutations in some familial cases (e.g., C9orf72, SOD1)  
- Oxidative stress and mitochondrial dysfunction  
- Impaired protein homeostasis  
- Autoimmune mechanisms potentially involved  

pathophysiology  
- Degeneration of upper and lower motor neurons  
- Progressive loss of muscle control and function  
- Formation of intracellular inclusions in motor neurons  
- Altered neurotransmitter release and excitotoxicity  
- Muscle atrophy and spasticity due to denervation  

signs and symptoms  
- Muscle weakness and wasting  
- Fasciculations  
- Spasticity and muscle cramps  
- Dysarthria and dysphagia  
- Respiratory difficulties  

investigations  
- Clinical examination for neurological signs  
- Electromyography (EMG) to assess muscle and nerve function  
- MRI of the brain and spinal cord to rule out other causes  
- Blood tests for genetic mutations and biomarkers  
- Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis, if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis primarily based on clinical criteria  
- Must demonstrate upper and lower motor neuron involvement  
- Exclusion of other diagnoses that mimic symptoms  
- Supportive criteria include specific symptoms and family history  
- Follow NICE guidelines for any required referrals or multidisciplinary reviews  

management  
- Multidisciplinary care to address various needs  
  - Neurological care for disease progression monitoring  
  - Physiotherapy and occupational therapy for support  
- Symptomatic treatment for muscle spasticity and pain  
  - Use of baclofen or tizanidine for spasticity  
  - Analgesics or gabapentinoids for neuropathic pain  
- Nutritional support and speech therapy for dysphagia  
  - Dietary modifications to prevent choking  
  - Use of swallowing aids and devices  
- Psychological support for mental health and emotional well-being  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy or counselling services  
  - Support groups for patients and families  
- Consideration of advanced care planning and palliative care  
  - Discuss goals of care with the patient and family  
  - Manage end-of-life decisions and preferences







Multi-organ dysfunction syndrome

Background  
- Multi-organ dysfunction syndrome is a life-threatening condition characterized by the failure of two or more organ systems.

Risk Factors  
- Sepsis  
- Trauma  
- Major surgery  
- Pancreatitis  
- Burns  

Causes  
- Infection  
- Ischaemia  
- Inflammation  
- Toxin exposure  
- Autoimmune diseases  

Pathophysiology  
- Systemic inflammatory response triggers activation of the coagulation cascade  
- Microcirculatory failure leads to inadequate organ perfusion  
- Disturbance in metabolic processes affects tissue oxygenation  
- Release of pro-inflammatory cytokines exacerbates organ damage  
- Organ system failure leads to further systemic complications  

Signs and Symptoms  
- Altered mental status  
- Respiratory distress  
- Oliguria or anuria  
- Hypotension  
- Jaundice  

Investigations  
- Blood tests (CBC, liver function tests, renal function tests)  
- Imaging studies (chest X-ray, ultrasound, CT scan)  
- Cultures (blood, urine, sputum)  
- Lactate levels  
- Electrolyte panels  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical criteria including the Sequential Organ Failure Assessment (SOFA) score  
- Identification of underlying cause  
- Evidence of organ dysfunction in at least two systems  
- Exclusion of other conditions that may mimic symptoms  
- Continuous monitoring of clinical status and organ function  

Management  
- Identify and treat underlying cause  
  - Initiate antibiotics if sepsis is suspected  
  - Address metabolic derangements and electrolyte imbalances  
- Supportive care for affected organs  
  - Oxygen therapy and mechanical ventilation if respiratory failure occurs  
  - Renal replacement therapy for acute kidney injury  
- Fluid resuscitation and vasopressors for hypotension  
  - Monitor fluid balance closely  
  - Titrate vasopressors based on blood pressure response  
- Nutritional support  
  - Early enteral nutrition if possible  
  - Monitor nutritional status and adjust as needed  
- Palliative care and end-of-life considerations  
  - Discuss prognosis and goals of care with patients and families  
  - Manage symptoms and ensure comfort







Multiple myeloma

Background  
- Multiple myeloma is a malignancy of plasma cells leading to various systemic complications  

Risk factors  
- Increasing age  
- Male gender  
- Family history of multiple myeloma  
- Exposure to certain chemicals or radiation  
- Obesity  

Causes  
- Exact aetiology is unknown  
- Genetic mutations in plasma cells  
- Abnormalities in the bone marrow microenvironment  
- Viral infections may contribute  
- Chronic inflammation   

Pathophysiology  
- Proliferation of malignant plasma cells in the bone marrow  
- Production of monoclonal immunoglobulin (M-protein)  
- Suppression of normal haematopoiesis  
- Osteolytic bone lesions due to osteoclast activation  
- Impact on renal function and immune system  

Signs and symptoms  
- Bone pain  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Recurrent infections  
- Hypercalcaemia (may present with nausea, confusion)  
- Renal impairment  

Investigations  
- Full blood count showing anaemia  
- Serum protein electrophoresis to detect M-protein  
- Bone marrow biopsy revealing abnormal plasma cells  
- Imaging studies (X-ray, MRI, CT) to assess bone lesions  
- Serum calcium and renal function tests  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosed using the presence of 10% or more plasma cells in bone marrow  
- Evidence of end-organ damage (CRAB criteria: hypercalcaemia, renal insufficiency, anaemia, bone lesions)  
- Exclusion of other plasma cell disorders  
- Confirmation via serum and urine immunofixation electrophoresis  
- Clinical evaluation alongside laboratory findings  

Management  
- Initiation of treatment based on disease stage and patient fitness  
   - Chemotherapy regimens (e.g., bortezomib, lenalidomide)  
   - Autologous stem cell transplant for eligible patients  
- Supportive care for symptoms and complications  
   - Bisphosphonates for bone health  
   - Management of renal impairment and infections  







Multiple sclerosis

Background  
- Multiple sclerosis is a chronic inflammatory demyelinating disease of the central nervous system

Risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Family history of multiple sclerosis  
- Vitamin D deficiency  
- Smoking  
- Epstein-Barr virus infection  

Causes  
- Autoimmune response targeting myelin  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental triggers  
- Viral infections  
- Immune system dysregulation  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammatory processes lead to demyelination  
- Formation of plaques in the white matter  
- Neurodegeneration and axonal loss  
- Interruption of normal nerve signal transmission  
- Results in diverse neurological deficits  

Signs and symptoms  
- Visual disturbances (e.g. blurred vision)  
- Sensory symptoms (e.g. numbness, tingling)  
- Motor symptoms (e.g. weakness, spasticity)  
- Fatigue  
- Cognitive impairment  

Investigations  
- Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) to identify lesions  
- Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis  
- Evoked potentials to assess nerve conduction  
- Clinical history and neurological examination  
- Blood tests to exclude other conditions  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical criteria based on symptoms and MRI findings  
- Evidence of dissemination in space and time  
- Exclusion of alternative diagnoses  
- Use of McDonald criteria for diagnosis  
- Collaborate with neurologist for confirmation  

Management  
- Disease-modifying therapies (e.g. interferons, monoclonal antibodies)  
  - To reduce relapse rates  
  - To slow disease progression  
- Symptomatic management (e.g. physiotherapy, medication for fatigue)  
  - To improve quality of life  
  - To address specific symptoms like spasticity or pain  
- Regular monitoring for disease progression  
  - Adjust treatment as needed  
  - Supportive care from multidisciplinary teams  
- Patient education and support  
  - Promote self-management strategies  
  - Access to support groups and resources  
- Consideration of lifestyle modifications  
  - Smoking cessation  
  - Regular exercise and healthy diet  







Mumps

Background  
- Mumps is a viral infection caused by the mumps virus, leading to swelling of the salivary glands.

Risk factors  
- Unvaccinated individuals  
- Close contact with infected persons  
- Presence in large communities or close living conditions  
- Travel to endemic regions  
- Immunocompromised states  

Causes  
- Infection with the mumps virus (Paramyxovirus)  
- Transmission via respiratory droplets  
- Direct contact with contaminated surfaces  
- Virus can be present in saliva and urine  
- Secondary spread in communities without vaccination  

Pathophysiology  
- Virus enters respiratory tract and replicates in local lymph nodes  
- Spreads to salivary glands and other tissues through the bloodstream  
- Causes inflammation and discomfort  
- Can lead to complications in some cases, such as orchitis or meningitis  
- Immune response contributes to pathology  

Signs and symptoms  
- Parotitis (swelling of the parotid glands)  
- Fever  
- Headache  
- Muscle pain  
- Fatigue and malaise  

Investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on swelling of parotid glands  
- Serological testing for mumps IgM antibodies  
- RT-PCR for mumps virus from saliva or oropharyngeal swab  
- Ultrasound can be used to assess gland swelling  
- Complete blood count may show leukopenia  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and history of exposure  
- Confirm with serology or PCR testing  
- Exclusion of other causes of parotitis  
- Assess vaccination history  
- Consider complications if symptoms worsen  

Management  
- Supportive care for symptom relief  
  - Analgesics for pain control  
  - Hydration to prevent dehydration  
- Isolation to prevent transmission  
  - Stay away from school or work until 5 days after onset  
  - Notify public health authorities if an outbreak is suspected  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Orchitis management if it occurs  
  - Hospitalisation for severe complications if needed  
- Vaccination with MMR for prevention  
  - Two doses recommended at 1 and 3 years of age  
  - Catch-up vaccination for unvaccinated individuals  
- Education on infection prevention strategies  
  - Importance of hand hygiene  
  - Avoiding contact with infected individuals  







Myasthenia gravis

background  
- Myasthenia gravis is an autoimmune disorder characterized by weakness and rapid fatigue of voluntary muscles

risk factors  
- Female gender, particularly ages 20-40  
- Male gender, particularly ages 60-80  
- Presence of thymic abnormalities, such as thymoma or thymic hyperplasia  
- Associated autoimmune diseases, such as rheumatoid arthritis or lupus  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  

causes  
- Autoantibodies targeting acetylcholine receptors at the neuromuscular junction  
- Autoantibodies against muscle-specific kinase (MuSK)  
- Thymic dysfunction leading to aberrant immune responses  
- Triggering infections or stress  
- Rarely, certain medications may exacerbate symptoms  

pathophysiology  
- Immune-mediated destruction of acetylcholine receptors  
- Decreased transmission of nerve impulses to muscles  
- Muscle receptor downregulation and complement-mediated damage  
- Fatigability of muscle results from impaired neuromuscular transmission  
- Thymic abnormalities play a role in autoantibody production  

signs and symptoms  
- Fluctuating muscle weakness, often worsens with activity  
- Ocular symptoms such as ptosis and diplopia  
- Bulbar symptoms including dysphagia and dysarthria  
- Generalised weakness, affecting limb and respiratory muscles  
- Exacerbations of weakness, sometimes following infection or stress  

investigations  
- Antibody testing for acetylcholine receptor and MuSK antibodies  
- Electromyography (EMG) showing decremental response on repetitive stimulation  
- Single-fibre EMG to detect increased jitter  
- CT or MRI of the chest to evaluate thymus gland  
- Serum analysis to check for associated autoimmune conditions  

diagnosis  
- Clinical examination reveals characteristic muscle weakness  
- Positive results for anti-acetylcholine receptor or MuSK antibodies  
- Confirmatory findings on EMG and imaging studies  
- Exclusion of other neurological disorders causing muscle weakness  
- Consideration of symptoms’ progression and fluctuating nature  

management  
- Acetylcholinesterase inhibitors such as pyridostigmine  
  - Improves neuromuscular transmission  
  - Symptomatic relief of weakness  
- Immunosuppressive therapies like corticosteroids or azathioprine  
  - Reduces autoimmune response  
  - Long-term management of disease  
- Thymectomy if thymoma is present or in generalised cases  
  - Can lead to remission or improvement  
  - Recommended in selected patients  
- Plasmapheresis or intravenous immunoglobulin for crisis management  
  - Rapidly reduces circulating antibodies  
  - Used for severe exacerbations  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring for disease progression or complications  
  - Adjust treatment regimen as necessary  
  - Educate patients on self-management strategies  







Myeloproliferative disorders

background  
- Myeloproliferative disorders are a group of conditions characterised by the overproduction of blood cells in the bone marrow 

risk factors  
- Age over 60 years  
- Family history of myeloproliferative disorders  
- Exposure to radiation  
- Certain chemical exposures (e.g., benzene)  
- Pre-existing blood disorders (e.g., polycythaemia vera)

causes  
- Mutations in JAK2, CALR, and MPL genes  
- Abnormalities in signalling pathways of haematopoietic stem cells  
- Chronic hypoxia leading to increased erythropoiesis  
- Autoimmune conditions contributing to abnormal cell growth  
- Secondary causes such as infections or inflammatory diseases

pathophysiology  
- Clonal proliferation of haematopoietic stem cells in the bone marrow  
- Increased production of one or more types of blood cells  
- Disruption of normal haematopoiesis due to mutations  
- Potential development into acute leukaemia in some cases  
- Bone marrow fibrosis may occur in advanced cases

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Headaches and dizziness  
- Splenomegaly and/or hepatomegaly  
- Symptoms of thrombosis or bleeding  
- Night sweats and weight loss

investigations  
- Complete blood count (CBC) showing elevated blood cell counts  
- Bone marrow biopsy to assess cellularity and morphology  
- JAK2 mutation analysis in suspected cases  
- Erythropoietin levels  
- Peripheral blood smear for abnormal cell morphology

diagnosis  
- Confirm elevated blood cell counts  
- Confirm diagnostic mutations where applicable  
- Use bone marrow biopsy results for differential diagnosis  
- Assess for symptoms compatible with myeloproliferative disorders  
- Rule out secondary causes of elevated cell counts

management  
- Phlebotomy for polycythaemia vera to reduce red blood cell mass  
  - Monitor haematocrit levels  
  - Use in conjunction with low-dose aspirin  
- Cytoreductive therapy for high-risk patients  
  - Hydroxycarbamide or interferon-alpha  
  - Consider ruxolitinib for JAK2-positive cases  
- Symptomatic treatment for splenomegaly  
  - Splenectomy in refractory cases  
  - Rituximab may be considered  
- Management of thrombosis risk  
  - Low-dose aspirin for prevention  
  - Anticoagulation in specific high-risk cases  
- Regular monitoring and follow-up  
  - Monitor blood counts and potential transformation to acute leukaemia  
  - Evaluate for complications consistently







Myocardial infarction

background  
- Myocardial infarction is the irreversible injury of the heart muscle due to a prolonged lack of blood supply

risk factors  
- Age over 45 for men and over 55 for women  
- Hypertension  
- Dyslipidaemia  
- Smoking  
- Diabetes mellitus  

causes  
- Coronary artery disease leading to plaque rupture  
- Coronary artery spasm  
- Embolism to coronary arteries  
- Demand ischaemia due to severe coronary artery stenosis  
- Other rare causes such as myocarditis or tachyarrhythmias  

pathophysiology  
- Formation of thrombus at the site of plaque rupture  
- Occlusion of coronary artery leading to ischaemia  
- Loss of ATP production in cardiac cells  
- Cellular necrosis and inflammation response  
- Replacement of necrotic tissue with scar formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain or discomfort often described as pressure or squeezing  
- Radiating pain to arms, neck, jaw, or back  
- Shortness of breath  
- Nausea or vomiting  
- Sweating or feeling light-headed  

investigations  
- 12-lead electrocardiogram to identify ST-segment changes  
- Cardiac biomarkers such as troponin I/T  
- Chest X-ray to assess for complications  
- Echocardiogram to evaluate cardiac function  
- Coronary angiography for assessing coronary artery disease  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history consistent with myocardial ischaemia  
- Elevated cardiac biomarkers indicating myocardial injury  
- ECG changes indicative of ischaemia or infarction  
- Imaging findings as necessary  
- Rule out other causes of chest pain  

management  
- Acetylsalicylic acid (aspirin) for antiplatelet therapy  
  - Loading dose followed by maintenance dose  
  - Consider clopidogrel if aspirin intolerant  
- Morphine for pain relief if severe chest pain  
  - Monitor for adverse effects such as respiratory depression  
  - Add antiemetics if needed  
- Beta-blockers to reduce myocardial oxygen demand  
  - Start if there are no contraindications  
  - Use with caution in heart failure or hypotension  
- Statins to manage dyslipidaemia  
  - Initiate high-intensity statin therapy  
  - Monitor lipid levels and liver function  
- Reperfusion therapy for STEMI patients  
  - Primary percutaneous coronary intervention preferred  
  - Fibrinolysis if PCI is not available within appropriate timeframe  







Myocarditis

Background  
- Myocarditis is an inflammatory condition of the myocardium, often causing heart muscle damage and dysfunction.  

Risk factors  
- Viral infections, particularly with *Coxsackievirus*  
- Autoimmune diseases like lupus or rheumatoid arthritis  
- Exposure to certain medications and toxins  
- Family history of myocarditis or cardiomyopathy  
- Age and male gender predisposition  

Causes  
- Viral infections, most commonly *Coxsackievirus B*  
- Bacterial infections such as *Staphylococcus* and *Streptococcus*  
- Fungal infections like *Candida* or *Aspergillus*  
- Autoimmune reactions  
- Drug-induced hypersensitivity reactions  

Pathophysiology  
- Viral invasion of cardiac myocytes leading to inflammation  
- Immune-mediated damage due to T-cell infiltration  
- Myocyte necrosis and apoptosis  
- Fibrosis and scarring of the myocardium  
- Impaired cardiac contractility and electrical conduction  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain or discomfort  
- Shortness of breath, especially during exertion  
- Palpitations or arrhythmias  
- Fatigue and general malaise  
- Symptoms of heart failure in severe cases  

Investigations  
- Blood tests including inflammatory markers and cardiac troponin  
- Electrocardiogram (ECG) for arrhythmias and ischaemic changes  
- Echocardiogram to assess cardiac function and structure  
- Cardiac MRI for detailed assessment of inflammation  
- Endomyocardial biopsy in selected cases  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination findings  
- Elevated cardiac biomarkers indicative of myocyte injury  
- Characteristic findings on ECG and imaging studies  
- Exclusion of other causes of myocarditis symptoms  
- Confirmatory biopsy in atypical or severe cases  

Management  
- Supportive care including rest and avoidance of triggers  
  - Potential use of non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for mild cases  
  - Hospitalisation for severe cases with heart failure symptoms  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Heart failure medications such as ACE inhibitors and beta-blockers  
  - Immunosuppressive therapy in autoimmune-related myocarditis  
- Close monitoring for arrhythmias and cardiac function  
  - Arrhythmia management with antiarrhythmic medications if necessary  
  - Consideration of implantable cardioverter-defibrillator in high-risk cases  
- Referral to a cardiologist for specialised care  
  - Cardiac rehabilitation for symptom management and recovery  
  - Long-term follow-up and management of chronic heart failure if needed  
- Education and support for patients and families  
  - Discuss potential complications and lifestyle modifications  
  - Provide psychosocial support and resources for coping strategies  







Necrotising enterocolitis

background  
- Necrotising enterocolitis is a serious gastrointestinal condition primarily affecting premature infants.

risk factors  
- Prematurity and low birth weight  
- Formula feeding  
- Intestinal ischaemia  
- Use of antibiotics in early life  
- Presence of congenital heart disease  

causes  
- Unknown, but believed to involve a combination of factors  
- Intestinal bacterial colonisation and dysbiosis  
- Immature gut barrier function  
- Inadequate blood flow to the intestines  
- Inflammatory responses in the gut  

pathophysiology  
- Damage to the intestinal mucosa leads to bacterial invasion  
- Ischaemic injury and inflammation cause necrosis of intestinal tissue  
- Formation of gas in the intestinal wall (pneumatosis intestinalis)  
- Risk of perforation and peritonitis  
- Potential systemic effects, including sepsis  

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal distension  
- Feeding intolerance or gastric residuals  
- Blood in stools  
- Lethargy and temperature instability  
- Signs of shock or respiratory distress  

investigations  
- Abdominal X-ray for signs of pneumatosis and free air  
- Ultrasound of the abdomen for bowel wall thickness  
- Complete blood count to check for infection or inflammation  
- Stool testing for occult blood  
- Monitoring of clinical signs and feeding tolerance  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis is primarily clinical based on symptoms and examination findings  
- Confirmatory imaging through X-ray or ultrasound showing pneumatosis  
- Ruling out other possible causes of abdominal symptoms  
- Monitoring the progression of symptoms over time  
- Assessment of feeding history and tolerance  

management  
- Immediate cessation of feeding  
  - Initiate intravenous fluids and nutritional support  
- Surgical intervention in severe cases  
  - Resection of necrotic bowel if necessary  
- Antibiotics for suspected infection  
  - Broad-spectrum antibiotics to cover gram-negative and anaerobic bacteria  
- Close monitoring in a neonatal intensive care unit  
  - Frequent assessment of vital signs and abdominal examination  
- Supportive care for complications  
  - Management of fluid and electrolyte imbalances  








Necrotising fasciitis

Background  
- A life-threatening soft tissue infection characterised by rapid progression and high mortality

Risk factors  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Immunosuppression  
- Chronic kidney disease  
- Intravenous drug use  
- Recent surgical procedures  

Causes  
- Group A Streptococcus (*Streptococcus pyogenes*)  
- Mixed flora including Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria  
- Clostridium species  
- Staphylococcus aureus, including MRSA  
- Fungal infections in immunocompromised patients  

Pathophysiology  
- Infection often starts in subcutaneous tissue or fascia  
- Release of toxins leads to widespread tissue necrosis  
- Inflammatory response causes vascular compromise  
- Rapid systemic spread can lead to septic shock  
- Potential for multi-organ failure  

Signs and symptoms  
- Rapidly progressing erythema and swelling  
- Severe pain disproportionate to clinical findings  
- Formation of blisters or bullae  
- Fever, chills, and malaise  
- Systemic signs of toxicity  

Investigations  
- Blood tests showing leukocytosis with a left shift  
- Blood cultures to identify infectious organisms  
- Imaging (e.g., ultrasound or CT) to assess extent of disease  
- Wound swabs may be taken  
- Serum lactate levels to assess for sepsis  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of characteristic signs and symptoms  
- Imaging to confirm the extent of the infection  
- Microbiological confirmation may be required  
- Exclusion of alternative diagnoses (e.g., cellulitis)  
- Assessment of systemic involvement through blood tests  

Management  
- Immediate surgical intervention for debridement  
  - Wound must be surgically explored and necrotic tissue removed  
  - Repeat debridement may be necessary  
- Broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotics initiated promptly  
  - Consideration of piperacillin-tazobactam plus clindamycin  
  - Modify antibiotic therapy based on culture results  
- Supportive care in a critical care setting  
  - Monitoring for complications such as septic shock  
  - Fluid resuscitation and vasopressors if required  
- Multidisciplinary team approach involved  
  - Involvement of surgeons, intensivists, and infectious disease specialists  
  - Psychological support for patients and families  
- Regular reassessment of clinical status  
  - Monitor for signs of improvement or deterioration  
  - Adjust treatment based on clinical response  







Nephrotic syndrome

Background  
- Nephrotic syndrome is a kidney disorder characterised by significant proteinuria and low serum albumin levels 

Risk factors  
- Children aged 1-5 years  
- Adults aged 30-50 years  
- Previous history of kidney disease  
- Family history of kidney disorders  
- Diabetes mellitus 

Causes  
- Minimal change disease  
- Focal segmental glomerulosclerosis  
- Membranous nephropathy  
- Diabetic nephropathy  
- Systemic diseases (e.g. lupus, amyloidosis) 

Pathophysiology  
- Damage to glomerular filtration barrier  
- Increased permeability to plasma proteins  
- Loss of albumin results in hypoalbuminaemia  
- Oedema due to reduced oncotic pressure  
- Compensatory hyperlipidaemia may occur 

Signs and symptoms  
- Oedema (periorbital and peripheral)  
- Frothy urine  
- Weight gain  
- Fatigue  
- Increased susceptibility to infections 

Investigations  
- Urinalysis (proteinuria >3.5g/24 hours)  
- Serum albumin level (low)  
- Serum cholesterol level (high)  
- Renal function tests (elevated creatinine)  
- Kidney biopsy (if cause unclear) 

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation of oedema and proteinuria  
- Low serum albumin levels  
- Exclusion of secondary causes  
- Kidney biopsy if adult onset or atypical  
- Classification based on underlying pathology 

Management  
- Control of underlying cause if secondary  
   - Immunosuppressive therapy (e.g. corticosteroids)  
   - Treat systemic diseases (e.g. diabetes)  
- Symptomatic management of nephrotic syndrome  
   - Diuretics for fluid retention  
   - Dietary modifications (high protein, low salt)  
- Antihypertensive medications if hypertensive  
   - ACE inhibitors to reduce proteinuria  
   - Monitor blood pressure regularly  
- Vaccinations to reduce infection risk  
   - Influenza vaccine annually  
   - Pneumococcal vaccine as indicated  
- Monitor for complications  
   - Regular follow-up for renal function  
   - Manage hyperlipidaemia if present  







Non-accidental injury

background  
- Non-accidental injury refers to harm inflicted on children, often through abuse or neglect.

risk factors  
- Younger children are at increased risk  
- Parental substance misuse or mental health issues  
- Domestic violence within the household  
- Social isolation and lack of support systems  
- Previous history of child protection concerns  

causes  
- Physical abuse from caregivers  
- Emotional abuse impacting mental well-being  
- Sexual abuse leading to both physical and psychological harm  
- Neglect resulting in inadequate care and supervision  
- Environmental factors including poverty and unstable housing  

pathophysiology  
- Direct trauma leads to tissue injury and pain  
- Psychological impact manifests through stress and trauma responses  
- Neglect results in malnutrition and developmental delays  
- Repeated injurious episodes can cause long-term psychological disorders  
- Disruption in the normal parent-child attachment effects emotional regulation  

signs and symptoms  
- Unexplained injuries or patterns inconsistent with reported incidents  
- Changes in behaviour such as withdrawal or aggression  
- Fearfulness around certain individuals or situations  
- Sudden changes in academic performance or social engagement  
- Developmental delays or failure to thrive  

investigations  
- Thorough history taking and physical examination  
- Imaging studies like X-rays to identify fractures  
- Bruising patterns assessed for significance  
- Laboratory tests to rule out other causes  
- Multi-agency discussions in cases of suspected abuse  

diagnosis  
- Evidence of injuries inconsistent with explanation or development  
- Identification of risk factors associated with abuse  
- Assessment of psychosocial context in the child's environment  
- Use of validated assessment tools and frameworks  
- Collaboration with child protection services where applicable  

management  
- Ensure child’s immediate safety and ongoing welfare  
   - Involvement of social services for protection and support  
   - Consideration of medical treatment for any injuries  
- Psychological support for the child and family  
   - Referral to child mental health services  
   - Therapeutic interventions addressing trauma  
- Legal actions if necessary through child protection services  
   - Court proceedings to ensure long-term safety  
   - Parent education on child welfare practices  
- Continuous monitoring and follow-up care  
   - Regular assessments by healthcare professionals  
   - Multi-disciplinary team involvement for holistic care  
- Support networks established for families  
   - Access to counselling and support groups  
   - Community-based resources to assist families in need  







Notifiable diseases

background  
- Notifiable diseases are infections or conditions that must be reported to health authorities to monitor public health

risk factors  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Travel to areas with known outbreaks  
- Poor vaccination status  
- Compromised immune system  
- Living in overcrowded conditions  

causes  
- Bacterial infections  
- Viral infections  
- Parasitic infections  
- Fungal infections  
- Contaminated food or water  

pathophysiology  
- Pathogens invade host tissues  
- Immune response triggers inflammation  
- Systemic symptoms arise from cytokine release  
- Complications occur if untreated  
- Organ dysfunction may develop  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever  
- Rash  
- Cough or respiratory distress  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms  
- Neurological signs  

investigations  
- Blood tests for specific pathogens  
- Cultures from relevant sites  
- Serological tests for antibodies  
- Imaging studies if indicated  
- Contact tracing and surveillance data  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and symptom assessment  
- Laboratory confirmation of infection  
- Epidemiological link to outbreaks  
- Exclusion of other diagnoses  
- Adherence to local guidelines  

management  
- Inform public health authorities  
  - Ensure appropriate investigation  
  - Monitor for outbreaks  
- Symptomatic treatment for patients  
  - Pain relief and hydration  
  - Antipyretics as needed  
- Isolation measures if required  
  - Prevent transmission  
  - Educate the patient and family  
- Treatment of specific infections  
  - Antibiotics for bacterial infections  
  - Antivirals for viral infections  
- Vaccination and prophylaxis for at-risk individuals  
  - Update immunisation status  
  - Educate on preventive measures  







Obesity and pregnancy

Background  
- Obesity in pregnancy significantly increases maternal and fetal risks

Risk factors  
- Maternal age over 35 years  
- Pre-existing health conditions (e.g., diabetes, hypertension)  
- High pre-pregnancy body mass index (BMI)  
- Lack of physical activity  
- Family history of obesity

Causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental factors (e.g., diet, lifestyle)  
- Socioeconomic status  
- Psychological factors (e.g., emotional eating)  
- Hormonal imbalances

Signs and symptoms  
- Increased weight gain during pregnancy  
- Symptoms of gestational diabetes  
- Hypertension or pre-eclampsia symptoms  
- Reduced physical mobility  
- Increased fatigue

Investigations  
- Pre-pregnancy and antenatal BMI assessment  
- Blood pressure monitoring  
- Glucose tolerance test  
- Ultrasound for fetal growth assessment  
- Assessment for signs of gestational hypertension

Diagnosis  
- BMI classification for gestational obesity  
- Identification of gestational diabetes using fasting plasma glucose or OGTT  
- Blood pressure criteria for pre-eclampsia  
- Clinical evaluation of maternal and fetal health  
- Multidisciplinary team input for complex cases

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications including diet and exercise  
  - Referral to dietetic services  
  - Regular physical activity guidance  
- Monitoring and managing gestational diabetes  
  - Education on glucose self-monitoring  
  - Insulin therapy if required  
- Management of hypertension and pre-eclampsia  
  - Antihypertensive medication as indicated  
  - Frequent monitoring and potential hospitalisation  
- Nutritional counselling to ensure adequate intake  
  - Focus on balanced diet and portion control  
  - Supplementation if deficient (e.g., iron, folic acid)  
- Support services for mental health and wellbeing  
  - Counselling services for emotional support  
  - Community resources for ongoing support







Obstructive sleep apnoea

Background  
- Obstructive sleep apnoea is a common sleep disorder characterised by repetitive interruptions in breathing during sleep due to upper airway obstruction  

Risk factors  
- Obesity  
- Age over 40 years  
- Male gender  
- Family history of sleep apnoea  
- Smoking  

Causes  
- Excess tissue in the neck  
- Enlarged tonsils or adenoids  
- Neuromuscular disorders  
- Structural abnormalities of the airway  
- Alcohol and sedative use  

Pathophysiology  
- Upper airway collapses during sleep due to loss of muscle tone  
- Increased negative intrathoracic pressure causes intermittent hypoxia  
- Activation of sympathetic nervous system leading to increased blood pressure  
- Sleep fragmentation results in excessive daytime sleepiness  
- Potential cardiovascular complications due to strain on the heart  

Signs and symptoms  
- Loud snoring  
- Pauses in breathing observed by a partner  
- Excessive daytime sleepiness  
- Morning headaches  
- Difficulty concentrating  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Epworth Sleepiness Scale  
- Overnight pulse oximetry  
- Polysomnography (sleep study)  
- Home sleep apnoea testing  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed obstructive sleep apnoea if apnoea-hypopnoea index is ≥5 events per hour with symptoms  
- AHI of 15 or more regardless of symptoms also confirms diagnosis  
- Evaluation of associated co-morbidities  
- Consider alternative diagnoses if symptoms do not align  
- Referral to a sleep centre if needed  

Management  
- Lifestyle modification such as weight loss  
  - Encourage diet and exercise  
  - Monitor progress  
- Continuous positive airway pressure (CPAP) therapy  
  - Provide patient education on device use  
  - Regular follow-up to assess adherence and effectiveness  
- Oral appliances may be considered  
  - Refer to a dentist trained in sleep medicine  
  - Monitor for any dental effects  
- Surgical options for selected cases  
  - Uvulopalatopharyngoplasty or other airway surgeries  
  - Assess risks versus benefits  
- Treat associated conditions such as hypertension  
  - Regular monitoring and management  
  - Consider referral to a specialist if needed  







Occupational lung disease

background  
- Occupational lung disease encompasses respiratory conditions caused by inhalation of harmful substances in the workplace 

risk factors  
- Exposure to hazardous substances such as dust, fumes, gases, and chemicals  
- Duration and intensity of exposure  
- Lack of proper ventilation and safety measures in the workplace  
- Smoking history, which may exacerbate lung conditions  
- Specific occupations such as mining, construction, and manufacturing  

causes  
- Inhalation of silica dust causing pneumoconiosis  
- Asbestos exposure leading to asbestosis and mesothelioma  
- Exposure to coal dust resulting in coal worker's pneumoconiosis  
- Allergens and irritants in specific work environments triggering asthma  
- Chemical fumes and vapours causing reactive airways dysfunction syndrome  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation and fibrosis of lung tissue due to toxic exposure  
- Immune response to inhaled allergens or irritants  
- Alveolar damage or scarring limiting gas exchange  
- Obstructive or restrictive lung disease patterns developing over time  
- Progressive decline in lung function related to chronic exposure  

signs and symptoms  
- Cough, often chronic and productive  
- Shortness of breath, especially on exertion  
- Wheezing or a whistling sound during breathing  
- Chest tightness or pain  
- Symptoms may vary based on the specific substance of exposure  

investigations  
- Detailed occupational history and exposure assessment  
- Spirometry testing to assess lung function  
- Chest X-ray or CT scan to identify structural changes  
- Bronchoscopy with biopsy for tissue analysis if indicated  
- Blood tests for specific occupational diseases, where applicable  

diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation based on history and symptoms  
- Confirmation through spirometry showing obstructive or restrictive patterns  
- Radiological findings consistent with occupational lung disease  
- Exclusion of other respiratory conditions  
- Use of specific criteria based on NICE guidelines for work-related asthma  

management  
- Removal from exposure to harmful substances  
  - Implement workplace engineering controls and personal protective equipment  
  - Educate workers about risks and safety measures  
- Symptomatic treatment for respiratory conditions  
  - Bronchodilators for asthma symptoms  
  - Corticosteroids for inflammatory lung diseases  
- Regular monitoring of lung function  
  - Scheduled spirometry assessments  
  - Follow-up with occupational health services  
- Referral to specialist services for advanced disease  
  - Pulmonology consultation for complex cases  
  - Multidisciplinary team involvement for comprehensive care  
- Supportive measures for affected individuals  
  - Smoking cessation programmes  
  - Psychological support for coping with chronic illness  







Oesophageal cancer

Background
- Oesophageal cancer is a malignant tumour in the oesophagus, often leading to significant morbidity and mortality 

Risk factors
- Age over 50 years
- Male gender
- Tobacco and alcohol use
- Gastroesophageal reflux disease 
- Obesity

Causes
- Squamous cell carcinoma associated with smoking and alcohol 
- Adenocarcinoma linked to Barrett's oesophagus 
- Genetic predisposition in some patients 
- Chronic inflammation and injury to the oesophagus 
- Dietary factors, such as low fruit and vegetable intake

Pathophysiology
- Abnormal cell proliferation in the oesophageal lining 
- Development of dysplasia leading to cancer 
- Local invasion into adjacent tissues 
- Potential for metastasis to regional lymph nodes and distant organs 

Signs and symptoms
- Dysphagia or difficulty swallowing 
- Odynophagia or painful swallowing 
- Weight loss and anorexia 
- Regurgitation and reflux symptoms 
- Chest pain or discomfort 

Investigations
- Oesophagogastroduodenoscopy with biopsy 
- Imaging studies like CT scan or PET scan 
- Endoscopic ultrasound for local staging 
- Barium swallow to assess oesophageal function 
- Full blood count and liver function tests 

Diagnosis
- Confirmed by histological examination of biopsy 
- Staging using imaging studies 
- Assessment of metastasis 
- Multidisciplinary team involvement for accurate diagnosis 
- Consideration of clinical presentation and risk factors 

Management
- Surgical resection for resectable tumours 
  - Oesophagectomy may be performed 
  - Minimally invasive approaches may be considered 
- Chemotherapy and radiotherapy for non-resectable cases 
  - Neoadjuvant therapy may be indicated 
  - Palliative care options for advanced disease 
- Endoscopic therapies for early-stage disease 
  - Endoscopic mucosal resection 
  - Ablation techniques 
- Nutritional support for impacted individuals 
  - Dietary consultation for weight management 
  - Enteral nutrition if inadequate oral intake 
- Regular follow-up for monitoring and managing complications 
  - Surveillance for recurrence 
  - Addressing symptoms and quality of life







Optic neuritis

background  
- Optic neuritis is an inflammatory condition affecting the optic nerve, often associated with multiple sclerosis  

risk factors  
- Age predominantly between 20 and 50 years  
- Female gender  
- History of autoimmune diseases  
- Vitamin D deficiency  
- Smoking  

causes  
- Multiple sclerosis  
- Idiopathic  
- Infections, e.g., viral infections such as mumps or herpes  
- Autoimmune conditions, e.g., neuromyelitis optica  
- Sarcoidosis  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the optic nerve leading to demyelination  
- Disruption of axonal conduction in the optic nerve  
- Immune-mediated damage to myelin sheath  
- Potential axonal degeneration over time  

signs and symptoms  
- Vision loss, often unilateral  
- Pain with eye movement  
- Dyschromatopsia, particularly red desaturation  
- Decreased visual acuity  
- Visual field defects  

investigations  
- Full neurological examination  
- Visual acuity tests  
- Optical coherence tomography (OCT)  
- MRI of the brain and orbits  
- Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis  

diagnosis  
- Clinical examination consistent with optic neuritis  
- MRI showing optic nerve enhancement or lesions  
- Exclusion of other causes of vision loss  
- Visual evoked potentials may indicate delayed response  
- Association with MS in recurrent cases  

management  
- Corticosteroids to reduce inflammation  
  - Methylprednisolone 500 mg IV for 3 days  
  - Oral prednisolone may be considered in some cases  
- Management of underlying conditions or MS  
  - Disease modifying therapies for MS  
  - Symptomatic treatment for visual deficits  







Osteoarthritis

background  
- Osteoarthritis is the most common form of arthritis, affecting joints and leading to pain and disability

risk factors  
- Age  
- Obesity  
- Joint injury  
- Family history  
- Occupational overuse

causes  
- Age-related cartilage degeneration  
- Joint stress from obesity or repetitive use  
- Joint injuries from trauma or sports  
- Inflammatory diseases like rheumatoid arthritis  
- Genetic predisposition

pathophysiology  
- Cartilage breakdown leads to increased friction between bones  
- Bone remodelling occurs, forming osteophytes  
- Synovial inflammation may develop  
- Increased levels of pro-inflammatory cytokines in the joint  
- Ultimately results in joint pain, stiffness, and reduced mobility

signs and symptoms  
- Joint pain during or after movement  
- Stiffness, particularly after periods of inactivity  
- Swelling around the joint  
- Loss of flexibility and range of motion  
- Crepitus or grating sensation when moving the joint

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- X-rays to assess joint space narrowing and osteophytes  
- MRI for detailed imaging, if necessary  
- Blood tests to rule out other types of arthritis  
- Joint aspiration if there is effusion, for synovial fluid analysis

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and physical examination  
- Symptoms consistent with osteoarthritis, particularly in weight-bearing joints  
- Radiological findings supporting the diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other arthritis types  
- Consideration of the patient's age and joint involvement

management  
- Lifestyle modifications for weight management and exercise  
  - Encourage low-impact activities  
  - Recommend physiotherapy for strength and flexibility  
- Pharmacological treatments  
  - Use paracetamol or NSAIDs for pain relief  
  - Consider intra-articular corticosteroid injections if severe  
- Consider referral for surgical options if conservative management fails  
  - Joint replacement may be indicated  
  - Arthroscopy for worsening symptoms in some cases  
- Patient education on self-management strategies  
  - Importance of adherence to exercise programmes  
  - Pain management techniques to improve quality of life  
- Regular follow-up to monitor progress and adjust management  
  - Review medication efficacy and side effects  
  - Evaluate functional outcomes and ensure compliance







Osteomyelitis

Background  
- Osteomyelitis is an infection of the bone, often due to bacteria, causing inflammation and bone destruction

Risk factors  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Immunocompromised states  
- Recent surgery or trauma  
- Intravenous drug use  
- Peripheral vascular disease  

Causes  
- Bacterial infections, commonly *Staphylococcus aureus*  
- Haematogenous spread from distant sites  
- Contiguous spread from adjacent soft tissue infections  
- Post-surgical infections  
- Chronic wounds or ulcers  

Pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to inflammation and purulent material accumulation  
- Bone marrow necrosis occurs due to reduced blood supply  
- Formation of sequestrum, an area of devitalised bone  
- Involvement of periosteum contributing to pain and swelling  
- Can lead to chronic osteomyelitis if untreated  

Signs and symptoms  
- Localised bone pain and tenderness  
- Swelling and warmth over the affected area  
- Fever and chills  
- Skin changes such as redness or ulceration  
- Limitation of movement in involved areas  

Investigations  
- Blood tests showing elevated white blood cell count and inflammatory markers  
- Bone imaging (X-ray, MRI or CT) to identify bone changes  
- Blood cultures to identify causative organisms  
- Bone biopsy for definitive microbiological diagnosis  
- ESR and CRP to monitor inflammation levels  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation and history suggestive of osteomyelitis  
- Positive cultures from blood or bone biopsy  
- Imaging studies confirming bone involvement  
- Assessing duration and type of symptoms  
- Excluding other causes of bone pain  

Management  
- Antibiotic therapy based on culture sensitivities  
  - Initial empirical treatment may include flucloxacillin  
  - Adjust based on susceptibility from cultures  
- Surgery for chronic cases or abscess drainage  
  - Debridement of necrotic bone  
  - Restoration of blood supply if possible  
- Analgesia for pain control  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs  
  - Opioids for severe pain  
- Management of underlying risk factors  
  - Optimising diabetes control  
  - Addressing any vascular issues  
- Regular follow-up to monitor response to treatment  
  - Clinical assessment of symptom resolution  
  - Repeat imaging if necessary 







Osteoporosis

background  
- A common skeletal disorder characterised by decreased bone density and increased fracture risk  

risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Female sex  
- Family history of osteoporosis  
- Low body mass index (BMI)  
- Sedentary lifestyle  

causes  
- Hormonal changes, especially postmenopause  
- Chronic diseases such as rheumatoid arthritis or hyperthyroidism  
- Long-term use of corticosteroids  
- Poor nutrition, particularly low calcium and vitamin D intake  
- Alcohol and tobacco use  

pathophysiology  
- Imbalance between bone resorption and formation  
- Osteoclast activity exceeds osteoblast activity  
- Decreased bone mineral density leading to structural deterioration  
- Altered bone microarchitecture  
- Increased risk of fragility fractures  

signs and symptoms  
- Often asymptomatic until a fracture occurs  
- Fractures with minimal trauma (fragility fractures)  
- Back pain from vertebral fractures  
- Loss of height  
- Kyphosis or stooped posture  

investigations  
- Dual energy X-ray absorptiometry (DEXA) scan for bone mineral density  
- Blood tests to assess calcium and vitamin D levels  
- Clinical risk factor assessment  
- X-rays for detecting fractures  
- MRI if complications are suspected  

diagnosis  
- T-score of -2.5 or lower on DEXA scan  
- History of fragility fracture  
- Assess clinical risk factors for fracture  
- Review of relevant medical history  
- Evaluate lifestyle factors contributing to osteoporosis  

management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Encourage regular weight-bearing exercise  
  - Advise on a balanced diet rich in calcium and vitamin D  
- Pharmacological treatments  
  - Bisphosphonates as first-line therapy  
  - Consider alternatives like calcium supplements or hormone replacement therapy  
- Fall prevention strategies  
  - Home safety assessments to reduce fall risk  
  - Strength and balance training programs  
- Regular monitoring  
  - Repeat DEXA scans as per NICE guidelines  
  - Monitor adherence and response to treatment  
- Patient education  
  - Inform about osteoporosis and its risks  
  - Discuss importance of medication adherence and lifestyle changes  







Otitis externa

Background  
- Inflammation of the external ear canal, commonly known as swimmer's ear 

Risk factors  
- Frequent water exposure  
- Use of earplugs or hearing aids  
- Trauma to the ear canal  
- Skin conditions like eczema or psoriasis  
- Allergies to ear products  

Causes  
- Bacterial infections (e.g. *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*)  
- Fungal infections (e.g. *Aspergillus*)  
- Allergic reactions  
- Dermatitis or eczema  
- Foreign bodies in the ear canal  

Pathophysiology  
- Disruption of the skin barrier in the ear canal  
- Overgrowth of pathogens due to moist environment  
- Inflammatory response leading to swelling and pain  

Signs and symptoms  
- Itching in the ear canal  
- Pain and tenderness in the outer ear  
- Discharge from the ear, often foul-smelling  
- Redness and swelling of the ear canal  
- Hearing loss if the ear canal is obstructed  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination of the ear canal  
- Otoscopic examination for discharge, swelling, or redness  
- Microscopy and culture of discharge if severe or persistent  
- Assessment for underlying skin conditions  
- Audiometry if hearing loss is significant  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical symptoms and otoscopic findings  
- Confirmation of infection type via culture if needed  
- Rule out other conditions (e.g., tympanic membrane perforation)  
- Consideration of chronicity and recurrent episodes  
- Assess for risk factors or associated skin conditions  

Management  
- Topical antibiotics for bacterial infections  
  - Advise on proper application of ear drops  
  - Consider systemic antibiotics for severe cases  
- Antifungal treatment for fungal infections  
  - Use antifungal ear drops or creams  
  - Educate on preventing moisture exposure  
- Pain relief with analgesics  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen as needed  
  - Avoiding ear manipulation to reduce pain  
- Cleaning the ear canal by healthcare provider  
  - Consider suction or curettage in severe cases  
  - Educate patients on ear care  
- Education on prevention strategies  
  - Advise on drying ears after swimming or bathing  
  - Caution against cotton swabs and trauma to the ear







Otitis media

Background  
- Common ear infection, often seen in children, causing inflammation in the middle ear  

Risk factors  
- Age under 2 years  
- Family history of otitis media  
- Attending nursery or playgroups  
- Exposure to tobacco smoke  
- Seasonal allergies  

Causes  
- Viral infections such as rhinovirus or influenza  
- Bacterial infections including *Streptococcus pneumoniae* and *Haemophilus influenzae*  
- Eustachian tube dysfunction  
- Upper respiratory tract infections  
- Allergic rhinitis  

Pathophysiology  
- Blockage of Eustachian tube leading to fluid accumulation  
- Bacterial or viral colonisation of fluid causing inflammation  
- Increase in pressure and pain in the middle ear  

Signs and symptoms  
- Ear pain or discomfort  
- Hearing loss  
- Fluid drainage from the ear  
- Fever  
- Irritability in children  

Investigations  
- Otoscopy showing bulging or retracted tympanic membrane  
- Pneumatic otoscopy to assess mobility of the tympanic membrane  
- Tympanometry to measure middle ear pressure  
- Audiometry for hearing assessment  
- Consideration of CT or MRI if complications suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination are primary  
- Presence of ear pain and tympanic membrane abnormalities confirm diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other conditions is necessary  
- Recurrent otitis media diagnosed after multiple episodes  
- Differentiation between acute and chronic forms  

Management  
- Analgesia for pain relief  
  - Use of paracetamol or ibuprofen  
  - Avoid aspirin in children  
- Antibiotics for bacterial infections  
  - Amoxicillin first-line therapy  
  - Consider alternative if no improvement in 48-72 hours  
- Watchful waiting for non-severe cases  
  - Encourage parental observation of symptoms  
  - Follow-up if symptoms persist  
- Surgical management for recurrent cases  
  - Tympanostomy tube insertion for ventilation  
  - Adenoidectomy if associated with upper airway obstruction  
- Preventive measures  
  - Encourage breastfeeding for infants  
  - Pneumococcal vaccination in children  







Ovarian cancer

Background  
- Ovarian cancer is a malignant tumour arising from the ovaries, often diagnosed at an advanced stage  

Risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Family history of ovarian or breast cancer  
- Genetic mutations (BRCA1/BRCA2)  
- Endometriosis  
- Nulliparity or having fewer children  

Causes  
- Exact cause is unknown  
- Genetic mutations linked to increased risk  
- Hormonal factors may contribute
