- Environmental factors under research  
- Possible association with obesity  

Pathophysiology  
- Uncontrolled growth of abnormal ovarian cells  
- Can spread locally or through the peritoneal cavity  
- Potential for metastasis to distant organs  
- Diverse histological subtypes (serous, mucinous, endometrioid)  
- Tumour microenvironment may influence malignancy  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal bloating or swelling  
- Pelvic or abdominal pain  
- Changes in urinary habits (urgency or frequency)  
- Difficulty eating or feeling full quickly  
- Unexplained weight loss  

Investigations  
- Pelvic examination  
- Transvaginal ultrasound  
- Serum CA-125 levels  
- CT scan or MRI of the abdomen and pelvis  
- Biopsy or staging laparotomy if indicated  

Diagnosis  
- Histological confirmation via biopsy  
- Imaging studies to assess stage  
- Elevated CA-125 levels alongside clinical findings  
- Must consider differential diagnoses  
- Multidisciplinary team involvement for complex cases  

Management  
- Surgical intervention (primary treatment)  
   - Total abdominal hysterectomy with bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy  
   - Staging and debulking if feasible  
- Chemotherapy (adjuvant or neoadjuvant)  
   - Platinum-based regimen (carboplatin with paclitaxel)  
   - Consideration of targeted therapy in advanced cases  
- Radiotherapy (rarely used, mainly for palliation)  
   - In cases of localised disease  
   - May be used for symptom control  
- Palliative care for advanced disease  
   - Focus on quality of life  
   - Symptom management strategies  
- Genetic counselling for hereditary cases  
   - Discuss implications for family members  
   - Consider risk-reducing strategies for at-risk individuals  







Pancreatic cancer

background  
- Pancreatic cancer is a malignant neoplasm of the pancreas, often diagnosed at an advanced stage leading to poor prognosis  

risk factors  
- Age over 60  
- Smoking  
- Obesity  
- Family history of pancreatic cancer  
- Chronic pancreatitis  

causes  
- Genetic mutations (BRCA2, CDKN2A)  
- Exocrine pancreas dysfunction  
- Inflammatory processes  
- Environmental exposures (chemicals, toxins)  
- Diabetes mellitus  

pathophysiology  
- Arises primarily from ductal cells of the pancreas  
- Invasive carcinoma that disrupts local structures  
- Potential for metastasis to liver, lungs, and peritoneum  
- Desmoplastic reaction leading to a fibrous stroma  
- Alterations in tumour microenvironment affecting immune response  

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain  
- Weight loss  
- Jaundice  
- Anorexia  
- New-onset diabetes  

investigations  
- CT scan of the abdomen  
- MRI with MRCP  
- Endoscopic ultrasound  
- CA 19-9 tumour marker assessment  
- Biopsy for histological confirmation  

diagnosis  
- Consider clinical history and examination findings  
- Imaging studies indicating mass or obstruction  
- Elevated CA 19-9 levels  
- Confirmation via histopathology from biopsy  
- Staging based on tumour size and metastasis  

management  
- Surgical resection (Whipple procedure for resectable tumours)  
  - Assess for surgical candidacy  
  - Postoperative monitoring and supportive care  
- Chemotherapy (FOLFIRINOX or gemcitabine + nab-paclitaxel)  
  - Adjuvant therapy post-surgery  
  - Palliative therapy for advanced disease  
- Radiotherapy (considered for local control)  
  - Use in combination with chemotherapy  
  - Palliative intent for symptom relief  
- Palliative care and symptom management  
  - Multidisciplinary team approach  
  - Focus on quality of life  
- Clinical trials for eligible patients  
  - Investigate novel therapeutic options  
  - Monitor and report outcomes  







Pancytopenia

Background  
- Pancytopenia is a condition characterised by a reduction in all three blood cell types: red blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets  

Risk factors  
- Age-related factors  
- Previous bone marrow disorders  
- Autoimmune diseases  
- Chronic infections  
- Exposure to certain medications or chemicals  

Causes  
- Bone marrow failure syndromes  
- Peripheral destruction of blood cells  
- Hypersplenism  
- Nutritional deficiencies (vitamin B12, folate)  
- Malignancies  

Pathophysiology  
- Impaired production of blood cells in bone marrow  
- Increased destruction or sequestration of cells in the spleen  
- Altered blood cell dynamics due to clonal expansion or autoimmune processes  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Shortness of breath  
- Easy bruising or bleeding  
- Frequent infections  
- Pallor  

Investigations  
- Complete blood count with differential  
- Bone marrow biopsy  
- Peripheral blood smear  
- Reticulocyte count  
- Specific tests for underlying causes (e.g., vitamin levels, autoimmune markers)  

Diagnosis  
- Identify a reduced level of red cells, white cells, and platelets  
- Exclude other causes of cytopenias  
- Confirm diagnosis through bone marrow examination if necessary  
- Consider aetiology and clinical context for systematic approach  
- Refer to guidelines for specific tests based on clinical suspicion  

Management  
- Treat underlying cause (e.g., vitamin supplementation for deficiencies)  
  - Address nutritional deficiencies  
  - Manage chronic conditions  
- Monitor blood counts and manage symptoms  
  - Surgical interventions for splenomegaly  
  - Transfusions for severe cases  
- Consider immunosuppressive therapy in autoimmune causes  
  - Use corticosteroids  
  - Explore other immunosuppressive agents  
- Evaluate for potential bone marrow disorders  
  - Refer to haematology for further assessment  
  - Consider advanced therapies (e.g., stem cell transplant)  
- Educate patients on signs of complications  
  - Instruct on recognising bleeding risks  
  - Advise on infection prevention strategies  







Parkinson’s disease

Background  
- Parkinson’s disease is a progressive neurodegenerative disorder characterised by motor and non-motor symptoms  

Risk Factors  
- Age over 60 years  
- Family history of Parkinson’s disease  
- Exposure to pesticides and heavy metals  
- Low levels of physical activity  
- Male gender  

Causes  
- Loss of dopaminergic neurons in the substantia nigra  
- Accumulation of alpha-synuclein protein forming Lewy bodies  
- Genetic mutations (e.g. PARK2, PARK7, LRRK2)  
- Environmental factors  
- Oxidative stress and mitochondrial dysfunction  

Pathophysiology  
- Degeneration of the nigrostriatal pathway leading to dopamine deficiency  
- Impaired communication between the basal ganglia and motor cortex  
- Altered neurotransmitter balances (dopamine, acetylcholine)  

Signs and Symptoms  
- Resting tremor  
- Bradykinesia  
- Rigidity  
- Postural instability  
- Non-motor symptoms (e.g. depression, sleep disturbances, cognitive impairment)  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment based on history and examination  
- Response to dopaminergic medication  
- Neuroimaging (e.g. MRI to rule out other conditions)  
- DAT scan to assess dopaminergic function  
- Blood tests to exclude other causes  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on characteristic motor symptoms  
- Confirmatory tests when atypical symptoms present  
- Assessment of non-motor symptoms  
- Consideration of age at onset and symptom progression  
- Exclusion of secondary parkinsonism  

Management  
- Introduction of dopaminergic medications (e.g. levodopa, dopamine agonists)  
  - Monitor for side effects and efficacy  
  - Adjust dosage as needed  
- Non-pharmacological interventions (e.g. physiotherapy, occupational therapy)  
  - Encourage exercise and mobility  
  - Support daily living activities  
- Advanced therapies (e.g. deep brain stimulation for advanced stages)  
  - Consider when medications are ineffective  
  - Assess patient suitability for surgery  
- Management of non-motor symptoms (e.g. antidepressants, sleep aids)  
  - Individualised treatment plans  
  - Regular reviews and adjustments  
- Multidisciplinary care approach  
  - Collaborate with neurologists, therapists, and dietitians  
  - Regular follow-ups to monitor disease progression and management efficacy  







Pathological fracture

background  
- Pathological fractures occur due to weakened bone structure, often from underlying diseases 

risk factors  
- Osteoporosis leading to reduced bone density  
- Malignancies that affect bone integrity  
- Chronic inflammatory diseases like rheumatoid arthritis  
- Age-related changes in bone metabolism  
- Long-term use of corticosteroids  

causes  
- Metastatic cancer causing bone lesions  
- Osteogenesis imperfecta resulting in fragile bones  
- Paget's disease leading to abnormal bone remodelling  
- Bone infections such as osteomyelitis  
- Nutritional deficiencies, particularly vitamin D deficiency  

pathophysiology  
- Weakening of bone structure due to disease processes  
- Increased osteoclastic activity or reduced osteoblastic activity  
- Altered bone mineral density and microarchitecture  
- Accumulation of pathological lesions disrupting normal bone function  
- Mechanical overload on weakened bones causing fractures  

signs and symptoms  
- Pain at the fracture site  
- Swelling or tenderness in the affected area  
- Reduced mobility or inability to bear weight  
- Deformity or angulation of the bone in some cases  
- History of minimal trauma prior to fracture  

investigations  
- X-rays to assess for fractures and underlying bone conditions  
- MRI or CT scans for detailed imaging of bone lesions  
- Bone density scan (DEXA) to evaluate osteoporosis risk  
- Blood tests for serum calcium, phosphate, and vitamin D levels  
- Biopsy may be indicated for suspected malignancy  

diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation focusing on history and physical examination findings  
- Imaging studies confirming the presence of a fracture  
- Identification of underlying aetiology through appropriate investigations  
- Assessment of risk factors contributing to bone weakness  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other fracture types  

management  
- Treatment of underlying cause (e.g., cancer therapy, osteoporosis management)  
  - Bisphosphonates for osteoporosis  
  - Chemotherapy or radiotherapy for malignancies  
- Pain relief using analgesics and anti-inflammatory medications  
  - Paracetamol for mild pain  
  - NSAIDs for moderate to severe pain  
- Surgical intervention may be necessary for stabilization  
  - Intramedullary nailing or plating for certain fractures  
  - Joint replacement for pathological neck of femur fractures  
- Rehabilitation through physiotherapy to restore function  
  - Gradual weight-bearing exercises  
  - Strength training to enhance bone strength  
- Preventive measures to reduce future fracture risk  
  - Nutritional counselling to address deficiencies  
  - Lifestyle modifications, including physical activity and fall prevention strategies  







Patient on anti-coagulant therapy

background  
- Patients on anti-coagulant therapy are at increased risk of bleeding and require careful monitoring and management  

risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Kidney impairment  
- Concurrent use of other medications that affect bleeding  
- History of bleeding disorders  
- Poor adherence to therapy  

causes  
- Atrial fibrillation  
- Venous thromboembolism  
- Mechanical heart valves  
- Pulmonary embolism  
- Stroke prevention  

investigations  
- Full blood count to assess for anaemia  
- Prothrombin time and International Normalised Ratio (INR)  
- Thrombin time if considering heparin therapy  
- Kidney function tests  
- Liver function tests  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment including risk factors for thrombosis or bleeding  
- Laboratory tests to monitor coagulation status  
- Assessment of adherence to therapy  
- Review of concurrent medications  
- Imaging if indicated to investigate thrombosis  

management  
- Regular INR monitoring for patients on warfarin  
   - Target INR based on indication  
   - Adjust dose to maintain therapeutic range  
- Education on signs of bleeding and safe practices  
   - Advise on lifestyle and dietary factors  
   - Provide emergency contacts for bleeding issues  
- Consider alternative anticoagulants when appropriate  
   - Direct oral anticoagulants may be suitable  
   - Assess patient preferences and renal function  
- Ensure appropriate use of bridging therapy when indicated  
   - Assess risks based on surgical or procedural interventions  
   - Collaborate with surgical teams for perioperative management  
- Ongoing review and follow-up to assess risk and therapy effectiveness  
   - Regular appointments to discuss adherence  
   - Adjustments made based on patient feedback and lab results  







Patient on anti-platelet therapy

background  
- Anti-platelet therapy is used to prevent blood clots and reduce cardiovascular events in high-risk patients  

risk factors  
- History of cardiovascular disease  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Smoking  

causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Myocardial infarction  
- Ischaemic stroke  
- Peripheral artery disease  
- Venous thromboembolism  

pathophysiology  
- Inhibition of platelet aggregation to prevent thrombus formation  
- Reduction of thromboxane A2 synthesis or blockade of platelet receptors  
- Leads to improved blood flow and reduced occlusion of vessels  

signs and symptoms  
- Angina or chest pain  
- Shortness of breath  
- Weakness or numbness in limbs  
- Symptoms of stroke (e.g., confusion, difficulty speaking)  
- Bruising or bleeding tendencies  

investigations  
- Complete blood count  
- Liver function tests  
- Assessment of renal function  
- Platelet function tests  
- Imaging studies (e.g., ultrasound, CT, MRI) where indicated  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Consideration of risk factors and symptoms  
- Laboratory tests to assess platelet function and bleeding risk  
- Imaging to rule out other conditions if necessary  
- Response to treatment assessed regularly  

management  
- Continue anti-platelet therapy as indicated  
  - Consider dual therapy in high-risk patients  
  - Monitor for signs of bleeding  
- Educate patients on lifestyle modifications  
  - Smoking cessation  
  - Dietary changes to reduce cardiovascular risk  
- Regular follow-ups to assess efficacy and safety  
  - Monitor for adverse effects  
  - Review necessity of ongoing therapy  
- Emergency protocols for bleeding complications  
  - Prepare for transfusion if necessary  
  - Provide reversal agents where applicable  
- Referral to specialists if indicated  
  - Cardiology for high-risk cases  
  - Haematology for bleeding disorders  







Pelvic inflammatory disease

background  
- Pelvic inflammatory disease is an infection of the female reproductive organs, commonly arising from sexually transmitted infections  

risk factors  
- Sexually transmitted infections such as *Chlamydia trachomatis* and *Neisseria gonorrhoeae*  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Previous history of pelvic inflammatory disease  
- Use of intrauterine devices (IUDs)  
- Young age, particularly under 25 years  

causes  
- Ascending infection from the cervix to the uterus, fallopian tubes, and ovaries  
- Most commonly caused by *Chlamydia trachomatis* and *Neisseria gonorrhoeae*  
- Other bacterial infections including *Mycoplasma* and *Ureaplasma* species  
- Post-abortal or post-surgical infections  
- Vaginal flora dysbiosis  

pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to inflammation of the reproductive organs  
- Formation of scar tissue and adhesions  
- Can result in chronic pelvic pain, infertility, or ectopic pregnancy  
- Immune response may exacerbate tissue damage  
- Persistent infection can cause tubo-ovarian abscess formation  

signs and symptoms  
- Lower abdominal or pelvic pain  
- Fever and chills  
- Abnormal vaginal discharge  
- Painful intercourse (dyspareunia)  
- Irregular menstrual bleeding  

investigations  
- Clinical examination and history taking  
- Urinalysis to rule out urinary tract infection  
- Swabs for *Chlamydia* and *Gonorrhoea* testing  
- Transvaginal ultrasound for signs of abscess or inflammation  
- Pelvic ultrasound or MRI if complications are suspected  

diagnosis  
- Pelvic pain with one or more physical findings of pelvic organ tenderness  
- Positive laboratory tests for sexually transmitted infection  
- Exclusion of other causes such as appendicitis or ectopic pregnancy  
- Imaging suggesting pelvic inflammatory disease  
- Response to antibiotics as a supportive diagnostic criterion  

management  
- Start broad-spectrum antibiotics as per NICE guidelines  
  - Consider oral or parenteral regimens based on severity  
  - Treatment for partners to prevent reinfection  
- Offer pain relief and supportive care  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) for pain  
  - Consider hospital admission for severe cases or abscess  
  








Peptic ulcer disease and gastritis

Background  
- Peptic ulcer disease and gastritis are common gastrointestinal disorders characterised by inflammation and ulceration of the gastric and duodenal mucosa 

Risk factors  
- Helicobacter pylori infection  
- Chronic use of non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
- Smoking  
- Excessive alcohol consumption  
- Stress

Causes  
- H. pylori infection  
- NSAID-induced mucosal damage  
- Hypersecretion of gastric acid  
- Malignancies (rarely)  
- Zollinger-Ellison syndrome (rarely)  

Pathophysiology  
- Disruption of the gastric mucosal barrier leads to erosion and ulceration  
- Increased gastric acid secretion damages underlying tissues  
- H. pylori promotes inflammation and ulcer development  

Signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain, often relieved by food or antacids  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Bloating and belching  
- Dyspepsia  
- Possibly gastrointestinal bleeding  

Investigations  
- Upper gastrointestinal endoscopy  
- Testing for H. pylori (urea breath test, stool antigen test)  
- Complete blood count (to check for anaemia)  
- Upper GI series with contrast (rarely used)  
- Transabdominal ultrasound or CT (to exclude complications)

Diagnosis  
- Presence of mucosal lesions confirmed by endoscopy  
- Evidence of infection with H. pylori  
- Exclusion of malignancy in cases of non-healing ulcers  
- Evaluation of risk factors and clinical symptoms  
- Consideration of a differential diagnosis for abdominal pain  

Management  
- Eradication therapy for H. pylori (triple therapy with PPI, amoxicillin, and clarithromycin)  
  - Duration typically 7-14 days  
  - Assess treatment success with follow-up testing  
- Acid suppression therapy with proton pump inhibitors (PPIs)  
  - Heal ulcers and alleviate symptoms  
  - Long-term maintenance in recurrent cases  
- Lifestyle modifications (smoking cessation, alcohol reduction, dietary changes)  
  - Promote mucosal healing  
  - Reduce recurrence risk  
- Avoidance of NSAIDs and use of alternative pain relief  
  - Use gastroprotective agents if NSAIDs are necessary  
  - Regular review of medication use with patient  
- Surgical intervention for complications (perforation, bleeding, obstruction)  
  - Indicated when conservative management fails  
  - Surgical options include vagotomy and antrectomy







Perianal abscesses and fistulae

Background  
- Perianal abscesses and fistulae are common conditions that arise from infection in the anal region

Risk factors  
- Previous history of anal abscess or fistula  
- Inflammatory bowel disease, especially Crohn's disease  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Immunocompromised states  
- Poor hygiene or anal trauma  

Causes  
- Anal fissures leading to infection  
- Obstruction of anal glands  
- Damage to the anal sphincter due to surgery or trauma  
- Chronic inflammatory conditions  
- Foreign bodies or infections  

Pathophysiology  
- Infection leads to the formation of pus in the perianal region  
- Perforation of the abscess may create an abnormal connection or fistula  
- Fistula formation occurs due to chronic inflammation and inadequate drainage  
- Abscesses can disrupt normal anatomic structures and lead to further complications  
- The inflammatory response may be exacerbated by underlying diseases  

Signs and symptoms  
- Severe pain in the anal region, especially with defecation  
- Swelling and tenderness in the perianal area  
- Fever and malaise  
- Discharge of pus or blood from the anus  
- Itching and irritation around the anal opening  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination including digital rectal examination  
- Anoscopy or proctoscopy to visualise the anal canal  
- Ultrasound or MRI for complex fistulas or abscesses  
- Fistulography if a fistula is suspected  
- Blood tests to rule out infection or other underlying issues  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on history and physical examination findings  
- Confirmation through imaging if complex abscesses or fistulas are present  
- Identification of any underlying predisposing factors or diseases  
- Exclusion of other causes of perianal pain or discharge  
- Multi-disciplinary input for complex cases  

Management  
- Surgical drainage of perianal abscess  
  - Can be done under local or general anaesthesia  
  - Incision and drainage should be performed to prevent recurrence  
- Fistula management  
  - Surgical intervention may involve fistulotomy or seton placement  
  - Management tailored to the complexity of the fistula  
- Antibiotics in case of systemic symptoms or cellulitis  
  - Empirical treatment may include broad-spectrum antibiotics  
  - Adjustments made based on culture results  
- Analgesia for pain control  
  - Non-opioid analgesics as first-line treatment  
  - Opioid analgesics for severe pain if necessary  
- Follow-up and monitoring for recurrence  
  - Regular check-ups for healing and symptom resolution  
  - Education on hygiene and preventative measures to reduce recurrence  







Pericardial disease

background  
- Pericardial disease involves inflammation or other disorders of the pericardium, the fibrous sac surrounding the heart  

risk factors  
- Previous history of cardiac surgery or injury  
- Autoimmune diseases such as lupus or rheumatoid arthritis  
- Viral infections, particularly by the Coxsackie virus  
- Chronic renal failure or kidney disease  
- Tuberculosis  

causes  
- Viral infections  
- Bacterial infections  
- Autoimmune conditions  
- Trauma or post-surgical complications  
- Malignancy  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the pericardium leading to pericardial effusion  
- Thickening of the pericardium resulting in constrictive pericarditis  
- Adhesions between the pericardium and myocardium can occur  
- Impaired diastolic filling of the heart due to pressure from effusion  
- Altered haemodynamics affecting cardiac output  

signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain, often sharp and worsens with deep breathing  
- Fever and malaise  
- Dyspnoea or difficulty breathing  
- Pericardial friction rub upon auscultation  
- Symptoms of fluid overload in cases of significant effusion  

investigations  
- ECG showing nonspecific ST elevation and PR depression  
- Chest X-ray to assess cardiomegaly or effusion  
- Echocardiography for visualisation of effusion and cardiac function  
- Blood tests for inflammatory markers and infection  
- Cardiac MRI for detailed imaging if required  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment including history and physical examination  
- Noted presence of chest pain and characteristic friction rub  
- Imaging studies confirm pericardial effusion or other structural changes  
- Laboratory tests suggest inflammation or infection  
- Diagnostic criteria may include ruling out other cardiac conditions  

management  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for mild cases  
  - Consider colchicine for recurrent pericarditis  
  - Monitor for response and side effects  
- Corticosteroids for severe or autoimmune-related cases  
  - Taper carefully to prevent exacerbation  
  - Evaluate underlying causes for targeted therapy  
- Pericardiocentesis for symptomatic effusion  
  - Assess fluid characteristics for underlying pathology  
  - Can provide rapid symptomatic relief  
- Surgical options like pericardiectomy for constrictive pericarditis  
  - Consider in refractory cases with significant symptoms  
  - Requires careful preoperative evaluation  
- Regular follow-up to monitor for recurrence and complications  
  - Educate patients on warning signs of worsening symptoms  
  - Coordinate multi-disciplinary care as needed  







Periorbital and orbital cellulitis

background  
- Periorbital and orbital cellulitis are infections affecting the eyelid and the surrounding tissues or the orbit itself  

risk factors  
- Sinusitis  
- Recent upper respiratory tract infection  
- Trauma to the eye or surrounding area  
- Immune compromise  
- Dermal infections in adjacent areas  

causes  
- Bacterial infections, mainly Staphylococcus aureus and Streptococcus species  
- Fungal infections in immunocompromised patients  
- Extension from adjacent infections like sinusitis or dental abscess  
- Orbital complications from conjunctivitis or blepharitis  
- Rarely, viral infections  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of soft tissues around the eye  
- Microbial invasion often from local sources or haematogenous spread  
- Potential for involvement of the orbital space leading to complications  

signs and symptoms  
- Swelling and redness around the eye  
- Pain and tenderness on palpation  
- Fever and malaise  
- Decreased visual acuity or double vision in severe cases  
- Proptosis or restricted eye movement in orbital cellulitis  

investigations  
- Clinical examination of the eye and surrounding structures  
- Blood cultures to identify causative organism  
- CT scan of the orbit/sinuses for orbital cellulitis  
- Complete blood count to assess for leukocytosis  
- MRI if complications or abscess suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination findings  
- Symptoms consistent with either periorbital or orbital cellulitis  
- Differentiation based on ocular signs indicating orbital involvement  
- Risk factors and pre-existing conditions considered  
- Rule out other causes of swelling or pain  

management  
- Admit to hospital for monitoring in suspected orbital cellulitis  
  - Intravenous antibiotics directed against common pathogens  
  - Surgical intervention if abscess or severe complication develops  
- Outpatient management for uncomplicated periorbital cellulitis  
  - Oral antibiotics as per NICE guidelines  
  - Follow-up to ensure resolution and monitor for complications  







Peripheral nerve injuries/palsies

background  
- Peripheral nerve injuries can lead to motor, sensory, and sometimes autonomic dysfunction, affecting quality of life  

risk factors  
- Trauma or injury to limbs  
- Compression from tumours or other lesions  
- Systemic conditions such as diabetes  
- Prolonged pressure on nerves  
- Alcoholism or nutritional deficiencies  

causes  
- Acute trauma such as fractures or dislocations  
- Overuse injuries or repetitive strain  
- Inflammatory conditions like Guillain-Barré syndrome  
- Ischaemia due to vascular disease  
- Infectious processes affecting nerves  

pathophysiology  
- Damage to nerve fibres leading to impaired nerve signal transmission  
- Either traumatic injury or compression can disrupt axonal continuity  
- Results in degeneration of the distal nerve segment  
- Schwann cells assist in regeneration if the nerve sheath is intact  
- Recovery depends on nerve type and injury severity  

signs and symptoms  
- Weakness or paralysis in the affected muscle groups  
- Altered sensation including numbness or tingling  
- Pain or discomfort in the region innervated  
- Reflex alterations or absence of reflexes  
- Atrophy of muscles over time  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and neurological examination  
- Nerve conduction studies to assess function  
- Electromyography (EMG) to evaluate muscle activity  
- Imaging with MRI or ultrasound to identify structural lesions  
- Blood tests for underlying conditions  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical history and neurological examination findings  
- Confirmed with nerve conduction studies and EMG results  
- Classification of injury severity (neuropraxia, axonotmesis, neurotmesis)  
- Exclude secondary causes such as systemic diseases  
- Assess functional impairment and disability  

management  
- Conservative measures including rest and physiotherapy  
  - Tailored rehabilitation programmes to restore function  
  - Pain management with medications like NSAIDs  
- Surgical intervention for severe cases  
  - Decompression or repair of affected nerves  
  - Reconstruction if nerve gaps are present  
- Management of any underlying conditions  
  - Control blood glucose in diabetic patients  
  - Nutritional support if deficiencies are present  
- Regular follow-up to monitor recovery progress  
- Use of assistive devices if necessary for mobility and functionality  







Peripheral vascular disease

Background  
- Peripheral vascular disease is a common circulatory problem that affects blood flow to the limbs

Risk factors  
- Smoking  
- Diabetes  
- Hypertension  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Age over 50  

Causes  
- Atherosclerosis  
- Thromboembolism  
- Vasculitis  
- Trauma  
- Inflammation  

Pathophysiology  
- Reduced blood flow due to narrowed or blocked arteries  
- Ischemia leads to inadequate oxygen supply to tissues  
- May progress to tissue necrosis if untreated  

Signs and symptoms  
- Intermittent claudication  
- Rest pain  
- Weak or absent pulses in affected limbs  
- Coldness in the lower extremities  
- Non-healing wounds or ulcers  

Investigations  
- Ankle-brachial index (ABI)  
- Duplex ultrasound  
- CT angiography  
- MRI angiography  
- Blood tests for cholesterol and glucose levels  

Diagnosis  
- Signs and symptoms of claudication  
- ABI less than 0.9 indicating possible PVD  
- Clinical assessment of risk factors  
- Imaging studies confirming arterial obstruction  
- Exclusion of other conditions causing limb symptoms  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Smoking cessation  
  - Regular exercise program  
- Pharmacological therapy  
  - Antiplatelet agents (e.g., aspirin)  
  - Statins to manage dyslipidaemia  
- Surgical interventions  
  - Angioplasty and stenting  
  - Bypass grafting for severe cases  
- Management of comorbidities  
  - Tight glycaemic control in diabetes  
  - Blood pressure optimisation  
- Regular follow-up  
  - Monitor for disease progression  
  - Assess for complications like critical limb ischaemia







Peritonitis

background  
- Inflammation of the peritoneum, often due to infection or irritants

risk factors  
- Presence of a perforated abdominal organ  
- History of abdominal surgery  
- Underlying conditions like cirrhosis or pancreatitis  
- Immunosuppressed states  
- Advanced age

causes  
- Bacterial infections, e.g. *Escherichia coli* or *Streptococcus pneumoniae*  
- Perforation of hollow viscera (e.g. appendix, stomach)  
- Peritoneal dialysis-related infections  
- Trauma or injuries to the abdomen  
- Chemical irritation from bile or pancreatic secretions

pathophysiology  
- Bacterial invasion and inflammation of the peritoneal lining  
- Increased vascular permeability leading to fluid accumulation  
- Cytokine release exacerbating inflammation  
- Potential development of abdominal compartment syndrome  
- Risk of sepsis and multi-organ failure

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain and tenderness  
- Fever and chills  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Abdominal distension  
- Altered bowel habits, either constipation or diarrhoea

investigations  
- Blood tests (full blood count, inflammatory markers)  
- Abdominal ultrasound to assess fluid collections  
- CT scan for diagnosis of intra-abdominal pathology  
- Peritoneal fluid analysis if indicated  
- Diagnostic laparoscopy for direct visualisation  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Imaging studies to identify underlying cause  
- Presence of peritoneal signs on examination  
- Laboratory findings indicating infection or inflammation  
- Exclusion of other abdominal pathologies

management  
- Initiate broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotics  
  - Commonly used regimens include piperacillin-tazobactam or cefotaxime plus metronidazole  
- Surgical intervention to address underlying cause  
  - Depending on the patient, this may include laparotomy or laparoscopy  
- Monitor vital signs and fluid status closely  
  - Assess for signs of sepsis and ensure IV fluid resuscitation is adequate  
- Manage complications or co-existing conditions  
  - Consult specialists as needed for support in severe cases  
- Provide supportive care and reassess treatment response  
  - Adjust care based on clinical improvement and laboratory results  







Personality disorder

Background  
- Personality disorders are enduring patterns of thoughts, feelings, and behaviours that deviate from cultural expectations

Risk factors  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Childhood trauma or abuse  
- Chronic stress or neglect  
- Family history of mental health issues  
- Substance misuse  

Causes  
- Complex interplay of genetic and environmental factors  
- Neurobiological abnormalities affecting emotion regulation  
- Dysfunctional family dynamics  
- Cultural and social influences  
- Major life stresses or conflicts  

Pathophysiology  
- Alterations in brain structure and function  
- Dysregulation of neurotransmitters like serotonin and dopamine  
- Inflexible behavioural patterns and maladaptive coping mechanisms  
- Impaired interpersonal skills  
- Increased sensitivity to stress  

Signs and symptoms  
- Pervasive distrust and suspicion  
- Emotional instability and mood swings  
- Difficulty in maintaining relationships  
- Impulsivity and risk-taking behaviour  
- Distorted self-image  

Investigations  
- Comprehensive clinical interview  
- Standardised assessment tools (e.g., SCID-5, Millon Clinical Multiaxial Inventory)  
- Psychological testing to assess personality traits  
- Evaluation of co-existing mental health conditions  
- Review of personal and family medical history  

Diagnosis  
- Identification of patterns as per DSM-5 or ICD-10 criteria  
- Evidence of symptoms present since adolescence or early adulthood  
- Symptoms causing significant distress or impairment in functioning  
- Exclusion of other mental health conditions  
- Comprehensive assessment involving multiple sources  

Management  
- Psychotherapy as first-line treatment  
  - Cognitive-behavioural therapy  
  - Dialectical behaviour therapy  
- Medication may be considered for associated symptoms  
  - Antidepressants for mood instability  
  - Antipsychotics for severe agitation or paranoia  
- Supportive therapies, such as group therapy  
  - Promoting social skills  
  - Enhancing emotional regulation  
- Lifestyle modifications encouraged  
  - Regular physical activity  
  - Mindfulness and stress management strategies  
- Regular follow-up and assessment of treatment efficacy  
  - Adjustments to treatment plan as needed  
  - Engagement with support networks  







Pituitary tumours

Background  
- Pituitary tumours are abnormal growths in the pituitary gland, often affecting hormone levels

Risk factors  
- Family history of pituitary or endocrine tumours  
- Genetic syndromes such as Multiple Endocrine Neoplasia type 1  
- Age (most common in adults aged 30-50)  
- Previous cranial radiation therapy  
- Gender (more common in females)  

Causes  
- Adenomas (benign tumours)  
- Craniopharyngiomas (benign but locally aggressive)  
- Pituitary carcinomas (malignant, rare)  
- Secretion of excess hormones leading to hyperplasia  
- Genetic mutations (e.g., GNAS mutation)  

Pathophysiology  
- Tumour growth disrupts normal pituitary hormone secretion  
- Increased or decreased levels of pituitary hormones depending on tumour type  
- Compression of surrounding structures (optic chiasm, hypothalamus)  

Signs and symptoms  
- Headaches  
- Visual disturbances (e.g., bitemporal hemianopia)  
- Hormonal imbalances (e.g., Cushing's disease, acromegaly, hyperprolactinaemia)  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Menstrual irregularities  

Investigations  
- Serum hormone levels (to assess hypersecretion)  
- MRI of the sella turcica (for localisation and characterisation)  
- Visual field testing  
- CT scan if MRI is contraindicated  
- Pituitary function tests  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation based on signs and symptoms  
- Imaging studies confirming tumour presence and effect on surrounding structures  
- Hormonal assays supporting diagnosis of specific syndromes  
- Histopathological examination in cases of surgery  
- Differential diagnosis to exclude other causes  

Management  
- Surgical resection if indicated  
  - Transsphenoidal approach for adenomas  
  - Craniotomy for larger or invasive tumours  
- Medical therapy for hormone-secreting tumours  
  - Dopamine agonists for prolactinomas  
  - Somatostatin analogues for acromegaly  
- Radiation therapy if surgery is not feasible or complete  
  - Stereotactic radiosurgery (e.g., Gamma Knife)  
  - Conventional radiation for residual disease  
- Regular monitoring of hormone levels and visual field  
  - Follow-up imaging to check for recurrence  
  - Endocrinology referral for hormone management  
- Multidisciplinary team approach for optimal care  
  - Involvement of neurosurgeons, endocrinologists, and oncologists  
  - Psychosocial support for patients and families







Placenta praevia

background  
- Placenta praevia is a condition characterised by the abnormal implantation of the placenta over or near the cervix

risk factors  
- Previous placenta praevia  
- Previous uterine surgery or caesarean section  
- Multiparity  
- Advanced maternal age  
- Smoking

causes  
- Abnormal implantation of the placenta  
- Multiple pregnancies  
- Uterine abnormalities  
- History of uterine surgery  
- Presence of fibroids

pathophysiology  
- Implantation of the placenta occurs in the lower uterine segment  
- Incomplete vascularisation leading to bleeding  
- Pre-term delivery may occur due to placental separation  
- Risk of maternal morbidity due to haemorrhage  
- Effects on foetal growth and development

signs and symptoms  
- Painless vaginal bleeding in late pregnancy  
- Possible abdominal cramping or contractions  
- Signs of foetal distress  
- Pre-term labour symptoms  
- Abnormal foetal position in severe cases

investigations  
- Transabdominal ultrasound to assess placental location  
- Transvaginal ultrasound for better visualisation  
- MRI in complex cases  
- Continuous monitoring of maternal and foetal heart rates  
- Blood tests to monitor for anaemia and coagulation

diagnosis  
- Confirmed by ultrasound showing placental localisation  
- Reassess at 36 weeks gestation if initially diagnosed  
- Diagnosis based on the degree of cervical coverage  
- Clinical history and presentation considered  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other causes of bleeding

management  
- Expectant management for stable patients  
  - Close monitoring for bleeding and uterine tone  
  - Discuss delivery planning with the patient  
- Delivery planning based on gestational age and bleeding  
  - Elective caesarean section if placenta covers the cervix  
  - Vaginal delivery may be possible if placenta marginally covers the cervix  
- Administration of corticosteroids for foetal lung maturity  
  - Consider if delivery is anticipated before 34 weeks  
  - Assess risks versus benefits  
- Monitor for complications during and after delivery  
  - Ensure availability of blood products for potential transfusions  
  - Manage postpartum haemorrhage promptly  
- Provide psychological support to the mother  
  - Discuss the implications of placenta praevia for future pregnancies  
  - Offer counselling if needed







Placental abruption

background  
- Placental abruption is a serious pregnancy complication where the placenta detaches from the uterus before delivery  

risk factors  
- Previous history of placental abruption  
- Trauma to the abdomen  
- Hypertension or pre-eclampsia  
- Advanced maternal age  
- Smoking or substance abuse  

causes  
- Vascular disorders  
- Trauma or injury  
- Excessive uterine contractions  
- Maternal hypertension  
- Inherited blood clotting disorders  

pathophysiology  
- Premature separation of the placenta leads to bleeding between the placenta and uterine wall  
- Loss of placental blood supply can result in fetal distress or stillbirth  
- May stimulate uterine contractions causing pain and complicating delivery  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of abdominal pain  
- Vaginal bleeding, which can be dark or bright red  
- Uterine tenderness and rigidity  
- Back pain or contractions  
- Fetal heart rate abnormalities  

investigations  
- Maternal history and physical examination  
- Ultrasound to assess placental position and fetal wellbeing  
- Non-stress test or cardiotocography for fetal monitoring  
- Complete blood count to check for anaemia  
- Coagulation profile to assess clotting factors  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on symptoms and history  
- Ultrasound confirms separation if placental abruption is suspected  
- Consider differential diagnosis for bleeding in pregnancy  
- Monitor fetal heart rate for abnormal patterns  
- Rule out other causes of abdominal pain and bleeding  

management  
- Immediate assessment and stabilisation of the mother and fetus  
  - Administer IV fluids and monitor vital signs  
  - Prepare for potential delivery depending on gestational age and severity  
- Delivery in cases of severe abruption or fetal distress  
  - Vaginal delivery for mild cases if stable  
  - Caesarean delivery for severe cases or fetal compromise  
- Monitor for complications postpartum  
  - Watch for signs of PPH (postpartum haemorrhage)  
  - Provide emotional support and counselling for the mother  
- Encourage future prenatal care and smoking cessation  
  - Discuss the possible implications for future pregnancies  
  - Offer referral to counselling services for substance use if needed  







Pneumonia

background  
- Pneumonia is an infection that inflames the air sacs in one or both lungs

risk factors  
- Age over 65 years  
- Smoking history  
- Chronic respiratory diseases  
- Immunosuppression  
- Recent respiratory infections  

causes  
- Bacterial pathogens like *Streptococcus pneumoniae*  
- Viral infections such as influenza  
- Fungal infections like *Histoplasma*  
- Aspiration of food or liquids  
- Hospital-acquired organisms like *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*  

pathophysiology  
- Inhalation or aspiration of pathogens leads to inflammation  
- Alveoli fill with fluid and pus  
- Gas exchange is impaired, causing hypoxaemia  
- Inflammatory mediators are released, worsening lung function  
- May lead to complications such as abscesses or pleural effusions  

signs and symptoms  
- Cough with sputum production  
- Fever and chills  
- Shortness of breath  
- Chest pain that worsens with breathing  
- Fatigue and malaise  

investigations  
- Chest X-ray to assess consolidation  
- Sputum culture for bacterial identification  
- Blood cultures to detect systemic infection  
- Complete blood count for white cell elevation  
- CRP levels for inflammation assessment  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Radiological confirmation of pneumonia  
- Microbiological confirmation if severe or atypical  
- Consideration of patient's history and risk factors  
- Assessment of severity using CURB-65 score  

management  
- Antibiotic therapy based on severity and guidelines  
  - Community-acquired pneumonia: Amoxicillin or doxycycline  
  - Hospital-acquired pneumonia: Piperacillin-tazobactam or ceftriaxone  
- Supportive care measures  
  - Oxygen therapy for hypoxaemia  
  - Fluid management for hydration  
- Vaccination for prevention  
  - Pneumococcal vaccine in at-risk populations  
  - Influenza vaccination annually  
- Follow-up to monitor recovery and complications  
  - Re-assess within 48-72 hours of treatment  
  - Consider referral for persistent or severe cases  
- Education on smoking cessation and lung health  
  - Provide resources for quitting smoking  
  - Encourage pulmonary rehabilitation for chronic lung conditions  







Pneumothorax

background  
- Pneumothorax is the presence of air in the pleural space, leading to lung collapse  

risk factors  
- Smoking history  
- Previous lung diseases, e.g., COPD  
- Tall, young males  
- Family history of pneumothorax  
- Sudden changes in atmospheric pressure  

causes  
- Spontaneous pneumothorax, either primary or secondary  
- Trauma to the chest  
- Mechanical ventilation with positive pressure  
- Iatrogenic causes from medical procedures  
- Lung disease, e.g., cystic fibrosis, tuberculosis  

pathophysiology  
- Air enters the pleural cavity, disrupting the negative pressure  
- Lung cannot expand fully, leading to atelectasis  
- Decreased lung compliance and impaired gas exchange  
- Mediastinal shift may occur if enough air accumulates  
- Can lead to respiratory distress and hypoxia  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden sharp chest pain  
- Dyspnoea  
- Decreased breath sounds on affected side  
- Tachypnoea  
- Cyanosis in severe cases  

investigations  
- Chest X-ray to confirm presence of air  
- CT scan for complex cases  
- Ultrasound for rapid assessment in emergencies  
- Arterial blood gas (ABG) analysis for respiratory function  
- Spirometry if chronic lung disease suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical examination revealing decreased breath sounds  
- Chest X-ray shows visceral pleural line  
- CT scan confirms pneumothorax presence  
- Assessment of severity based on size and symptoms  
- Consider patient history and risk factors  

management  
- Observation for small, asymptomatic pneumothorax  
  - Follow-up with repeat imaging  
  - Educate on warning signs  
- Needle decompression for tension pneumothorax  
  - Immediate relief with needle thoracostomy  
  - Follow with chest drain insertion  
- Chest drain for larger or symptomatic pneumothorax  
  - Seldinger technique for insertion  
  - Connect to underwater seal drain  
- Surgery for recurrent or persistent pneumothorax  
  - Pleurodesis to adhere lung to thoracic wall  
  - Video-assisted thoracoscopic surgery (VATS) may be indicated  
- Discharge with advice for follow-up  
  - Avoid flying or scuba diving for weeks  
  - Educate patient on symptoms indicating complications  







Polycythaemia

Background  
- A condition characterised by an increase in red blood cell mass, leading to increased blood viscosity

Risk factors  
- Male sex  
- Older age  
- Smoking  
- High altitude exposure  
- Chronic hypoxia  

Causes  
- Primary causes including polycythaemia vera  
- Secondary causes related to chronic hypoxia, such as COPD or sleep apnoea  
- Tumours producing erythropoietin  
- Renal disease  
- Genetic mutations affecting erythropoiesis  

Pathophysiology  
- Increased red blood cell production leading to hyperviscosity  
- Increased blood volume results in impaired circulation  
- Potential for thrombosis, hypertension, and organ damage  
- Hypercellularity of the bone marrow  
- Altered blood flow dynamics  

Signs and symptoms  
- Headaches  
- Dizziness or light-headedness  
- Flushing of the skin  
- Visual disturbances  
- Fatigue  

Investigations  
- Complete blood count demonstrating elevated haemoglobin and haematocrit  
- Erythropoietin levels  
- Bone marrow biopsy if primary cause is suspected  
- Oxygen saturation and carboxyhaemoglobin levels  
- JAK2 V617F mutation testing  

Diagnosis  
- Elevated haemoglobin (>16.5 g/dL in men, >16 g/dL in women)  
- Increased haematocrit (>49% in men, >48% in women)  
- Presence of secondary causes ruled out  
- Confirmation of polycythaemia vera through further tests if indicated  
- Classification into primary or secondary polycythaemia  

Management  
- Phlebotomy to reduce red blood cell mass  
  - Target haematocrit <0.45  
  - Frequency adjusted based on clinical response  
- Low-dose aspirin for thromboprophylaxis  
  - Reduces risk of thrombotic events  
  - Advised unless contraindicated  
- Hydroxycarbamide for high-risk patients  
  - Reduces white blood cell count  
  - To be monitored for side effects  
- Management of underlying causes in secondary polycythaemia  
  - Address chronic hypoxia or tumours producing erythropoietin  
  - Multi-disciplinary approach as needed  
- Regular follow-up for monitoring and adjustment of treatment  
  - Assessing for complications  
  - Continuous evaluation of treatment efficacy  







Polymyalgia rheumatica

background  
- Polymyalgia rheumatica is an inflammatory disorder causing muscle pain and stiffness, primarily in older adults  

risk factors  
- Age over 50 years  
- Female sex  
- Northern European descent  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  
- Presence of giant cell arteritis  

causes  
- Exact cause unknown  
- Likely involves immune system dysregulation  
- Genetic predisposition may play a role  
- Environmental factors may trigger the condition  
- Associated with other autoimmune diseases  

pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the muscles and joints  
- Synovitis and bursitis due to immune response  
- Release of pro-inflammatory cytokines  
- Local tissue damage and pain  
- Stiffness resulting from inflammation  

signs and symptoms  
- Bilateral shoulder pain and stiffness  
- Hip pain and stiffness  
- Morning stiffness lasting more than 30 minutes  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Weight loss  

investigations  
- Blood tests showing elevated ESR or CRP  
- Complete blood count to rule out anaemia  
- Imaging studies to exclude other conditions  
- Ultrasound can help detect subclinical inflammation  
- Temporal artery biopsy if giant cell arteritis is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on signs and symptoms  
- Exclusion of other causes of pain and stiffness  
- Elevated inflammatory markers supporting diagnosis  
- Duration of symptoms typically longer than 2 weeks  
- Response to corticosteroids strengthens diagnosis  

management  
- Start corticosteroids (e.g., prednisolone)  
   - Initial dose of 15-20 mg daily  
   - Taper over time based on response  
- Consider methotrexate for patients with relapsing disease  
   - Use as a steroid-sparing agent  
   - Monitor for side effects and regular blood tests  
- Encourage exercise and physiotherapy  
   - To improve mobility and reduce stiffness  
   - Tailored programmes for individual patients  
- Regular monitoring for disease activity  
   - Assess response to treatment  
   - Adjust medication as necessary  
- Educate patients about the condition  
   - Importance of adherence to treatment  
   - Discuss potential complications and follow-up requirements  







Postpartum haemorrhage

Background  
- Postpartum haemorrhage is excessive bleeding following childbirth, defined as blood loss greater than 500ml after vaginal delivery or 1000ml after cesarean section 

Risk factors  
- Previous history of postpartum haemorrhage  
- Uterine atony  
- Prolonged labour or high parity  
- Retained placental tissue  
- Coagulation disorders  

Causes  
- Uterine atony  
- Trauma to the birth canal  
- Retained products of conception  
- Coagulation abnormalities  
- Uterine rupture  

Pathophysiology  
- Uterine atony leads to inadequate contraction of the uterus, failing to compress blood vessels  
- Trauma may result in direct injury to blood vessels and tissues  
- Retained placental tissue prevents normal uterine contraction  
- Coagulation defects contribute to uncontrolled bleeding  
- Uterine rupture can sever major blood vessels  

Signs and symptoms  
- Excessive vaginal bleeding  
- Hypotension and tachycardia  
- Pallor and dizziness  
- Decreased urine output  
- Signs of shock  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment of blood loss and vital signs  
- Blood tests: FBC, coagulation profile  
- Ultrasound to assess retained products of conception  
- Crossmatch for potential blood transfusion  
- CT if rupture is suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation of blood loss exceeding 500ml or 1000ml  
- Assessment of patient’s vital signs and symptoms  
- Identification of potential aetiology through history and examination  
- Imaging if indicated to discover retained products or trauma  
- Lab results indicating anaemia or abnormal coagulation  

Management  
- Immediate resuscitation with IV fluids and blood products  
  - Ensure early identification of fluid loss  
  - Monitor vital signs closely for shock  
- Uterine massage and oxytocics for uterine atony  
  - Administer oxytocin as frontline treatment  
  - Consider additional uterotonics if needed  
- Surgical intervention if bleeding persists  
  - Uterine compression sutures or B-Lynch  
  - Hysterectomy as a last resort  
- Address coagulopathy as necessary  
  - Administer clotting factors if indicated  
  - Correct underlying deficiencies  
- Close monitoring in a high dependency or critical care setting  
  - Continuous observation for ongoing bleeding  
  - Reassess interventions and patient response







Pre-eclampsia, gestational hypertension

background  
- Pre-eclampsia and gestational hypertension are pregnancy-related disorders characterised by high blood pressure and potential organ dysfunction

risk factors  
- Previous history of pre-eclampsia  
- Chronic hypertension  
- First pregnancy  
- Multiple pregnancies  
- Maternal age over 40 years  

causes  
- Unknown exact aetiology  
- Abnormal placentation leading to impaired blood flow  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Maternal immune response  
- Dysfunction of endothelial cells  

pathophysiology  
- Placental insufficiency results in release of factors causing systemic vascular endothelial dysfunction  
- Increased vascular permeability leads to proteinuria and oedema  
- Activation of the coagulation and inflammatory pathways  
- Systemic vasoconstriction contributes to hypertension  
- Possible progression to eclampsia  

signs and symptoms  
- Elevated blood pressure (≥ 140/90 mmHg)  
- Proteinuria or signs of end-organ dysfunction  
- Oedema (especially in hands and face)  
- Headaches  
- Visual disturbances  

investigations  
- Blood pressure measurement  
- Urine dipstick test for proteinuria  
- Blood tests (liver enzymes, renal function, platelet count)  
- Ultrasound for fetal growth and placental assessment  
- Assessment of fetal heart rate and wellbeing  

diagnosis  
- Elevated blood pressure after 20 weeks of gestation  
- Proteinuria present (urine dip ≥ 1+ on dipstick or 300 mg/24 hours)  
- Signs of end-organ dysfunction (e.g. elevated liver enzymes)  
- New-onset hypertension and proteinuria  
- Assess for severe features (e.g. severe hypertension or neurological symptoms)  

management  
- Antihypertensive therapy to maintain BP < 150/100 mmHg  
  - Methyldopa, labetalol, or nifedipine as first-line options  
- Close monitoring for maternal and fetal wellbeing  
  - Regular follow-up and assessment of blood pressure and fetal status  
- Consideration of corticosteroids for fetal lung maturity if preterm delivery is anticipated  
  - Administer betamethasone or dexamethasone if indicated  
- Planning for delivery based on severity and gestational age  
  - Induction or Caesarean section may be necessary in severe cases  
- Postpartum monitoring for resolution of hypertension and other complications  
  - Schedule follow-up appointments within 7-10 days after delivery  







Pressure sores

background  
- Pressure sores, also known as pressure ulcers or bedsores, are localised injuries to the skin and/or underlying tissue due to prolonged pressure  

risk factors  
- Immobility or reduced mobility  
- Advanced age  
- Poor nutritional status  
- Incontinence  
- Decreased sensation or neurological disorders  

causes  
- Sustained pressure leading to tissue ischaemia  
- Shear forces contributing to skin damage  
- Friction against surfaces  
- Moisture from bodily fluids  
- Poor blood flow due to underlying conditions  

pathophysiology  
- Pressure leads to reduced blood flow, causing hypoxia and tissue necrosis  
- Inflammatory response initiated by tissue injury  
- Breakdown of collagen and elastin in the dermis  
- Formation of ulcers as necrotic tissue develops  
- Systemic effects possible if infection occurs  

signs and symptoms  
- Discoloured or reddened skin  
- Skin temperature changes (hot or cool)  
- Pain or tenderness in the affected area  
- Open sores or ulcers  
- Potential for drainage or infection  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of the ulcer stage  
- Assessment of overall health and mobility  
- Nutritional evaluation  
- Imaging may be considered to assess for osteomyelitis  
- Laboratory tests if infection is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Based on visual assessment of ulcer staging (NICE guidelines)  
- Determine underlying risk factors and contributing conditions  
- Identify presence of infection or complications  
- Monitor for systemic signs indicating severity  
- Document and assess any improvement over time  

management  
- Relieve pressure through repositioning and use of pressure-relieving devices  
  - Routine repositioning every 2 hours  
  - Specialty mattresses or cushions  
- Wound care management including cleaning and dressing  
  - Use of appropriate dressings based on ulcer stage  
  - Consider topical therapies to promote healing  
- Nutritional support to optimise healing  
  - High-protein and calorie diet  
  - Supplements as needed  
- Infection management with antibiotics if indicated  
  - Assess for signs of systemic infection  
  - Tailor antibiotic therapy based on culture results  
- Education for patients and caregivers on prevention strategies  
  - Importance of skin care and moisture control  
  - Training on proper repositioning techniques  







Prostate cancer

Background  
- Prostate cancer is the most common cancer in men in the UK 

Risk factors  
- Age over 50  
- Family history of prostate cancer  
- Obesity  
- Ethnicity (higher in African-Caribbean men)  
- Genetic mutations (e.g. BRCA1/BRCA2)  

Causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Hormonal factors (androgens)  
- Environmental exposures (e.g. diet, chemicals)  
- Inflammation of the prostate  
- Age-related changes in prostate cells  

Pathophysiology  
- Prostate cancer originates from glandular cells in the prostate  
- Androgens promote growth of cancer cells  
- Tumour progression may lead to local invasion or metastasis  
- Alterations in cellular signalling pathways contribute to malignancy  
- Tumours can vary in aggressiveness based on Gleason score  

Signs and symptoms  
- Urinary symptoms (frequency, urgency, nocturia)  
- Pain during urination or ejaculation  
- Haematuria  
- Unexplained weight loss  
- Bone pain (indicating metastasis)  

Investigations  
- Digital rectal examination (DRE)  
- Prostate-specific antigen (PSA) test  
- Transrectal ultrasound (TRUS)  
- Prostate biopsy for histological confirmation  
- Imaging studies (MRI, bone scan) if metastasis is suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Clinically significant prostate cancer diagnosed based on DRE findings and PSA levels  
- Histological confirmation via biopsy showing cancerous cells  
- Staging determined by TNM classification  
- Gleason scoring assesses tumour differentiation  
- Multidisciplinary team involvement for comprehensive assessment  

Management  
- Active surveillance for low-risk tumours  
  - Regular PSA tests and follow-up  
  - Biopsies if needed to assess progression  
- Localised treatment options  
  - Radical prostatectomy or radiotherapy  
  - Hormonal therapy for advanced cases  
- Palliative care for metastatic disease  
  - Symptom management and psychosocial support  
  - Consideration of palliative radiotherapy or pain management strategies  
- Chemotherapy for hormone refractory cases  
  - Docetaxel or cabazitaxel as options  
  - Supportive care for side effects monitoring  
- Referral to specialist services as indicated  
  - Urology for surgical interventions  
  - Oncology for chemotherapy and advanced care strategies  







Psoriasis

Background  
- Psoriasis is a chronic autoimmune skin condition characterised by hyperproliferation of keratinocytes.

Risk factors  
- Family history of psoriasis  
- Smoking and alcohol consumption  
- Obesity  
- Stress and anxiety  
- Certain infections such as streptococcal throat infections  

Causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Immune system dysfunction  
- Environmental triggers  
- Psoriatic arthritis  
- Medications (e.g., lithium, beta-blockers)  

Pathophysiology  
- T-cell activation leads to inflammatory cytokine release  
- Keratinocyte hyperproliferation and abnormal differentiation  
- Angiogenesis in the dermis  
- Formation of psoriatic plaques  
- Autoantigen exposure exacerbates the immune response  

Signs and symptoms  
- Well-defined erythematous plaques with silvery scales  
- Pruritus and discomfort  
- Nail changes (pitting, onycholysis)  
- Psoriatic arthritis features (if present)  
- Skin lesions typically found on elbows, knees, scalp and lower back  

Investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and physical examination  
- Skin biopsy (if atypical features present)  
- Potential serological tests for associated conditions  
- Assessment of body surface area affected  
- Evaluation of joint involvement if symptoms present  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment confirming typical plaque morphology  
- Exclusion of other skin conditions (e.g., eczema, fungal infections)  
- Assessment of family history  
- Evaluation for comorbidities  
- Use of severity scoring tools like PASI  

Management  
- Topical treatments  
  - High-potency corticosteroids  
  - Vitamin D analogues 
- Phototherapy  
  - Narrowband UVB therapy  
  - PUVA (photochemotherapy) if severe  
- Systemic treatments  
  - Methotrexate or cyclosporine for moderate to severe cases  
  - Biologics targeting specific immune pathways  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Weight management to reduce flare-ups  
  - Smoking cessation and limiting alcohol intake  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring for treatment effectiveness and side effects  







Pulmonary embolism

Background  
- Pulmonary embolism is a blockage in the pulmonary arteries usually caused by blood clots from the legs or other parts of the body

Risk factors  
- Prolonged immobility  
- Recent surgery, especially orthopaedic  
- Active cancer or cancer treatments  
- Previous history of venous thromboembolism  
- Obesity

Causes  
- Deep vein thrombosis (most common)  
- Air embolism  
- Fat embolism  
- Amniotic fluid embolism  
- Calcium or foreign body embolism

Pathophysiology  
- Occlusion of pulmonary artery leading to impaired blood flow  
- Decreased gas exchange and potential lung tissue damage  
- Increased right ventricular workload and risk of heart failure  
- Inflammatory response and potential thrombolysis  
- Hypoxia due to ventilation-perfusion mismatch

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset dyspnoea  
- Chest pain, often pleuritic  
- Cough, possibly with haemoptysis  
- Tachycardia  
- Hypotension, sometimes leading to syncope

Investigations  
- D-dimer level to rule out thrombus presence  
- CT pulmonary angiography as the gold standard  
- Ultrasound of lower limbs for deep vein thrombosis  
- Chest X-ray to exclude other diagnoses  
- ECG to assess for right heart strain

Diagnosis  
- Clinical probability assessment using validated tools (e.g., Wells score)  
- Confirmation via CT pulmonary angiography or V/Q scan  
- D-dimer considered in low-probability cases  
- Exclusion of other potential causes of symptoms  
- Caution in pregnant women regarding radiation exposure

Management  
- Initiate anticoagulation therapy promptly  
  - Unfractionated heparin or low molecular weight heparin  
- Consider thrombolytics for severe cases or haemodynamic instability  
  - Use with caution given bleeding risk  
- Assess need for inferior vena cava filter with recurrent embolisms  
  - Reserved for patients contraindicated for anticoagulation  
- Long-term anticoagulation management for recurrent VTE  
  - Typically warfarin or direct oral anticoagulants  
- Patient education on signs of recurrence and follow-up  
  - Emphasise adherence to medication and lifestyle changes  







Pulmonary hypertension

background  
- Pulmonary hypertension is a condition characterised by elevated blood pressure in the pulmonary arteries.  

risk factors  
- Family history of pulmonary hypertension  
- Connective tissue diseases  
- Congenital heart defects  
- Chronic lung diseases  
- Obesity  

causes  
- Idiopathic pulmonary arterial hypertension  
- Left heart disease  
- Chronic thromboembolic pulmonary hypertension  
- Lung diseases and hypoxia  
- Chronic kidney disease  

pathophysiology  
- Increased resistance in pulmonary circulation  
- Endothelial dysfunction leading to vasoconstriction  
- Intimal hyperplasia and muscularisation of pulmonary arteries  
- Increased proliferation of vascular smooth muscle cells  
- Right ventricular strain and hypertrophy  

signs and symptoms  
- Dyspnoea on exertion  
- Fatigue  
- Chest pain  
- Palpitations  
- Syncope  

investigations  
- Echocardiogram to estimate pulmonary artery pressure  
- Right heart catheterisation for definitive diagnosis  
- Exercise testing to assess functional capacity  
- Pulmonary function tests  
- CT pulmonary angiography to identify thromboembolic causes  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed with right heart catheterisation showing elevated pulmonary artery pressure  
- Echocardiogram findings supporting pulmonary hypertension  
- Exclusion of secondary causes  
- Assessment of functional class using WHO classification  
- Review of clinical presentation and history  

management  
- Lifestyle modifications including exercise and dietary changes  
  - Encourage a low-sodium diet  
  - Promote regular physical activity within limits  
- Pharmacological therapy tailored to underlying cause  
  - Endothelin receptor antagonists for pulmonary arterial hypertension  
  - Phosphodiesterase-5 inhibitors for symptom relief  
- Oxygen therapy for patients with hypoxia  
  - Administer supplemental oxygen as needed  
  - Monitor oxygen saturation levels regularly  
- Advanced therapies for severe cases  
  - Consider lung transplantation  
  - Use of continuous intravenous prostacyclin therapy  
- Regular follow-up for monitoring and adjusting treatment  
  - Schedule routine echocardiograms  
  - Assess for disease progression and side effects of therapy  







Pyloric stenosis

background  
- Pyloric stenosis is a condition in infants characterised by the narrowing of the pylorus, leading to gastric outlet obstruction  

risk factors  
- Male gender  
- Family history of pyloric stenosis  
- Increased birth order (firstborn infants)  
- Premature birth  
- Exposure to macrolide antibiotics in early infancy  

causes  
- Congenital hypertrophy of pyloric muscle  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Abnormalities in the embryological development of the gastrointestinal tract  
- Possible association with maternal smoking during pregnancy  
- Male gender as a primary factor  

pathophysiology  
- Thickening of pyloric muscle leads to narrowing of the pylorus  
- Impaired gastric emptying results in projectile vomiting  
- Electrolyte imbalance due to persistent vomiting  
- Dehydration and weight loss in infants  
- Potential for metabolic alkalosis from loss of gastric acid  

signs and symptoms  
- Projectile vomiting after feeding  
- Constant hunger and irritability  
- Dehydration signs like dry skin and decreased urine output  
- Palpable "olive" mass in the epigastric region  
- Weight loss or failure to gain weight  

investigations  
- Clinical examination for signs of dehydration and palpable mass  
- Abdominal ultrasound to assess pyloric muscle thickness  
- Upper gastrointestinal series (barium swallow) if ultrasound inconclusive  
- Electolyte panel to assess for imbalances  
- Abdominal X-ray to rule out other conditions  

diagnosis  
- Diagnosis is primarily clinical based on history and examination findings  
- Confirmation through ultrasound showing thickened pylorus  
- Electolyte imbalances may support diagnosis  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other causes of vomiting  
- Consideration of medical history and symptoms  

management  
- Surgical intervention (pyloromyotomy) is definitive treatment for pyloric stenosis  
  - Typically performed laparoscopically  
  - Pre-operative hydration and electrolyte correction are essential  
- Post-operative care includes monitoring for feeding tolerance and complications  
  - Gradual reintroduction of feeding  
  - Assess for any signs of infection or obstruction post-surgery  







Radiculopathies

background  
- Radiculopathies are conditions caused by compression or irritation of spinal nerve roots, leading to pain, weakness, or sensory changes.

risk factors  
- Age-related degenerative changes  
- Occupational hazards with repetitive movements  
- Obesity  
- Previous spinal injuries  
- Genetic predisposition to spinal disorders  

causes  
- Herniated discs  
- Spondylosis and degenerative disc disease  
- Spinal stenosis  
- Tumours  
- Infections  

pathophysiology  
- Compression of the nerve root leads to inflammation  
- Impaired nerve function causes pain and motor/sensory deficits  
- Radiculopathy may affect multiple nerve roots depending on the level of involvement  

signs and symptoms  
- Radicular pain following a dermatomal distribution  
- Numbness or tingling in the affected limb  
- Muscle weakness related to the affected nerve root  
- Reflex changes  
- Pain may worsen with specific movements  

investigations  
- Clinical examination and neurological assessment  
- MRI of spine to visualise nerve root involvement  
- CT scan if MRI is contraindicated  
- Electromyography (EMG) to assess muscle involvement  
- Nerve conduction studies for further assessment  

diagnosis  
- Confirm symptoms consistent with dermatomal distribution  
- Correlate clinical findings with imaging results  
- Rule out other causes of neurological symptoms  
- Assess for progressive neurological deficits  
- Refer to guidelines for red flags prompting further investigation  

management  
- Conservative management with education and reassurance  
  - Physiotherapy to maintain mobility and strength  
  - Activity modification to reduce symptoms  
- Analgesia including NSAIDs and neuropathic pain medications  
  - Gabapentin or amitriptyline for neuropathic pain  
  - Short-term oral corticosteroids for inflammatory cases  
- Surgical intervention if conservative management fails  
  - Decompression surgery for herniated discs  
  - Fusion surgery for instability or severe cases  
- Multidisciplinary approach for complex cases  
  - Pain management specialists for persistent symptoms  
  - Rehabilitation services for functional recovery  
- Regular follow-up and reassessment of the treatment efficacy  
  - Monitor for new or worsening neurological deficits  
  - Adjust management as necessary based on patient feedback  







Raised intracranial pressure

background  
- Raised intracranial pressure (ICP) can lead to serious neurological impairment and requires timely diagnosis and management  

risk factors  
- Head trauma  
- Brain tumours  
- Cerebral haemorrhage  
- Infection such as meningitis  
- Hydrocephalus  

causes  
- Increased brain mass (e.g. tumours, oedema)  
- Increased blood volume (e.g. venous obstruction, hypercapnia)  
- Increased cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) production or decreased absorption  
- Obstruction of CSF pathways  
- Systemic factors (e.g. hypertension, hypoxia)  

pathophysiology  
- Elevated pressure compresses brain tissues and blood vessels  
- Results in decreased cerebral perfusion and potential ischaemia  
- Leads to herniation syndromes if uncontrolled  
- May trigger a cascade of biochemical changes  
- Progresses to neurological deficits and mortality without intervention  

signs and symptoms  
- Headache, often worsening with position changes  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Altered consciousness or confusion  
- Visual disturbances (e.g. blurred vision, papilloedema)  
- Seizures  

investigations  
- CT or MRI to identify mass lesions or structural abnormalities  
- Lumbar puncture if safe to assess CSF dynamics  
- Blood tests to evaluate for infection, inflammation, or metabolic derangements  
- Continuous ICP monitoring in severe cases  
- Ophthalmological examination for signs of papilloedema  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and neurological examination  
- Imaging studies confirming elevated ICP or causative lesions  
- Evidence of papilloedema on fundoscopic exam  
- Confirmation of other potential causes through investigations  
- Exclusion of conditions that mimic raised ICP  

management  
- Elevate the head to 30 degrees to improve venous drainage  
- Administer hyperosmolar therapy (e.g. mannitol or hypertonic saline)  
   - Monitor serum electrolytes and osmolarity  
   - Adjust treatment based on ICP readings  
- Intubation and ventilation if patient is severely compromised  
   - Ensure adequate oxygenation and ventilation  
   - Consider sedation to reduce metabolic demand  
- Surgical intervention if indicated (e.g. decompressive craniectomy or CSF diversion)  
   - Tailor approach based on underlying cause  
   - Engage in multidisciplinary team for optimal care  
- Treat underlying causes (e.g. tumour resection, treat infection)  
   - Use appropriate therapies based on the aetiology  
   - Evaluate long-term management strategies for chronic conditions  







Reactive arthritis

background  
- Reactive arthritis is an inflammatory arthropathy that typically occurs following an infection in another part of the body  

risk factors  
- Male gender  
- Age between 20 and 40 years  
- Positive family history of autoimmune disorders  
- Recent gastrointestinal or genitourinary infection  
- Presence of HLA-B27 antigen  

causes  
- Chlamydia trachomatis infection  
- Gastrointestinal infections (e.g., *Salmonella*, *Shigella*, *Campylobacter*)  
- Urinary tract infections  
- Viral infections (e.g., hepatitis B, HIV)  
- Post-streptococcal arthritis  

pathophysiology  
- Immune response triggered by infection leading to synovitis  
- Dysregulation of T cells and cytokines contributing to inflammation  
- Microbial antigens present in synovial fluid  
- Cross-reactivity of immune response due to molecular mimicry  
- Chronic inflammation can result in joint damage  

signs and symptoms  
- Oligoarticular arthritis, commonly involving lower limbs  
- Morning stiffness and reduced range of motion  
- Enthesitis and dactylitis  
- Conjunctivitis or urethritis may be present  
- Fever and malaise  

investigations  
- Joint aspiration and analysis of synovial fluid  
- Blood tests for inflammatory markers (ESR, CRP)  
- Serological tests for specific pathogens  
- HLA-B27 antigen test  
- Imaging studies (X-rays, MRI) to assess joint involvement  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of recent infection followed by arthritis  
- Presence of arthritis in one or more joints  
- Exclusion of other arthropathies  
- Positive HLA-B27 in some cases  
- Supportive findings from investigations  

management  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for pain relief  
  - Ibuprofen or naproxen  
  - Monitor for gastrointestinal side effects  
- Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs for persistent symptoms  
  - Methotrexate or sulfasalazine  
  - Consider regular monitoring of liver function  
- Corticosteroids for severe cases or when NSAIDs are inadequate  
  - Intra-articular injection for localised joint involvement  
  - Tapering dose after symptom control  
- Treat underlying infections if identified  
  - Antibiotics for bacterial infections  
  - Follow-up to ensure resolution of infection  
- Referral to rheumatology for complex cases  
  - Multidisciplinary approach  
  - Long-term management and surveillance for persistent arthritis  







Respiratory arrest

background  
- Respiratory arrest is a critical condition where breathing stops, leading to oxygen deprivation and potential death if not promptly addressed  

risk factors  
- Chronic respiratory diseases  
- Neuromuscular disorders  
- Drug overdose  
- Severe trauma  
- Obesity  

causes  
- Airway obstruction  
- Ventilatory failure  
- Severe pneumonia  
- Acute exacerbation of asthma or COPD  
- Cardiac arrest  

pathophysiology  
- Lack of oxygen leads to hypoxia in tissues  
- Carbon dioxide accumulation causes acidosis  
- Potential cellular and organ damage if prolonged  
- Reflex mechanisms may initiate gasping or other abnormal breathing patterns  
- Brainstem dysfunction can prevent normal respiratory drive  

signs and symptoms  
- Absence of breathing  
- Cyanosis of lips and extremities  
- Unresponsiveness or decreased consciousness  
- Abnormal gasping or laboured breathing  
- Changes in heart rate and blood pressure  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of airway and breathing  
- Arterial blood gas analysis  
- Pulse oximetry for oxygen saturation  
- Chest X-ray if indicated  
- ECG to assess cardiac activity  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed absence of breath sounds   
- Deteriorating blood gas values suggesting hypoxia  
- Patient history indicating risk factors or triggering events  
- Unconsciousness or altered mental status consistent with respiratory failure  
- Rapid clinical assessment identifying immediate need for intervention  

management  
- Ensure airway patency using basic life support techniques  
  - Perform rescue breaths if trained and safe to do so  
  - Consider airway adjuncts such as oropharyngeal or nasopharyngeal airways  
- Administer oxygen supplementation as per patient's needs  
  - Initial high-flow oxygen via non-rebreather mask  
  - Adjust titration based on pulse oximetry readings  
- Initiate advanced life support protocols if necessary  
  - Commence CPR if no pulse present  
  - Use defibrillation if indicated in case of cardiac arrest  
- Treat underlying causes as they are identified  
  - Administer bronchodilators for asthma or COPD exacerbations  
  - Reverse opioids if overdose is suspected  
- Consider transfer to intensive care for ongoing management  
  - Assess for need for advanced ventilation support  
  - Monitor and manage potential complications closely  







Respiratory failure

Background  
- Respiratory failure is a clinical syndrome marked by inadequate gas exchange, leading to hypoxia and/or hypercapnia.  

Risk factors  
- Chronic lung diseases such as COPD or fibrosis  
- Obesity and sleep apnoea  
- Neuromuscular disorders like myasthenia gravis  
- Severe pneumonia or pulmonary embolism  
- Advanced age  

Causes  
- Acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS)  
- Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease exacerbation  
- Severe asthma attacks  
- Neuromuscular weakness or failure  
- Central respiratory depression (e.g., due to drugs)  

Pathophysiology
