- Impaired gas exchange in the lungs resulting in hypoxia  
- Accumulation of carbon dioxide leading to hypercapnia  
- Ventilation-perfusion mismatch affecting oxygenation  
- Increased work of breathing due to obstructive or restrictive lung diseases  
- Compensatory mechanisms may fail as metabolic demand surpasses respiratory capacity  

Signs and symptoms  
- Dyspnoea or difficulty breathing  
- Cyanosis, particularly around lips and fingertips  
- Tachypnoea or abnormal respiratory patterns  
- Use of accessory muscles during respiration  
- Altered mental state due to hypoxia or hypercapnia  

Investigations  
- Arterial blood gas analysis  
- Chest X-ray for underlying pathology  
- Oximetry to assess oxygen saturation  
- Lung function tests for chronic conditions  
- CT scan if pulmonary embolism or structural defects are suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Assess clinical history, presenting symptoms, and physical examination findings  
- Confirm via arterial blood gas analysis indicating hypoxaemia or hypercapnia  
- Rule out other conditions with imaging or lab tests  
- Identify and manage any underlying cause contributing to respiratory failure  
- Classify as acute or chronic based on duration and severity  

Management  
- Provide supplemental oxygen to maintain adequate saturation levels  
  - Use non-invasive ventilation if indicated  
  - Intubation and mechanical ventilation for severe cases  
- Treat underlying cause of respiratory failure  
  - Administer bronchodilators or corticosteroids for obstructive airway diseases  
  - Antibiotics for pneumonia or other infections  
- Monitor regularly with arterial blood gases and vital signs  
  - Adjust treatment based on response and ongoing assessment  
  - Consider referral to specialist services if needed  
- Educate patients on management of chronic respiratory conditions  
  - Encourage smoking cessation and pulmonary rehabilitation  
  - Discuss end-of-life options if applicable  
- Have an emergency plan in place for acute exacerbations  
  - Provide access to rescue medications  
  - Ensure regular follow-ups with healthcare providers  







Retinal detachment

background  
- Retinal detachment is an emergent condition where the retina separates from the underlying retinal pigment epithelium  

risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- History of cataract surgery  
- High myopia  
- Previous eye surgery or trauma  
- Family history of retinal detachment  

causes  
- Rhegmatogenous: tears or holes in the retina  
- Tractional: pulling on the retina by scar tissue  
- Exudative: fluid accumulation under the retina without a tear  
- Spontaneous detachment  
- Systemic conditions like diabetes or hypertension  

pathophysiology  
- Separation of the sensory retina from the retinal pigment epithelium  
- Accumulation of subretinal fluid  
- Disruption of retinal blood supply and function  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of floaters  
- Flashes of light (photopsia)  
- Shadow or curtain over the visual field  
- Decreased vision in the affected eye  
- Visual disturbances that may worsen  

investigations  
- Visual acuity test  
- Fundoscopy to assess the retina  
- Ultrasound B-scan if the view is obstructed  
- Optical coherence tomography (OCT)  
- Binocular indirect ophthalmoscopy  

diagnosis  
- Identify the type of retinal detachment  
- Determine the presence of retinal tears or holes  
- Assess extent and location of detachment  
- Check for associated ocular pathologies  
- Consider a differential diagnosis including posterior vitreous detachment  

management  
- Surgical repair is the primary treatment  
  - Options include scleral buckle, vitrectomy, or pneumatic retinopexy  
- Manage underlying conditions to prevent recurrence  
  - Regular follow-ups and monitoring of high-risk patients  








Rheumatoid arthritis

background  
- Chronic autoimmune disorder affecting joints and surrounding tissues 

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Age (peak onset 30-60 years)  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  
- Smoking  
- Obesity  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental factors (e.g., infections)  
- Hormonal influences  
- Immune system dysfunction  
- Inflammatory mediators (e.g., cytokines)  

pathophysiology  
- Autoimmune attack on synovial tissue  
- Inflammation leads to synovitis  
- Formation of pannus (abnormal tissue growth)  
- Joint erosion and damage  
- Systemic effects due to inflammation  

signs and symptoms  
- Morning stiffness lasting more than 30 minutes  
- Symmetrical joint pain and swelling  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Joint deformities (e.g., ulnar deviation)  
- Increased rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP antibodies  

investigations  
- Full blood count (anaemia of chronic disease)  
- Erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR) or C-reactive protein (CRP)  
- X-rays of affected joints  
- MRI or ultrasound for early detection  
- Joint aspiration for synovial fluid analysis  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Imaging studies to assess joint damage  
- Laboratory tests for autoantibodies  
- Exclusion of other conditions  
- Classification criteria (ACR/EULAR)  

management  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
  - Provide symptom relief  
  - Monitor for gastrointestinal side effects  
- Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs (DMARDs)  
  - Methotrexate is first-line  
  - Regular monitoring for toxicity  
- Biologic agents for severe cases  
  - Target specific components of the immune system  
  - Requires ongoing assessment for infections  
- Corticosteroids for flare-ups  
  - Short-term use recommended  
  - Monitor for long-term side effects  
- Physiotherapy and occupational therapy  
  - Promote joint function and mobility  
  - Educate on joint protection strategies  







Rhinosinusitis

background  
- Inflammation of the nasal cavity and paranasal sinuses, commonly causing nasal obstruction and facial pain 

risk factors  
- Previous upper respiratory tract infections  
- Allergic rhinitis  
- Smoking  
- Asthma  
- Dental infections  

causes  
- Viral infections, commonly rhinovirus  
- Bacterial infections, frequently *Streptococcus pneumoniae*  
- Fungal infections, notably in immunocompromised patients  
- Allergic responses  
- Structural abnormalities, such as septal deviation  

pathophysiology  
- Obstruction of sinus outflow tracts  
- Accumulation of mucus leading to increased pressure  
- Inflammatory response causing tissue swelling  
- Potential secondary bacterial infection  
- Chronic inflammation may occur in prolonged cases  

signs and symptoms  
- Nasal congestion or obstruction  
- Facial pain or pressure, especially when bending forward  
- Purulent nasal discharge  
- Reduced sense of smell  
- Cough, particularly at night  

investigations  
- Clinical evaluation and history-taking  
- Nasal endoscopy for direct visualisation  
- CT scan of the sinuses in chronic cases  
- Allergy tests if allergic rhinitis suspected  
- Microbiological cultures if bacterial infection is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria of symptoms lasting over 10 days  
- Confirmed with imaging if complications are suspected  
- Differentiation between acute and chronic forms based on duration  
- Criteria for bacterial infection include severe symptoms and failure to improve  
- Rule out differential diagnoses such as nasal polyps  

management  
- Analgesics and NSAIDs for pain relief  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen as first-line  
  - Assess for contraindications  
- Intranasal corticosteroids to reduce inflammation  
  - Consider as maintenance therapy  
  - Evaluate adherence and technique  
- Saline nasal irrigation for symptom relief  
  - Can be used regularly  
  - Preferably isotonic solutions  
- Antibiotics for confirmed bacterial infections  
  - First-line options include amoxicillin  
  - Consider alternatives based on allergy history  
- Referral to ENT specialist for refractory cases  
  - If recurrent or chronic symptomatic  
  - Assess for possible surgical intervention







Right heart valve disease

background  
- Right heart valve disease affects the structure and function of the tricuspid and pulmonary valves, impacting right ventricular function  

risk factors  
- Age-related degeneration  
- Rheumatic heart disease  
- Congenital heart defects  
- Infective endocarditis  
- Previous cardiac surgery  

causes  
- Tricuspid regurgitation from right ventricular dilation  
- Pulmonary stenosis from congenital abnormalities  
- Infective endocarditis affecting valve structure  
- Rheumatic fever leading to valvular damage  
- Degenerative changes with age leading to valve dysfunction  

pathophysiology  
- Impaired flow from right atrium or ventricle due to valve dysfunction  
- Increased right atrial or ventricle pressure from regurgitation or obstruction  
- Right heart failure due to volume overload or pressure overload  
- Dilatation of cardiac chambers over time  
- Potential for arrhythmias from atrial enlargement  

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and exercise intolerance  
- Peripheral oedema due to congestive heart failure  
- Jugular venous distension  
- Ascites from right heart failure  
- Murmurs associated with valve dysfunction  

investigations  
- Echocardiography for valve structure and function assessment  
- Electrocardiogram for arrhythmias and chamber enlargement  
- Chest X-ray to assess heart size and pulmonary circulation  
- Cardiac MRI for detailed anatomical evaluation  
- Blood tests for markers of heart failure  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and physical signs  
- Echocardiographic confirmation of valve abnormalities  
- Identification of functional impact on right heart chambers  
- Evaluation of causes such as infective endocarditis or rheumatic disease  
- Assessment of severity and impact on patient's functional status  

management  
- Medical management of heart failure symptoms  
  - Diuretics for fluid overload  
  - ACE inhibitors or beta-blockers if indicated  
- Surgical intervention when indicated  
  - Valve repair for suitable cases  
  - Valve replacement for severe dysfunction  









Rubella

background  
- Rubella is a viral infection that can cause serious birth defects if contracted during pregnancy  

risk factors  
- Maternal infection during the first trimester  
- Lack of vaccination  
- Travel to regions with active rubella transmission  
- Immune deficiency in the mother  
- History of rubella in the family  

causes  
- Infection by the rubella virus, a contagious virus  
- Transmission primarily via respiratory droplets  
- Maternal-fetal transmission during pregnancy  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Rarely through blood transfusion or organ transplantation  

pathophysiology  
- Rubella virus enters the body through the nasopharynx or respiratory tract  
- Viral replication occurs in the lymph nodes, leading to viremia  
- Spread to various organs, including the placenta, during pregnancy  
- Immune response contributes to symptoms  
- Congenital rubella syndrome results from fetal infection  

signs and symptoms  
- Mild fever and malaise  
- Maculopapular rash that progresses from the face to the body  
- Lymphadenopathy, particularly posterior auricular and cervical  
- Arthralgia or arthritis in adults  
- Mild respiratory symptoms  

investigations  
- Serology for rubella-specific IgM and IgG antibodies  
- PCR testing of nasopharyngeal swabs or urine  
- Complete blood count in symptomatic patients  
- Ultrasound in suspected congenital rubella syndrome  
- Assessment of maternal vaccination history  

diagnosis  
- Positive rubella IgM or rising IgG titres  
- Clinical presentation consistent with rubella infection  
- History of exposure to the virus or outbreak  
- Evidence of congenital infection based on ultrasound  
- Exclusion of other causes of similar symptoms  

management  
- Supportive care for mild cases  
  - Antipyretics for fever  
  - Hydration and rest  
- Prevent congenital rubella syndrome through vaccination  
  - MMR vaccine (measles, mumps, rubella)  
  - Given to non-immune individuals before pregnancy  
- Isolation during the infectious period  
  - Avoid contact with susceptible individuals  
  - Inform healthcare providers of possible exposure  
- Monitor newborns for congenital rubella syndrome if maternal infection suspected  
  - Regular follow-ups for developmental milestones  
  - Referral to specialists as needed  
- Public health notification in case of outbreaks  
  - Encourage vaccination programmes  
  - Monitor immunisation coverage in populations  







Sarcoidosis

background  
- Sarcoidosis is a systemic granulomatous disease of unknown aetiology

risk factors  
- Female gender  
- Age between 20 and 40 years  
- African or Scandinavian descent  
- Family history of sarcoidosis  
- Exposure to certain occupational or environmental factors

causes  
- Exact cause remains unidentified  
- Possible involvement of infectious agents, such as *Propionibacterium acnes*  
- Genetic predisposition with HLA class II associations  
- Environmental triggers, including silica dust  
- Autoimmune dysfunction

pathophysiology  
- Formation of non-caseating granulomas in affected organs  
- Activated macrophages and T-lymphocytes involved in granuloma formation  
- Dysfunction in immune regulation  
- Chronic inflammation leading to tissue damage  
- Potential for organ fibrosis over time

signs and symptoms  
- Asymptomatic in many cases  
- Respiratory symptoms such as cough and dyspnoea  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Skin lesions, including erythema nodosum  
- Eye symptoms, such as uveitis or conjunctivitis

investigations  
- Chest X-ray to assess lung involvement  
- High-resolution CT scan to evaluate pulmonary abnormalities  
- Serum angiotensin-converting enzyme levels  
- Bronchoscopy with biopsy for histological confirmation  
- Pulmonary function tests to assess lung impairment

diagnosis  
- Clinical evaluation and symptom assessment  
- Radiographic findings consistent with sarcoidosis  
- Histological evidence of non-caseating granulomas  
- Exclusion of other granulomatous diseases  
- Use of expert consensus criteria for diagnosis

management  
- Observation for asymptomatic cases  
  - Regular follow-up and monitoring  
  - Education about the condition  
- Corticosteroids for symptomatic management  
  - Prednisolone as first-line treatment  
  - Dose tapering based on response  
- Immunosuppressants for refractory cases  
  - Methotrexate or azathioprine as alternatives  
  - Monitor for adverse effects  
- Encourage lifestyle modifications  
  - Smoking cessation  
  - Exercise and stress management  
- Referral to specialists if necessary  
  - Pulmonology for lung involvement  
  - Ophthalmology for ocular symptoms







Scabies

background  
- Scabies is a highly contagious skin infestation caused by the mite *Sarcoptes scabiei*

risk factors  
- Close personal contact  
- Crowded living conditions  
- Weakened immune system  
- Poor hygiene  
- Shared clothing or bedding  

causes  
- Infestation by *Sarcoptes scabiei*  
- Transfer through skin-to-skin contact  
- Indirect contact with contaminated objects  
- Sexual activity can increase transmission  
- Outbreaks common in institutional settings  

pathophysiology  
- Mites burrow into the upper layer of the skin  
- Immune response leads to intense itching  
- Secondary bacterial infection may occur due to scratching  
- Damage to skin barrier promotes further inflammation  
- Papules and burrows result from hypersensitivity reactions  

signs and symptoms  
- Intense itching, especially at night  
- Rash with erythematous papules and burrows  
- Commonly affects web spaces between fingers  
- Excoriations and crusting may be present  
- Secondary infections can lead to pustules  

investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and examination  
- Skin scraping to identify mites or eggs  
- Incidental findings on microscopy for diagnosis  
- Consideration of differential diagnoses  
- Patient's epidemiological exposure history  

diagnosis  
- Primarily clinical, based on characteristic signs  
- Evidence of live mites, eggs, or faeces  
- Consideration of itch pattern and distribution  
- Rule out other skin conditions  
- Persistent symptoms in treated patients suggest reinfestation  

management  
- First-line treatment with topical permethrin  
  - Apply to all skin surfaces overnight  
  - Wash bedding and clothing in hot water  
- Oral ivermectin for extensive or resistant cases  
  - Seek specialist advice for repeated treatment  
  - Monitor for side effects including gastrointestinal symptoms  
- Antihistamines for symptomatic relief of itching  
  - Non-sedating antihistamines preferred  
  - Sedating options may be considered at night  
- Treat close contacts simultaneously  
  - Important to prevent reinfestation  
  - Educate on hygiene practices  
- Follow-up assessment to ensure resolution of symptoms  
  - Check for complications like secondary infections  
  - Assess adherence to treatment protocol  







Schizophrenia

Background  
- Schizophrenia is a chronic mental disorder characterised by disturbances in thought, perception, and behaviour

Risk factors  
- Family history of psychosis or schizophrenia  
- Psychosocial stressors, such as trauma or abuse  
- Substance misuse, especially cannabis  
- Adverse prenatal conditions, including infection or malnutrition  
- Urban upbringing or migration  

Causes  
- Genetic predisposition influences vulnerability  
- Neurodevelopmental factors contribute to brain structure abnormalities  
- Neurotransmitter imbalances, particularly dopamine and glutamate  
- Environmental stressors can trigger symptoms  
- Psychosocial factors play a significant role in onset  

Pathophysiology  
- Dysfunctional neural circuits in the prefrontal cortex and limbic system  
- Abnormalities in cortical and subcortical brain regions  
- Altered synaptic plasticity affects communication between neurons  
- Changes in neurotransmitter systems lead to symptoms  
- Immune system dysfunction may be involved  

Signs and symptoms  
- Positive symptoms include hallucinations and delusions  
- Negative symptoms involve apathy, lack of emotion, and social withdrawal  
- Cognitive symptoms affect memory, attention, and executive function  
- Disorganised behaviour may manifest as erratic actions  
- Reduced ability to function in daily life  

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment through structured interviews  
- Patient history and mental state examination  
- Rule out medical conditions contributing to symptoms  
- Assess for substance misuse through questionnaires  
- Imaging (CT or MRI) for structural brain abnormalities  

Diagnosis  
- Based on DSM-5 or ICD-10 criteria  
- Presence of at least one core symptom (delusions, hallucinations, disorganised speech)  
- Symptoms must persist for at least six months  
- Exclusion of mood disorders or substance-induced psychosis  
- Functional impairment in work, relationships, or self-care  

Management  
- First-line treatment is atypical antipsychotics  
  - Examples include risperidone, olanzapine, or aripiprazole  
  - Regular monitoring for side effects is essential  
- Psychological therapies like CBT and family intervention  
  - CBT aims to manage symptoms and improve coping  
  - Family interventions enhance communication and support  
- Education and support for patients and families  
  - Psychoeducation allows better understanding of the condition  
  - Support groups provide community resources  
- Social interventions to promote rehabilitation  
  - Vocational training helps with work reintegration  
  - Supportive housing options improve living conditions  
- Regular follow-up to monitor treatment response  
  - Adapt management plans according to symptoms  
  - Engagement with community mental health services







Scleritis

background  
- Scleritis is an inflammatory condition affecting the sclera, often associated with systemic diseases.

risk factors  
- Autoimmune disorders such as rheumatoid arthritis  
- Previous eye injuries or surgeries  
- Infectious conditions  
- Use of certain medications, like bisphosphonates  
- Female gender and age between 30-50  

causes  
- Autoimmune responses  
- Infectious agents like bacteria or viruses  
- Trauma to the eye  
- Idiopathic cases without identifiable cause  
- Systemic vasculitis  

pathophysiology  
- Infiltration of immune cells leading to scleral inflammation  
- Disruption of scleral tissue integrity  
- Impaired ocular perfusion and oxygenation  
- Potential extension to adjacent ocular tissues  
- Could result in tissue necrosis if severe  

signs and symptoms  
- Severe eye pain, often worse at night  
- Redness or discolouration of the sclera  
- Visual disturbances or blurred vision  
- Photophobia and tearing  
- Tenderness upon palpation  

investigations  
- Comprehensive eye examination  
- Ultrasound biomicroscopy  
- Optical coherence tomography  
- Blood tests for autoimmune markers  
- Further imaging if systemic involvement suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and symptoms consistent with scleritis  
- Exclusion of other ocular conditions such as episcleritis  
- Positive findings in imaging or laboratory tests supportive of the diagnosis  
- Identification of associated systemic diseases if present  
- Multidisciplinary approach may be required for systemic causes  

management  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for pain relief  
  - Topical or oral preparations may be used  
  - Monitor for gastrointestinal side effects  
- Corticosteroids for severe cases  
  - Topical, oral, or periocular injections based on severity  
  - Taper dose carefully to prevent rebound inflammation  
- Immunosuppressive agents for underlying autoimmune disease  
  - Consider if unresponsive to corticosteroids  
  - Monitor for potential side effects  
- Referral to an ophthalmologist for comprehensive management  
  - Regular follow-ups for visual status  
  - Adjustment of treatment regimen as necessary  
- Eye protective measures and patient education  
  - Encourage regular eye check-ups  
  - Stress importance of adherence to treatment plan  







Self-harm

background  
- Self-harm is a behaviour where individuals intentionally harm their bodies as a way to cope with emotional distress  

risk factors  
- History of mental health disorders  
- Previous self-harm behaviour  
- Substance misuse  
- Childhood trauma or abuse  
- Social isolation or bullying  

causes  
- Emotional distress or psychological pain  
- Low self-esteem or negative self-image  
- Coping mechanism for overwhelming feelings  
- Influence of peers or social environment  
- Coexisting mental health issues  

pathophysiology  
- Complex interplay of psychological and biological factors  
- Dysregulation of mood and emotions  
- Impacts on neurotransmitter systems  
- Potential genetic predisposition  
- Response to stress or trauma  

signs and symptoms  
- Visible injuries or scars  
- Changes in behaviour or mood  
- Withdrawal from social interactions  
- Expressions of self-hatred or hopelessness  
- Sudden changes in academic or work performance  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and psychiatric evaluation  
- Standardised questionnaires for mental health  
- Risk assessment scales  
- Physical examination for associated injuries  
- Multidisciplinary team input, if needed  

diagnosis  
- Based on clinical criteria from DSM-5 or ICD-10  
- Acknowledgement of underlying mental health issues  
- Consultation with a mental health professional  
- Review of patient's history and support systems  
- Ongoing risk assessment for self-harm behaviours  

management  
- Cognitive behavioural therapy as first-line treatment  
   - Focus on addressing maladaptive thought patterns  
   - Encourage development of coping strategies  
- Pharmacotherapy if indicated (e.g., antidepressants)  
   - Monitor efficacy and side effects  
   - Consider in conjunction with therapy  
- Safety planning with the patient  
   - Identify warning signs and coping strategies  
   - Establish a support network  
- Family involvement and education  
   - Provide information about self-harm  
   - Support families in understanding the behaviour  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring  
   - Assess mental health progress and risk  
   - Adapt management plan as necessary  







Sepsis

Background  
- Sepsis is a life-threatening organ dysfunction caused by a dysregulated host response to infection

Risk factors  
- Age over 65 years  
- Chronic medical conditions such as diabetes or COPD  
- Immunosuppression including HIV or cancer  
- Recent surgery or invasive procedures  
- Intravenous drug use 

Causes  
- Bacterial infections  
- Viral infections  
- Fungal infections  
- Parasitic infections  
- Healthcare-associated infections 

Pathophysiology  
- Infection leading to systemic inflammatory response  
- Release of pro-inflammatory cytokines  
- Endothelial dysfunction and increased vascular permeability  
- Activation of coagulation pathways  
- Multi-organ failure due to hypoperfusion and ischaemia 

Signs and symptoms  
- Fever or hypothermia  
- Tachycardia and tachypnoea  
- Confusion or altered mental status  
- Oliguria or anuria  
- Hypotension 

Investigations  
- Blood cultures for pathogen identification  
- Complete blood count including white blood cell count  
- Serum lactate level measurement  
- Liver and renal function tests  
- Imaging studies to identify source of infection 

Diagnosis  
- Suspected infection with two or more SIRS criteria  
- Organ dysfunction as defined by the SOFA score  
- Blood culture positive or another source of infection identified  
- Monitor lactate levels  
- Identify underlying cause 

Management  
- Immediate intravenous antibiotics within the first hour  
  - Broad-spectrum therapy initially based on local guidelines  
  - Adjust treatment based on culture results later  
- Fluid resuscitation with crystalloids  
  - 30 ml/kg within the first 3 hours  
  - Monitor response to therapy  
- Vasopressors if hypotension persists  
  - Norepinephrine as first-line agent  
  - Consider addition of vasopressin if needed  
- Source control for infection  
  - Surgical intervention if necessary  
  - Drainage of abscess or removal of infected device  
- Consider adjunctive therapies  
  - Corticosteroids if septic shock  
  - Monitor glucose control and manage electrolyte imbalances  







Septic arthritis

Background  
- Septic arthritis is a joint infection causing inflammation and potential joint damage

Risk factors  
- Age over 60 years  
- History of joint disease such as rheumatoid arthritis  
- Immunosuppression or diabetes  
- Recent joint surgery or trauma  
- Injection drug use  

Causes  
- Bacterial infections, commonly *Staphylococcus aureus* and *Streptococcus* species  
- Viral infections such as parvovirus B19 or HIV  
- Fungal infections, primarily in immunocompromised individuals  
- Atypical pathogens like *Mycobacterium tuberculosis*  
- Lyme disease in endemic areas  

Pathophysiology  
- Bacteria invade the synovial fluid and tissue  
- Inflammatory response leads to synovitis and pus formation  
- Increased intra-articular pressure impacts blood flow and joint function  
- Potential for cartilaginous and bony destruction if left untreated  

Signs and symptoms  
- Joint pain, swelling, and tenderness  
- Fever and chills  
- Reduced range of motion in affected joint  
- Warmth and redness over the joint  
- Systemic symptoms may appear with severe infections  

Investigations  
- Joint aspiration for synovial fluid analysis and culture  
- Blood cultures to identify systemic infection  
- Blood tests including inflammatory markers (CRP, ESR)  
- Imaging studies such as X-ray or MRI if osteomyelitis is suspected  
- Ultrasound to assess joint effusion  

Diagnosis  
- Positive joint fluid culture or gram stain  
- Elevated white blood cell count in synovial fluid  
- Elevated inflammatory markers in blood tests  
- Clinical presentation consistent with septic arthritis  
- Exclusion of other joint conditions  

Management  
- Immediate surgical intervention for drainage if necessary  
  - Arthroscopy or arthrotomy based on the severity  
- Empirical intravenous antibiotics based on local guidelines  
  - Adjust according to culture results and sensitivities  
- Analgesia and anti-inflammatory medications for symptom relief  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)  
- Physiotherapy and rehabilitation following infection control  
  - Focus on restoring joint function and mobility  
- Regular follow-up to monitor recovery and prevent recurrence  
  - Assess for potential chronic arthritis development  







Sickle cell disease

Background  
- Sickle cell disease is a genetically inherited blood disorder characterized by abnormal haemoglobin leading to distorted red blood cells

Risk factors  
- Family history of sickle cell disease or trait  
- Ethnic background, particularly African, Caribbean or Mediterranean descent  
- Having a partner with sickle cell trait or disease  
- Presence of other haemoglobinopathies  
- Increased risk in people born in endemic regions

Causes  
- Mutation in the HBB gene on chromosome 11  
- Production of abnormal haemoglobin, known as haemoglobin S  
- Inheritance pattern is autosomal recessive  
- Synthesis of haemoglobin S occurs due to a single nucleotide substitution  
- Environmental factors, such as low oxygen levels, can exacerbate symptoms

Pathophysiology  
- Sickling of red blood cells occurs under low oxygen conditions  
- Deformed cells cause vaso-occlusion and tissue ischaemia  
- Haemolysis results in anaemia and increased bilirubin  
- Inflammation and vaso-occlusive events lead to pain crises  
- Chronic complications include organ damage from repeated occlusion

Signs and symptoms  
- Painful crises due to vaso-occlusion  
- Anemia with fatigue and pallor  
- Swelling of hands and feet (dactylitis)  
- Frequent infections due to spleen dysfunction  
- Delayed growth and puberty in children

Investigations  
- Complete blood count to assess anaemia  
- Peripheral blood smear to identify sickle-shaped cells  
- Haemoglobin electrophoresis to determine haemoglobin types  
- DNA analysis for HBB gene mutation confirmation  
- Screening for infections, especially in children

Diagnosis  
- Clinical features and family history  
- Laboratory confirmation through haemoglobin electrophoresis  
- Newborn screening tests in high-risk populations  
- Genetic testing if required to determine risk in family members  
- Diagnosis established if there is evidence of sickling and associated symptoms

Management  
- Pain management with analgesics, including opioids for severe pain  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for mild to moderate pain  
  - Consider patient-controlled analgesia for severe episodes  
- Hydroxycarbamide therapy to reduce frequency of pain crises  
  - Regular monitoring of blood counts during treatment  
  - Consideration of offering to patients with frequent crises  
- Blood transfusions for severe anaemia or acute chest syndrome  
  - Use of simple or exchange transfusion depending on clinical scenario  
  - Careful monitoring for transfusion reactions  
- Vaccinations and prophylactic antibiotics to prevent infections  
  - Recommended vaccinations include pneumococcal, meningococcal, and influenza  
  - Prophylactic penicillin until age 5 years or longer if indicated  
- Regular follow-up and education on recognising symptoms and crisis management  
  - Psychosocial support and access to multidisciplinary care  
  - Genetic counselling for affected families and future planning  







Somatisation

Background  
- Somatisation is the expression of psychological distress through physical symptoms without a medical explanation  

Risk factors  
- History of childhood trauma or abuse  
- Comorbid psychiatric disorders  
- High levels of stress or anxiety  
- Personality traits such as neuroticism  
- Cultural factors influencing expression of distress  

Causes  
- Psychological factors such as unresolved conflict  
- Chronic stress or anxiety  
- Biological predisposition to somatic symptoms  
- Sociocultural influences on health perceptions  
- Previous negative health experiences  

Pathophysiology  
- Dysregulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis  
- Altered neurotransmitter levels affecting pain perception  
- Disruption in the brain-gut axis  
- Psychosomatic interactions between mind and body  
- Chronic physical symptomatology affecting mental wellbeing  

Signs and symptoms  
- Physical symptoms affecting one or more body systems  
- Symptoms may mimic serious illnesses  
- High levels of functional impairment  
- Associated with significant distress or dissatisfaction  
- May present with frequent medical visits  

Investigations  
- Comprehensive clinical history and physical examination  
- Exclusion of medical conditions through relevant tests  
- Psychological assessment to determine mental health status  
- Screening for anxiety and depression disorders  
- Consideration of symptom chronicity and impact on daily life  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis based on DSM-5 criteria for somatic symptom disorder  
- Evidence of significant distress or impairment in functioning  
- Exclusion of other mental or physical health conditions  
- Symptoms must persist for more than six months  
- Acknowledgement of the somatic complaints as real experiences  

Management  
- Cognitive behavioural therapy (CBT) to address thought patterns  
  - Individualised therapy sessions  
  - Focus on coping strategies and symptom management  
- Pharmacotherapy for associated symptoms such as anxiety  
  - SSRIs as first-line treatment  
  - Monitoring for potential side effects  
- Regular follow-up and support for symptom monitoring  
  - Build a trusting doctor-patient relationship  
  - Encourage open communication about symptoms and concerns  
- Education about the condition and self-management strategies  
  - Promote understanding of the mind-body connection  
  - Introduce relaxation techniques or mindfulness practices  
- Multidisciplinary approach when necessary  
  - Involvement of physiotherapists or occupational therapists  
  - Referral to mental health services for deeper intervention  







Spinal cord compression

background  
- Spinal cord compression is a neurological emergency that occurs when pressure is applied to the spinal cord.

risk factors  
- Malignancy leading to metastatic disease  
- Trauma to the spine  
- Degenerative spinal diseases such as herniated discs  
- Infectious processes like abscesses or osteomyelitis  
- Prior spinal surgery

causes  
- Tumours (primary or secondary)  
- Disc herniation  
- Spinal stenosis  
- Epidural abscess  
- Fractures from trauma or osteoporosis

pathophysiology  
- Increased pressure disrupts blood flow, causing ischaemia to spinal cord tissues  
- Neuronal injury occurs due to direct compression  
- Potential development of secondary inflammatory responses  

signs and symptoms  
- Local pain in the back or neck  
- Motor weakness in limbs  
- Sensory deficits including numbness or tingling  
- Bowel and bladder dysfunction  
- Hyperreflexia or diminished reflexes

investigations  
- MRI is the gold standard imaging modality  
- CT scan for assessing bony structures  
- X-rays to rule out fractures  
- Neurological examination for clinical assessment  
- Blood tests to identify causes such as infection or malignancy  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and neurological examination  
- MRI findings of spinal cord compression  
- Identification of underlying causes such as tumours  
- Exclusion of other diagnoses  
- Assessment of symptom severity and duration  

management  
- Urgent surgical intervention for immediate decompression  
   - Laminectomy or tumour excision depending on aetiology  
   - Stabilisation if indicated by instability  
- Radiotherapy for malignant compressions  
   - Palliative care for symptom relief  
   - Consideration of corticosteroids to reduce inflammation  
- Supportive care for rehabilitation  
   - Physical therapy to regain function  
   - Occupational therapy for activities of daily living  
- Pain management approaches  
   - Analgesics including NSAIDs or opioids  
   - Adjunct therapies like nerve blocks  
- Regular monitoring and follow-up  
   - Neurological assessments for recovery progress  
   - Ongoing imaging as required







Spinal cord injury

background  
- Spinal cord injury is a damage to the spinal cord that can result in loss of movement, sensation, or function.

risk factors  
- High-risk activities such as sports or extreme behaviours  
- Age, particularly in males aged 16-30  
- Road traffic accidents  
- Falls, especially in older adults  
- Pre-existing spinal conditions or previous injuries  

causes  
- Trauma from accidents, falls, or violence  
- Compression from tumours or infections  
- Ischaemia from vascular compromise  
- Congenital abnormalities such as spina bifida  
- Degenerative diseases affecting the spine  

pathophysiology  
- Damage to the spinal cord can disrupt pathways for motor and sensory information  
- Inflammation occurs, leading to secondary injury and cell death  
- Neurotransmitter release can trigger further neuronal damage  
- Axonal regeneration occurs in some cases but is often limited  
- Resulting dysfunction depends on the level and extent of injury  

signs and symptoms  
- Loss of sensation below the injury site  
- Muscle weakness or paralysis  
- Spasticity or flaccidity in affected areas  
- Altered autonomic function, including blood pressure changes  
- Bowel and bladder dysfunction  

investigations  
- Clinical examination for neurological assessment  
- MRI for soft tissue evaluation and cord integrity  
- CT scan if fractures or bony abnormalities are suspected  
- X-rays for initial assessment of vertebral injury  
- Electromyography to assess nerve and muscle function if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Assessment of neurological function using the ASIA scale  
- Identification of level and completeness of injury  
- MRI findings correlating with clinical presentation  
- Exclusion of other possible causes of symptoms  
- Multidisciplinary team discussion for comprehensive diagnosis  

management  
- Immediate immobilisation of the spine in acute cases  
  - Use of a cervical collar or spinal board  
  - Avoid any unnecessary movement  
- Surgical intervention if indicated for decompression or stabilization  
  - Decompressive laminectomy for pressure relief  
  - Stabilisation with hardware if fractures present  
- Pharmacological management with corticosteroids in the acute phase  
  - Methylprednisolone if initiated within 8 hours  
  - Pain management with appropriate analgesics  
- Rehabilitation therapy to aid recovery and function  
  - Physical therapy for mobility training  
  - Occupational therapy for activities of daily living  
- Psychological support and counselling for emotional well-being  
  - Addressing body image and lifestyle changes  
  - Providing access to support groups or counselling services  







Spinal fracture

Background  
- Spinal fractures are injuries to the vertebrae, often resulting from trauma or underlying conditions

Risk factors  
- Age (older individuals at higher risk)  
- Osteoporosis  
- High-impact sports or activities  
- Falls, particularly in the elderly  
- Road traffic accidents  

Causes  
- Trauma from accidents or falls  
- Osteoporotic fractures from minimal trauma  
- Tumour infiltration weakening the vertebrae  
- Infection leading to structural compromise  
- Congenital deformities  

Pathophysiology  
- Structural failure of the vertebral body due to excessive force  
- Potential damage to surrounding tissues, including spinal cord or nerves  
- Changes in biomechanics leading to instability  
- Possible development of kyphosis or scoliosis  
- Risk of chronic pain or neurological deficits  

Signs and symptoms  
- Localised back pain  
- Radiating pain, weakness, or numbness  
- Limited mobility  
- Possible spinal deformity  
- Neurological deficits if spinal cord is affected  

Investigations  
- Plain X-rays for initial assessment  
- CT scan for detailed bone imaging  
- MRI to assess soft tissue and spinal cord involvement  
- Bone density scan in suspected osteoporosis cases  
- Neurological examinations to assess deficits  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of history and symptoms  
- Imaging results supporting fracture presence  
- Exclusion of other conditions (e.g., infection or malignancy)  
- Neurological assessment to determine injury severity  
- Evaluation of risk factors, including age and comorbidities  

Management  
- Conservative management for stable fractures  
  - Pain relief with analgesics  
  - Activity modification and physical therapy  
- Surgical intervention for unstable fractures  
  - Decompression if spinal cord is compromised  
  - Stabilisation procedures such as fusion or instrumentation  
- Rehabilitation to improve function  
  - Physiotherapy for mobility and strength  
  - Occupational therapy for daily living assistance  
- Monitoring and follow-up care  
  - Regular assessments for healing and complications  
  - Bisphosphonates for osteoporosis if indicated  
- Education on fall prevention strategies  
  - Home safety assessments  
  - Exercise programmes for strength and balance  







Squamous cell carcinoma

background  
- Squamous cell carcinoma is a malignant tumour originating from squamous epithelial cells, commonly affecting skin and mucous membranes

risk factors  
- Long-term sun exposure  
- Exposure to carcinogenic substances such as tobacco and industrial chemicals  
- Chronic skin conditions such as actinic keratosis  
- Immunosuppression due to diseases or medications  
- Human papillomavirus (HPV) infection  

causes  
- DNA damage from UV radiation  
- Chronic inflammation leading to dysplasia  
- Genetic mutations, particularly in tumour suppressor genes  
- Viral infections contributing to cellular changes  
- Environmental factors such as pollutants  

pathophysiology  
- Initiation of keratinocyte mutations  
- Abnormal differentiation and proliferation of squamous cells  
- Invasion of local tissues and potential metastasis  
- Angiogenesis facilitating tumour growth  
- Immune evasion mechanisms  

signs and symptoms  
- Localised skin lesions that may appear rough or scaly  
- Non-healing ulcers or sores  
- Raised, wart-like growths  
- Changes in colour or texture of existing lesions  
- Symptoms of metastasis such as pain or systemic illness  

investigations  
- Clinical examination of the lesion  
- Histopathological analysis via biopsy  
- Imaging studies such as ultrasound or CT scan for staging  
- Lymph node assessment if regional spread is suspected  
- Blood tests for overall health and organ function  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed through histopathology of biopsy samples  
- Diagnosis classification based on the appearance and behaviour of cells  
- Staging based on tumour size, nodal involvement, and metastasis  
- Evaluation of risk factors and patients' history  
- Monitoring for recurrence after initial treatment  

management  
- Surgical excision of the tumour  
  - Mohs micrographic surgery for facial lesions  
  - Wide local excision for larger tumours  
- Radiotherapy for non-surgical candidates or adjunct treatment  
  - Palliative care for advanced cases  
  - Adjuvant therapy in high-risk patients  
- Chemotherapy in cases of metastasis or unresectable tumours  
  - Systemic therapies as per tumour profile  
  - Clinical trials for emerging treatments  
- Regular follow-up to monitor for recurrence  
  - Skin examinations every 3-6 months post-treatment  
  - Education on sun protection and self-skin examinations  
- Referral to specialist units for complex cases  
  - Multidisciplinary team approach  
  - Supportive care for psychosocial aspects of the disease  







Stroke

Background  
- Stroke is a medical emergency characterised by sudden neurological deficits due to interruption of blood flow to the brain

Risk factors  
- Hypertension  
- Atrial fibrillation  
- Smoking  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hyperlipidaemia  

Causes  
- Ischaemic stroke due to thrombus or embolus  
- Haemorrhagic stroke due to intracerebral or subarachnoid haemorrhage  
- Transient ischaemic attack  
- Venous thromboembolism  
- Vascular malformations  

Pathophysiology  
- Ischaemia leads to neuronal cell death and impaired function  
- Inflammatory response exacerbates tissue injury  
- Reperfusion injury may occur upon restoration of blood flow  
- Haemorrhage increases intracranial pressure  
- Neurotransmitter imbalance contributes to neuronal damage  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset of unilateral weakness or numbness  
- Difficulty speaking or understanding speech  
- Vision problems in one or both eyes  
- Sudden severe headache with no known cause  
- Dizziness, loss of balance or coordination  

Investigations  
- Non-contrast CT scan to differentiate between ischaemic and haemorrhagic stroke  
- MRI for further evaluation of brain tissue damage  
- Blood tests including full blood count and coagulation profile  
- ECG to assess for arrhythmias like atrial fibrillation  
- Carotid Doppler ultrasound to evaluate stenosis or occlusion  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment using the FAST (Face, Arms, Speech, Time) acronym  
- Imaging studies to confirm stroke type  
- Neurovascular assessment to determine underlying causes  
- Blood tests to identify risk factors  
- Multidisciplinary assessment for acute management  

Management  
- Immediate assessment and stabilisation  
   - Maintain airway, breathing, and circulation  
   - Administer oxygen if hypoxic  
- Thrombolysis for ischaemic stroke within 4.5 hours  
   - Consider eligibility for alteplase  
   - Monitor for bleeding complications  
- Antiplatelet therapy following ischaemic stroke  
   - Aspirin as first-line treatment  
   - Clopidogrel if intolerance to aspirin  
- Management of risk factors  
   - Blood pressure control with antihypertensives  
   - Lifestyle modification and education on stroke prevention  
- Rehabilitation services for recovery  
   - Occupational therapy for daily living skills  
   - Physiotherapy for mobility and balance training







Subarachnoid haemorrhage

Background  
- Subarachnoid haemorrhage is bleeding into the subarachnoid space, often presenting as a medical emergency 

Risk factors  
- Family history of cerebral aneurysms or haemorrhage  
- Smoking  
- Hypertension  
- Heavy alcohol consumption  
- Female sex  

Causes  
- Rupture of cerebral aneurysms  
- Trauma to the head  
- Arteriovenous malformations  
- Coagulopathy or anticoagulant therapy  
- Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis  

Pathophysiology  
- Blood enters the subarachnoid space, leading to increased intracranial pressure  
- Inflammatory response triggers vasospasm of cerebral arteries  
- May result in cerebral ischaemia or delayed neurological deficits  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden severe headache often described as a "thunderclap" headache  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Photophobia  
- Neck stiffness  
- Altered consciousness or focal neurological deficits  

Investigations  
- CT scan of the head to identify haemorrhage  
- Lumbar puncture if CT is negative, looking for xanthochromia  
- MRI for further characterisation if necessary  
- CTA or DSA for identifying aneurysms  
- Coagulation profile to rule out coagulopathy  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation consistent with subarachnoid haemorrhage  
- CT brain showing evidence of haemorrhage  
- Presence of xanthochromia in CSF  
- Identification of a vascular lesion on angiography  
- Exclusion of other causes 

Management  
- Immediate supportive care including monitoring of vital signs  
  - Administer intravenous fluids  
  - Maintain airway and breathing  
- Surgical intervention for cerebral aneurysm  
  - Coiling of aneurysm  
  - Clipping of aneurysm  
- Prevent and manage complications such as vasospasm  
  - Administer calcium channel blockers (e.g. nimodipine)  
  - Close monitoring in ICU  
- Treat secondary causes if identified  
  - Address any coagulation abnormalities  
  - Manage hypertension  
- Early rehabilitation and multidisciplinary approach  
  - Involvement of physiotherapy and occupational therapy  
  - Regular follow-up with neurological assessments  







Subdural haemorrhage

background  
- Subdural haemorrhage is a collection of blood between the dura mater and the brain, often resulting from trauma 

risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Anticoagulant therapy  
- Alcoholism  
- History of falls  
- Brain atrophy  

causes  
- Traumatic brain injury  
- Non-traumatic causes like rupture of blood vessels  
- Shaking injuries, particularly in infants  
- Coagulopathy disorders  
- Tumours leading to vascular fragility  

pathophysiology  
- Tear in bridging veins leads to blood accumulation  
- Increasing intracranial pressure and potential brain herniation  
- Reorganisation of brain tissue and secondary injury  
- Neutrophilic and macrophage response contributing to inflammation  
- Can develop into chronic subdural haematoma  

signs and symptoms  
- Headache  
- Confusion or altered consciousness  
- Neurological deficits (e.g. weakness, slurred speech)  
- Seizures  
- Dizziness or changes in balance  

investigations  
- CT scan of the head for rapid assessment  
- MRI if more detail needed or in chronic cases  
- Blood tests to evaluate coagulation status  
- Neurological examination to assess function  
- Glasgow Coma Scale for level of consciousness  

diagnosis  
- History of trauma within an appropriate timeframe  
- Characteristic imaging findings on CT or MRI  
- Symptoms correlating with clinical findings  
- Exclusion of other intracranial pathology  
- Monitoring for changes over time  

management  
- Surgical intervention for larger or symptomatic haematomas  
   - Craniotomy for direct evacuation  
   - Burr hole drainage for smaller collections  
- Medical management for stable patients  
   - Close neurological monitoring  
   - Management of any underlying coagulopathy  
- Rehabilitation services referral for recovery support  
   - Physiotherapy for mobility  
   - Occupational therapy for daily activities  
- Consideration for anticoagulant reversal if applicable  
   - Use of vitamin K or prothrombin complex concentrates  
   - Risk assessment for further thromboembolic events  
- Psychosocial support for patients and families affected  
   - Providing counselling services  
   - Support groups for those with neurological deficits  







Substance use disorder

background  
- Substance use disorder is a chronic condition characterised by compulsive substance use despite harmful consequences  

risk factors  
- Family history of substance use disorders  
- Co-occurring mental health disorders  
- Early exposure to substance use  
- Socioeconomic factors and instability  
- Peer pressure and social environment  

causes  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Neurobiological changes due to substance use  
- Environmental factors such as trauma or stress  
- Availability and accessibility of substances  
- Cultural attitudes towards substance use  

pathophysiology  
- Alterations in neurotransmitter systems, primarily dopamine  
- Development of tolerance and withdrawal symptoms  
- Changes in brain circuitry affecting decision-making and impulse control  
- Impact on the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis  
- Neuroinflammation and damage to brain structures  

signs and symptoms  
- Increased tolerance to substances  
- Withdrawal symptoms when not using  
- Loss of control over substance use  
- Neglect of responsibilities and relationships  
- Continued use despite negative consequences  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Standardised screening tools such as the DSM-5 criteria  
- Urine drug screening  
- Blood tests to assess for liver function or co-existing conditions  
- Mental health evaluations for co-occurring disorders  

diagnosis  
- Based on DSM-5 criteria, including levels of severity  
- Confirmation of diagnosis through evidence of distress or functional impairment  
- Assessment of the substance type and patterns of use  
- Consideration of any co-occurring mental health issues  
- Evaluation of withdrawal symptoms or tolerance  

management  
- Psychosocial interventions such as cognitive-behavioural therapy  
  - Motivational interviewing techniques  
  - Family therapy to support recovery  
- Pharmacotherapy, including SARIs or methadone  
  - Consideration of buprenorphine for opioid dependence  
- Community support resources such as self-help groups  
  - Engagement with local support services  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring for relapse  
  - Adjustments to treatment based on patient progress  
- Education on substance use and prevention strategies  
  - Providing information on harm reduction techniques  







Surgical site infection

background  
- Surgical site infections are complications occurring at or near surgical incisions within 30 days post-operation

risk factors  
- Contaminated surgical site  
- Poor wound care  
- Obesity  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Prolonged surgical duration  

causes  
- Bacterial infections, primarily by *Staphylococcus aureus* and *Escherichia coli*  
- Contamination during surgery  
- Skin flora translocation  
- Haematogenous spread from distant infections  
- Immunosuppression  

pathophysiology  
- Bacteria penetrate skin integrity   
- Inflammatory response leads to tissue damage  
- Formation of pus and potential abscess  
- Impaired wound healing due to local and systemic factors  
- Increased risk of systemic infection if untreated  

signs and symptoms  
- Redness and swelling around the incision  
- Pain or tenderness in the affected area  
- Warmth at the surgery site  
- Purulent or cloudy discharge from the wound  
- Fever and increased heart rate in systemic infections  

investigations  
- Clinical examination of the surgical site  
- Wound swab for microbiological analysis  
- Blood tests including complete blood count and CRP  
- Imaging studies if abscess or deeper infection is suspected  
- Assessment for underlying conditions or risk factors  

diagnosis  
- Clinical criteria based on symptoms and signs  
- Confirmation via microbiological culture of wound samples  
- Exclusion of other causes of postoperative complications  
- Use of surgical site infection definitions from NICE guidelines  
- Follow up on patients at risk based on surgical history  

management  
- Clean and debride the wound if necessary  
  - Administer appropriate antibiotics based on culture results  
  - Consider surgical intervention in cases of abscess  
- Monitor and educate on wound care practices  
  - Advise on signs of infection  
  - Schedule follow-up appointments to assess healing  
- Manage underlying risk factors  
  - Optimise diabetes control if present  
  - Encourage weight loss in obese patients  
- Implement infection prevention measures during surgery  
  - Administer prophylactic antibiotics if indicated  
  - Maintain aseptic technique throughout the procedure  
- Ensure multidisciplinary approach for complex cases  
  - Involve infection control team  
  - Collaborate with surgery and primary care teams  







Syphilis

background  
- Syphilis is a sexually transmitted infection caused by the bacterium *Treponema pallidum*

risk factors  
- Unprotected sexual intercourse  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- History of previous STIs  
- Male-to-male sexual contact  
- Immunocompromised status  

causes  
- Infection by *Treponema pallidum* during sexual contact  
- Vertical transmission from mother to child during pregnancy  
- Blood transfusions in rare cases  

pathophysiology  
- Spirochete enters the body through mucous membranes or broken skin  
- Multiplication and dissemination in the bloodstream  
- Formation of lesions in various tissues and organs  

signs and symptoms  
- Primary: painless ulcer (chancre) at the site of entry  
- Secondary: rash, mucous membrane lesions, fever, lymphadenopathy  
- Latent: asymptomatic phase after secondary stage  
- Tertiary: gummatous lesions, cardiovascular, or neurological complications  
- Congenital: can cause stillbirth, hydrocephalus, or bone deformities  

investigations  
- Serological tests: non-treponemal (RPR, VDRL) and treponemal tests (TPPA, EIA)  
- Darkfield microscopy of exudate from a chancre  
- CSF analysis if neurological syphilis is suspected  
- HIV test due to co-prevalence  
- Pregnancy test in women of childbearing age  

diagnosis  
- Positive non-treponemal test followed by confirmatory treponemal test  
- Clinical presentation correlating with syphilis stages  
- History of potential exposure or risky behaviour  
- Consideration of other differential diagnoses  

management  
- Benzathine benzylpenicillin for all stages  
  - Primary: single dose of 2.4 million units  
  - Latent/tertiary: three doses of 2.4 million units at weekly intervals  
- Alternative treatment for penicillin allergies  
  - Doxycycline or tetracycline for non-pregnant individuals  
  - Desensitisation for pregnant women allergic to penicillin  
- Regular follow-up for serological testing  
  - Repeat testing at 3, 6, and 12 months  
  - Monitor for treatment response  
- Advise safe sexual practices  
  - Use of condoms  
  - Regular STI screening  
- Inform and treat sexual partners  
  - Encourage partners to be tested  
  - Provide treatment as necessary  







Systemic lupus erythematosus

background  
- Systemic lupus erythematosus is a chronic autoimmune disease affecting multiple organ systems and characterised by inflammation.

risk factors  
- Female gender, particularly those aged 15-45  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  
- Ethnicity, with higher prevalence in Black and Asian populations  
- Exposure to ultraviolet light  
- Certain medications, such as hydralazine and procainamide  

causes  
- Exact cause unknown, but believed to involve genetic predisposition  
- Environmental triggers such as infections or sunlight exposure  
- Hormonal influences due to prevalence in women  
- Immune dysregulation leading to autoantibody production  
- Combination of genetic and environmental factors  

pathophysiology  
- Immune system attacks self-tissues due to loss of tolerance  
- Formation of autoantibodies and immune complexes  
- Inflammation and damage to various tissues and organs  
- Multi-organ involvement due to systemic nature  
- Fluctuating course with periods of exacerbation and remission  

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Malar rash (butterfly rash)  
- Arthralgia or arthritis in multiple joints  
- Renal involvement (lupus nephritis)  
- Serositis, such as pleuritis or pericarditis  

investigations  
- Full blood count and renal function tests  
- Antinuclear antibody (ANA) test  
- Anti-double stranded DNA and anti-Smith antibodies  
- Urinalysis for proteinuria and casts  
- Imaging studies for organ involvement, as indicated  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Positive ANA test alongside clinical features  
- Autoantibody testing supporting diagnosis  
- Exclusion of other conditions with similar presentations  
- Multidisciplinary approach may be warranted  

management  
- Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for pain and inflammation  
   - Monitor for gastrointestinal side effects  
   - Use in conjunction with gastroprotective agents if needed  
- Corticosteroids for controlling flares  
   - Tapered to lowest effective dose  
   - Long-term use requires careful monitoring for side effects  
- Antimalarials such as hydroxychloroquine for skin and joint symptoms  
   - Regular ophthalmic monitoring for retinal toxicity  
   - Effective for chronic management  
- Immunosuppressives, including azathioprine or mycophenolate mofetil in severe cases  
   - Individualised treatment based on disease activity  
   - Regular monitoring for infection and blood counts  
- Regular follow-up and multidisciplinary care to manage complications  
   - Involves rheumatology, nephrology, and dermatology as appropriate  
   - Patient education on sun protection and lifestyle modifications







Tension headache

Background  
- Tension headache is a common form of primary headache often characterised by mild to moderate bilateral pain.

Risk factors  
- Stress or anxiety  
- Poor posture  
- Eye strain  
- Sleep disturbances  
- Muscle tension in the neck and shoulders  

Causes  
- Muscle contraction in the head, neck, and shoulders  
- Stress-related changes in the brain  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Environmental factors  
- Hormonal changes  

Pathophysiology  
- Altered pain processing in central nervous system  
- Increased sensitivity of peripheral nociceptors  
- Dysfunction in pain modulation systems  
- Psychosocial stressors contributing to muscle tension  
- Neuroinflammation may play a role  

Signs and symptoms  
- Bilateral, pressing or tightening pain  
- Mild to moderate intensity  
- No associated nausea or vomiting  
- Sensitivity to light or sound may occur  
- Duration can range from 30 minutes to several days  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Exclusion of secondary headache disorders  
- Neurological examination to assess function  
- MRI or CT if red flags are present  
- No routine tests needed for uncomplicated cases  

Diagnosis  
- Diagnosis made based on clinical criteria  
- Must fulfil International Classification of Headache Disorders criteria  
- Exclude secondary causes of headache  
- Evaluate patient history and symptom pattern  
- Assess frequency and duration of headaches  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Stress management techniques  
  - Regular exercise programme  
- Pharmacological treatment  
  - Analgesics like paracetamol or NSAIDs as needed  
  - Consider preventive therapy for chronic cases  
- Psychological support  
  - Cognitive behavioural therapy for stress management  
  - Referral to counselling or psychologist if needed  
- Education and reassurance  
  - Educate patients about headache triggers  
  - Encourage maintaining a headache diary  
- Regular follow-up  
  - Monitor treatment efficacy and adjust as required  
  - Reassess if headaches change in pattern or severity  







Termination of pregnancy

background  
- Termination of pregnancy refers to the medical or surgical ending of a pregnancy before the fetus can live independently outside the womb  

risk factors  
- Previous terminations  
- Younger age at pregnancy  
- Unplanned or unwanted pregnancy  
- History of mental health issues  
- Socioeconomic disadvantages  

causes  
- Contraceptive failure  
- Maternal health concerns  
- Fetal abnormalities  
- Rape or sexual assault  
- Decision based on personal circumstances  

pathophysiology  
- Termination can occur through hormonal changes or surgical methods  
- Surgical methods include suction aspiration or dilation and curettage  
- Medical methods often involve a combination of mifepristone and misoprostol  
- Involves changes in the uterine lining and expulsion of pregnancy tissue  
- Complications can arise affecting both physical and mental health  

signs and symptoms  
- Absence of menstruation  
- Pregnancy tests confirm pregnancy  
- Cramping or abdominal pain  
- Vaginal bleeding  
- Emotional distress or anxiety  

investigations  
- Urine or serum pregnancy tests  
- Ultrasound to confirm gestational age and viability  
- Blood tests for haemoglobin and Rh status  
- Psychological assessment if indicated  
- STI screening when appropriate  

diagnosis  
- Confirm pregnancy via tests  
- Assess gestational age using ultrasound  
- Evaluate for contraindications to the procedure  
- Consider patient's physical and mental health status  
- Document patient's consent and decision-making process  

management  
- Discuss options including medical and surgical termination  
  - Provide information on risks and benefits of each method  
  - Ensure informed consent is obtained  
- Follow-up care and support  
  - Arrange for post-termination check-up  
  - Provide access to counselling or mental health resources  
- Offer contraceptive advice to prevent future unwanted pregnancies  
  - Discuss long-acting reversible contraception options  
  - Ensure continuation of contraception post-procedure  








Testicular cancer

background  
- Testicular cancer is a rare malignancy primarily affecting younger men aged 15 to 49  

risk factors  
- History of cryptorchidism  
- Family history of testicular cancer  
- Previous testicular cancer  
- Age  
- Ethnicity, with higher incidence in Caucasians  

causes  
- Genetic predispositions, such as mutations in the *KRAS* gene  
- Abnormal development of the testes  
- Hormonal factors during prenatal development  
- Environmental factors, including exposure to carcinogens  
- Possible links to trauma or infection  

pathophysiology  
- Involves malignant transformation of germ cells in the testes  
- Can present as seminoma or non-seminomatous germ cell tumours  
- Tumour growth can lead to local invasion and metastasis  

signs and symptoms  
- Painless lump or swelling in one testis  
- Change in size or shape of the testis  
- Aching or heaviness in the scrotum  
- Pain in the lower abdomen or groin  
- Symptoms of metastasis, such as back pain or respiratory issues  

investigations  
- Clinical examination, including testicular ultrasound  
- Serum tumour markers (e.g., AFP, hCG, LDH)  
- CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis  
- MRI for further evaluation if necessary  
- Biopsy may be performed in specific cases  

diagnosis  
- Histological confirmation via testicular biopsy or surgical excision  
- Elevated serum tumour markers indicating specific cancer type  
- Imaging studies confirming the presence of a mass  
- Clinical history and physical examination suggestive of malignancy  
- Ruling out other causes of testicular swelling or masses  

management  
- Surgery to remove the affected testis (orchiectomy)  
  - Perform inguinal approach for the procedure  
  - Immediate assessment of the tumour type  
- Chemotherapy for advanced or high-risk cases  
  - Based on platinum-based regimens  
  - Follow-up imaging to assess response  
- Radiotherapy for certain types of seminomas  
  - Considered after surgical removal if high risk of recurrence  
  - Targeted to regional lymph nodes  
- Surveillance protocols post-treatment  
  - Regular monitoring of serum markers  
  - Follow-up imaging as indicated  
- Multidisciplinary team involvement for comprehensive care  
  - Include urologist, oncologist, and support services  
  - Provide information and emotional support to patients and families  







Testicular torsion

Background  
- Testicular torsion is a surgical emergency involving the twisting of the spermatic cord, compromising blood supply to the testicle  

Risk factors  
- Adolescent males are at highest risk  
- History of previous torsion or surgery  
- Abnormal testicular anatomy, such as bell clapper deformity  
- Physical activity or trauma to the testicles  
- Family history of testicular torsion  

Causes  
- Sudden movement or trauma  
- Anatomical predisposition leading to inadequate fixation  
- Congenital abnormalities affecting the spermatic cord  
- Rapid growth during puberty  
- Increased testicular mobility  

Pathophysiology  
- Twisting of the spermatic cord leads to compromised blood flow  
- Ischaemia occurs due to venous occlusion followed by arterial compromise  
- Testicular necrosis can develop if not treated within a few hours  
- Inflammatory response and increased intra-testicular pressure  
- Recovery potential decreases sharply after the initial 6 hours  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sudden onset severe unilateral scrotal pain  
- Nausea and vomiting may accompany pain  
- Swelling and tenderness of the affected testicle  
- High-riding testicle or abnormal position  
- Absence of cremasteric reflex on the affected side  

Investigations  
- Urgent physical examination focusing on scrotal findings  
- Doppler ultrasound to evaluate blood flow to the testicle  
- Consideration of a urine dipstick for signs of infection  
- Serum markers may be evaluated to rule out other conditions  
- Surgical exploration if clinical suspicion remains high despite imaging  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation of acute scrotal pain and tenderness  
- Absence of cremasteric reflex and abnormal testicle position  
- Imaging findings suggestive of decreased blood flow from Doppler ultrasound  
- Urgent referral for surgical evaluation if torsion is suspected  
- Differential diagnosis to exclude epididymitis or trauma  

Management  
- Immediate surgical detorsion and fixation of the testicle  
  - Testicular salvage rate decreases with time (ideally within 6 hours)  
  - Bilateral fixation performed to prevent recurrence  
- Analgesia to manage pain during evaluations and transport  
  - Adequate pain relief is crucial for patient comfort  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs may be used  
- Post-operative care includes monitoring for complications  
  - Regular follow-up for pain and healing assessment  
  - Education regarding signs of recurrence or complications  
- Consider orchidopexy for patients with anatomical concerns  
  - Long-term follow-up may be necessary for growth and function  
  - Genetic counselling if familial predisposition identified  
- Discussion on fertility preservation options in older adolescents  
  - Sperm banking may be considered prior to surgery  
  - Reassurance and psychological support for affected individuals  







Thyroid eye disease

background  
- Thyroid eye disease is an autoimmune condition affecting the orbit, commonly associated with hyperthyroidism  

risk factors  
- Female sex  
- Age over 30  
- Smoking  
- Pre-existing thyroid disease  
- Family history of autoimmune diseases  

causes  
- Autoimmune reaction against thyroid-stimulating hormone receptors  
- Increased production of glycosaminoglycans  
- Inflammation of orbital tissues  
- Increased retro-orbital fat and muscle volume  
- Graves' disease is the most common associated thyroid condition  

pathophysiology  
- Activation of T lymphocytes targeting orbital fibroblasts  
- Accumulation of glycosaminoglycans in orbital tissue  
- Swelling of extraocular muscles  
- Increased orbital tissue pressure  
- Resulting in proptosis and diplopia  

signs and symptoms  
- Proptosis or exophthalmos  
- Diplopia or double vision  
- Eyelid retraction or lag  
- Orbital pain or discomfort  
- Vision changes or loss  

investigations  
- Thyroid function tests (TSH, T3, T4)  
- Orbit imaging (CT or MRI)  
- Visual acuity assessment  
- Exophthalmometry (to measure proptosis)  
- Thyroid antibodies testing (e.g., TSI)  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of signs and symptoms  
- Exophthalmos present in 90% of patients  
- Based on thyroid function test results  
- Imaging to assess severity and rule out other conditions  
- Confirmed association with Graves' disease  

management  
- Smoking cessation advice  
- Symptomatic management with lubricating eye drops  
- Corticosteroids for moderate to severe cases  
  - Oral or intravenous administration  
  - Monitor for side effects  
- Surgical options for severe cases  
  - Orbital decompression surgery  
  - Strabismus surgery for diplopia  
- Radioactive iodine in instances of hyperthyroidism  
  - May modify course of eye disease  
  - Monitor thyroid function regularly  







Thyroid nodules

background  
Thyroid nodules are common, with most being benign and often discovered incidentally during imaging or physical examination  

risk factors  
Female gender  
Advanced age  
History of radiation exposure to the neck  
Family history of thyroid disease  
Previous thyroid surgery  

causes  
Colloid nodules  
Thyroid adenoma  
Thyroid cysts  
Malignancy (e.g., papillary, follicular carcinoma)  
Hashimoto's thyroiditis  

pathophysiology  
Formation occurs due to hyperplasia, neoplasia, or inflammation within the thyroid gland  
Benign nodules often arise from overproduction of thyroid hormones or structural abnormalities  
Malignant nodules may result from genetic mutations or environmental factors influencing cell growth  

signs and symptoms  
Painless neck swelling  
Dysphagia or difficulty swallowing  
Pressure sensation or hoarseness  
Thyroid dysfunction (hyperthyroidism or hypothyroidism)  
Lymphadenopathy (may suggest malignancy)  

investigations  
Thyroid function tests (TFTs) to assess hormonal status  
Ultrasound assessment of the nodule's characteristics  
Fine needle aspiration (FNA) biopsy for cytological evaluation  
Thyroid scan (radionuclide scan) if hyperthyroid  
CT or MRI if suspicious for malignancy or for local staging  

diagnosis  
Determine if nodules are benign based on characteristics and cytology  
Assess any suspicious features on ultrasound that may indicate malignancy  
Refer to guidelines regarding nodule size for FNA consideration  
Evaluate thyroid function tests for any hormonal abnormalities  
Consider family history and clinical presentation  

management  
Observation for benign nodules without significant symptoms  
- Regular ultrasound monitoring  
- Review every 6-18 months  
Surgical intervention for nodules with malignant features  
- Total or partial thyroidectomy based on extent  
- Ensure proper post-operative care and follow-up  
Treat thyroid dysfunction if present  
- Manage hyperthyroidism with antithyroid medications or radioactive iodine  
- Manage hypothyroidism with levothyroxine replacement therapy  
Refer to endocrinology for complex cases or uncertain malignancy risk  







Thyrotoxicosis

Background  
- Thyrotoxicosis is a condition resulting from excessive thyroid hormone levels in the body

Risk factors  
- History of autoimmune diseases  
- Family history of thyroid disorders  
- Female gender  
- Age between 20 and 40 years  
- Recent pregnancy or childbirth  

Causes  
- Graves' disease  
- Toxic multinodular goitre  
- Thyroiditis  
- Excessive iodine intake  
- Overmedication with thyroid hormone  

Pathophysiology  
- Elevated levels of thyroid hormones increase metabolic rate  
- Leads to increased heart rate, temperature, and energy expenditure  
- Alters the function of multiple organ systems  
- Can cause increased catecholamine sensitivity  
- Results in symptoms of hypermetabolism  

Signs and symptoms  
- Weight loss despite normal or increased appetite  
- Heat intolerance and excessive sweating  
- Palpitations and tachycardia  
- Tremors and anxiety  
- Fatigue and muscle weakness  

Investigations  
- Serum thyroid hormone levels (T3, T4)  
- Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH) levels  
- Thyroid autoantibodies tests  
- Radioactive iodine uptake test  
- Ultrasound of the thyroid gland  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmatory serum tests showing low TSH with elevated T3 and T4  
- Assessment of clinical symptoms extensively  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other endocrine disorders  
- Imaging studies if indicated for structural assessment  
- Review of medication history  

Management  
- Antithyroid medications (propylthiouracil or carbimazole)  
  - Monitor for adverse effects  
  - Regular thyroid function tests  
- Beta-blockers for symptomatic relief  
  - Control of tachycardia and anxiety  
  - Adjust dosage based on response  
- Radioactive iodine treatment for definitive management  
  - Contraindicated in pregnancy  
  - Requires monitoring for hypothyroidism post-treatment  
- Surgery (thyroidectomy) in specific cases  
  - Consider in large goitres or malignancy suspicion  
  - Requires thyroid function normalisation preoperatively  
- Regular follow-up and monitoring of thyroid function  
  - Adjust treatment based on symptomatology and lab results  
  - Educate about signs of recurrence or complications  







Tonsillitis

Background  
- Tonsillitis is the inflammation of the tonsils, commonly due to infection  

Risk factors  
- Age: more common in children aged 5 to 15  
- Frequent exposure to infections  
- Poor hygiene  
- Presence of allergies  
- Family history of recurrent tonsillitis  

Causes  
- Viral infections: e.g. *Epstein-Barr virus*, *adenovirus*  
- Bacterial infections: e.g. *Streptococcus pyogenes*  
- Fungal infections  
- Allergic reactions  
- Environmental irritants such as smoke  

Pathophysiology  
- Tonsillar tissue responds to pathogens with inflammation  
- Hyperplasia of lymphoid tissue occurs due to immune response  
- Suppuration may occur in bacterial infections  
- Possible capsule formation leading to peritonsillar abscess  
- Recurrent infections can lead to chronic tonsillitis  

Signs and symptoms  
- Sore throat  
- Difficulty swallowing  
- Fever  
- Swollen and red tonsils  
- Bad breath  

Investigations  
- Throat swab for microbiological analysis  
- Rapid antigen detection test for Group A Streptococcus  
- Complete blood count for signs of infection  
- Lateral neck X-ray if suspected complications  
- Assessment of past medical history  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment of symptoms and signs  
- Consideration of duration and frequency of episodes  
- Evaluation for accompanying symptoms such as cough or conjunctivitis  
- Confirmation of bacterial aetiology if applicable  
- Consider other differential diagnoses  

Management  
- Ensure adequate hydration and rest  
  - Encourage fluid intake to prevent dehydration  
  - Advise rest to support immune function  
- Pain relief with analgesics  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen for symptom relief  
  - Avoid aspirin in children due to Reye's syndrome risk  
- Antibiotic therapy if bacterial infection is confirmed  
  - Use penicillin or amoxicillin for streptococcal infections  
  - Consider alternatives for those with penicillin allergy  
- Surgery for recurrent or severe cases  
  - Tonsillectomy may be indicated after multiple episodes  
  - Referral to ENT specialist as necessary  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Be vigilant for signs of peritonsillar abscess  
  - Follow up if the condition does not improve after treatment  







Toxic shock syndrome

Background  
- Toxic shock syndrome is a life-threatening condition caused by bacterial toxins, primarily associated with *Staphylococcus aureus* and *Streptococcus pyogenes*

Risk factors  
- Menstruating women using superabsorbent tampons  
- Surgical wounds or recent surgical procedures  
- Skin injuries, such as burns or insect bites  
- Presence of foreign bodies, such as nasal packing  
- Immunocompromised individuals  

Causes  
- Toxin-producing strains of *Staphylococcus aureus*  
- Toxin-producing strains of *Streptococcus pyogenes*  
- Contaminated equipment or devices  
- Infections following childbirth or abortion  
- Skin infections leading to systemic spread  

Pathophysiology  
- Bacterial exotoxins enter the bloodstream  
- Toxins activate immune response and cause cytokine release
