- Resulting in vascular leakage and multi-organ dysfunction  
- Hypotension and shock from altered vascular permeability  
- Activation of the coagulation cascade leading to clot formation  

Signs and symptoms  
- High fever over 38.9°C  
- Rash resembling a sunburn, peeling skin  
- Hypotension or consistent low blood pressure  
- Multisystem involvement symptoms such as confusion, muscle aches  
- Gastrointestinal symptoms, such as vomiting and diarrhoea  

Investigations  
- Blood cultures to identify bacterial pathogens  
- Complete blood count to assess leukocytosis or thrombocytopenia  
- Liver and renal function tests for organ impairment  
- Electrolyte levels to monitor for imbalances  
- Imaging studies if an abscess or focus of infection is suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation consistent with TSS  
- Evidence of systemic involvement (two or more organ systems)  
- Isolation of causative organism from a sterile site  
- Exclusion of other causes for severe illness  
- Confirmation of elevated toxin levels may support diagnosis  

Management  
- Immediate hospitalisation for supportive care  
   - Intravenous fluids to restore circulation  
   - Vasopressors if hypotension does not improve  
- Antibiotic therapy tailored to the organism  
   - Clindamycin for toxin suppression  
   - Consideration of additional agents based on culture results  








Transfusion reactions

Background  
- Transfusion reactions are adverse responses occurring following blood transfusions, ranging from mild to life-threatening

Risk factors  
- Previous blood transfusions  
- Alloimmunisation (antibody formation to transfused blood)  
- ABO and Rh incompatibility  
- Underlying medical conditions (e.g., renal disease, immunocompromised status)  
- Age extremes (very young or elderly patients)  

Causes  
- ABO mismatch  
- Rh incompatibility  
- Febrile non-haemolytic reactions  
- Allergic reactions (e.g., urticaria, anaphylaxis)  
- Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI)  

Pathophysiology  
- Immune-mediated destruction of transfused red blood cells  
- Activation of complement system leading to inflammation  
- Release of cytokines and inflammatory mediators  
- Activation of coagulation pathways  
- Microvascular injury and organ dysfunction  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fever and chills  
- Rash, urticaria, or itching  
- Shortness of breath and chest pain  
- Dark urine or jaundice  
- Hypotension and tachycardia  

Investigations  
- Blood sample for serology (group and crossmatch)  
- Direct Coombs test  
- Blood cultures for bacterial contamination  
- Urinalysis to check for hemoglobinuria  
- Imaging (e.g., chest X-ray for TRALI)  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and timing of symptoms  
- Laboratory confirmation of transfusion reaction  
- Ruling out alternative diagnoses  
- Correlation of symptoms with transfusion records  
- Evaluation of blood samples for abnormalities  

Management  
- Immediate cessation of transfusion  
  - Maintain venous access with normal saline  
  - Inform the blood bank and clinical team  
- Supportive measures based on symptoms  
  - Antipyretics for fever and chills  
  - Corticosteroids or antihistamines for allergic reactions  
- Investigation of the reaction  
  - Send blood samples for serological testing  
  - Document and report the incident as per local protocols   
- Monitor vital signs closely  
  - Continuous observation for worsening symptoms  
  - Assess for possible organ dysfunction  
- Follow up with patient and family  
  - Provide information about potential risks for future transfusions  
  - Discuss emotional and psychological support if necessary  







Transient ischaemic attacks

background  
- Transient ischaemic attacks are temporary episodes of neurological dysfunction due to focal brain ischaemia.

risk factors  
- Age over 55  
- Hypertension  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hyperlipidaemia  
- Smoking  

causes  
- Embolic events from carotid artery stenosis  
- Cardiac embolism from atrial fibrillation  
- Small vessel disease  
- Vasculitis  
- Hypoperfusion   

pathophysiology  
- Brief interruption of blood supply to the brain  
- Neuronal dysfunction without permanent damage  
- Reversible ischaemic changes in brain tissue  
- Loss of neural function correlates with ischaemic area  
- Restoration of blood flow typically restores function  

signs and symptoms  
- Sudden weakness or numbness on one side of the body  
- Difficulty speaking or understanding speech  
- Visual disturbances in one or both eyes  
- Dizziness or loss of balance  
- Duration usually less than 24 hours  

investigations  
- CT or MRI brain scans to rule out stroke  
- Carotid Doppler ultrasound for stenosis assessment  
- ECG to detect cardiac arrhythmias  
- Blood tests including lipids and glucose  
- Transoesophageal echocardiogram if cardioembolic source suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of symptoms resolving within 24 hours  
- Exclusion of other neurological conditions  
- Confirmation of risk factors and vascular pathology  
- Utilisation of imaging studies for assessment  
- Identification of minor stroke or transient ischaemic attack  

management  
- Immediate referral to a specialist  
   - Consider neurology or stroke unit referral   
   - Initiate secondary prevention strategies  
- Antiplatelet therapy initiation  
   - Aspirin or clopidogrel recommended  
   - Consider dual therapy in select cases  
- Management of risk factors  
   - Control of hypertension, diabetes, and lipids  
   - Smoking cessation support  
- Lifestyle modifications  
   - Encourage regular exercise and a healthy diet  
   - Promote weight loss if overweight or obese  
- Monitoring and follow-up  
   - Regular assessments for recurrent events  
   - Adjustment of treatment based on risk factor control  







Trichomonas vaginalis

background  
- Trichomonas vaginalis is a sexually transmitted protozoan that causes vaginitis in females and urethritis in males  

risk factors  
- Multiple sexual partners  
- Lack of condom use  
- Previous history of sexually transmitted infections  
- Female sex  
- Immunocompromised state  

causes  
- Infection with the protozoan *Trichomonas vaginalis*  
- Sexual transmission  
- Possible non-sexual transmission via sharing damp towels or bathing suits  
- Presence of other concurrent STIs  
- Disruption of vaginal flora  

pathophysiology  
- *T. vaginalis* invades the epithelial cells of the urogenital tract  
- Causes inflammation and immune response  
- Alters the local microenvironment, affecting normal flora  
- Can lead to mucosal damage and discharge  
- In males, can cause urethritis and prostatitis  

signs and symptoms  
- Copious, frothy, greenish-yellow discharge in females  
- Vulvar itching and irritation  
- Dyspareunia  
- Asymptomatic cases in males  
- Dysuria or penile discharge in males  

investigations  
- Microscopic examination of vaginal secretions  
- Culture of *T. vaginalis* from discharge or urine  
- Nucleic acid amplification tests (NAATs)  
- pH testing of vaginal secretions (typically >4.5)  
- Wet mount microscopy  

diagnosis  
- Clinical presentation and signs  
- Microscopy showing motile trophozoites  
- Positive culture for *T. vaginalis*  
- Positive NAATs  
- Exclusion of other causes of vaginitis or urethritis  

management  
- Oral metronidazole or tinidazole (1-2 g as a single dose)  
    - Treat both partners to prevent reinfection  
    - Consider alternative regimens for pregnant patients  
- Advise on safe sex practices and regular screening  
    - Provide education about STI prevention  
    - Encourage the use of condoms  









Trigeminal neuralgia

background
- Trigeminal neuralgia is a chronic pain condition affecting the trigeminal nerve, characterised by recurrent episodes of intense facial pain

risk factors
- Age over 50 years 
- Female gender 
- Multiple sclerosis 
- Vascular compression of the trigeminal nerve 
- Family history of trigeminal neuralgia 

causes
- Idiopathic in many cases 
- Compression of the trigeminal nerve by blood vessels 
- Multiple sclerosis leading to demyelination 
- Tumours affecting the trigeminal nerve 
- Structural abnormalities in the brainstem

pathophysiology
- Ectopic action potential generation in the trigeminal nerve 
- Loss of myelin insulation leading to hyperactivity 
- Dysfunction in central pain processing mechanisms 
- Increased excitability of trigeminal neurons 
- Dysfunction in GABAergic neurotransmission 

signs and symptoms
- Severe, sharp, stabbing facial pain 
- Pain triggered by light touch, eating, or movement 
- Episodes lasting seconds to minutes 
- Unilateral facial distribution typically in the jaw, cheek, or forehead 
- Possible sensory loss or altered sensation

investigations
- Clinical assessment of symptoms 
- Cranial MRI to rule out structural lesions 
- CT scan if MRI is contraindicated 
- Neurological examination for secondary causes 
- Blood tests if associated with systemic conditions 

diagnosis
- Diagnosis based on clinical history and symptom characteristics 
- Presence of paroxysmal facial pain with specific triggers 
- Exclusion of secondary causes through imaging 
- Consideration of age and symptom onset 
- Confirmation of diagnosis through response to treatment 

management
- First-line: Carbamazepine or oxycarbazepine
  - Monitor for side effects and therapeutic levels
  - Consider switching agents if ineffective
- Second-line: Lamotrigine or baclofen
  - Assess efficacy and tolerability 
  - Use in conjunction with first-line agents if necessary 
- Surgical: Microvascular decompression or rhizotomy 
  - Consider for refractory cases or significant side effects
  - Discuss potential risks and benefits with the patient 
- Non-pharmacological: Acupuncture or psychological support 
  - Consider as adjunctive therapy 
  - Evaluate individual patient response 
- Referral to a specialist pain clinic if required 
  - Discuss comprehensive pain management strategies 
  - Collaborate for optimal patient care







Tuberculosis

Background  
- Tuberculosis is a contagious bacterial infection primarily affecting the lungs but can involve other organs  

Risk factors  
- Close contact with an infected individual  
- Immunocompromised states (e.g., HIV, diabetes)  
- Malnutrition or low socioeconomic status  
- Travel to or residence in endemic areas  
- Substance abuse (e.g., alcohol, drugs)  

Causes  
- Infection by *Mycobacterium tuberculosis*  
- Airborne transmission through inhalation of respiratory droplets  
- Reactivation of latent tuberculosis infection  
- Infection from direct contact with contaminated surfaces or sputum  
- Rarely, zoonotic transmission from animals carrying *Mycobacterium bovis*  

Pathophysiology  
- Inhaled bacilli reach alveoli, where they are phagocytosed by macrophages  
- Bacilli survive and multiply within macrophages, leading to granuloma formation  
- Granulomas can either control the infection or become necrotic, resulting in tissue damage  
- Activated immune response contributes to chronic inflammation and fibrosis  
- Potential spread to other organs can occur through the lymphatic or haematogenous routes  

Signs and symptoms  
- Persistent cough lasting more than three weeks  
- Haemoptysis (coughing up blood)  
- Night sweats and fever  
- Unexplained weight loss and fatigue  
- Chest pain and difficulty breathing  

Investigations  
- Tuberculin skin test or interferon-gamma release assays for latent TB  
- Chest X-ray to assess pulmonary involvement  
- Sputum smear microscopy for acid-fast bacilli  
- Sputum culture for definitive diagnosis  
- Nucleic acid amplification tests for rapid diagnosis  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Positive tuberculin skin test or IGRA in symptomatic individuals  
- Identification of *Mycobacterium tuberculosis* via culture or PCR  
- Chest X-ray showing typical features of TB disease  
- Exclusion of other diagnoses  

Management  
- First-line antituberculous therapy (e.g., isoniazid, rifampicin, pyrazinamide, ethambutol)  
  - 6-month regimen for drug-susceptible pulmonary TB  
  - Extended treatment duration for extrapulmonary or drug-resistant TB  
- Directly observed therapy to ensure adherence  
  - Regular follow-up and monitoring for treatment side effects  
  - Support services for social and psychological needs  







Unstable angina

Background  
- Unstable angina is a form of acute coronary syndrome characterised by increased frequency or severity of chest pain

Risk factors  
- Advanced age  
- Smoking  
- Hypertension  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Hyperlipidaemia  

Causes  
- Plaque rupture in coronary arteries  
- Thrombus formation  
- Coronary vasospasm  
- Aortic dissection  
- Severe anaemia  

Pathophysiology  
- Imbalance between myocardial oxygen supply and demand  
- Reduced coronary blood flow due to occlusion  
- Ischaemic episodes lead to chest pain and potential myocardial infarction  

Signs and symptoms  
- Chest pain that occurs at rest or with minimal exertion  
- Pain may radiate to jaw, shoulder, or arm  
- Shortness of breath  
- Sweating or clammy skin  
- Nausea or light-headedness  

Investigations  
- 12-lead ECG to identify ischaemic changes  
- Cardiac biomarkers (e.g., troponin) to assess myocardial injury  
- Chest X-ray to rule out other causes  
- Echocardiography to evaluate heart function  
- Coronary angiography for definitive assessment  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history of sudden onset chest pain  
- Exclusion of other causes of chest pain  
- Positive cardiac biomarkers indicating myocardial injury  
- Ischaemic changes on ECG  
- Angiographic evidence of coronary artery disease  

Management  
- Immediate assessment and stabilisation of the patient  
  - Administer oxygen if hypoxic  
  - Use nitroglycerin for pain relief  
- Antiplatelet therapy initiation  
  - Aspirin and clopidogrel  
  - Consider anticoagulation (e.g., heparin)  
- Risk factor modification  
  - Lifestyle changes  
  - Pharmacotherapy for hypertension, diabetes, and lipids  
- Revascularisation procedures  
  - Percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI)  
  - Coronary artery bypass grafting (CABG) if indicated  
- Cardiac rehabilitation following discharge  
  - Structured exercise programme  
  - Education on medication adherence and lifestyle changes  







Upper limb fractures

background  
- Common injuries in both adults and children, often resulting from falls or trauma  

risk factors  
- Age-related bone density loss  
- High-impact sports or activities  
- Osteoporosis  
- Poor balance or coordination  
- Previous fractures  

causes  
- Falls, particularly from height  
- Direct blows to the arm  
- Sports injuries  
- Road traffic accidents  
- Osteoporotic bone fragility  

pathophysiology  
- Discontinuity of cortical and trabecular bone structures  
- Muscle and soft tissue injury accompanying fracture  
- Inflammatory response leading to swelling and pain  
- Healing involves callus formation, bone remodelling, and restoration of anatomy  

signs and symptoms  
- Pain and tenderness at the fracture site  
- Swelling and bruising  
- Deformity or abnormal positioning of the limb  
- Reduced range of motion  
- Numbness or tingling if nerves are affected  

investigations  
- Clinical examination to assess deformity and function  
- X-rays to confirm fracture type and location  
- CT scan if intra-articular involvement suspected  
- MRI for soft tissue injury evaluation  
- Ultrasound for paediatric fractures  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of trauma  
- Physical examination findings correlating with fracture  
- Radiological evidence from imaging studies  
- Differentiation from other musculoskeletal injuries  
- Classification of fracture type (e.g., simple, compound, displaced)  

management  
- Initial immobilisation with a splint or sling  
  - Analgesia for pain relief  
  - Elevation to reduce swelling  
- Surgical intervention if necessary  
  - ORIF for displaced fractures  
  - External fixation for complex injuries  
- Non-surgical management for stable fractures  
  - Closed reduction and cast  
  - Regular follow-up for healing assessment  
- Physiotherapy post-fracture healing  
  - Strengthening exercises  
  - Range of motion rehabilitation  
- Patient education on fracture prevention  
  - Advice on lifestyle modifications  
  - Discuss osteoporosis management if applicable







Upper limb soft tissue injury

background  
- Upper limb soft tissue injuries encompass a range of conditions affecting muscles, tendons, ligaments, and fascia  

risk factors  
- Participation in high-risk sports  
- Previous injury to the same area  
- Poor flexibility and strength  
- Advanced age  
- Inadequate warm-up before activities  

causes  
- Trauma from falls or accidents  
- Overuse injuries from repetitive motions  
- Acute strains or sprains  
- Contusions from impact  
- Chronic inflammatory conditions  

pathophysiology  
- Damage to connective tissues leading to inflammation  
- Disruption of vascular supply causing ischaemia  
- Tissue necrosis if untreated  
- Activation of nociceptive pathways contributing to pain  
- Formation of scar tissue during healing  

signs and symptoms  
- Localised pain and tenderness  
- Swelling and bruising in the affected area  
- Decreased range of motion  
- Weakness in the limb  
- Crepitus or abnormal sounds during movement  

investigations  
- Clinical examination to assess function and pain  
- X-rays to rule out fractures  
- Ultrasound to evaluate soft tissue structures  
- MRI for detailed imaging of soft tissue  
- Blood tests if infection or systemic issues are suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history to identify the mechanism of injury  
- Physical examination to localise symptoms  
- Confirmatory imaging tests if needed  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other conditions  
- Assessment of functional impairment  

management  
- R.I.C.E. protocol for acute injuries  
  - Rest the affected limb  
  - Ice application to reduce swelling  
- Physiotherapy and rehabilitation exercises  
  - Strengthening and flexibility training  
  - Gradual return to activity  
- Pain management with analgesics  
  - Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs  
  - Prescription medications if needed  
- Surgery for severe or persistent cases  
  - Repair of torn ligaments or tendons  
  - Debridement of damaged tissue  
- Education on prevention and lifestyle changes  
  - Ergonomic assessments  
  - Guidance on safe exercise practices  







Upper respiratory tract infection

Background  
- Common viral illnesses affecting the nasal passages, sinuses, throat, and larynx

Risk factors  
- Young age with immature immune systems  
- Exposure to tobacco smoke  
- Allergies or asthma  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Immunocompromised states  

Causes  
- Rhinoviruses as the most common viral pathogen  
- Influenza viruses  
- Coronaviruses including *SARS-CoV-2*   
- Adenoviruses  
- Bacterial pathogens like *Streptococcus pneumoniae*  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammation of the upper respiratory epithelium  
- Increased mucosal secretion and nasal obstruction  
- Disruption of mucociliary clearance  
- Possible secondary bacterial infection  
- Immune response leading to local symptoms  

Signs and symptoms  
- Nasal congestion or rhinorrhoea  
- Sore throat  
- Cough  
- Fever and malaise  
- Headache  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Nasal swab for viral testing if necessary  
- Throat swab for bacterial culture in severe cases  
- Complete blood count in complicated cases  
- Chest X-ray if pneumonia is suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and history  
- Exclusion of serious conditions like bacterial infections  
- Consideration of symptom duration and severity  
- Use of specific tests only in atypical presentations  
- Diagnosis of acute pharyngitis if mainly tonsil inflammation  

Management  
- Symptomatic relief with analgesics and antipyretics  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen for pain and fever  
  - Throat lozenges for sore throat  
- Hydration and rest  
  - Encouraging fluid intake  
  - Advising adequate sleep and rest  
- Decongestants for nasal congestion  
  - Oral pseudoephedrine  
  - Topical oxymetazoline for short-term use  
- Antihistamines for allergy-related symptoms  
  - Cetirizine or loratadine  
  - Avoiding sedating antihistamines in some cases  
- Antibiotics only for confirmed bacterial infections  
  - Amoxicillin for *S. pneumoniae*  
  - Follow-up if symptoms worsen or do not improve  







Urinary incontinence

background  
- Urinary incontinence is the involuntary loss of urine, impacting daily life and mental well-being  

risk factors  
- Female sex  
- Increasing age  
- Obesity  
- Neurological disorders  
- Previous pelvic surgery  

causes  
- Overactive bladder  
- Stress incontinence due to pelvic floor weakness  
- Overflow incontinence from urinary retention  
- Functional incontinence related to cognitive or mobility issues  
- Neurological conditions like multiple sclerosis or stroke  

pathophysiology  
- Detrusor muscle overactivity causing frequent contractions  
- Weak pelvic floor muscles leading to insufficient support for the bladder  
- Obstruction in urinary tract resulting in overflow  
- Impaired neurological control affecting bladder storage and voiding  
- Conditions altering sensory or motor pathways  

signs and symptoms  
- Urgency to urinate  
- Frequency of urination, particularly at night  
- Leakage of urine during physical activity, sneezing, or coughing  
- Urinary retention with overflow  
- Inability to reach the toilet in time  

investigations  
- Patient history and bladder diary  
- Physical examination including pelvic examination  
- Urinalysis to rule out infection  
- Post-void residual volume measurement  
- Urodynamic studies if necessary  

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on patient history and symptoms  
- Consideration of specific type of incontinence  
- Rule out other conditions  
- Use of questionnaires to evaluate impact on quality of life  
- Diagnosis confirmed through symptom pattern and investigations  

management  
- Lifestyle modifications such as weight loss and fluid management  
- Pelvic floor muscle training, including Kegel exercises  
- Pharmacological treatment, such as anticholinergic agents for overactive bladder  
  - Also consider beta-3 agonists  
- Surgical options for stress incontinence, such as mid-urethral sling procedures  
  - Refer to surgical consultation as necessary  
- Management of underlying conditions to reduce incontinence symptoms  







Urinary tract calculi

Background  
- Urinary tract calculi, or kidney stones, are solid deposits formed from minerals and salts that can cause obstruction and pain.

Risk factors  
- Family history of urinary stones  
- Previous history of stones  
- Dehydration or inadequate fluid intake  
- Certain dietary habits, such as high salt or protein intake  
- Obesity and metabolic disorders  

Causes  
- Supersaturation of urine with stone-forming constituents  
- Urinary tract infections leading to struvite stones  
- Hypercalcemia from various conditions causing calcium stones  
- Cystinuria causing cystine stones  
- Uric acid necrosis in metabolic conditions  

Pathophysiology  
- Crystallisation of solutes in the urine due to supersaturation  
- Growth and aggregation of crystals into calculi  
- Obstruction of urine flow causing hydronephrosis and renal colic  
- Inflammation and pain due to stone impaction  
- Infection in the urinary tract can occur with some stones  

Signs and symptoms  
- Severe flank pain or renal colic  
- Hematuria (blood in urine)  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Dysuria (painful urination)  
- Urgency and frequency in urination  

Investigations  
- Urinalysis to check for blood, crystals, and infection  
- Non-contrast CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis  
- Ultrasound of the kidneys and urinary tract  
- X-ray of the kidneys, ureters, and bladder (KUB) if indicated  
- 24-hour urine collection for stone analysis  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and imaging findings  
- Identification of the type of stone from urine analysis  
- Assessment of metabolic risk factors  
- Documenting presence of infection or obstruction  
- Exclusion of other causes of flank pain  

Management  
- Pain relief with NSAIDs or opioids  
  - Assess renal function before NSAIDs  
  - Monitor for potential side effects  
- Increase fluid intake to facilitate stone passage  
  - Aim for 2-3 litres of fluid intake daily  
  - Use electrolytes if recurrent stones are an issue  
- Medical therapy for specific stone types  
  - Thiazide diuretics for calcium stones  
  - Allopurinol for uric acid stones  
- Surgical intervention for large stones or obstruction  
  - Ureteroscopy or percutaneous nephrolithotomy as required  
  - Shockwave lithotripsy for certain stone sizes  
- Education on dietary and lifestyle modifications  
  - Recommend low-sodium and low-animal protein diet  
  - Encourage regular exercise and weight management  







Urinary tract infection

Background  
- Common bacterial infection affecting the urinary system, especially among women

Risk factors  
- Female gender due to shorter urethra  
- Sexual activity  
- Urinary retention or obstruction  
- Diabetes mellitus  
- Use of urinary catheters  

Causes  
- Escherichia coli (most common pathogen)  
- Other bacteria such as Klebsiella, Proteus, and Enterococcus  
- Fungal infections, particularly in immunocompromised individuals  
- Urinary tract abnormalities  
- Recent antibiotic use leading to flora alteration  

Pathophysiology  
- Bacteria ascend from the urethra to the bladder  
- Inflammatory response leads to symptoms  
- Possible progression to pyelonephritis if untreated  

Signs and symptoms  
- Dysuria (painful urination)  
- Urgency and frequency of urination  
- Flank pain or suprapubic discomfort  
- Cloudy or foul-smelling urine  
- Fever and chills in severe cases  

Investigations  
- Urinalysis to detect nitrites, leucocytes, and blood  
- Urine culture for bacterial identification  
- Microscopy of urine for validation of findings  
- Blood tests if pyelonephritis is suspected  
- Ultrasound if structural abnormalities are a concern  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical symptoms and urinalysis findings  
- Positive urine culture confirming infection  
- Consideration of patient’s history and risk factors  
- Exclusion of other possible urinary conditions  
- Use of scoring systems if necessary for complex cases  

Management  
- Antibiotics based on culture sensitivity  
  - Trimethoprim or nitrofurantoin for simple cases  
  - Ciprofloxacin for complicated infections  
- Pain relief and hydration  
  - Analgesics for symptomatic relief  
  - Increased fluid intake to aid flushing of bacteria  








Urticaria

Background  
- Urticaria is a common skin condition characterised by itchy wheals and hives.

Risk factors  
- Allergic reactions to foods or medications  
- Insect stings or bites  
- Physical triggers such as heat or pressure  
- Stress and autoimmune disorders  
- Viral infections

Causes  
- Immunological responses to allergens  
- Direct mast cell activation  
- Drugs, including NSAIDs and antibiotics  
- Infections, particularly viral  
- Chronic conditions such as lupus or thyroid disease

Pathophysiology  
- Degranulation of mast cells releases histamine and other mediators  
- Increased vascular permeability leads to swelling and redness  
- Can be acute or chronic based on duration and triggers  
- Chronic urticaria often has an autoimmune component  
- Symptoms result from local inflammatory responses

Signs and symptoms  
- Itchy wheals on the skin, often raised and erythematous  
- Angioedema may occur, particularly around eyes and lips  
- Symptoms can vary in intensity and duration  
- Episodes may be recurrent or persistent  
- Potential for anaphylaxis in severe cases if triggers include allergens  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Allergy testing to identify specific triggers  
- Blood tests to assess for underlying conditions  
- Skin tests for contact dermatitis  
- Diary of symptoms to track triggers and patterns

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and history  
- Rule out other causes of hives through investigations  
- Consider acute versus chronic classification  
- Assess for any associated symptoms like angioedema  
- Adapt diagnosis depending on the presence of underlying conditions

Management  
- Avoidance of known triggers  
  - Educate patients on potential allergens  
  - Consider allergist referral for severe cases  
- Pharmacological management with antihistamines  
  - First-generation or second-generation antihistamines as needed  
  - Advise on dosage and timing for maximum effect  
- Corticosteroids for severe or persistent urticaria  
  - Short courses to manage acute flares  
  - Avoid long-term use due to side effects  
- Immunotherapy for chronic cases unresponsive to medications  
  - Under specialist supervision and assessment  
  - Monitor for potential side effects  
- Consider referral to dermatology or allergy specialists  
  - For ongoing management and treatment changes  
  - For assessment of less common forms of urticaria







Uveitis

Background  
- Uveitis is an inflammation of the uveal tract, which includes the iris, ciliary body, and choroid  

Risk factors  
- Autoimmune diseases  
- Infectious diseases  
- Previous eye trauma  
- Family history of uveitis  
- Age (more common in young adults)  

Causes  
- Infections (e.g. *Toxoplasma*, *Syphilis*)  
- Autoimmune conditions (e.g. Behçet's disease, sarcoidosis)  
- Idiopathic cases  
- Inflammatory bowel disease  
- Systemic lupus erythematosus  

Pathophysiology  
- Inflammatory cell infiltration into the uveal tract  
- Increased vascular permeability leading to exudation  
- Disruption of blood-aqueous and blood-retinal barriers  
- Potential secondary neuronal damage  
- May involve immune-mediated mechanisms  

Signs and symptoms  
- Redness of the eye  
- Pain or discomfort  
- Photophobia  
- Blurred vision  
- Floaters  

Investigations  
- Slit-lamp examination  
- Fundoscopy  
- Blood tests (e.g. autoimmune markers, infection screens)  
- Imaging studies (e.g. OCT, ultrasound)  
- Anterior chamber tap if infectious aetiology suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings  
- Identification of underlying cause  
- Exclusion of secondary causes  
- Consideration of associated systemic diseases  
- National Institute for Health and Care Excellence guidelines for specific cases  

Management  
- Topical corticosteroids  
  - Reduce inflammation  
  - Control symptoms  
- Systemic corticosteroids (if severe)  
  - Tapering doses to manage inflammation  
  - Monitor for adverse effects  
- Immunosuppressive agents  
  - Use in chronic/recurrent cases  
  - Regularly assess for side effects  
- Treat underlying causes (e.g. antibiotics for infections)  
  - Target specific pathogens  
  - Adjust treatment based on response  
- Referral to ophthalmology for persistent or complicated cases  
  - Coordination of care with specialist  
  - Comprehensive follow-up for recurrence prevention  







Varicella zoster

background  
- Varicella zoster virus causes chickenpox and shingles, impacting mainly children and immunocompromised individuals.

risk factors  
- Lack of vaccination  
- Exposure to an infected individual  
- Age under 12 years  
- Immunocompromised states  
- Stress or physical trauma  

causes  
- Varicella zoster virus, a member of the herpesvirus family  
- Transmitted via respiratory droplets or direct contact with lesions  
- Reactivation of latent virus in sensory nerve ganglia  
- Contact with vesicular fluid from shingles outbreak  
- Maternal varicella infection during pregnancy  

pathophysiology  
- Virus enters through respiratory tract or mucous membranes  
- Initially replicates in regional lymph nodes  
- Spreads to skin via bloodstream, causing rash and vesicles  
- Latency established in sensory ganglia  
- Reactivation leads to shingles, often associated with age or immune suppression  

signs and symptoms  
- Fever and malaise  
- Pruritic vesicular rash that progresses from macules to vesicles  
- Rash usually appears in crops over several days  
- Shingles presents as unilateral dermatomal rash and pain  
- Complications may include pneumonia, encephalitis, and secondary bacterial infections  

investigations  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and rash appearance  
- Serology for VZV IgM and IgG antibodies when needed  
- PCR testing for confirmation in severe cases  
- Direct fluorescent antibody testing in atypical presentations  
- Differential diagnosis may include hand, foot and mouth disease or impetigo  

diagnosis  
- Based on typical clinical presentation of chickenpox or shingles  
- Consider serological testing to confirm in uncertain cases  
- Review of patient history, including vaccination status  
- Exclude other conditions with similar symptoms  
- Assessment of complications if present  

management  
- Symptomatic relief with antihistamines for itching  
  - Calamine lotion may be applied  
  - Paracetamol for fever relief  
- Antiviral treatment (e.g., aciclovir) for high-risk individuals  
  - Initiate within 24 hours of rash onset  
  - Consider for those with severe disease or complications  
- Vaccination recommendations for at-risk individuals  
  - Varicella vaccine for unvaccinated children and adults  
  - Shingles vaccine for individuals over 70 years  
- Prevent secondary infection  
  - Keep lesions clean and dry  
  - Advise on hand hygiene  
- Monitor for complications  
  - Hospitalisation may be necessary for severe cases  
  - Follow-up for persistent nerve pain after shingles  







Varicose veins

Background  
- Varicose veins are dilated, tortuous veins, commonly affecting the legs, and can lead to complications.

Risk factors  
- Genetic predisposition  
- Female sex  
- Age over 50  
- Obesity  
- Prolonged standing or sitting  

Causes  
- Venous valve incompetence  
- Increased venous pressure  
- Pregnancy  
- Hormonal changes  
- Muscle pump dysfunction  

Pathophysiology  
- Weakness in vein walls leads to dilation  
- Incompetence in valves causes blood reflux  
- Increased venous pressure results in vein tortuosity  
- Blood pooling contributes to further venous damage  
- Surrounding tissues may become ischaemic  

Signs and symptoms  
- Visible swollen veins  
- Aching, heavy legs  
- Itchiness or discomfort  
- Muscle cramps  
- Skin changes like eczema or pigmentation  

Investigations  
- Clinical examination  
- Duplex ultrasound to assess venous function  
- Ankle-brachial pressure index if arterial disease suspected  
- Detailed history taking  
- Consideration of other vessels if needed  

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on visible varicosities  
- Symptoms coincide with venous insufficiency  
- Consider duplex ultrasound findings  
- Rule out alternative diagnoses  
- Classified into the C5 and C6 categories for complications  

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications such as weight loss and exercise  
  - Encourage regular physical activity  
  - Advise proper leg elevation  
- Compression therapy using graduated compression stockings  
  - Use stockings with appropriate pressure  
  - Provide education on proper use and care  
- Surgical intervention if symptoms severe or complications arise  
  - Options include saphenofemoral ligation  
  - Consider endovenous laser therapy or radiofrequency ablation  
- Sclerotherapy for smaller varicosities  
  - Administer appropriate sclerosant  
  - Monitor for any adverse reactions  
- Regular follow-ups to monitor progression and complications  
  - Assess for recurrence of symptoms  
  - Provide ongoing support for self-management strategies  







Vasa praevia

background  
- Vasa praevia is a rare obstetric complication where fetal blood vessels cross or run near the cervical os

risk factors  
- Presence of velamentous cord insertion  
- Succenturiate placenta  
- Multiple gestations  
- Previous history of vasa praevia  
- Abnormal placentation

causes  
- Abnormalities in placental or fetal vessel attachment  
- Cord insertion abnormalities  
- Pre-existing uterine anomalies  
- Previous surgical procedures affecting the uterus  
- Closely located placental lobes

pathophysiology  
- Fetal blood vessels are unprotected by the placenta and membranes  
- Rupture of these vessels during membrane rupture leads to fetal haemorrhage  
- Hypoxia and potential death of the fetus if not detected early  
- The distance between vessels and cervical os is critical for assessment  
- Increased tension on vessels during contractions exacerbates the risk

signs and symptoms  
- Abnormal fetal heart rate patterns on monitoring  
- Sudden vaginal bleeding during labour  
- Fetal distress or signs of hypoxia  
- May be asymptomatic until rupture occurs  
- High risk of perinatal mortality if undiagnosed

investigations  
- Ultrasound imaging to detect blood vessels near the cervix  
- Colour Doppler ultrasound to assess blood flow in vessels  
- MRI for complex cases or when ultrasound is inconclusive  
- Serial ultrasound examinations for high-risk pregnancies  
- Fetal heart rate monitoring during labour 

diagnosis  
- Confirmed via ultrasound detection of fetal vessels  
- Clinical suspicion in high-risk patients  
- Diagnosis may occur during emergency situations   
- Careful assessment of cord insertion site  
- Confirmed diagnosis during labour when rupture occurs

management  
- Planned caesarean delivery is recommended prior to labour  
  - Timing typically between 34 and 37 weeks of gestation  
  - Continuous fetal monitoring during procedure  
- Close monitoring of pregnancies identified at risk  
  - Regular ultrasound evaluations  
  - Coordination with a multidisciplinary team  









Vasovagal syncope

Background  
- Vasovagal syncope is a common cause of transient loss of consciousness due to a reflex response.

Risk factors  
- Younger age  
- Female gender  
- Prolonged standing  
- Stress or anxiety  
- Heat exposure  

Causes  
- Emotional distress  
- Pain  
- Anaemia  
- Dehydration  
- Vasodilation due to stimuli  

Pathophysiology  
- Sudden drop in heart rate and blood pressure  
- Impaired cerebral blood flow  
- Activation of the vagus nerve  
- Associated with baroreceptor reflex dysfunction  
- Often triggered by a specific stimulus  

Signs and symptoms  
- Brief loss of consciousness  
- Dizziness or light-headedness  
- Nausea or vomiting  
- Pallor  
- Possible prodromal symptoms  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Postural blood pressure measurement  
- ECG to rule out arrhythmias  
- 24-hour Holter monitoring if indicated  
- Tilt-table testing in uncertain cases  

Diagnosis  
- Confirmed by history of typical fainting episodes  
- Exclusion of other causes of syncope  
- Review negative findings on investigations  
- Tilt-table testing if doubt remains  
- Consider diagnosis of 'reflex syncope'   

Management  
- Lifestyle modifications such as increased fluid and salt intake  
  - Advise on regular meals and avoiding triggers  
  - Educate on posture and physical countermeasures  
- Reassurance and education on condition prognosis  
  - Provide information on what to expect in future events  
  - Encourage gradual change of position to prevent recurrence  
- Referral to specialist if syncope is frequent or atypical  
  - Consider cardiology or neurology evaluation  
  - Assess for serious underlying conditions  
- Pharmacological treatment in severe cases  
  - Beta-blockers or fludrocortisone may be considered  
  - Monitor for side effects and efficacy  
- Emergency management for high-risk patients  
  - Educate on safe environments and avoidance of triggers  
  - Establish a management plan for emergencies  







Venous ulcers

background  
- Venous ulcers are chronic wounds resulting from inadequate venous return, primarily affecting the lower extremities

risk factors  
- Chronic venous insufficiency  
- Obesity  
- Previous leg injuries or surgeries  
- Age over 65  
- Family history of venous disease  

causes  
- Venous hypertension leading to increased pressure in veins  
- Valve dysfunction in the deep venous system  
- Thrombosis or blood clots within veins  
- Lack of calf muscle pump function  
- Prolonged immobility or sedentary lifestyle  

pathophysiology  
- Damage to the venous valves leads to venous reflux  
- Increased hydrostatic pressure causes fluid leakage into surrounding tissues  
- Tissue ischaemia and inflammation develop due to impaired blood flow  
- Skin breakdown occurs as pressure and fluids compromise integrity  
- Chronic inflammation hinders normal healing processes  

signs and symptoms  
- Irregularly shaped ulcers, often around the medial malleolus  
- Surrounding skin discolouration and swelling  
- Oedema in the lower leg  
- Pain that worsens with standing  
- Presence of venous varicosities  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment of ulcer and surrounding skin  
- Duplex ultrasound to assess venous flow  
- Ankle-brachial pressure index (ABPI) to evaluate arterial perfusion  
- Laboratory tests for infection if indicated  
- Skin biopsy if malignancy is suspected  

diagnosis  
- Clinical observation of ulcer characteristics and patient history  
- Identification of underlying venous insufficiency via imaging  
- Exclusion of arterial disease using ABPI  
- Assessment of limb mobility and functional status  
- Consideration of comorbidities impacting wound healing  

management  
- Compression therapy to improve venous return  
  - Use of graduated compression bandaging or stockings  
  - Ensure appropriate fitting and monitoring  
- Wound care and dressing  
  - Use appropriate dressings to promote healing  
  - Regularly assess for signs of infection  
- Elevation of the affected limb  
  - Encourage elevation to reduce oedema  
  - Advise leg elevation during rest  
- Treat underlying venous insufficiency  
  - Consider referral for surgical interventions if needed  
  - Explore options like sclerotherapy or laser treatment  
- Promote patient education and self-management  
  - Teach proper care of the ulcer and surrounding skin  
  - Emphasise the importance of lifestyle modifications  







Viral exanthema

Background  
- Viral exanthema encompasses various viral infections characterised by rashes in children and can indicate systemic illness

Risk factors  
- Age, particularly young children  
- Close contact with infected individuals  
- Lack of vaccination against common viruses  
- Immune compromised status  
- Skin integrity issues 

Causes  
- Measles virus  
- Rubella virus  
- Varicella-zoster virus  
- Enteroviruses  
- Human herpesvirus 6 

Pathophysiology  
- Viruses infect epithelial cells, triggering immune response  
- Cytokine release leads to inflammation and rash development  
- Viral replication varies based on the specific virus  

Signs and symptoms  
- Fever  
- Rash that spreads and changes over time  
- Respiratory symptoms like cough or nasal congestion  
- Lymphadenopathy  
- Malaise and irritability 

Investigations  
- Clinical assessment based on history and examination  
- Serology for specific viral antibodies  
- PCR assays for virus detection  
- Throat swabs for culture, if needed  
- Lumbar puncture if meningitis is suspected 

Diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on characteristic rash and associated symptoms  
- Consideration of vaccination history  
- Differential diagnosis to rule out other causes  
- Serological confirmation in atypical cases  
- Assessment of exposure history for outbreak recognition 

Management  
- Symptomatic treatment with antipyretics for fever  
  - Paracetamol or ibuprofen as needed  
- Supportive care including hydration and rest  
  - Monitoring for complications or secondary infections  
- Isolation to prevent viral spread  
  - Advise on exclusion from school or nursery if necessary  
- Vaccination strategy for prevention  
  - Ensure childhood immunisations are up to date  
- Referral to specialist if complications arise  
  - Consider paediatric infectious disease consultation







Viral gastroenteritis

Background  
- Viral gastroenteritis is an inflammation of the stomach and intestines caused by viral infections, leading to gastroenteritis symptoms

Risk factors  
- Young children and the elderly  
- Immunocompromised individuals  
- Poor hygiene practices  
- Close contact settings (e.g., schools, nursing homes)  
- Recent travel to areas with outbreaks  

Causes  
- Norovirus  
- Rotavirus  
- Adenovirus  
- Astrovirus  
- Sapovirus  

Pathophysiology  
- Viruses invade intestinal epithelium  
- Disruption of absorptive and secretory functions  
- Altered intestinal motility  
- Inflammation and immune response leading to symptoms  
- Fluid and electrolyte imbalance from diarrhoea and vomiting  

Signs and symptoms  
- Watery diarrhoea  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Abdominal cramps  
- Fever and malaise  
- Dehydration in severe cases  

Investigations  
- Clinical history and physical examination  
- Stool sample testing for viruses  
- Electrolyte panel if dehydration is suspected  
- Consideration of other causes if atypical presentation  
- Imaging tests rarely required unless complications suspected  

Diagnosis  
- Based on clinical presentation and symptoms  
- Stool testing for confirmation if necessary  
- Exclusion of other infectious or non-infectious causes  
- Assessment of dehydration severity  
- Consideration of outbreak history  

Management  
- Rehydration with oral rehydration solutions  
  - Fluid replacement to prevent dehydration  
  - Intravenous fluids if oral intake is inadequate  
- Symptomatic treatment for nausea and diarrhoea  
  - Use of antiemetics if necessary  
  - Avoidance of anti-diarrhoeal medications unless advised  
- Maintenance of good hygiene practices  
  - Frequent hand washing  
  - Isolation of symptomatic individuals  
- Patient education on dietary management  
  - Gradual reintroduction of normal diet  
  - Encouragement of bland foods  
- Monitoring for complications  
  - Regular assessment for signs of dehydration  
  - Referral to hospital if severe symptoms arise  







Viral hepatitides

background  
- Viral hepatitis includes inflammation of the liver caused by various viruses, leading to acute or chronic liver disease  

risk factors  
- Unprotected sexual contact  
- Sharing needles or drug paraphernalia  
- Occupational exposure to blood  
- Travel to endemic areas  
- Underlying liver disease  

causes  
- Hepatitis A virus (*HAV*)  
- Hepatitis B virus (*HBV*)  
- Hepatitis C virus (*HCV*)  
- Hepatitis D virus (*HDV*)  
- Hepatitis E virus (*HEV*)  

pathophysiology  
- Viral replication leads to hepatocyte damage  
- Immune response causes liver inflammation  
- Possible progression to fibrosis or cirrhosis  
- Risk of hepatocellular carcinoma with chronic infection  
- Disruption of normal liver function  

signs and symptoms  
- Jaundice  
- Fatigue and malaise  
- Abdominal pain or discomfort  
- Dark urine and pale stools  
- Loss of appetite and nausea  

investigations  
- Liver function tests  
- Serological tests for viral antigens and antibodies  
- Polymerase chain reaction (PCR) for viral RNA or DNA  
- Liver biopsy if indicated  
- Imaging studies (e.g., ultrasound)  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history and examination findings  
- Positive serological markers for specific viruses  
- Elevated liver enzymes consistent with hepatitis  
- Imaging showing liver abnormalities  
- Exclusion of other causes of hepatitis  

management  
- Supportive care for acute hepatitis  
  - Rest and hydration  
  - Symptom management  
- Antiviral therapy for chronic hepatitis  
  - Nucleos(t)ide analogues for *HBV*  
  - Direct-acting antivirals for *HCV*  
- Vaccination for *HAV* and *HBV* prevention  
  - High-risk populations should be targeted  
  - Post-exposure prophylaxis if indicated  
- Monitoring for liver disease progression  
  - Regular liver function tests  
  - Liver cancer screening in high-risk patients  
- Lifestyle modifications  
  - Alcohol avoidance  
  - Nutrition and weight management  







Visual field defects

background  
- Visual field defects involve loss of part or all of the visual field and can indicate underlying neurological conditions

risk factors  
- Age-related changes  
- History of eye conditions  
- Systemic diseases such as diabetes  
- Family history of visual disorders  
- Head trauma

causes  
- Glaucoma  
- Brain tumours  
- Stroke affecting the visual pathways  
- Retinal detachment  
- Multiple sclerosis

pathophysiology  
- Damage to optic pathways disrupts visual signal transmission  
- Retinal damage can affect photoreceptors and their connections  
- Lesions in the visual cortex produce characteristic field losses  
- Pathways affected depend on the location and extent of damage  
- Compensatory mechanisms may develop over time

signs and symptoms  
- Decreased visual acuity  
- Difficulty with peripheral vision  
- Scintillating scotomas or flashes of light  
- Blind spots or scotomas  
- Inability to see objects in certain visual fields

investigations  
- Visual field testing (perimetry) to map visual loss  
- Optical coherence tomography for retinal assessment  
- MRI or CT scan to identify brain lesions  
- Fundoscopy to evaluate the optic nerve and retina  
- Blood tests for systemic causes

diagnosis  
- Clinical assessment based on history and symptoms  
- Confirmation through visual field testing results  
- Imaging studies to determine structural causes  
- Differential diagnosis to exclude other conditions  
- Multidisciplinary evaluation if neurological involvement is suspected

management  
- Treat underlying cause (e.g. glaucoma medication, surgical intervention)  
  - Medications may lower intraocular pressure  
  - Surgery for lens replacement or retinal repair  
- Rehabilitation with visual aids and training  
  - Low vision support services  
  - Orientation and mobility training  
- Regular monitoring of visual fields  
  - Follow-up appointments to track changes  
  - Adjustments to management based on progression  
- Patient education on visual impairment  
  - Provide resources for coping strategies  
  - Discuss implications of vision loss on daily life  
- Referral to specialists as necessary  
  - Ophthalmologist for eye-related issues  
  - Neurologist for suspected neurological causes







Vitamin B12 and/or folate deficiency

background  
- Vitamin B12 and folate deficiencies can lead to megaloblastic anaemia and neurological disorders  

risk factors  
- Vegan or vegetarian diet without supplementation  
- History of gastric surgery or atrophic gastritis  
- Malabsorption syndromes such as coeliac disease  
- Chronic alcoholism  
- Certain medications (e.g., methotrexate)  

causes  
- Inadequate dietary intake of Vitamin B12 or folate  
- Malabsorption due to intestinal conditions  
- Increased physiological needs (e.g., pregnancy)  
- Autoimmune conditions like pernicious anaemia  
- Medications that interfere with absorption  

pathophysiology  
- Impaired DNA synthesis resulting in ineffective erythropoiesis  
- Abnormal red blood cell development leading to megaloblastic changes  
- Neurological damage due to Vitamin B12 deficiency affecting myelin  

signs and symptoms  
- Fatigue and weakness  
- Pale or jaundiced skin  
- Glossitis and angular cheilitis  
- Neurological symptoms such as paraesthesia or balance issues  
- Shortness of breath and palpitations  

investigations  
- Full blood count showing macrocytic anaemia  
- Blood film showing megaloblastic changes  
- Serum Vitamin B12 and folate levels  
- Methylmalonic acid and homocysteine levels (for B12)  
- Bone marrow biopsy if required  

diagnosis  
- Confirmed by low serum Vitamin B12 or folate levels  
- Response to supplementation can aid in differentiation  
- Exclude other causes of macrocytic anaemia  
- Check for signs of neurological involvement  
- Assess for underlying causes or risk factors  

management  
- Oral or intramuscular Vitamin B12 supplementation  
  - Consider lifelong treatment for pernicious anaemia  
  - Monitor for response with follow-up bloods  
- Oral folate supplementation for folate deficiency  
  - Educate on dietary sources of folate  
  - Monitor duration based on clinical response  
- Address underlying causes of deficiency  
  - Manage malabsorption syndromes  
  - Review medications affecting absorption  
- Regular monitoring of blood counts and vitamin levels  
  - Adjust supplementation based on response  
  - Provide dietary advice  
- Referral to specialist if no improvement  
  - Consider haematology for severe cases  
  - Neurology referral for persistent neurological symptoms  







Volvulus

background  
- A volvulus is a surgical emergency resulting from the twisting of a loop of intestine, leading to obstruction and ischaemia  

risk factors  
- Age over 65  
- Chronic constipation  
- Previous abdominal surgery  
- Conditions causing intestinal malrotation  
- Pregnant women  

causes  
- Sigmoid volvulus due to chronic constipation  
- Caecal volvulus often in older adults  
- Intestinal malrotation in younger patients  
- Tumours leading to intestinal obstruction  
- Adhesions from previous surgeries  

pathophysiology  
- Twisting of the intestine causes obstruction of blood supply  
- Ischaemia and necrosis may occur if not promptly relieved  
- Bacterial overgrowth can lead to peritonitis  
- Fluid and electrolyte imbalances develop from obstruction  
- Symptoms worsen rapidly, typically within hours  

signs and symptoms  
- Abdominal pain (often colicky)  
- Abdominal distension  
- Nausea and vomiting  
- Constipation or obstipation (no passage of stool)  
- Signs of peritonitis in advanced cases  

investigations  
- Abdominal X-ray to identify large bowel obstruction  
- CT scan for detailed imaging of the abdomen  
- Ultrasound for specific cases, particularly in children  
- Blood tests to assess for infection or electrolyte imbalance  
- Assessment for signs of dehydration  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and physical examination  
- Imaging studies confirming the site and nature of obstruction  
- Distended bowel loops observed in radiological findings  
- Exclusion of other causes of abdominal pain and obstruction  
- Urgent management is essential to reduce complications  

management  
- Immediate hospital admission for suspected volvulus  
  - Monitor vital signs and hydration status  
  - Provide intravenous fluids as necessary  
- Decompression using nasogastric tube for bowel relief  
  - And/or endoscopic detorsion for sigmoid volvulus  
  - Surgical intervention if ischaemia or perforation is suspected  
- Surgery to detorse and fixate the bowel as needed  
  - Resection of necrosed bowel if indicated  
  - Consider long-term preventative measures in high-risk patients  
- Post-operative care covering fluid management and nutrition  
  - Gradual reintroduction of oral intake  
  - Monitor for potential complications like infection  
- Referral for long-term follow-up in recurrent cases  
  - Assess gastrointestinal function post-surgery  
  - Determine need for behavioural or dietary modifications  







VTE in pregnancy and puerperium

background  
- Venous thromboembolism (VTE) is a significant cause of morbidity and mortality in pregnancy and the puerperium

risk factors  
- Previous history of VTE  
- Obesity (BMI ≥30)  
- Prolonged immobilisation (e.g., prolonged bed rest)  
- Inherited thrombophilia (e.g., Factor V Leiden)  
- Major surgery during pregnancy or postpartum  

causes  
- Physiological changes in coagulation during pregnancy  
- Decreased venous return from compression of pelvic veins  
- Increased venous stasis due to enlarged uterus  
- Hormonal influences, particularly increased oestrogen levels  
- Vessel wall injury during pregnancy or peri-partum  

pathophysiology  
- Hypercoagulable state is established due to increased levels of clotting factors  
- Decreased fibrinolysis contributes to thrombus formation  
- Venous stasis occurs due to anatomical changes  
- Endothelial injury can occur from mechanical factors  
- Resultant clots can lead to deep vein thrombosis (DVT) or pulmonary embolism (PE)  

signs and symptoms  
- Swelling, pain, or tenderness in one leg (DVT)  
- Warmth or redness in affected area (DVT)  
- Sudden onset of dyspnoea (PE)  
- Chest pain, which may worsen with deep breathing (PE)  
- Coughing up blood (haemoptysis) (PE)  

investigations  
- Doppler ultrasound of the affected limb for DVT  
- CT pulmonary angiography for suspected PE  
- D-dimer testing may assist in evaluating suspected VTE  
- Clinical probability scoring tools (e.g., Wells score)  
- Blood tests to check for inherited thrombophilia if indicated  

diagnosis  
- Confirm DVT via Doppler ultrasound in symptomatic patients  
- Use CT pulmonary angiography to confirm PE in high-risk individuals  
- Clinical assessment must weigh risk factors and symptoms  
- Consider differential diagnoses for chest pain and leg swelling  
- Utilise laboratory results in conjunction with clinical findings  

management  
- Prophylaxis with low molecular weight heparin (LMWH) in high-risk pregnancies  
   - Continue until 6 weeks postpartum  
   - Adjust dosage according to weight and renal function  
- Treatment of established VTE with anticoagulation (e.g., LMWH)  
   - Review need for unfractionated heparin in severe cases  
   - Transition to warfarin if long-term anticoagulation is required  
- Monitor for complications during anticoagulation therapy  
   - Check for signs of bleeding  
   - Regular follow-up required for high-risk patients  
- Offer education on signs and symptoms of VTE  
   - Provide information on when to seek medical attention  
   - Encourage lifestyle modifications to reduce risk  
- Collaborate with multidisciplinary teams for complex cases  
   - Involve obstetricians, haematologists, and pharmacists  
   - Ensure comprehensive care throughout pregnancy and postpartum  







Wernicke’s encephalopathy

background  
- Wernicke's encephalopathy is an acute neurological condition caused by thiamine deficiency, often associated with alcohol misuse

risk factors  
- Chronic alcoholism  
- Malnutrition or dietary deficiencies  
- Gastrointestinal disorders affecting absorption  
- Hyperemesis gravidarum in pregnancy  
- Chronic infection or beriberi  

causes  
- Thiamine (Vitamin B1) deficiency  
- Alcohol malabsorption  
- Insufficient dietary intake  
- Prolonged vomiting  
- Increased metabolic demands (e.g., during severe illness)  

pathophysiology  
- Thiamine is essential for carbohydrate metabolism and neurotransmitter synthesis  
- Deficiency leads to impaired energy production in neurons  
- Results in neuronal injury and cell death in key brain areas  
- Affects the thalamus, hypothalamus, and brainstem  
- Causes disruption in normal neurological function  

signs and symptoms  
- Ocular abnormalities (ophthalmoplegia, nystagmus)  
- Ataxia and gait disturbances  
- Altered mental status (confusion, disorientation)  
- Hypotension and hypothermia  
- Peripheral neuropathy may also occur  

investigations  
- Clinical assessment and history taking  
- Blood tests for thiamine levels (though not always routine)  
- MRI or CT scan to rule out other causes  
- Neurological examination  
- Alcohol use assessment  

diagnosis  
- Clinical diagnosis based on history and symptoms  
- Acute confusion, ophthalmoplegia, and ataxia are key signs  
- Consider exclusion of other neurological disorders  
- Early recognition is critical to avoid progression  
- Diagnosis should prompt immediate treatment  

management  
- Administer thiamine intravenously (e.g., 200-500 mg for 2-3 days)  
  - Followed by oral thiamine supplementation  
- Address any underlying conditions or contributing factors  
  - Nutritional support and dietary advice  
- Monitor for response to treatment and symptom improvement  
  - Regular assessment of neurological function  
- Alcohol cessation support if relevant  
  - Pharmacological or psychosocial interventions as needed  
- Consider safety and rehabilitation measures if ataxia persists  
  - Occupational therapy referral if applicable  







Whooping cough

background  
- Whooping cough is a highly infectious respiratory disease caused by *Bordetella pertussis*  

risk factors  
- Unvaccinated individuals  
- Infants under 12 months  
- Pregnant women in their third trimester  
- Close contact with infected persons  
- Decreased vaccine coverage  

causes  
- Infection with *Bordetella pertussis*  
- Secondary bacterial infections  
- Lack of vaccination or incomplete vaccination schedule  
- Transmission via respiratory droplets  
- Increased circulation of the pathogen in the community  

pathophysiology  
- Inhalation of *Bordetella pertussis* bacteria  
- Bacteria attach to ciliated epithelial cells of the nasopharynx  
- Release of toxins leading to inflammation and respiratory symptoms  
- Disruption of normal ciliated function  
- Immune response contributes to symptoms  

signs and symptoms  
- Severe coughing fits  
- Characteristic ‘whoop’ sound during inhalation  
- Post-tussive vomiting  
- Apnoea in infants  
- Associated fever and runny nose  

investigations  
- Nasopharyngeal swab for PCR testing  
- Culture of *Bordetella pertussis* from respiratory secretions  
- Serological tests for antibodies (IgG, IgA)  
- Complete blood count to assess leukocytosis  
- Chest X-ray to rule out complications  

diagnosis  
- Clinical history of persistent cough lasting more than 2 weeks  
- Presence of paroxysmal coughing fits or ‘whoop’  
- Laboratory confirmation via PCR or culture  
- Assessment of vaccination status  
- Exclusion of other respiratory infections  

management  
- Administer macrolide antibiotics (e.g. azithromycin or clarithromycin)  
  - Early treatment recommended to reduce transmission  
  - Most effective in the catarrhal stage  
- Provide supportive care  
  - Ensure adequate hydration  
  - Monitor for complications, especially in infants  
- Vaccination for unvaccinated contacts  
  - Offer DTaP/IPV/Hib vaccine to infants  
  - Consider Tdap booster in pregnant women  
- Advise on infection control  
  - Isolation of infected individuals  
  - Encourage hand hygiene and respiratory etiquette  
- Review and follow-up  
  - Monitor symptom resolution and potential complications  
  - Educate about the disease and prevention measures
